This book is dedicated to the few dozen pioneers of industrial design who, in the 1920s and 1930s, and in spite of the Great Depression that gripped America, embraced the Machine Age and transformed millions of   
mass-produced products from clumsy, ugly, mechanical devices into   
svelte, attractive, modern designs appropriate to the 20th century.

## Acknowledgments

I am indebted to so many for their generous assistance in the development of this book. Among these are my good friend and design historian colleague, Victoria Matranga, H/IDSA (honorary member of Industrial Designers Society ofAmerica), who shares my passion in recognizing pioneers of the industrial design profession, and documenting their work and contributions to the field. She has been design programs coordinator for the International Housewares Association (IHA) for many years, organizing juries for annual IHA student design competitions, and serving as IHA's historian, publicist, and researcher. She is also an nsuan cahx u firms, and private organizations, as well as an accomplished writer of design history. For this book, she shared numerous photographs and vital facts about pioneer designers from her vast collection of design history information.

My late friend, Tucker Madawick, before his death in 2006, graciously provided me with extensive literature and photos concerning the history of automotive design and many of its pioneers, for which I am eternally grateful. His career only started in 1939, so he was not one o the original pioneers of the early 130s, but his artistic talent, business sense, leadership skills, and personality exceeded many of his worthy predecessors. He was a tower of strength to the profession, and is sorely missed.

## Preface

In January 1930, just three months into the Great Depression, my father bought a used 1927 Studebaker and took it on a two-week shakedown drive (at 30 miles per hour) from Reading, Pennsylvania, to Miami, Florida, and back, before he reported to his new job (he haust st iol. Durig he ,he wrote is 18-yr fncée my oin June.

When my mother passed away in 1983, I found these letters that she had saved (he had died 30 years before her). They included trivial remarks and jokes from radio comedians of the time (Amos and Andy), as well as private phrases of endearment, descriptions of the weather, sights, accommodations and landmarks—all the ordinary things anyone might write to a loved one back home. What caught my attention, however, was the unusual way he referred to his c  Te letter, "I took the machine to Lansdale and I guess I will go over Friday night and get it."

I was puzzled: machine? Why didn't he call it the automobile? The auto? The car? The Studebaker? Why "the machine"? I now realize that like many in his generation who bought their first cars, he was incredibly proud to possess his very own "machine"! Not the machines exclusive to industry or corporations, such as steam engines, airplanes, zeppelins, skyscrapers, or dynamos that had enthralled the public for decades, but finally, his very own, personal, mechanical "machine," providing him the freedom to travel for fun, or for jobs in other places. The automobile was the magic carpet of the 20th century. People were no longer destined, like their parents and ancestors, to remain near the place they were born. My father was now emotionally part of, and committed to, the future: the "Machine Age," as were so many others of his day.

The Machine Age was a time when public fascination with machines and the future inspired a new art form, "modern design," which transformed ordinary consumer products from mechanical curiosities into futuristic fantasies. The creators of these designs were called industrial designers, and their work inspired me to become one of them. As I pursued my lifelong career of industrial design, I began to realize that it hardly existed before my birth. I wondered, Where did it come from? How and why did it begin? Who or what started it? As my career progressed, I met and interviewed many founding designers who had in fact invented it, and I began to appreciate the incredible change in industry and popular culture that they had accomplished. So, after my retirement, I began to research these remarkable men and women, realizing that when they died, their role in this extraordinary period of history would be lost.

As a lifelong practitioner of industrial design, I already had a library ofbooks, magazines, professional society newsletters, and articles concerning the early profession, but about 20 years ago, I began writing obituaries of deceased designers for my professional organization by referring to my files or by contacting their descendants. I also interviewed or corresponded with those who had retired in order to learn more about their early careers. Some provided informal autobiographies or documents they had written, while others related incredible stories about their early experiences. In some instances, they had donated their papers to various museum collections.

Most books about individual industrial designers focus on the handful of pioneer designers who became public celebrities in the 1930s, such as Raymond Loewy, Walter Dorwin Teague, Harold Van Doren, Norman Bel Geddes, or Henry Dreyfuss, all of whom wrote books about their careers and dominated the field well into the 1960s. Some may imagine that these few alone created the incredibly rapid and complete transformation of our physical environment to modern design in the 1930s, but this is not the case. There were dozens of others who shared in the creation of this new profession, some of whom preceded the big names by years, but who are little known or remembered today.

This book is about the origins of industrial design in that time of economic trauma, its evolution over the years, and in particular the remarkable, relatively small group of individuals who conceived and created it. Their work includes many of the most popular design icons of the 20th century. This is the first time their careers and biographies have been merged into a single, chronological narrative of the rise of the profession. You will meet them, one by one, as they enter the business arena as young men and women, and follow their careers, some to fame and fortune, others to anonymity.

Their unusual backgrounds, accomplishments, and careers, as can best be known at this time, are followed until their demise. Most were born between 1895 and 1910, and a few even survived into the 21st century. Of course, after these pioneers created the profession in the h myself, until there are now more than 50,000 industrial designers. While a number of these designers are mentioned in the book as their ranks grew through the 1940s, 1950s, 1960s, and later, it is those few pioneers who started it all who are the stars and focus of this book.

# Introduction

F helpful to summarize the overall context of the time.

The "Macieis  n er ha ers ot ust a whc  be around in industry or a century before, but to the new way in which the public perceived them. In the middle of the 120s, people suddenly realized that mechanical machines were not just for large-scale industrial purposes, but that they actually could purchase machines sonal use around the home. They began to regard machines as personal possessions, rather than abstract technology.

At thesame time peolelso bemeware  style,  quality previusy cnsiderny in decorative products for display in the home, but oflittle significance in functional, mechanical products, such as their new household appliances. Now that they were able to afford such products, and regarded them as personal possessions, however, they were extremely proud of them and wanted them to be seen by friends and neighbors as evidence of their increasing lcs. S tey nhe et yjus i he u designs being promoted by upscale department stores as fashionable.

In addition to these new perceptions, people began to realize that they could influence the way these products looked and functioned by expressing their preference of competing products on the market through purchase choice, or through customer surveys, commonly used by advertising agencies. Such public demand soon caused some manufacturers of funcehanl pduc pyn hepn oduc artists to redesign them. Most, however, saw no such need, since business was booming, and changing tooling was costly.

In the midst of these interrelated developments, the Great Depression hit in 1929, and manufacturers not only lost half of their sales volume, but faced going out of business completely, which many in fact did. They became desperate, and when they learned of industrial designers in 1934, they rushed to locate a design consultant, only to find that the few available were already over their heads with work. Manufacturers' demand for such designers was so intense that it attracted designers from numerous other fields to fll the need, and the industrywide transformation of industrial products into modern styles had begun. It has not stopped since. They were, and are, called industrial designers.

Today there are more than 50,000 industrial designers in the United States. By the time the profession of industrial design first became well known, in 1935, there were only about 36 piners who wee pracicing i thefeld They cme from al walk f lie n busiesse, mostly in the creative arts, to address a national problem: the economy was as bad as it had ever been, unemployment was at 25 percent, and industry was on life support because consumers were not spending. America was far behind Europe in design, particularly in massproduced electromechanical consumer products, because manufacturers of these products relied solely on engineers for their design. Mechanical products were devoid of humanistic attributes such as attractive appearance, convenience of use, or the latest styles.

The pioneer "machine designers," primarily commercial artists, stage designers, and craftsmen, changed all this within a decade, by working with engineers, salesmen, and the public to transform the man-made landscape into what we now call the modern world by redesigning mechanical products to become sleek, stylish, shiny, and attractive. Their creative work attracted consumers, who began spending the money they had been hoarding because of bad economic times. Increased sales reinvigorated industry, brought America out of the Depression, and led to the highest standard of living in the world after World War II.

Awill   heuy objective sought by many European design educators since the beginning of the 20th century to provide well-designed products to the masses. Before that, 19th-century craftsmen had dreamed of providing ordinary people with handcrafted designs previously only affordable by the wealthy. Now, in the midst of the Great Depression, the machine designers accomplished thisdream by founding a new profession, whichcreatedffordable and artistic designs available in mass-produced quantities.

The original pioneer designers continued to work diligently, and by the end of the 1930s they dominated the rapidly growing field. After World War II, some headed huge consulting firms or corporate design departments to meet the enormous postwar consumer demand for new and better products. As the leaders of the profession, the pioneers confirmed the status of their activity as a legal profession, established professional organizations, recommended educational programs, implemented codes ofethics, and initiated national annual design awards prrams.Aew bcmnteational celeri and eheratiotainald organizations, or assisted in the economic and design development of war-torn nations after World War II as design consultants abroad. By the time some of the pioneers approached retirement, they had established large, successful organizations that continue to this day.

This is the story of those few dozen pioneers who founded modern industrial design in product at a time.

## Chapter 1

## The "Artless" and "Art" Industries

The Machine Age (19181941), as it was known even at the time, not only included the Great Depression, but also the preceding decade after World War I when America led the worlnindustrialization, scice, tehnology, engineering, and consuerism. Excet for brief postwar depression from 1920 to 1921, as President Warren Harding took office, the 1920s in America were a period of economic boom and prosperity, as American industry returned to peacetime production to meet consumer demand for the many new mechanical b     - tities and at affordable prices.

Among these were automobiles, led by Ford's Model T, which by 1918 comprised 50 percent of the auto market and which epitomized American mass production, also known then as Fordism. Many home appliances used electricity, which, although only half the country was electrified, mostly in urban areas, was becoming more widespread. Machines, large and small, were the leading edge of technology. Refrigerators were replacing iceboxes, ranges were replacing woodstoves, and vacuum cleaners were replacing carpet sweepers. There were electrical appliances such as mixers, fry pans, coffee pots, waffle irons, juicers, irons, roasters, and toatrs. To many consumers, many of whom were women, these industrial products for the kitchen were perceived to be as magical a technology as personal home robots would seem to us today.

Prices sound very reasonable today (\$260 for the Model T and \$52 for a vacuum cleaner in 1918), but in 2013 dollars, the Ford would cost \$4,020 and the vacuum cleaner \$804. Still, many households had good wages (\$2,000 per year in 1918, or \$30,700 in 2013 dollars), and since almost 50 percent of working-class wives worked for wages, many were two-income families. Credit buying was also available. Can you imagine paying 40 percent of your annual salary for a vacuum cleaner? Still, to many families, and with installment plans becoming quite common, it seemed like the American Dream coming true. After 50 years of invention and development of a massive industrial infrastructure, including gigantic steam engines, hydroelectric plants, electrified cities, factories, railroads, bridges, and paved roads, average citizens were finally seeing the products of mass production and industrialization in their own homes. Technology had finally reached their personal, daily lives.

The production system had been refined, improved, and made more efficient by Philadelphia engineer Frederick Winslow Taylor (1856-1915), who in 1911 published The Principles of Scientific Management, a system of efficiency experts using stopwatches to measure every step of production in order to reduce time and labor. This process of industrial organization became known as Taylorism, and was known everywhere as the companion to Fordism, the twin jewels in the crown of mass production.

All mass-produced, new, electrical, and mechanical consumer products were invented, designed, and built by engineers, the backbone and champions of 20th-century industrialization. Many were famous and known worldwide. Herbert Hoover, who would become U.S. president in 1928, was himself a wealthy mining engineer. Thomas Edison (1847-1931), Henry Ford (18631947), and Orville Wright (18711948) were still alive and being celebrated as American heroes as late as 1929.

They had brought electric light, the phonograph, the automobile, and the airplane to the world. All engineers were highly respected and admired as heroes of the age. The number of engineers had increased exponentially since 1870, when there were only a hundred. By 1900 there were 45,000, and by 1930, half a million. They were building modern bridges, dams, roads, skyscrapers, airplanes, ships and trains, as well as consumer products such as automobiles, cash registers, telephones, appliances, industrial machinery, clocks, tractors, shovels and pitchforks.

Engineers were trained in hundreds of technical schools and were familiar with a range of materials, stresses, structures, manufacturing processes, costs, and the many technologies, such as electricity, steam, and combustion engines, that were required to power the manufacturing equipment or the mechanical devices. The overriding objective of all engineers was to create a physical structure which, whether a suspension bridge, an airplane, a vacuum cleaner, or a toaster, was strictly functional, economical, and efficient. These were, after all, machines intended to provide humans with transportation, power, and labor saving, and these were the functions expected, highly admired and acclaimed by the public.

Most consumers were delighted with any of the new mass-produced electromechanical goods being made available, despite the fact that they were generally rather mechanical looking and ungainly in appearance, and although for many they were still rather expensive. But their mechanistic look was initially a positive image of technology. The main thing was that they performed their functional objective, were available at reasonable prices, and offered a higher standard of everyday living. For those many new consumers who had never had a vacuum cleaner before, its function, not its appearance, was of primary interest and the greatest source of user satisfaction. Functional performance, power, and labor saving were the coins of the realm, and all the new appliance manufacturers competed successfully on that basis.

Therefore, there were few "artists" needed or desired in the new mechanical product industries, other than those that applied a few surface decorations to the finished product, jst peihe geti tokn un" ri u. It s then that these products, which included autos, locomotives, cash registers, clocks, optical goos, sewingmachines, washingmachines, fountain pens, refrigerators, and electrical/mehaniclmachinery, were enerally cateorize t the timeas he rtless inustris,"beuy were generally designed and produced by engineers, not artists.

W eMh t,"h orative objects or crafts with limited production. Not only did machines of mass production manufacture these mechanical products in the millions, but in individual function and character, the products themselves were in fact mechanical, or machinelike devices, often with multiple moving components, such as motors, mechanisms, or engines, and were often operated by electricity.

to show the look and function of anobject before it is built or made."A designer, o course, is the person producing such a plan or drawing. Designers differ frorn artists in that they create workthathas  urpotherhan thatndividual selexpreisthebeauty.Ote the purpose is to meet the needs of an individual, a company, an audience, a functional operation, or all combined.

W  l ativual lh rhiyhri ple produced products that previously were designe solely by engineers, inordr to make them more attractive to consumers, to make them more convenient and comfortable for users, to use more appropriate and economical materials, and yet to accommodate the normal business interestsof cost, schedules, and manufacturing ease.

T sketches of their ideas to communicate them to others, but to produce mechanical drawings to instruct engineers in the speciic dimensions and fnal shape to be manufactured by production tools.They had to work, not  an individual, but s a tem member with oth pecialized industry disciplines, such as engineering, marketing, manufacturing, and sales. In short, they became multitalented artists-designers-engineers-businessmen-salesmen, inventing a brand new profession that as yet had no specific name.

The term "artless industries" had evolved to distinguish certain mechanical products from the traditional "art industries," as defined by the Twelfth Exhibition of Contemporary American Industrial Art at the Metropolitan Museum of Art, New York, October 12 to November 22, 131.' The "art industries" were the only industrial "art" supported, promoted, and exhibited by museums. These were industries producing decorative arts, which included ceramics, glassware, enamelwork, furniture, leatherwork, printing, jewelry, art metalwork, lighting fixtures, carpets, drapes, silverware, textiles, and wallpaper, or any product intended as a decorative object for the home. A number of these were in fact functional as well as decorative, so their designers were necessarily involved with form and function as well as surface decoration.

By and large, these "decorative arts" industries grew out of the Arts and Crafts movement of the 19th century, where skilled craftspeople originally selected appropriate materials and made both decorative and functional objects individually by hand, shaping and/or decorating the surfaces manually or by hand-operated machines of the era. The craftsman was in control ofall the physical and functional aspects of a product, as well as its final appearance. Even after machines were developed to produce such products in quantity, many were still designed before manufacture by craftspeople, who therefore still controlled both the functional form and its external decorative treatments.

This personal involvement of a single individual craftsman in the total design of a particular product was common historically, and resulted in famous craftsmen names such as Paul Revere (silverware), Thomas Chippendale (furniture), Eli Terry (clocks), and Duncan Phyfe (furniture). Ever since Leonardo DaVinci (1452-1519), art and engineering had gone hand in hand. From the beginning of industrialization, art and industry were closely related. In the early 19th century, the "mechanic arts" or "useful arts," as they were called, included a wide range of technical skills, such as mechanical design, decorative design, mechanical drawing, materials analysis, crafts, machine design, production planning, art, factory design, physics, mathematics, etc. Over the century, as industrialization advanced and as educational institutions tried to teach such comprehensive technical skills, these all became individually named specialties and were separated from one other. Thus did art and engineering become separate disciplines, with specialized skills and differing motives.

As 20th-century manufacturing produced more and more consumer products in greater and greater quantities, and as factories become more complex technologically, more impersonal, and more industrial in character, fewer and fewer American craftspeople or artists were interested in the artless industries dominated by engineers, dirty machinery, time clocks, and crass commercialism. Even the art industries held limited appeal to artists because such applied art generally went unrecognized and required working nine to five in a factory environment that was less than clean, quiet, or dignified.

So even though many educational institutions offered programs in "industrial art" or p rt,mo piiart tend  preer e rts  pigo scue, where they could receive personal recognition and prestige as exhibitors in galleries or museums and could work in their own personal studios. This was the essential conclusion of a \$120,000 comprehensive study of industrial arts education from 1920 to 1922 by Charles R. Richards of the Cooper Union, funded by the General Education Board of the federal government.3

At the time, the terms "industrial art" and "applied art" were the most commonly used terms for art careers in industry, and as noted above, they were not popular choices for aspiring artists. But there was another, more obscure term used occasionally in the fine arts field since 1: "industrial design," also referring to the use of artists for industrial purposes. This term had more recently been specifically defined for industrial purposes in 1913 by the U.S. commissioner of patents, Edward B. Moore, who urged modifications of existing regulations to protect the property of "industrial design, the distinguishing form of products that have marketable value" (italics in original).

The existing regulations he referred to were design patents, which protected the exterior appearance of an object against copying since 1842, when they were first initiated by the patent office for "any new and original shape or configuration of any article of manufacture not known or used by others before."4 Many museums encouraged the copying of European products by American manufacturers because they were not protected outside of the country of origin. Commissioner Moore was concerned that even U.S. design patents were not being enforced or upheld aggressively in America, thus discouraging innovative designs by Americans.

America found itself in a conflicted situation in the 1920s. It commanded the most productive manufacturing industry in the world, as well as the most lucrative market for consumer products. Factories were making as many products as possible, and a stable economy enabled a mass market of middle-class consumers to buy as many products as industry could produce. Nevertheless, a few art museums and organizations, who were familiar with the European potential to dominate world markets, were warning that America was far behind Europe in applied arts and was in desperate need of more future designers in industry to compete globally. In 1918, Walter Sargent (18681927), director of the Department of Fine and Industrial Art at the University of Chicago, had written,

T l ni a i hal  u thaoval  ab n   il need its own .. we shall have to depend on ourselves more than in the past, not only for designers, but also for styles of design.

Meanwhile, during the same time, the situation in Europe was quite different. While America enjoyed the postwar benefits of mass production and a strong economy, Europeans were suffering from wartime devastation and inflation, with virtually no industrial production to restore economic health and recovery, and practically no consumer markets. America was fortunate in that it had no foreign competition.

Epee before the war. In 1909, Italian poet Filippo Tommaso Marinetti (18761944) founded a fine a movmentallFuturi, whi promoe spee, tenolgy, yuh,autobe, and u  WW European countri from cmpeting effectively with U.S. manufacturing for another 30 yars. In Germany, he march  Nationalt Socialim would within ten years lad inexorably o Hitler and German rearmament, and n Russia, the Bolsheviks would transform the cuntry into communism, where nationalized industry would support the government, not individuals.

However, Europeans still led the world in modern art. In fact, the modernist movement h beu  r   uye,, uc I n  Nv yuc Henri Matisse, Fernand Léger, Georges Braque, Marcel Duchamp, Francis Picabia, and André . introduced Cubism, an art movement in which objects are analyzed, broken up, and reassembled in an abstract form, often depicting objects from a multitude of viewpoints. Cubism has bee cer heos ifluenlr oven the 0th cury feir suue, architecture, and music.

The works of many of these European artists and a few Americans were first shown in America at the International Exhibition of Modern Art in New York in 1913, the so-called Armory Show, since it was held at the city's 69th Infantry Regiment Armory, at Lexington and 26th Street. Most American viewers, including President Theodore Roosevelt, rejected the new styles as obscene or primitive, certainly not art, and most American artists continued to paint or sculpt in highly traditional styles. America was not yet ready for modern art.

In 1914, as World War I began, modern artist Duchamp (1887-1968) took a step that challenged the very definition of art. He took a common manufactured wire bottle rack, signed his name to it, and declared that it was art because he said it was art. In 1915, he and Francis Picabia (1879-1953) both visited New York and were publicized as prominent French artists who for the first time were, as they said, "seeking America in matters of art." Picabia praised what he saw:

I adore New York. There is much about it, which is like the Paris of the old days.. I have been profoundly impressed by the vast mechanical development in America. The machine has become more than a mere adjunct of human life.. I intend to work on until I attain the pinnacle of mechanical symbolism.6

Americans were flattered to be recognized and admired by famous French artists, and Picabia was true to his word. He soon produced a number of drawings inspired by spark plugs, meshing gears, pistons, and cylinders that were published by Alfred Steiglitz's art journal, . Picabia's work clearly assumed that mechanical objects were worthy as works of art, a concept that at the time most Americans failed to share.

The unconventional work of European artists had no effect whatsoever upon the 1915 Panama-Pacific International Exposition in San Francisco, which had been planned since 1911, primarily to celebrate the completion of the Panama Canal, but also to herald the recovery of San Francisco from the disastrous effects of its 1906 earthquake.

The architecture, sculpture, and murals featured were clearly in the traditions of the 19th century, with highly realistic, classical, but allegorical statues and murals, mostly nudes, suggesting abstract ideas of abundance, progress, conquest, creation, power, etc., and historical depictions of conquistadors, pirates, and miners. The art at the exposition was a public display of America's imitation of European art as it was in the 1870s, not an appropriate expression of

America's 20th-century leadership in technology, science, or manufacturing. It was art o the past. A more effective global advertisement of America's obsolescence in art and design could not be imagined.

Yet despite criticism from traditionalists, modern art marched forward. In 1917 Duchamp submitted a mass-produced ceramic urinal, titled Fountain, to the Independent Artists Exhibition in New York as "art," which aroused outraged protests but nevertheless established a fundamental principle of modern art: art is whatever the artist chooses to define as art. In this case, it also suggested that common utilitarian objects could be viewed as art, which may have been a surprise to the manufacturer of the urinal, or any company making similar utilitarian products.

Other European countries evolved independent styles of modern art. In 1917, Piet Mondrian (1872-1944) and Theo van Doesburg (1893-1931) initiated the Dutch movement of de Stijl (the Style), which featured primary colors arranged asymmetrically in rectangular paintings, or abstract furniture. In Russia after the 1917 Revolution, Alexandr Rodchenko (1891-1956) and Varvara Stepanova (1894-1958) founded an artistic movement called Constructivism in 1921, and in 1922 published their manifesto to "set itself the task of finding the communistic expression o material structures.

Leon Trotsky (1879-1940) inspired Communist Party encouragement of avant-garde art. He rejected fine art but promoted the more "productive" and "constructive" fields of architecture and mass production. So artists were asked to "go into the factories, where the real body of life is made."8 Rodchenko and Stepanova inspired the founding of an avant-garde art and design school called Vkhutemas (an abbreviation of its formal title: Higher State Artistic and Technical Workshops).

After the war, Europe also led in architecture and design, which was often inspired by the American model of mass production. In the particular category of consumer product design, the primary focus of this book, the English Arts and Crafts movement dominated the latter 19th century. As the 20th century began, Arts and Crafts leadership broadened to other European countries, as France dominated Art Nouveau. In Austria in 1903, the Wiener Werkstätte (Viennese Workshops) were formed by architect Josef Hoffmann (1870-1956) and Koloman Moser (18681918) who, like most Arts and Crafts leaders, were opposed to mass production and rejected the evils of what they saw as cheap and shoddy products produced under abhorrent conditions. They wanted to counteract this machine-made trend by making handcrafted products that were both innovative in design and superior in quality and workmanship.

Ar and Craf workhops in mei we thie hihy whicdrewar line betweenhandmade crafts nd industrialmass production, sgregatin art froidust the basis of morality and artistic superiority. The core belief of the Arts and Crafts movement was that production by cold machines and unskilled labor (often under poor working condiions,ve, alen

However, acceptance of mass production was growing. In 1906, a member of the Dresden (Germany) Workshops, Richard Riemerschmid (18681957), designed a line of Machinen-l process, rather than in the one-at-a-time tradition of craftsmen. Still, most Arts and Crafts workshops continued to oppose mass production as a threat to their livelihood, while innovative German pple rt educators adically sought a final solution (sorry—not the best

In 1907, German applied arts educator Hermann Muthesius (18611927) formed the Deutscher Werkbund (German Union of Work), a trade association. It consisted of 12 induslisnrieictlpark G

T  W member Peter Behrens (18681940) was appointed as "artistic consultant" to Allgemeine Elekricitäts Gesellschaft (AEG), the German equivalent of America's General Electric, where H the United States. It would be 50 years before an American corporation engaged a similar (IBM, 1956).

Muthesius in 1907 also proposed the development ofa new Machinenstil (Machine Style) ouc rca ye pu e    es cated their intended function, as well as their manufacture by machine processes. The significant purpose of this proposal was to restore the craftsman's role in shaping mass-produced consumer products by an artistic hand and eye, a role that had been lost at the beginning of the century when engineers took over the entire process of mass production. This had ended the Arts and Crafts era and relegated artistic contribution in manufacturing to mere surface decoration then known as applied art. The innovative concept of a Machine Style would soon be advanced by one of Germany's leading applied arts schools. In 1908, Bruno Paul (1874 1968), one of the 12 artist/architects who founded the German Werkbund, developed a Typenmöbel (standard furniture), which permitted an unlimited number of combinations of standardized, machine-made elements.

After the war in 1919, Walter Gropius (18831969), an architect, member of the Deutscher Werkbund, and former employee and protégé of Peter Behrens in Berlin, returned from military service to take over as director of the Weimar Kunstgewerbeschule (Weimar Arts and Crafts School), founded in 1902 by Belgian architect Henry Van de Velde (1863-1957), an Arts and Crafts leader who had become a member of the Werkbund, but as a foreign national was forced to leave the country and abandon the school during the war.

Gropius renamed the school Das Staatliches Bauhaus (State School of Building), commonly known only as the Bauhaus. Gropius was an avid admirer of American Fordism and Taylorism, and of American grain silos for their architectural simplicity and monumentality. He shared the belief of many in postwar Germany that setting new standards of industrial art would secure German global design leadership in the economic recovery. There at the Bauhaus, over the next decade, an avant-garde art and design faculty and their students developed principles for the design of modern architecture and products that featured simple, undecorated, geometrical modern forms, which were exquisitely crafted in quality materials.

Although staffed by many of Europe's most renowned modern artists, the school in Weimar was patterned after medieval craft guild workshops. Professors were known as masters, and students progressed from Lehrlinge (apprentices) to Gesellen (journeymen) to Jungenmeister (young masters). Gropius wanted to elevate Arts and Crafts to the level of fine art. In 1923, Làszló Moholy-Nagy (18951946) joined the faculty as master of the metal workshop. He was born in Hungary, studied law in Budapest, and later served in World War I. Convalescing from a war wound, he took up painting and worked his way across Germany as a letterer and sign painter, arriving in Berlin in 1921, where he took up painting and experimental photography. In 1922 he participated in the Dada and Constructivist conference organized by Theo van Doesburg in Weimar.

In general, many of these student designs met the idealistic standard of Muthesius's Machinenstil, and a few would be manufactured in limited quantities for the commercial market. Althoug the chool was plagued bynancial and politicl diffulties it woul furi u

1924, when political demonstrations and Weimar budget cuts forced Gropius to relocate the school to Dessau, where it would come under increasing pressure by the Nazis, who were determined to rid the country of forward-looking (avant-garde) intellectuals.

Another former employee and protégé of Peter Behrens, Swiss-born architect Charles Edouard Jenneret Gris (1887-1965), was also intrigued by American Fordism and Taylorism, only he used it in reverse. Designing a slaughterhouse in Paris in 1916, he included a conveyor system to "disassemble" the animals. This practice, used in the United States since 1867, was often claimed by Ford to be the inspiration of his assembly line for automobiles. In 1917 Gris established a small brick-making factory, and by 1920 he considered himself an industrialist as well as an architect. It was then that he adopted his pseudonym, "Le Corbusier" (the Raven), under which he began publishing L'Esprit Nouveau (The New Spirit), a periodical with themes of order, rationality, engineering, and technology. Twenty-eight issues were published over the next five years.

As an architect, Corbusier dreamed of producing houses the way Ford produced automobiles. In 1921 he published sketches of a house called Maison Citrohan (Citrohan House), using the analogy of France's mass-produced car based on Ford's assembly line, the Citroën. His concept, he declared, could complete houses in three days made of artificial materials such as concrete. Over the next few years, Corbusier designed a number of private homes in Paris based on his Citrohan House concept. They featured stark interior white walls, furniture made of tubular steel, and single bare bulbs in light fixtures. The effect was pure, simple, modernist design that followed the German Bauhaus style and which he called "a machine for living in." In 1922, Corbusier developed plans for a "Contemporary City" for three million inhabitants that he hoped would reorganize society along lines of American industrial models. In 1923, he published Vers une Architecture (Toward an Architecture), a book summarizing his philosophy of a future world functioning like a machine.

Most Americans, enjoying the first fruits of mass production in a new consumer culture, had no idea of the extensive European design or art theories that had evolved on the continent either before or after the war. Modern art was simply not part of American culture. Art, to most Americans, was pretty landscape or portrait paintings on museum walls; Greek sculptures in gardens; or expensive, handcrafted, decorative home furnishings in traditional styles imported from Europe. The concept of combining functional, mechanical products and art was literally unimaginable.

By 1925, Europe had not yet recovered economically, but Paris was not only stll at the young Americans, but had also become the center of fashion, both in style and entertainment. African American entertainer Josephine Baker (1906-1975) became a stage sensation for her exodancing at the Folies Bergére cabaret and would later popularize a dance step called the Charn. Jazz was he os ur uhe te, bu he te thinexcitin andfun.Georg Gershwi's coposition o 8,An American n Paris" eoke the sights and sounds of Paris in the 1920s, and constituted most Americans' sole impression of Paris.

Gabrielle "Coco" Chanel (18831971) had opened her boutique in Paris in 1919, setting loa hn tn' h h   lac   's l 1920s. She introduced Chanel No. 5 in 1924, the first perfume to feature a designer's name. She epitomized Paris of the 1920s, where women tasted new freedoms to wear what they liked, to cut ("bob") their hair short, to wear short skirts (above the knees), to wear straight dresses without waistlines, to wear makeup and perfume, to expose bare legs, to wear high heels, to wear "cloche" (helmet-type) felt hats, to drive automobiles, or to smoke in public. Whatever happened in Paris soon became fashionable around the world.

Young American women of the "Roaring Twenties," who had only recently been granted the right to vote (19th Amendment to the Constitution, May 19, 1919), shortly after being deprived of the legal right to buy alcohol (Prohibition: 18th Amendment to the Constitution, January 1, 19, wer infuence by these new Paris fashins. Many who followe the new syl were callppers,"nd thebehavir was perive peatively ymay uri hibition as outlandish, irresponsible, frivolous, sexually provocative, and in general as flouting socil and sexual"norms." Retrospectively, ofcourse, women were merely expressing their ee dom from residual Victorian restraints aist men, andat the me t tablishingshi  ivi scl e  centuy, pariculary woen  thye consumers of the new household appliances now becoming available.

Such appliances were featured in France in 1923 at the Salon des Arts Ménages (Salon of the todeve ew yo eain, an oby py hem yal duction of their design, thus sharing risks as well as rewards. Many of the new designs were based on the basic geometric shapes of Cubist modern art. By and large, such products were not produced in large quantities, as manufacturing in Europe had not yet recovered from the war, nor had it evolved to the level required for significant competition with America.

But Europe still retained its historical reputation as the center of art and design, and in fact Europeans have a number of legitimate historical claims for initiating what we now call industrial design because of the many Arts and Crafts groups that advanced the quality of design esthetically in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. However, what we call industrial design today is more broadly defined than as simply designing consumer products in a modern style, but as the process of designing products for mass production by machines in collaboration with engineering, marketing, and manufacturing, for the purpose of consumer acceptance and affordability, as well as for corporate profit, as it is practiced today. It is the Machine Age in America, in the 1920s and 1930s, that defined, pioneered, and implemented this modern process in reality, rather than in European theoretical prototypes that were designed without any manufacturing, cost, or sales considerations, and were therefore only of academic, or esthetic value.

Before that, as we have seen, the only major industries in the United States involving artist/designers were the "art industries" in general, and of course there were many designers in these fields for decades before. Some of these designers made the transition to the new field of industrial design before others and could claim, as some did, to be the first "industrial designers" in the United States, long before it became well known as a specific profession. A number of them were veterans of World War I.

One of the art industries was the manufacture of decorative lamps, and in about 1920, 24-year-old Chauncey Eugene (Chick) Waltman (1896-1962) was already well known for his lamp designs for the Art Lamp Company on Wabash Avenue in Chicago, where he served as designer, salesman, and advertising manager. The most successful lamp he designed at about this time was the Almco lamp. Waltman was born near Columbus, Indiana, the son of a minister. The family moved to Cincinnati, Ohio, and lived near the famed Rookwood Pottery, where Waltman, as a boy, was inspired by artists he saw working there. The family moved several times around Michigan, ending up in Grand Rapids, where Waltman was at the top of his art class in high school. While still in school, he worked in the decorating department of the Berkery and Guy Furniture Company, and by 1912 he started a decorating department at the Imperial Furniture Company.

After high school he took a job in Chicago with John A. Colby and studied decorating and furniture design at night school, but after the start of World War I in 1917, he joined the Air Force and was trained at Eastman Kodak, in Rochester, New York, to prepare maps with photos and hand sketching from airplanes. In 1918, during the flu epidemic, he was sent to Cornell University for further training and to earn a commission as second lieutenant. But suddenly the war was over and he returned to Chicago and resumed night school.

He soon got a job as a lamp designer for Valentine and Seaver and later with the Art

![](images/0cf10bfe68e916b5f484b06806869a5ed46514beecb1adf65ae7e1d6abad5277.jpg)  
Chauncey "Chick" Waltman, ca. 1930s (courtesy William Waltman).

Lamp Company, where, as advertising manager, he used ads with borders by Walter Dorwin Teague (more about him later). In 1920, he and his first wife built a home in Oak Park, the upscale Chicago suburb, near where Frank Lloyd Wright's home and studio were located. But the marriage foundered and he took a new position with the Kaplan Lamp and Shade Company.

In 1924, Waltman made a lucrative business deal with Kaplan. For half his normal weekly salary of \$150, he would go to Paris to study art, and spend two days a week drawing lamp designs in the new styles and send them to Kaplan for manufacture. For about a year, he studied at the Grande Chamber des Arts, biked through England and France, and traveled to Italy and North Africa. When he returned to Chicago in 1925, he set up his own freelance office, designing in wood, cast metal, wrought iron, and glass." After industrial design became well known in the 1930s, his office would participate as Waltman Associates, at 165 Chicago Avenue.

Alnheru ws eiuu which almost exclusively produced traditional and historical styles (many still do). Among designers in this field was Kem Weber (18891963) who began in 1921 at age 32 to work in the design of furniture for the Barker Brothers store in Los Angeles, then the largest furniture retailer in the United

States. Weber had been born in Germany as Karl Emanuel Martin (K.E.M.) Weber, apprenticed as a cabinetmaker and had graduated from the Berlin Kunstgewerbemuseum (Berlin Academy oFine Arts) in 1912, studying under the well-known Bruno Paul (18741968) and after graduation working for Paul.

In 1914 Paul sent him as a designer for Germany's exhibit at the 1915 Panama-Pacific Exposition in San Francisco, but he was stranded there by the start of World War I. At first he opened a modern design studio in Berkeley.But after the sinking of the Lusitania in May 1915, American sentiment turnedagainst Germany, and as a German, he had trouble findingclients, so he worked as a lumberjack in the High Sierra. Toward the end of the war he operated a chicken farm in Los Gatos with his young wie Erika. In 918 they settled in Santa Barbara, California, where Weber taught art classes and opened a small factory in an old church to producmodern n prifurniture, bute met wihnlymoest uccs. WhenBarker Brohrs offered him a position in its design department in 1921, he jumped at the chance and moved to Los Angeles.

Otherartists were in the dvertising busine, which promotedmass-produced artless industry consumer products such as automobiles, appliances, furniture, machinery, tools, and other functional products designed by engineers. Gradually, as the demand for more attractive products grew, these artists suggested ideas for product improvement to their advertising clients.

One such individual was New Zealander Joseph "Jo" Sinel, who in 123 at age 34 was working for the Lennon Mitchell advertising agency in New York, and who designed graphics a prouctorcis alhoughseci roudes b hie ot  un Sinel became a prominent industrial designer and educator, and we will later describe his career in more detail.

![](images/521d8dfd3204be1a8c7ee5b3afae130810765cfef6a2fb4892b28932185a9e94.jpg)  
John Vassos, ca. 1934 (February 1934 Fortune magazine).

Another artist who would become legendary in industrial design, John Vassos (1893-1985), designed a face lotion bottle in 1924 that became popular (with the lotion removed) when used as a hip flask during Prohibition, and sales increased dramatically by 700 percent. Vassos was born John Plato Vassacopoulos in Romania. His family moved to Constantinople where his father became a Greek publisher and diplomat. As a teenager, young Vassos drew a cartoon in his father's newspaper that offended the Turkish senate, and he was forced to leave the county in 0a  .He  Bi hip  ie. u World War I, he served as a cabin steward on a Belgian steamer, which was torpedoed. He was rescued by the U.S. Navy and landed in London in 1915. He obtained papers and traveled to Boston in 1919 where his older brother Alex lived.

There, he studied at the Fenway Art School; studied art and illustration with John Singer Sargent (18561925), the leading portrait artist ohis geneation; and assisted with stage designs for the Boston Opera Company. He came to New York in 1924, became an American citizen, changed his name to Vassos, opened a commercial studio called the New York Display Company, studied at the Art Students League, worked as an illustrator for Harper's and New Yorker magazines, and illustrated and published books.

Furniture designers became prominent in the emerging field of industrial design as more modern designs were demanded. Among these was Leo Jiranek (1900-1990), who in 1924 took over his father's furniture design business at age 24 and became one of the industry's first freelancers. In fact, he was considered by many to be the "Dean of Furniture Designers." Jiranek, known as Jerry, was born in Grand Rapids, Michigan, and after prep school at Western Military Academy, he spent two years at Norwich University, then transferred to Princeton University in 1920, where he graduated as an engineer in 1922. Over the years, he would contribute to more furniture companies than any other designer.

But in 1925, there was one furniture designer who abandoned all tradition by introducing a style intended to represent the vitality of modern America, inspired by the new steel towers rising all over Manhattan. Generally, his pieces were bookcase-cabinet combinations or desks, which featured unornamented block forms that rose in terraced levels and provided various functional storage boxes and shelves, often lacquered red, blue, or green. Typical were his Skyscraper cabinet and Skyscraper bookcase. Pieces were tall, but with a narrow footprint, perfect for small Manhattan apartments. The designer was 38-year-old Paul T. Frankl (1886- 1958), whose new Skyscraper furniture immediately became popular and was probably the first evidence of a modernistic style originating in America.

Frankl was a furniture designer and maker, architect, painter, and writer born in Vienna, Austria, the son of a wealthy real estate dealer. He had been a member of the Wiener Sezession (Vienna Secession) that had early in the 20th century sought to create a new style that owed nothing to historical influence. After completing his architectural studies at the Berlin Polytechnic, he spent time in Berlin and Copenhagen before arriving in the United States in 1914 and settling in New York. He brought an outsider's perspective and a desire to create a uniquely American design esthetic.

By 1922 he had established Frankl Galleries on 48th Street in New York, and by 1924 he had a showroom on Madison Avenue, where he sold furniture of his own design as well as imported modern textiles and wallpapers, calling his company Skyscraper Furniture. He described his attraction to the Skyscraper style:

Modernity expresses itself in the energy of the vertical thrust of red husks of the city of the past. 2

![](images/4e9bae4d59862d8a7d868350b21d79fe80942a66943c58bf1500d8630a800619.jpg)  
Paul T. Frankl, ca. 1938.

In fac, Frank knew that desnin furniture neinspire by he srucures would have a mass appeal to Americans, and he was right. But he was a rare practitioner of modern design in America. Europe was where the action really was.

# The Arrival of Art Deco

France was the mai atcinin he rt wore 0s annataly wane t motadreinforcei prewar reputatin n culture  fashion,style,decorative rts nfine arts. By the early 1920s, many aspiring young American artists were being influenced by the rampacFrenmoernervis   suPr p rience in the good life of Europe.

So from April to October of 1925, an Exposition International des Arts Décoratifs et Industriels Modernes (International Exposition of Modern Decorative and Industrial Arts) was held in Paris, the first "World's Fair" since the one in San Francisco ten years before. The lenghy French tie would much later be shortened to Art Deco to describe the many European varieties of new designs and their subsequent impact around the world, but at the time, the designs were generally described as "modernistic." The exposition was unique in one key respect: former similar expositions traditionally celebrated past national accomplishments. In this one the intention of the organizers was to establish new standards. Exhibitors were required to display only "those exhibits that were not dependent of the art of the past."1 Invitations went out to all countries except Germany, which was still regarded as the villain of the Great War. Twenty-six countries accepted, except for China and the United States.

China did not accept because of disruptions in political matters, and the United States ostensibly because the State Department did not recommend appropriations to do so, due to lack of support from commercial interests. As Herbert Hoover, then secretary of commerce and in charge of the Coolidge administration's industrial policy, explained to the Fourth Annual Exposition of Women's Arts and Industries:

The advice I received from manufacturers was that while we produced a vast volume of goods of much artistic value, they did not consider that we could contribute sufficiently varied design of unique character or of special expression in American artistry to warrant such a participation.2

Obviously, U.S. manufacturers saw no significant consumer market potential in Europe, but more to the point, they had nothing to display that met the exposition's intent of new standards or its specific criteria of products that were "not dependent of the art of the past." Most U.S. products of the time were, in fact, copies of European designs. Hoover's decision did not set well with the cultural establishment, where many were already quite aware of how advanced European design and fashion had become. When Charles Richards, the same art educator who conducted the 1922 disappointing study of industrial arts education, asked

Hoover to delegate a commission of 100 representatives of trade associations, along with some artists and designers, to visit and report on the exposition, Hoover gracefully complied.

The six-month exposition opened on April 28 with 4,000 guests at the inauguration, and thousands of visitors arrived on following days. It was located between the esplanade of Les Invalides and the entrances of the Grand Palais and Petite Palais, a 57-acre site, two-thirds of which contained French displays. Exhibits included work by famous glass and jewelry designer René Lalique (1860-1945), who also designed the exposition's crystal tower fountain. There were displays by a number of Parisian department stores such as Bon Marché, Printemps, and Magazins du Louvre, which created entire room "ensembles" in modernist styles. The work of French furniture and interior designer Émile-Jacques Ruhlmann (1879-1933) was displayed in a special, classical "Pavilion of the Collector" designed by French architect Pierre Patout (1879-1965). In a far corner of the exposition, generally ignored by the press, was Le Corbusier's Pavilion de L'Esprit Nouveau, a spare modern home interior and furnishings along the lines of his Maison Citrohan concept, that attracted both admiration and criticism for its lack of decoration.

Russia had a pavilion displaying the work of Alexandr Rodchenko, Varvara Stepanova, and students of the art and design school, Vkhutemas, which they had established. The students won several prizes. The popular Swedish pavilion was simple in form and devoid of decoration, featuring modern crafts in typical Scandinavian styles inspired by Gregor Paulsson's (1889- 1977) 1919 book, More Beautiful Things for Everyday Life, which challenged the past and encouraged Arts and Crafts workers to use new forms of the modern age. Danish architect adesrAre Jacbsen 10 iunt, on  siler meahischair. Austria contributed Frederick Kiesler's (1890-1965) City in Space exhibit to house displays of the work of Josef Hoffman (1870-1956), architect, furniture designer, and one of the founders of the Vienna Secession.

With 16 million people visiting the exposition, it was destined to have a lasting impact on applied arts around the world. Certainly Le Corbusier influenced architecture in what would become known as International Style in 1932. The decorative arts were influenced by the unusual new styles that were generally called modernistic but which included an entire n av metric, or as a Machine Age look. Some imagery was clearly derived from French Cubism, German Bauhaus, Italian Futurism and Russian Constructivism, all described earlier.

American critiHele Appleton Read (18971974) reporteon severalmportant principles of modern design she observed at the exposition:

It is characteristic of the new décor that the nature of the material is at all times respected and is allwto dca eet.Wo i woo o s on;he tast that vaiy ss when the attempt is made to give the quality of one material to another, to make wood look [like] o marble ke ace is voeThis univeral tndenc towar spliciy teho e o te e  e wil ha  l note. All design in this country is governed by the factor of whether or not it can be reproduced in mass production. It costs no more to get out a good design than it does a bad one, and the fact that the best designs of the new décor e the simplest to the point of being geomericmaks them so much the more easy to put on the market.3

Rea aspn that smr weas  prduceay se gil, but n c most manufacturing processes could produce complex forms as easily as simple ones. Few design forms are as complex as the Model T or a 1922 kitchen stove, both popular designs of the time. Many manufacturers would argue that copying an existing design was much easier than investing in a new design, which required extensive design work as well expensive new tooling. They would argue that the cheapest of all was to keep producing the old design as forms:

Tw into our consciousness, we ended up by creating designs which reflected it, and which in turn are the most suitable for machine made processes.

Ryav    yv e tnt rs ha iTheple pln,hoe have been that as artists and designers often do, they simply made things different than the past to demonstrate progress in the creative arts, or to be more inventive than those who went I love to create new things!

The Ameran Comissionubmittrort  h Deparent Comme, ag that the modern movement would inevitably reach the United States, and that Americans needed to

initiate a parallel effort of our own upon lines calculated to appeal to the American consume.. As a nation we now live artistically on warmed-over dishes. In a number of lines of manufacture we are little more than producing antiquarians. We copy, modify, and adapt the older styles with few suggestions of a new idea. It is true that this practice of reproducing older forms has been an invaluable education to our people. It is also true that the adaptation of old motives when performed with intelligence and skill continues and probably will continue to give us a large proportion of the decorative manifestations acceptable to American taste. It would seem equally true, on the other hand, that the richness and complexity of American life call for excursions into new fields that may yield not only innovations but examples well suited to the living conditions of our times.

The ambiguity of this report clearly reflected conflicting opinions among commission members as to what to do about the obvious differences between the new European design direction and the traditional practices of design in the United States. Copying of older styles was justified as educational and acceptable, but innovation may yet be of some value. Some members doubted that the new modern styles would appeal to Americans. The report gave the Department of Commerce no clear recommendations for action, and even if it had, it is doubtful that Commerce could have done anything to change the current traditions and practices of manufacturers. Any shift in direction would have to come from the manufacturers themselves based on consumer demand, and at this moment in history, the general public was blissfully unaware of what was happening in Paris. This would soon change.

Two and a half million Americans now had radios and were becoming more aware of world events. In England, John Logie Baird was even demonstrating a mechanical television system. Reports from other Americans who attended the Paris exposition quickly circulated within the art and design communities and the press. The Art-in-Trades Club issued a manifesto challenging American artists to compete in the design of modern furnishings. In January 1926, an editorial about the Paris exposition in the New York Times urged industry to change because the public would soon catch up with Europe in recognizing the competitive value of new designs:

American captains of industry must soon change their traditional characters. True, the public may still cling to its conception of them as iron-jawed, level headed giants of efficiency … but if the

present international competition keeps up, we may find them, like the Germans, openly cooperating with museums and art schools; or, like the French, proclaiming themselves merchants of beauty.6

One of the attendees at the Paris Exposition had been Kem Weber, mentioned earlier, who had been working at the Barker Brothers furniture store in Los Angeles and represented their interest abroad. He had become an American citizen in 1924 and had Americanized his German name to only its initials, K.E.M. In 1926, he had become art director for Barker Brothers. Up to that time, Barker had exclusively featured period revival furniture in its five stores. Inspired by what he had seen in Paris, Weber in the fall of 1926 opened a new store-within-astore, christened "Modes and Manners," on the fourth floor of Barker's eleven-story building. Weber designed the wall treatments, display cases and furniture in a space with jagged shapes, bright colors, and wild patterns. Modern furniture by Weber's Austrian friend in New York, Paul Frankl, was also displayed. It was not only the first outlet for modern furniture and accessories in Southern California, but also one of the first in the country. Over the next decade, Weber would work to promote modernism with the furniture he designed there in his store studio, the Silver Tree. The store had its own production facilities to manufacture the furniture and soon opened a new special Hollywood branch, which Weber also designed.7

Museums were anxious to promote the new designs, and in February the American Association of Museums imported a collection of 400 French and Swedish objects from Paris, called Selected Collection of Objects from the International Exposition of Modern Decorative and Industrial Art, which was exhibited at the Metropolitan Museum of Art and subsequently sent to tour other American member museums. The exhibit explained that it was

not to stimulate demand for European products, nor to encourage copying of European creations, but to bring about an understanding of this modern movement in design in the hope that a paralle movet m ateinntr origus toe r prriate o e conditions of the twentieth century.8

In Vogue magazine, patterns of textiles and paper goods were illustrated and described as "modern designs" or of "modernistic influence." Advertisements in national magazines began to adapt Art Deco design formats and motifs to give their ads and products a sense of modernity. Even ads for the most modest, utilitarian objects could be glorified when presented in a modern artistic format. Ready-to-wear fashions quickly adopted Art Deco elements such as -r ui rtn. Such syle snppeare vy cteory shin  priclary jewycmpacts, handbags, hats and scarves.

Younger generations today probably wonder why there was such a fuss about these European designs, when fashionably designed products are available today from allover the world. It   bi har trelae, I nwBut dend n your e, you y nee  e wha it was like in the 1950s, when home television sets became available, or in the 1980s, when personal computers were introduced, or in the 2000s when iPhones hit the market.

Thepub w xcwhenoy sbbuy able by large institutions, finally becomes inexpensive enough and available to ordinary people, or when a fashion trend, such as short skirts in the 1960s, casual wear in the 1980s, or sport shoes in the 10s, suddenly erupts everywhere in a short period of time. In the 120s, the latest technology was, in fact, mechanical devices, such as new electric appliances or any new household decorative accessories, furniture, or bathroom and kitchen equipment. People simply wanted to buy the most modern-looking things, and at that time, "modern" was dramatically different from the past. Or as flappers would have said in the 1920s, "Modern design is swell!

The bee's knees! The cat's meow!" (Translation: Cool! Awesome! Mind-blowing!) They were no different from young consumers today.

While the new modern design styles were being promoted aggressively everywhere, there in lower consumer prices. The vacuum cleaner that cost \$52 in 1918, in 1926 was priced at \$29.95, 49 percent lower (in 2013 dollars, that's a reduction from \$804 to \$394). So the new mass-produceproducts were much moreafordable, for many more people. Inaddition,more jobs were available at higher salaries. The economy was better than it had ever been since the war. Pople were prime to buy, and they wanted the latest styles.

Now it was up to the manufacturers, many of whom had neither the inclination nor the specialized skills to provide such new styles.

nize the public desire for new design styles was in fact the automobile industry, in which 43 firms produced four million new cars each year. Travel by car was increasing rapidly with the opening of the Lincoln Highway, the first coast-to-coast roadway, connecting San Francisco n New York (now Route 0. In 126, the Model T, which in 123 had commanded 5 percent of the U.S. market but was virtually unchanged since its 1913 introduction, was now challenged by Chevrolet's new 1926 model with bold new styling and colors.

General Motors (GM) head Alfred P. Sloan had initiated annual styling changes because he was convinced that new styling was the key to success. Chevy sales doubled, and Ford dropped by 25 percent. Within a year, Chevrolet would win 45 percent of the market, and by 1927 Ford's share would drop to 15 percent. Ford was forced to drop the Model T and produce its redesigned Model A in 1928, designed by Edsel Ford,

Henry's son. Some manufacturers figured that if Henry Ford himself, the king of Fordism, needed styling, they had better follow suit.

Now, in early 1927, about when Charles Lindbergh flew solo across the Atlantic, GM's Sloan charged Harley J. Earl (1893- 1969) with designing the 1927 LaSalle, a cheaper, productionmodel version of GM's luxury Cadillac. It was to be the first massproduced car to have its appearance completely styled from bumper to bumper by a single individual, and the first to use chrome plating on the trim. Earl used full-scale clay models to preplan every detail and blend all components into an integrated form.

Earl had been born in Hollywood, California, had attended the University of Southern California in 1912 and studied engineering at Stanford University from 1914 to 1917. He began his career with the firm his father had founded in 1889 as the Earl

![](images/ccf1d3b04b6d0418fd3e8a5b72c47c33b1ad4430b23c1bb0a3e181111316dcc1.jpg)  
Harley J. Earl, ca. 1930s (General Motors).

Carriage Works, and after 1908 called the Earl Automobile Works, which built custom car bodies for film stars starting in 1911. Clients included Roscoe "Fatty" Arbuckle, Tom Mix, Mary Pickford, Cecille B. DeMille, and Al Jolson, among others. Earl designed the first complete car produced by the firm in 1918.

That year, Don Lee, a West Coast Cadillac distributor, head of Don Lee Coach and Body Works, bought the Earl Company and kept Harley on as designer and general manager of its custom body shop in 1919. In 1921 Earl patented a design that suggested the wraparound windshield he later would pioneer. In 1926, the Fisher Brothers of General Motors, impressed with

Earl's technique of using modeling clay to develop the forms of his designs, hired him as a consultant to Cadillac, and he relocated to Detroit. His first assignment was to design the LaSalle, a low-cost Cadillac. The 1927 LaSalle would become an instant success and would convince Sloan to establish the first internal corporate design department at GM, called the Art and Colour Section, and to name Earl as its first director. Within a year Earl would hire a staff of 50 automotive designers.

In 1927, promotion of modern design to the public continued on many fronts. The Metropolitan Museum of Art held an exhibit of Swedish industrial arts, introducing Scandinavian design to the U.S. public for the first time. In May, Jane Heap (1887-1964), editor of a literary journal called The Little Review, organized a Machine Age Exposition in Steinway Hall in Midtown Manhattan. Inspired by Le Corbusier's Pavilion de L'Esprit Nouveau, and by Soviet Constructivism, Heap displayed actual machines: a Studebaker crankshaft, a Curtis airplane engine, and a Hyde Windlass propeller, along with photographs of skyscrapers, the Bauhaus in Dessau, Soviet industrial buildings, and architecture by Walter Gropius. E. B. White (1899- 1985) wrote in the New Yorker that the exposition demonstrated "that the machine is the tutelary symbol of the universal dynamism."9 Department stores, such as Macy's and Franklin Simon of New York, Wanamaker's in Philadelphia, and Marshall Field in Chicago, inspired by the success of Paris department store ensembles during the 1925 exposition, staged exhibitions in May 1927 featuring the new Art Deco designs. Typical was Macy's Art and Trade exhibition, which was attended by 40,000 people and was hyped aggressively by radio announcements and talks by notable participants.

Consumer goods manufacturers were being advised to incorporate modern design into their products by advertising agencies. In the August 1927 issue of Atlantic Monthly, Ernest Elmo Calkins (1868-1964), head of the Calkins & Holden advertising agency, who was called by many the "Dean of Advertising Men," wrote an influential article, "Beauty, the New Business Tool," in which he stated the case for more attractive products:

We passed from the hand to the machine, we enjoyed our era of triumph of the machine, we acquired wealth, and with wealth, education, travel, sophistication, a sense of beauty; and then we began to miss something in our cheap but ugly products. Efficiency was not enough. The machine did not satisfy the soul. Man could not live by bread alone. And thus it came about that beauy, o what e conceive as beauty, becme a fcor in the production nd marke of goods. 10

## Calkins continued to define beauty:

Ten rt at   o rt ta s ea   ne th s a sound economic foundation. It is to be hoped that manufacturers in the search for design to beauiy their products wil start with a cler conetion o what beauty i, epecially beauty in an article of use . the surest guide in divining new beauty in machine-made things is to grasp and interpret the beauty they naturally and intrinsically possess."

In other words, beauty in useful products is not extraneous artistic decoration or embelcharacter. Whether Calkins knew of the Bauhaus or not, he was expressing the exact same philosophy of design, but in business terms.Calkins asked his advertising colleagues, "How coul as work, if the styling of products did not express the qualities claimed for it?"2

Many manufacturers by this tie were paying attention to ther advertiing agencis, which were already incorporating the modern look into their ads. In many cases, such manufacturers asked their agencies for help in designing products, requests that were honored by mee r  e n  Ocurug deigassistance by advertising for mployees or consultants. One can imagine what such a help wanted ad for an ndustrial dsigner might have looked like in 1927:

W cay X.Applcnt e mir w u  s iiig millg weldg, fstening, and asbling, nd wilig to work collaortively lD ve style. Applicants must provide examples of mass-produced products they have designed recently. Compensation for such work is to be negotiated.

An advertisement like that would have attracted few applicants, because at that time, practically no one existed with such extensive qualifications that required both art and engineering training, and who would be willing to work in mass-production facilities. As we have seen, few art students would even consider such a position, even if they had been trained in engineering or manufacturing matters, and of course none were. As noted earlier, most graduates of art schools wanted nothing to do with industry.

Engineers who were trained in manufacturing processes, and may have designed massproduced products, had absolutely no art education, nor any inclination to acquire it. The industrial art position described in the ad would have been, as some would say, "neither h o w,  rt.Ti  r had, at least in America, left industry without an essential, competitive, business tool.

Jane Heap, who had organized the Machine Age Exposition in New York, wrote that an effective artist "must affiliate with the creative artist in other arts and with the constructive men of his epoch: engineers, scientists, etc." In 1927 there were a few professionals who recognized the increasing demand for industrial design, were trained in art and design, and responded enthusiastically to the call by manufacturers, knowing full well that this would require their willing collaboration with industry, engineers, and scientists. They were the farseeing individuals who almost single-handedly became pioneers in what was to become a brand new profession. They were interesting and unusual men and women. They were the machine designers.

Here's where things began to get interesting. As the demand for modern design increased both by manufacturers and by the public, professional people in a number of art-related, advertising, or commercial fields responded with interest, or were sought out and targeted by industry. Some, of course, were already in the art industries, including furniture and lighting products.

One of these was Donald Deskey (1894-1989), then 33, who had just opened an office, Deskey-Vollmer, in New York in 1927, with his partner, Phillip Vollmer, specializing in the design of furniture and lighting. Deskey was born in Blue Earth, Minnesota, and had studied architecture at the University of California, Berkeley, and painting at the California School of Design, the School of the Art Institute of Chicago, and the Art Students League of New York. After military service in World War I, he started working with advertising agencies in Chicago and New York, but soon left to study painting in Paris from 1920 to 1922, where he also married. He returned to the United States to begin a career in painting; to teach art at Juniata College in Huntington, Pennsylvania; and to exhibit his work in Pittsburgh. In 1925 he returned to France to attend the Paris exposition and visited the Bauhaus in Dessau, both of which greatly influenced his approach to design, and upon his return in 1926, he settled in New York to work for furniture designer Paul Frankl, mentioned earlier. He designed furniture and lamps for Frankl, but also designed an apartment interior for Adam Gimbel, the founder of Gimbel's department store, who had bought out rival Saks & Co. and opened an uptown branch called Saks Fifth Avenue in 1923.

![](images/9b6820b773a71e98dcc0032db5a48e3fb7a7415d952f278634fe0ff7c2a3bca5.jpg)

Meanwhile, in California, Kem Weber left the Barker Brothers store in 1927 to open an independent design studio in Los Angeles, but continued to work as a consultant for Barker, shaping its products well into the 1930s. His design of an armchair for the John Bissinger residence in San Francisco in 1928 was in the sleek and sophisticated modernism that would come to define the Hollywood of the 1930s. In his new office, he sought out collaboration with manufacturers and explored new materials.

Deskey; Kem Weber; set designer Lee Simonson (1888-1967); set designer, architect, and artist Frederick Kiesler (1890-1965); Paul Frankl; and a few other designers were justifiably concerned with the dishonest but common practice of manufacturers copying designs, a practice that museums historically encouraged by displaying traditional European designs as superior models of design quality. At the time, there was no reason to believe that the new innovative modern designs in America would not be subjected to the same practice. Now, as early as 1927, the new modern designers realized that, for the first time, American designs could be more innovative than European ones and felt that such borrowing, as Frankl put it, "degrades the producers and corrupts the taste of the public."14 They formed an organization called the American Union of Decorative Artists and Craftsmen (AUDAC), which idealistically aimed to "cooperate with manufacturers and the public in the placing of American arts and crafts on a basis of honesty, dignity, and merit."

Another lighting designer was Walter von Nessen (1889-1943), then 38, who opened Nessen Studios in New York's Murray Hill section in 1927 with his wife, Greta von Nessen (c. 19001975), then 27, to design and fabricate architectural lighting. His designs, such as his innovative swing-arm lamps for table, floor, and wall applications, were simple, polished metal, modern, and highly functional in allowing the lamp to be easily pushed into a range of horizontal positions without moving the floor or desk lamp base. The Nessens also designed furniture and accessories for the Chase Brass and Copper Company, including a tubular steel chair. Von Nessen was born and trained in Germany, and his wife studied at the School for Industrial Arts in Stockholm, Sweden. They both emigrated to the United States in 1925.

There was also Gilbert Rohde (18941944), then 33, who in 1927 traveled to France and Germany and was inspired by modernism there to design and produce custom furniture in a modern style. That same year, he opened his own office in New York, designing furniture and showrooms and consulting with manufacturers such as General Electric and the Hudson Motor Car Company.
