Rohde was born in New York and became familiar with cabinetmaking in his German father's shop. He studied at the Art Students League and the Grand Central School of Art, both in New York. His early work was as a drama and music critic, cartoonist, reporter, and furniture illustrator.

In an environment where women led the fashion industry, it semed appropriate that some o these women would be attracted to the design of moern products. One of these was Helen Dryden (18871981) who, inspired by the flurry of modern design activity after the Paris Art Deco Exposition of 1925, turned her career in costume design to industrial design in 1927 at about age 40. She designed tableware and lamps for the Revere C  u decativj, texties lassre n was on aimes heighepiw artist."

![](images/0292c0a0fdca65f9682af9815cecc347b0ecfac9921cb74c342cce89bf6a6a46.jpg)  
Gilbert Rohde, ca. 1940 (courtesy Herman Miller, Inc.).

Dryden ws born in Balimore wih unuul artistibiliydesigning and sellingcothes for paper doll that appeared in newspaper fashion sections. She trained in landscape painting under Hugh Breckenrige and studied briely at the Philadelphia Academy of Fine Arts before moving to New York to begin her career in fashion design illustration at Vogue magazine in 1909. From 1913 to 1922, she designed dramatic and outstanding Vogue covers, as well as covers for Charm, Harper's Bazaar, House & Garden, Vanity Fair, and Harper's Weekly. From 1914 well into the 1920s, she designed costumes for Broadway shows and fabrics for Stehli Silks and Aberfoyle Fabrics. In 1925, she lived at 9 East 10th Street in New York with her 25-year-old Philippine-born cook and butler, Ricardo Lampitok.

![](images/e041de058bb612ab25f10503941a2bf26791a14c21274ba27c450e0334234064.jpg)  
Walter Dorwin Teague, ca. 1934 (February 1934 Fortune magazine. Photograph by Lazernick).

Other entrants thenew pofesion wre in heveg business. In 1927, Walter Dorwin Teague (18831960), then 44, was awarded a contract with Eastman Kodak to design cameras, starting on January 1, 1928. Teague immediately added "Industrial Design" to his firm's letterhead, since he was already a highly successful advertising artist, who had opened his own typographic studio in 1912 and by 1920 had been involved in commercial packaging.

Born in Decatur, Indiana, as one of six siblings, Teague's grandfather had moved from North Carolina to Pendleton, Indiana, where one of America's largest Quaker communities existed. Teague's father became a circuit-riding Methodist minister and later a tailor in Pendleton. At age 16, while still in school, Teague worked as a handyman at the local newspaper, and later as a reporter. He was inspired to become an artist by books on architecture in his high school library. At age 19, he left Indiana for

New York City, where he studied painting from 1903 to 1907 at the Art Students League of New York, an art school founded in 1875 for both amateurs and professionals that offered reasonably pric casses Sudents coul sudy part  full time, ut here were odegree proms or grades awarded. The school is still operating today in the same manner.

Teague earned money by checking hats at the YMCA in downtown Manhattan, and also by painting signs. His skill in lettering soon evolved into drawing illustrations for mail-order catalogs, such as for neckties and shoes. This led to more elaborate advertising illustrations, which caught the attention of Walter Whitehead, an advertising executive at the Ben Hampton Advertising Agency he had met at the YMCA, and who promptly hired him.

When Whitehead left the Ben Hampton Agency in 1908 and joined the more prestigious New York agency of Calkins & Holden, Teague went with him. In his four years there, Teague became known for his distinctive frames, or borders, for advertising art, which blended Baroque and Renaissance influence with a unique simplicity. After a year of freelancing, Teague left Calkins & Holden in 1912 to work from his own typographic studio, creating graphic designs for magazine ads. In 1920, when he designed frames for the famous Arrow Collar ads, he was already recognized widely for what were known as " Teague borders."

By the mid—1920s, the demand for border designs weakened, and Teague became involved with commercial packaging. He was intrigued by the Paris exposition of 1925, and in June 1926 he traveled to Europe to view Bauhaus work in Italy and was inspired by the work and writings of Le Corbusier. Upon his return, he reestablished his office devoted to package design.

In 1927, Richard F. Bach (1887-1968), curator of the Metropolitan Museum of Art, recommended Teague to Adolph Stuber, a top manager of the Rochester, New York-based Eastman Kodak Company, which was considering engaging an artist to design cameras. Teague took Stuber on a tour of the modern art shops in New York, including the contemporary shop of Rena Rosenthal, in order to show him the clean lines of home furnishings that were coming into style."5

Teague had no knowledge of cameras or how they were made, but he proposed working on-site with Kodak's engineers, claiming that "designing according to engineering necessities ultimately leads to greater beauty and heavier sales."6 Stuber apparently agreed. Teague's relationship with Eastman would result in dozens of camera designs over many years, and he would continue to consult with Eastman for the remainder of his life.

Teague's first designs for Kodak would include its 1930 Art Deco gift camera and packaging, and its Beau Brownie, both of which featured a colorful geometric pattern of rectangles and squares reminiscent of Dutch artist Piet Mondrian (1872-1944) and De Stijl art. By February 1928, Teague was already promoting the new profession of industrial design by writing an article, "Modern Design Needs Modern Merchandising," in Forbes, a business magazine. He advised, "The designer who gets results for the manufacturer plans with all departments of a business before he ever lays a pencil to drawing board."17

That same year, 1928, manufacturers were trying to make design changes in their products. But rather than changing the form of their old products, which would require expensive tooling it  u he an h c. In F azine recalled that in the early 1920s, "kitchen and bathroom were a porcelain and surgical white. Furniture was in natural wood tints, surfaced with a colorless finish. The stove was black. Faucets were metallic." Now, in a typical home in 1928,

in the cellar stood a Redflash Boiler, more crimson than the flames of the adjacent furnace. On the door of the orange refrigerator was perched a green parrot. Not only the dishes, but the pots and pans were decked out in bright gay tints and tones.

A green-tiled bathroom was set off with green tub, green towels, and, monstrously enough, green toilet paper. For afternoon tea there were blue glasses and a yellow cloth. A yellow alarm clock—a cherry garbage can, a scarlet typewriter—surfaces had turned suddenly into palettes.

Fortune concluded, "The acceptance of color was undoubtedly significant from the standpoint of old traditions being abandoned and old suppressions being released."18

The word was getting around the advertising world that designing products for industry was financially lucrative. An early example of this was the ticket scale designed for International in 1929 by Joseph "Jo" Claude Sinel (18891975), a 40- year-old artist working for the Lennon Mitchell advertising agency in New York. Sinel in a later interview recalled,

![](images/1dda28732e711e5581465bb5a44a2e22c535522c68e614e0a114fb640b4b6e46.jpg)  
Joseph "o"Sinel, 128 urte Cl College of the Arts Libraries).

Monte (wooden) fruit case.. I took the model in to show the executives of the company and they

Sinel led a most unconventional life for his era. In later times, he might have been called a wanderinghippe," but his earnings in commercial art demonstrateoutstanding skill and diligence in his career. He was born in Auckland, New Zealand, where his father, Thomas, ran a stevedoring operation and sired ten children. Jo's primary school's headmaster recognized his talent for drawing and urged him to attend the Elam School of Art. From 1904 to 1909, he served an apprenticeship as a lithographic artist for the New Zealand Herald newspaper. He    Wuh,hi shearer.

Impulsively, he left for England and arrived in Liverpool in the middle of World War I, uheaoubendngj HuonScotts an arpherinC

![](images/54b3e792c7c15c978593ccd59194737cb73b6c6f68992a1bc2751b74f0109a8b.jpg)  
1929 International Ticket Scale by Sinel (Designscape magazine, February 1977).

Next was Carlton Studios, England's most prominent art studio, and after that, the C. F. Hyme advertising agency in London. The war became increasingly dangerous to Londoners due to Zeppelin air raids, so Jo shipped out as a merchant seaman during wartime to avoid military conscription. After unloading guns off a British ship in Newport News, Virginia, he landed once again in Australia where he easily obtained a high-salary art job. When Australia passed a statute saying that men of military age could not leave the country after a certain date, Sinel decided it was time to leave.

In 1918 he left for the United States and landed in San Francisco at the time of the influenza epidemic, when people on the street were wearing surgical masks. Again, he landed an art position at Foster & Kleiser advertising agency but soon quit his job and set out for the high Sierras to build a cabin with an artist friend on the shores of Lake Susie. When snow arrived, he headed back to San Francisco to find several job offers waiting.

The one he chose was in New York with First National Pictures, a film company in which Charlie Chaplin and Douglas Fairbanks were associated. For First National, he designed promotional graphics for film distribution, but soon, driven by wanderlust and fascinated with automobiles, bought one in 1920 and drove across America, which was virtually unpaved at the time, to see its natural wonders and cities. He had advertisements for First National Pictures painted on the side of his car that attracted photographers at many cities, fueling Jo's natural flair for publicity. Returning to San Francisco, he worked simultaneously for Foster & Kleiser and the western branch of the McCann-Erikson advertising firm. When Jo refused to do a certain job at Foster & Kleiser, they fired him, but he and three others, including Harold von Schmidt, set up an independent business to design posters for various San Francisco advertising agencies, including Foster & Kleiser.

In 1921 and 1922, Sinel taught design and lettering at the California School of Arts and Crafts (CCAC, but now, California College of Arts, CCA) under its founder, Frederick Meyer. Jo would continue his association with CCAC, off and on, until his death. But in 1922 he to  psin r r  .C.Rals, whows rg evei in Montreal, Canada.

After five weeks there, Sinel went to New York to do freelance work with von Schmidt in a studio in Greenwich Village, where they were members of the Society of Illustrators and the Artists' Guild. Through these contacts, Sinel landed a job with the Lennon Mitchell advertising agency in 1923. In 1924, he published a book, A Book of American Trademarks and Devices. During this period, Sinel claimed, so he probably did, work on a number of product designs, as was not uncommon for advertising agencies of those days, but documented examples have not yet been found. After his International scale design in 1929, he would design many products for industry throughout the 1930s.20

# Modern Design Meets the Depression

Anoer professnattce tothe newflmoden den was theceativ theal and stage world. The theatrical set design profession was suffering from the increasing popu-Al Joi  ow wy Bwyny h e had staged live performances were now converting to movie theaters.

![](images/d9d39cddae680a8b65b2d77805fce821ab6522b444cbf4d5dd1e36b144b980f8.jpg)

Norman Bel Geddes (18931958), a nationally recognized ee l niv when urged by Ray Graham, of the Graham-Paige Motor Corporation in 1927. Then 34, Bel Geddes proceeded to design a series of five brass concept car models for Graham, each design representing a 20 percent increase in modernization over the previous one, demonstrating progressive annual model changes, each of which was not to be out of style for five years. Car No. 1 was designed in 1928, intended to be prototyped in fall of 1929, but it was never built due to the stock market crash and the onset of the Depression. Despite this setback, he was to become one of the most flamboyant and imaginative pioneers of the new profession.

Norman Bel Geddes, ca. 1934 (February 1934 Fortune magazine. Photograph by Maurice Goldberg).

Bel Geddes was born in Adrian, Michigan, as Norman Melancton Geddes. Raised in New Philadelphia, Ohio, he was a high school dropout and tried unsuccessfully to acquire formal art training at the Cleveland Institute of Art and the School of the Art Institute of Chicago. In 1913 he began work as advertising illustrator and art director for the Peninsular Engraving Company in Chicago, and then for the Barnes-Crosby Advertising Agency in Detroit. He also worked in 1916-1917 for Aline Barnsdall's Little Theatre in Los Angeles as a set designer, where he was attracted to the theater. In 1916, when he married Helen Belle Schneider, they incorporated their names to form the surname Bel Geddes. In 1918, he became scene designer for the Metropolitan Opera and designed and directed a number of very successful theatrical works, as well as an ice show starring worldwide skating star Sonja Henie. Bel Geddes's daughter, Barbara Bel Geddes, was born in 1922 and would become a well-known Broadway and film actress starting in 1947. The essence of Bel Geddes's theatrical set design career was to create environments and costumes to entertain and take people into magical worlds of imagination.

In the mid-1920s, Bel Geddes, for his own amusement and that of his friends, began creati elaborate table games. In the previous ten years, a public ascination with sal moels had been developing. Construction of model airplanes that actually flew became popular with young boys, and electric model railroads at family Christmastime evolved into hobbyists constructing elaborate and extensive basement layouts with realistic miniature trains, bridges, lakes, mountains, trees, people and buildings. The idea of realistic miniature replications of the real world apparently also fascinated Bel Geddes.

He started with a five-by-three-foot football field, with players kicking, running, and tackling. That was followed by a mechanical baseball game and an indoor golf game, built with wooden blocks, wire screen, and paste-soaked newspaper. He added green velvet and shrubbery." The game took over the dining room table and disrupted family life.

In 1925, Bel Geddes was creating film set designs for D. W. Griffith and the Cecil B. DeMille film Feet of Clay. He also designed costumes for Max Reinhardt, the Austrian-born stage and film actor and director. During this period, his wife of ten years left him and took his two children. Depressed, Bel Geddes was taken to Belmont Stakes racetrack for distraction, and he was inspired by the pageantry there to invent a new game, which he called the Nutshell Jockey Club. It was a 20-inch-wide racetrack built on a 28-foot-long table.

Running times were identical to real track times. There was green velvet turf, white fencing, bleachers, a judges' stand, and boxes for stable owners. The game was electrical and featured 800 cast-iron horses, each three inches long and realistically painted. Twenty horses lined up behind the electric starting gate and were pulled by invisible silk threads connected to unseen pulleys. A "chance machine" varied the speed of individual horses to simulate an actual race. The setup took over the whole basement at his Manhattan brownstone on East 37th Street that doubled as his office, and was estimated to have cost Bel Geddes \$4,000, plus the labor of his staff. Every Saturday evening, eight races were run, announced over an amplifier, with a recording of racetrack bedlam and the sound of horses' hooves to lend atmosphere. Race betting, sale of horses and stables, and odds were conducted through a racing secretary. It was a live horse race in every respect except the weather.

Within two months, nearly 100 people owned stables in the Nutshell Jockey Club. The game was a magnet for visiting Hollywood notables, since racing in California would not be legalized until 1933. They included Charlie Chaplin; Douglas Fairbanks, Jr.; Leslie Howard; Ethyl Barrymore; Adolph Menjou; and Basil Rathbone. It was so successful that New York ao J W s people" out.' Bel Geddes was already famous.

After he opened his industrial design office in 1927, Bel Geddes left the theatrical field, ext  ca set or the 3 Brwa prouction Dead En.Despi offer b ambling syndicates to operate the Nutshell Jockey Club (one offered "the blond little Leonardo S NeYork B Gn

Department stores continued to enthusiastically promote modern design. In 1928, the B. Altman department store in New York held an exhibition called Twentieth Century Taste. Lord and Taylorannounced an international competition in design and trade symbols. Frak Simon engaged none other than Norman Bel Geddes to develop a modern approach for window displays. His windows featured dramatically lit, very modern-looking, Cubistic pyramids andmetalic mannequins. When revealed to the publ, they created massive traff js and crowded sidewalks. Within 90 days, every store on Fifth Avenue had changed their windows to the new modern style. Also in 1928, Bel Geddes was commissioned by the Simmons Company to design steel beds that would go to market in 1932.

Mac a aaic Inteatial ExpsitionAr  Inustry  gz by tee e Aus uTe c, most designed by well-known European designers, such as a powder room with products from the Wiener WerkstätViena Workshop) by Jos Hoffmann, direcor he Austri Academy of Fine Art; a dining room by French jewelry designer Maurice Dufrène; a man's study and dining room by Bruno Paul, head of the German Vereinigte Staatsschulen für Freie und Angewandete Kunst (United State Schools for Fine andApplied Arts); and a butcher shop and living room by Italian designer Gio Ponti (1891-1979), who had just founded Domus (Home), an Italian architectural and design magazine that promoted the Wiener Werkstätte as wells the Italian Novocento Nine Hundre group to which Ponti belonged since its founding in 1926.

European émigré designer Kem Weber, who had just designed a skyscraper side table/ vanity; architect William Lescaze (1896-1969); and architect Eugene Schoen (1880-1957) designed the American rooms, which included work by German silversmith émigré Peter MüllerMunk, wholater woul beceladinginustril desner. Europen des was sill the main draw.

From February 12 to March 24, 1928, Richard Bach, of the Metropolitan Museum of Art, decided to emulate the 1925 Paris exposition with French room ensembles in an exhibition, Contemporary American Design, the eleventh in the series, which was intended to conclude in March, but by then, 100,000 had attended and he extended the exhibition to September 2. The subtitle, The Architect and the Industrial Arts, revealed that Bach favored prominent American architects such as John W. Root, Ralph Walker, Raymond Hood, Ely Kahn, and European architects such as Eliel Saarinen, Joseph Urban, and Eugene Schoen, to lead the new profession of industrial design, just as architects had led in Europe, and that was in fact the very purpose of the exhibition. As Bach said in the catalog,

[The Museum] has secured the enthusiastic cooperation of nine architects of distinction in this and other cities, who have made the exhibition practically their own by planning every detail of it themselves, from the general design of the gallery as a whole to the minutiae of their individual exhibits. Hence the appropriateness of the special title of the exhibition, "The Architect and the Inustrial Arts," as it is a fne andinstructiv illustration o what the architect can do outsie of the field which we commonly associate with his profession, and it was gratifying to us to learn that they welcomed the opportunity of showing this very thing.2

American designers and AUDAC members were understandably appalled that they were

![](images/648693e4408db5022d84174f986310669586a5f2e3beee4855a2c57ec4ba1806.jpg)

excluded from these shows in favor of European and American architects, particularly in the industrial arts arena, where they had demonstrated their abilities in product design far more than architects had. In retaliation, they began planning their own show for the following year.

Another designer, 36-year-old Raymond Loewy (1893- 1986), opened his New York office in 1929. His first product client was Sigmund Gestetner, head of the British Gestetner Company, for which he redesigned a Model 66 duplicator (mimeograph) machine from a hodgepodge of shapes into a smooth single form after building a clay model. Gestetner liked it so well he paid

Loewy \$2,000. It would be introduced to the market in 1933, and the same design would be used for 15 years afterward.

Raymond Fernand Loewy was born in Paris, and as a 15-year-old in 1908, he saw the Brazilian inventor Alberto Santos Dumont (1873-1932) fly his handmade airplane about a hundred yards over a polo field in the Bois de Boulogne in Paris. Inspired, Loewy designed a rubber-band-powered scale-model airplane and named it the Monoplan Ayrel. It won the James Gordon Bennett Cup for model airplanes. Bennett was the American editor of the Paris edition of the New York Herald and the Paris Herald. Teenage boys offered to buy Loewy's plane in such numbers that he patented it and formed a company, the Ayrel Corporation, to produce quantities in rented space. It was advertised and merchandized all over France, and it sold so well he had an offer to purchase his company, and promptly sold it so he could continue his studies.3

He graduated from Paris University in 1910 and from 1914 to 1918 served in the French Army as a captain in the Engineering Corps during World War I, where at the front he decorated his dugout with flowered wallpaper, draperies, and tufted pillows. During four years of war, during which he was burned by mustard gas, he rose from private to captain and received the French Legion of Honor. In 1918 he graduated from the Ecole de Lanneau in Paris.

Loewy immigrated to America in 1919 to join his brother Georges, a doctor in Manhattan, with only \$40 in his pocket and still wearing his captain's uniform (the only clothing he had). His sketching on the ship so impressed Sir Harry Gloster Armstrong, then British consul general in New York, that he gave Loewy a note of introduction to the publisher Condé Nast, where he got his first job. He also worked as a fashion illustrator for Vogue magazine, Harper's Bazaar magazine, and Wanamaker's, Saks Fifth Avenue, and Bonwit Teller department stores. He designed costumes for Florenz Ziegfeld before establishing his office. Later, when asked his first impressions upon arriving in America, Loewy replied,

To put it briefly, I was amazed at the chasm between the excellent quality of much American production and its gross appearance, clumsiness, bulk, and noise. Could this be the leading nation in the world, the America of my dreams? I could not imagine how such brilliant manufacturers, nist,  uese cul put  wi it r .

As mentioned earlier, the acclaimed "Dean of Advertising," Ernest Elmo Calkins, had been a vocal proponent of more stylish and attractive products for several years. Now he put his money where his mouth was. In 1929, he appointed 40-year-old Egmont Arens (1889-1966) as art director for Calkins & Holden, where Arens initiated and headed an internal industrial design departent. Arens was born in Cleveland, Ohio, and at a young age was sent by his family to New Mexico to recover from tuberculosis. From 19l1l to 1914 he attended the University of New Mexico, and from 1915 to 1916, the University of Chicago. He began his career as sports editor of the Albuquerque Tribune-Citizen in 1916, and from 1917 to 1923 operated his own Washington Square Bookstore on West Eighth Street in New York, where he established the Flying Stag Press, printing magazines with an upstairs press.

His interest in the arts led to editing and printing Playboy magazine (no, not that one) in 1919, the first American magazine devoted to modern art, named after Irish playwright John Million ST    W. I h - and fashion magazines in the country, and later served as managing editor of Creative Arts magazine.

y

1927 he was designing and seeing to the manufacture of a line of lamps and other consumer pructs, hic  cught the Calkins & Holen.

At an American Management Association (AMA) annual meeting in 1929, an officer of the KitchenAid Manufacturing Company of Dayton, Ohio, E.B. French, reported that Egmont Arens's 1928 redesign ofhis company' KitchenAid mixer (possibly KitchenAid Model G) had h . An bri \$150. A representativ  the L.C.Smih typewrer cmpany id that his compy iryy .

Busine as we eigct he ealiyendehiy needed to deal with. Earlier in 1929, the AMA had conducted seminars for businessmen on whether "intelligent and profitable use [can] be made of the modernistic style of design." Seminar titles included "How the Retailer Merchandizes Present-Day Fashion, Style, and Art"; How he Manufacturer Copes with Fashion, Style, and Art"; and The Renaissance  Art in American Business."

At the AMA annual meeting cited in the paragraph above, keynote address speaker E. Grosvenor Plowman, advisor on merchandising problems to the Associated Industries of Massachusetts, stated that the modernistic style was the "offspring" of the Jazz Age, and that "the modern style will remain as long as the machine in its present form is the characteristic of world production, [changing] its outward form from year to year, just as women's dress styles change," and that manufacturers would have to learn to balance faddism and permanence.6 Plowman clearly thought that modern design was "faddish" and that "permanence" (the way products always looked) was quite acceptable. The date of this particular AMA annual meeting was coincidentally on October 29, 1929, the exact day of the stock market crash that initiated the Great Depression.

Investors had been buying stocks on margin, that is, on credit, for years. Brokerage firms would lend \$9 for every \$1 an investor deposited, which vastly overpriced the stock market. When the market fell, brokers called in these loans, which could not be paid back, so many investors were wiped out. RCA stock, at \$549 per share in 1929, dropped to almost \$0 by 1932. Even before the stock market crashed, many businesses were making more products than they could sell and were overstocked. But now, many Americans drastically reduced their spending on consumer goods and services. Manufacturing and sales were soon down by 50 percent, and unemployment would rise to 25 percent.

Many people lost their jobs, and among these was Helen Dryden, the "highest-paid woman artist." She held her art director job at the Dura Company until the stock market crash of 1929, when she was let go and replaced by George W. Walker (18961993), who had also just lost his job at Graham Paige. Walker was born in Chicago and attended the Cleveland School of Art and the Otis Art Institute, Los Angeles, in 1916. In 1920 he began his career as an advertising illustrator in a Los Angeles department store. He returned to Cleveland as a freelance illustrator and established his own firm, working for department stores and Premier Press.

He entered the automotive business in 1928 by working for the Peerless Auto Company, and then for the Baker-Raulang body company, both in Cleveland. Amos Northup (1889- 1937), then chief designer for the Murray Corporation of America in Detroit, visited Baker-Raulang and urged Walker to come to Detroit, where he interviewed and worked briefly for Harley Earl and John Tjaarda at General Motors, and then for Graham Paige, working with Amos Northup until the company failed in 1929 after the crash. Now he was with Dura, which was also an automotive parts supplier.

Northup, an automotive designer born in Bellevue, Ohio, had attended Cleveland Polytechnic Institute, where he studied design, and began his career as a furniture and interior designer. In 1918, he joined Pierce Arrow as the company's truck designer, but soon he was designing interior colors and trim for passenger cars. In 1921 he left Pierce Arrow and opened his own private studio, doing interiors for Pierce Arrow, Leon Rubay, and the 1924 Wills Sainte Claire. In 1927, he was hired by the Murray Corporation, the second-largest independent body producer, and shared in the design of the 1928 Hupmobile. In 1928, he would leave Murray to join Willys-Overland in Toledo, Ohio.

Norman Bel Geddes was now concerned with other enterprises. In 1929, Bel Geddes was designing products and buildings for the Toledo Scale Company, a commission enabled by his uncle, Frederick Geddes, a director of the company and a trustee of the Toledo Museum of Art, within which Bel Geddes would design a theater stage. But because of the stock market crash, his product and factory designs for Toledo never saw fruition.

One of Bel Geddes's former apprentices, also a successful set designer, ventured into the new profession. In 1929, 25-year-old Henry Dreyfuss (1904-1972) opened his own office at

580 Fifth Avenue, New York, for stage and industrial design activities. He had just won a competition for the design of a "Phone of the Future" sponsored by Bell Laboratories and would begin work on it in 1930, although it would not be produced until 1937. Dreyfuss was born in Brooklyn, New York, to a family in the theatrical materials supply business. He completed studies as an apprentice to Norman Bel Geddes in 1924 and produced 250 stage sets for a number of theaters before 1928, when, as we know, Bel Geddes closed his career in the theater and entered the industrial design field. Dreyfuss was now on his own.

![](images/e110d5b47d2a093439d7db79cdb6c018ad884ff340470954c53b18a85b191354.jpg)

Over the year before the 1929 stock market crash, several art/design schools had initiated programs to educate engineers in art and design, at the request of major manufacturing corporations. In Toledo, Ohio, the Owens Bottle Glass Company convinced the Toledo Museum School of Design to initiate courses in industrial design for its engineering employees in 1928.

Henry Dreyfuss, ca. 1934 (February 1934 Fortune magazine. New York Times/ Redux).

Wilfred Readio, head of CIT's Industrial Arts program, Department of Painting and Decoration, which taught commercial illustration, costumedesign, textile design, interior decoration, stage design, and printing, headed the course.?

There were three sections in the course, and Donald Dohner, who taught design, and Alexanelow w   eerTe  o under Readio.

Westinghouse in 1930 hired Donald Roscoe Dohner (1892-1943), one of the CIT instructors, to become director of art in the engineering department at Westinghouse, a position in which he controlle the perance   products manucure in the mpany'plants. Over the next four years, with a staff of eight, most recently graduated students from CIT, Dohner would design many Westinghouse products from locomotives to ashtrays, including meters, control panels, and switchboards. He worked with toolmakers, die makers, shop foremen, and superintendents. He studimarket materials, andmanuacturig perations and made a point of working in close contact with engineers, not only on appearance and sales apal, u n ost as manacture nie n y m.

![](images/8b3f959138a12d7bf2c51e468e8b02b24196450ec0cf8b01aa834c1d7788ded6.jpg)

Another major manufacturer, the Owens-Illinois Glass Company, in 1930 hired Edwin W. Fuerst (1903-1988) as a designer and stylist of its Toledo design studio, which specialized in glass containers and proprietary bottles. Born in Ottawa, Ohio, Fuerst left high school during his junior year after the death of his father and worked at the Detroit Steel Range Company. He

Donald R. Dohner, ca. 1934 (February 1934 Fortune magazine. Photograph by Bachrach).

then worked at Willys-Overland Motors, Inc., for a short time and returned to Detroit Steel Ran paymaster. While there, in 1927, he started attending classes at the Toledo Museum School of

Design, mentioned earlier, until 1932. But in 1929, at age 26, he established his own design office in Toledo. One of his first clients was Owens-Illinois, who hired him as designer and stylist of its design studio.

Other manufacturers were beginning to see the light. In 1930, Gustav Jensen (1898-1954) was hired by the International Nickel Company to design a new sink using Monel metal (a copper-nickel alloy) to popularize this new material. He also designed a telephone for AT&T (as one of ten entries in an AT&T "Telephone of the Future" competition won by Henry Dreyfuss)

![](images/fc4157c803036da8e5424b235c23adc22bd4c9d257d0288a0b1f9a8124681d5d.jpg)

![](images/c4da39335c410c27891cc8c66027818cd49fff2da1badd5151e156f5706e6fbb.jpg)  
Radio by Jensen (Pencil Points magazine,

and a water heater for L.O. Koven & Brothers. Jensen was born in Copenhagen, Denmark, where his father was a banker and lawyer, and his mother came from a long line of ministers. He studied philosophy at

Gustav Jensen, ca. 1934 (February 1934 Fortune magazine. Photograph by Ruth Bernhard).

the University of Copenhagen but wanted to become an opera singer. Nevertheless, he developed an interest in architecture, which led him to art and typography.

He emigrated to the United States in 1918, settling in New York City at the age of 20. He soon had clients including Colophon Quarterly, Covici-Friede, United Drug Company, and DuPont, for whom he designed book jackets, bindings, and cosmetics. He had begun work in industrial design in 1928 and was soon designing letters, borders, perfume bottles, cosmetic boxes, telephones, radios, silverware, toasters, and, as noted above, kitchen sinks. He called himself a "designer to industry" and would be productive up to World War II.

Forty-two-year-old famed sculptor Alfonso Iannelli (1898-1965) in 1930 turned to industrial design by beginning to work as a designer for Sunbeam Appliance Company. He designed logos and appliances with modernist shapes, including a Cubist style, chrome-plated faucet for the Mueller Mixing Valve Company. Born in Italy to a poor family, he apprenticed with a decorator before immigrating to America with his family in 1898. He studied at the Art Students League in New York and in 1900 moved to California to work in architecture, architectural decoration, and stained glass. From 1910 to 1915 he designed hundreds of posters and show cards for the Orpheum Theater Chain. In 1914 he came to Chicago to design sculptural ornamental work for Midway Gardens, a huge entertainment center on Lake Michigan, designed by Frank Lloyd Wright, but Wright claimed all of Iannelli's work as his own. Iannelli refused to ever work for Wright again. In 1915, he established Iannelli Studios and began teaching at the Art Institute of Chicago, where in 1922 he was honored with a one-man exhibition of his work, which included modernist lamps and furnishings.

In 1930, Nathan George Horwitt (1898-1990) designed the Beta chair, using steel, chrome-plated tubing, for the Howell Manufacturing Company. Horwitt was born in Russia and as a child immigrated to the United States, where he attended the City College of New York and New York University. He later attended the Art Students League of New York and served in the U.S. Army during World War I. He was hired as an advertising copywriter and was eventually promoted to director of advertising. He established the firm of Design Engineers in Manhattan in the late 1920s, and after that he developed patents to be sold to manufacturers.

![](images/094ab1592682deea4ad3f67e7b4274af473d1ba66a962ab044c9c840f685356e.jpg)

Norman Bel Geddes was becoming interested in the future. He wrote an editorial in 1930 entitled "Ten Years from Now" for the Ladies' Home Journal, predicting 50 industrial innovations, including the following:

Nathan George Horwitt, ca. 1960 (courtesy Movado Group).

Aluminum will replace steel in railroad car construction . synthetic materials will replace products of nature in structural materials … synthetic materials will replace wool, cotton, and silk in textiles . aircraft will attain a speed of six miles per minute [360 mph] . a net of airlines will encircle the world … [and most prophetically] artists will be thinking in terms of industrial problems of their age.8

Hisvisn  the uue would wihin to years inspe hi o pric n desg naic fuuricrais planes, anhistreettigbok.Bel Gees was activel his work during 193132 with similar magazine articles and mounting dozens o exhibitions of his work across the country and abroad, including Toledo, New York, and Vienna.

Designers were beginning to work together to promote their work. Members of the American Union of Decorative Artists and Craftsmen (AUDAC), formed in 1927 but ignored by what they could do. They mounted their own exhibition in early 1930 at the Grand Central Galleries in New York to counter museum promotions of European designs. The exhibition featured five modern interiors filled with American products already on the market to demonstrate that human comfort, practicality, and utility alone were appropriate American design hal

Well over 120 exhibitors were represented, including 27 industrial designers. The October 1932 issue of Popular Mechanics reviewed the exhibition and reported that the exhibition was proof that art and the machine have formed a partnership" and that "the best manufacturing enterprises .. were seeking counsel from the small group of men who are equipped to design beauy into the products actory machines."Among he any exhibit outilrian ts were Gustav Jensen's Monel metal sink, Walter Dorwin Teague's scale model of his Marmon car design, Jo Sinel' International ticket scale, andRussel Wright'scocktail shaker. Wright lso n  haay m  wu at uc   MMeAt. Wr' wr succesl that e began usnes Russel Writ Incooat, wih his wi My, designing and producing spun aluminum accessories for the home.

Russel Wright (1904-1976) was born into a historic old American family in Lebanon, Ohio. His mother had direct lineage with two signers of the Declaration of Independence, and his father and grandfather were local judges. Wright trained in art under Frank Duveneck, American figure and portrait painter, at the Art Academy of Cincinnati while still in high school. In 1921 and 1924, he attended the Art Students League in New York and the Columbia Universityo Architecture. Despite hisinterest in art, he dutifully followed his family tradition by studying for a legal career at Princeton University. While there, he was a member of the Princeton Triangle Club and won several Tiffany & Company prizes for outstanding World War I memorial sculptures. When he left Princeton, he abandoned law as a career and immediately became a theatrical set designer for Norman Bel Geddes in New York, and later for fhea rs. Inehe rh n theatrical props and small decorative cast metal objects.

In 1931, AUDAC would publish its Annual of American Design to promote its members and to record its exhibition of 1930 with 176 pages, 300 photos of products, and essays about design. Frank Lloyd Wright (1857-1959) wrote about the "Principles of Design"; historian Lewis Mumford (1895-1990) wrote about "Culture and Machine Art"; Norman Bel Geddes wrote about "Business Interiors"; Kem Weber described the "Modern Shop"; Paul Frankl wrote about the "Home of Yesterday, Today, and Tomorrow"; and Richard Bach, of the Metropolitan Museum of Art, wrote about the "Resurgence of Quality," among other contributors. Sections of the book included "The Shop," "The Home," "Textiles," "New Materials," "Industrial Design," "Architecture Halls, Walls, and Fittings," "Graphic Arts in Advertising," and "Commercial Photography." Bel Geddes praised the successful collaboration between art and industry.9

The publication, a 9% x 12/4 inch hardcover book, was a masterpiece of Art Deco design and probably the earliest documentation of American industrial design, as it entered the public scene. Soon there would be much more visibility for industrial designers, with the help of museums, business magazines, and national merchandizing organizations.

From May 2 through July 1, 1931, the Brooklyn Museum held an exhibition of Modern Decoration and Design by members of the American Union of Decorative Artists and Craftsmen (AUDAC), the organization's first large general museum exhibition, which was billed by the museum as "about the first opportunity the public has had to see a representative indication of the trend and importance of modern design among American craftsmen." Well-known exhibitors included Frank Lloyd Wright, Rockwell Kent, Lee Simonson, Joseph Urban, Paul T. Frankl, Egmont Arens, Donald Deskey, Helen Dryden, Norman Bel Geddes, Nathan George Horwit, Gustav Jensen, Walter Dorwin Teague, Walter von Nessen, and Russel Wright, among others. Kem Weber, of Hollywood, California, designed a suite of rooms for the 'investor of moderate means."10

By 1931, several additional major organizations were initiating departments of industrial design. One was Montgomery Ward in Chicago, the mail-order giant, which hired 31-yearold Anne Swainson (1900-1955), a Swedish émigré and fashion coordinator, as head of its new Bureau of Design, which was described as "a service to buyers in evaluating the quality and appearance of products that Montgomery Ward ordered from thousands of outside suppliers." Swainson was educated in applied arts at Columbia University and had been working as a "fashion coordinator" at Chase Copper & Brass. The Bureau concept was her invention."

She and her staff redesigned Ward's catalog, replacing old-fashioned woodcuts with photography, and insisting on the authority to approve or reject all projects. She was the first woman executive at Montgomery Ward and staffed her department with arts graduates from the Armour Institute of Technology (later to become the Illinois Institute of Technology, IIT) and the Art Institute of Chicago. Among them was George A. Beck (1908-1977), a recent graduate of the Chicago Academy of Fine Arts. Over the years, the Bureau would employ dozens of designers who would later become prominent in the field. With her staff, Swainson assisted in merchandising, product, or packaging design for many of the companies that pro-

duced soft or hard goods to be listed in the catalogs, as many often had no internal design capability. Products repackaged or designed by the Bureau of Design were adorned with the "MW" (for Montgomery Ward) logo.

Another major manufacturing firm, the American Radiator & Standard Sanitary Corporation, the Kohler of its time, which produced bathroom and kitchen fixtures in porcelain enamel, as well as heating equipment, engaged a 34-year-old designer from one of the art industries, glassware. George Sakier (1897-1988) began in 1931 as a consultant and later became head of the company's Bureau of Design Development.

![](images/1bf321563f2f1b30c1a9039a6df94941440a2650cc37de3f19bf3af5b00f1665.jpg)

Sakier was a man with multidisciplinary talents. Born in the United States, he studied at the Engineering School of Columbia University and at Pratt Institute in Brooklyn. At age 19, in 1916, he composed the text Machine Design and Descriptive Geometry.

George Sakier, ca. 1934 (February 1934 Fortune magazine).

During World War I, he worked as a camouflage technician, and after the war he continued to work as an artist, combining technology with classicism. In the 1920s, he worked in art direction for Vogue, Harper's Bazaar, and Modes and Manners. As a contributor to European art periodicals, he stimulated the rescue of a Mayan collection of artifacts from the cellar of the alais uTrodéro a palace built in 8r the Worl's Fair Pris, provig to ance that America had an ancient art.

Sakier's fine art paintings were shown in the Julien Levy ultramodern gallery in New York, established in 1931, which also displayed the work of Pablo Picaso, Max Ernst, Marcel Duchamp, and Salvadore Dali. From 129 until 7, Sakier worke as primary consultant for the Fostoria Glass Company of Moundsville, West Virginia, and designed the company's Art Deco designs, including black Art Deco vases No. 2404 and No. 2428 in 1930.

Other companies were engaging design consultants to ensure that their products were of the latt ode esigThe Tole Scal Copay  Toleo, Ohio i Decbe n  a nership of Harold Van Doren and John Gordon Rideout, forming the firm Van Doren and healt pern weigher"scale i colorful,Art Decosysaper yls.
