![](images/9e86c88e3198a35280eb3786e09785a018d0781bd948847305127bbeb5bb16d1.jpg)  
Harold Van Doren, ca. 1934 (February 1934 Fortune magazine. Photograph by Souren).

to Paris from 1922 to 1924 with a fellowship in art history. He worked as a lecturer at the Louvre in Paris, as an artist for the Chicago Tribune's Paris edition, as an actor in a Jean Renoir film, and as a translator of books on French artists Cézanne and Renoir. He would have had an opportunity to attend the 1925 Art Deco exhibition in Paris. In 1927, he became assistant director of the Minneapolis Institute of Arts while serving as an art director for Minneapolis newspapers and writing articles for the Saturday Evening Post and Encyclopedia Britannica.

In 1930, his college classmate H.D. Bennett recruited him to Toledo as a color consultant for Bennett's new enterprise in the development of synthetic materials. Van Doren developed commercial applications for Plaskon, a plastic molding compound, and formed his design partnership with John Gordon Rideout (1898-1951), who was born in St. Paul, Minnesota.

Rideout entered the University of

Washington as an architecture student, but World War I interrupted his study and he served in the Navy in 1917. In the 1920s he worked as a graphic designer in Chicago and was one of the founders of the Chicago Society of Typographic Arts. In 1931 he came to Toledo to partner with Van Doren in industrial design.

With the Depression deepening and more manufacturers seeking modern design to boost sales, more and more design-

![](images/8225b2c105a3c38849b7bfb1274cea9069a61e5c8bcbe222c0c0479634d4f33a.jpg)  
John M. Little, ca. 1949 courtesy Linda Braming).

ers were getting into this new profession of industrial design. John McLeod Little (1906-1996) opened an industrial design firm, J.M. Little and Associates, in Minneapolis in 1931. Born in Minneapolis, he studied at the Chicago Academy of Fine Arts, the Art League of Minneapolis, and the Minneapolis School of Art from 1927 to 1929, after which he worked for the Batten, Barton, Durstine &

![](images/1f92f9b20ed23b5eea9c5386205e7ed2a31b1c289cb9acd4eb45c1cb5a98daa8.jpg)  
John Gordon Rideout, 1937, on his wedding day (courtesy Alyce Gordon Rideout).

Osborn (BBDO) advertising agency. While managing his office in the 1930s, he accepted professorships in the field of design at the University of Michigan, Ann Arbor, and later at the University of California at Berkeley.

Museums were still promoting modern design. The Twelfth Exhibition of Contemporary American Industrial Art was held

at the Metropolitan Museum of Art in New York, October 12 to November 22, 1931. Industrial designers among the exhibitors of furniture included Norman Bel Geddes, Donald Deskey, . Frank, Walter von Nessen, Gilbert Rohde, Joseph Sinel, nd Walter Dorwn Teague;

exhibitors of metalwork and lighting fixtures included Donald Deskey, Henry Dreyfuss, Paul T.Frank,and Walter von Nesse;anan exhibto silvrware was eterMüll-M

The public was increasingly aware of modern design through popular motion pictures, which almost inevitably were filmed in sets that were completely modern in Art Deco décor, costumes, furnishings, and accessories. While most people suffered economically because of the Depression and lived quite frugally, they seemed to love escaping to theaters to see their favorite stars in modern, luxurious and extravagant settings. With all the bad economic news described in the Depression, it is easy to assume that many did not have enough money to spend on consumer goods. But the most prominent advertiser of the time disputed that.

In 1932, Elmo Calkins of the leading advertising agency, Calkins & Holden, wrote an introduction to a book, What Consumer Engineering Really Is (an unfortunate title for what would have been better called industrial design), written by Egmont Arens and Roy Shelton of Calkins' industrial styling division. Analyzing the Depression, Calkins wrote,

Many stopped buying while still able financially to continue, and many are still able but restrained by fear and misplaced thrift. The sudden cessation of buying slowed up the entire industrial machine. Retail storekeepers curtailed orders to factories. Factories cut down production, reduced wages, laid off men, still further reducing the number of customers for goods. In a comparatively short time, with all the resources of the country still intact, we had Depression. There were no longer enough buyers for the large quantities of goods we had learned to make and distribute so abundantly. B3

Not only did Calkins correctly analyze the problem, but he had a theory, "obsoletism," for solving it:

Obsoletism is another device for stimulating consumption. The element of style is a consideration in buying many things. Clothes go out of style and are replaced long before they are worn out. That principle extends to other products—motorcars, bathrooms, radios, foods, refrigerators, furniture. People are persuaded to abandon the old and buy the new to be up-to-date, to have the right and correct thing. Does there seem to be a sad waste in the process? Not at all. Wearing things out does not increase prosperity, but buying things does. Thrit in the industrial society in which we now live consists of keeping all the factories busy. Any plan that increases consumption ofgoods is justifiable if we believe that prosperity is a desirable thing. If we do not, we can turn back the page to earlier and more primitive times when people got along with little and made everything last as long as possible. We have built up a complicated industrial machine and we must go on with it, or throw everything in reverse and go backward.14

Calkins expressed what allindustrial designers sensed instinctively but many manufacturers and businessmen of the time did not. Although "planned obsolescence" would acquire a negative connotation 25 years later (Hidden Persuaders, by Vance Packard, 1957), nevertheless it was precisely what was needed to turn around the Depression, and is still a basic source of u previously owned" market for goods, many lower economic classes were able to own products nhu har

The Museum of Modern Art (MoMA) had been founded in 1929 to promulgate and become an arbiter of modern art. Its first exhibit that year featured the "Big Four" of Postimpressionism: Cézanne, Gauguin, Seurat, and Van Gogh. In February and March, 1932, MoMA held its first architectural exhibition, The International Exhibition of Modern Architecture, and published a book, The International Style: Architecture Since 1922. It was MoMA's intention to establish architecture as a form of modern art to rival painting, and as the intellectual origin of international modern design. Organizers of the exhibition were Alfred H. Barr, Jr. (1902- 1981), the founder and head of the museum; Henry Russell Hitchcock (19031987), an architectural historian; and the wealthy connosseur/collector/architect Philip Johnson (1906 2005).

Hitchcock and Johnson were fascinated with European modernists such as Bauhaus architects Walter Gropius and Mies van der Rohe, as well as architects Le Corbusier and J.J.P. Oud, all owhom Hitchcock had called the "New Pioneers" in his 1929 book, Modern Architecure. Other architects represented included George Howe, Richard Neutra, Raymond Hood, and Rudolph Schindler. This new style feature spare, rectangular, steel an glass structures. The term "International Style" became the formal name of modern architecture and would dominate the architectural scene for the next 30 years.

Interestingly, Frank Lloyd Wright (1867-1959), America's greatest architect, was invited to participate but declined because he had misgivings about this new style, which he detested because it was counter to his own "organic" style of architecture. In fact, Wright had inspired many of the lauded European architects of the so-called International Style, including Gropius, Theo van Doesburg, Le Cobusier, and Richard Neutra, with his Wasmuth Portfolio published in Europe in 1910. Now, in 1932, Wright was regarded by many U.S. architects as an old-school, irascible, has-been. Philip Johnson had in fact disparagingly referred to Wright as "America's greatest 19th-century architect.

The architect that Wright had inspired in Germany, Walter Gropius, was now, however, having difficult problems with the increasing power of Adolf Hitler.

# Streamlining and the Chicago Fair

The German Bauhaus, much admired by MoMA, was in serious trouble in Dessau, Germany. Faced with political and financial pressure, Gropius and Moholy-Nagy had resigned from the Bauhaus in 1928 and went to England, while architect Hannes Meyer, a committed Marxist who attempted to link the school to a vision of social and political transformation, assumed leadership. Some students were communists, others Nazis, both escalating political tensions. Architect Ludwig Mies van der Rohe (1886-1969) had become director of the Bauhaus in 1930, replacing Meyer, who had been forced by Nazis to resign at the same time that communist students were expelled.

In the month before the MoMA exhibition, the Dessau city council, pressured by the powerful National Socialist (Nazi) German Workers Party under Adolph Hitler, who was not yet in full power, dissolved the Bauhaus in that city, due to a "Jewish-Marxist art manifestation," a travesty that may have inspired MoMA to elevate the legacy of the Bauhaus to near martyrdom.

As a last-ditch effort to save the school, Mies van der Rohe, the last director of the Bauhaus, would move the school to Berlin, but within a year, Hitler would close the school in April 1933, and its faculty and students would scatter around the world, many coming to the United States later in the decade. There were other similar schools in Germany that were much larger in scope and number of students than the Bauhaus, such as the Industrial Art School in Berlin, with distinguished graduates such as Bruno Paul, Adolph Meyer, Kem Weber, and Mies van der Rohe himself, but such schools were not shut down by Hitler and thus were essentially forgotten in the United States.

In July 1932, Belle Kogan (1902-2000) opened her own design office, Belle Kogan Associates, at 185 Madison Avenue in New York City with a retainer from the Quaker Silver Company. The company had hired her in 1929 and sponsored her training in glass and silver manufacturing. Kogan was born in Ilyashevka, Russia, and had emigrated to the United States in 1906 with her parents, who settled in Allentown, Pennsylvania. She was the first of eight children, and she showed interest in art at an early age. Her Bethlehem High School art teacher encouraged her to study mechanical drawing, where she was the only girl in the class.

After graduation, she chose to attend the Pratt Institute in Brooklyn while teaching her high school class in mechanical drawing. But in 1920, she was forced to leave during her first m  Pratt nge th'jwey Durhi pe he he Ar Students League of New York as time permitted. After Quaker hired her, the company sent he  e no  G Art School in Pforzheim, Germany. The first female industrial designer was now in business for herself.

Other industrial designers werealready making names for themselves. Both Raymond Loeyn HeyDreypearn aazie overs. Dres nevi y Pulr Mecanic agazine n curn he Otoe issue workon is Tpeator wash-Iu hbe u erations of cost, function, and convenience.

Gilbert Rohde had designed furniture for the Heywood Wakefield Company, including a side chair in 1931, 250,000 of which would be sold over the next eight years. He had establish  eationhi  .Dee, resient h Herma Mil Furniure Copy, in 1929. Rohde convinced DePree that the company should manufacture modern furniture. In 1932, DePree appointed him as director of design to shift the company's orientation from tradiinl modeniturenistiue the olput Roeame

product. Rohde's designs of 1933 and 1934, including the clock he designed in 1933 for the Herman Miller Clock Company, would be equally strikingly modern, spare, and simple as many later designs of the 1950s.

A Design for Machine exhibit held in Philadelphia in 1932 featured furniture by Gilbert Rohde and designs by Russel Wright in a breakfast room that included lamps, tables, flatware, and accessories in aluminum, a material that had been used previously only in cookware. Since 1930, Wright and his wife had been designing and making decorative aluminum pieces in attractive, modern styles, some of which were cooking utensils attractive enough to be carried directly from the stove to the serving table. Wright had also designed an early table radio model along with his aluminum housewares.

More manufacturers were seeking designers. Jo Sinel in 1932 designed an Accousticon hearing aid for Dictaphone, a Radio-Victrola for RCA, Toastmaster tray accessories, and a Folmer-Graflex camera. Joseph B. Platt (18951968) in 1932 was engaged by the Parker Pen Company to design its Vacumatic fountain pen and its famous arrow-shaped

![](images/23afc0cdaa8bd2fec7ec26122db91ff3892660da1dd3f951983afa5ad529d3d6.jpg)  
Parker Pen clip by Platt, 1932 (U.S. Patent Office, D88,821).

ya ried June Evans (1898-1977), the daughter of Rudolph Evans, the sculptor who wouid create the 1,000-pound bronze statue of Thomas Jefferson that would be added to the Jefferson Memorial in Washington in 1947.

In the 1920s, Platt and his new wife had relocated to the French commune of Senlis, an expatriate American community north of Paris, where Platt worked as a correspondent, editor, and illustrator for Vanity Fair, Vogue, and House & Garden magazines. He illustrated magazine covers from about 1923 to 1925, and probably attended the Paris Art Deco Exposition. He and his wife hoped to become community leaders at Senlis, but this did not work out. Returning to the United States in about 1929, the Platts lived in New York City and in Little Compton, Rhode Island, a beach resort.

Their apartment in New York was in the same building as that of Raymond Loewy and his new wife, Jean Thompson, and the couples became good friends. Loewy was probably responsible for getting Platt the job at Parker Pen. Platt's wife, June, was also, according to Loewy, "an incomparable and creative cook," and starting in 1936 with June Platt's Party Cookbook, she used Loewy's name as an endorsement on the book jacket. She would write a number of popular cookbooks well into the 1950s, including the June Platt Cook Book in 1958. During the Depression, Platt and his wife collaborated in painting murals, probably WPA projects, one of which wrapped the dining room of the Country Club of Detroit in Grosse Pointe Farms, Michigan.

In 1932, another major manufacturer, the B.F. Goodrich Company in Akron, Ohio, engaged H. Creston Doner (1902-1991), who had just opened his design office in Akron, Ohio, to design the company's rubber products. Doner was born in Nevada, Ohio, and was raised on farms in Nebraska and Pennsylvania. After high school he installed radios and worked on oil wells in Oklahoma. Doner was flamboyant in both appearance and behavior. In 1929 he had designed a stylish English sport jacket in Oklahoma and had it tailored to his own specifications and patented. He always dressed fashionably and regularly wore an ascot with a jacket. In 1954, he would be selected as one of the 25 men to become a charter member of the

American Society of Beau Brummels (honoring those welldressed men in the spirit of George Bryan Brummell, the leader of English fashion at the beginning of the 19th century).

Alfons Bach (19041999) opened his own design firm in 1932, Alfons Bach Associates, in New York. Clients included Heywood-Wakefield, its subsidiary, Lloyd Manufacturing Company (Bach was a colleague of Kem Weber, a fellow German, who also worked for Lloyd); International Silver; Philco; and Trans World Airlines (TWA). Bach was born in Magdeburg, Germany, grew up in Munich; and studied in Berlin. He worked in set design and studied architecture at the Reimann School and the University of Berlin. He knew people at the Bauhaus but immigrated to the United States in 1926 and became a freelance designer.

Adolph Daniel "Al" Lambach, Sr. (19122008) arrived in New York in 1932 to work for the Progressive Table Company designing dinette furniture. Lambach was born in Germany and

![](images/6c52816bb44921b95e93ee5066316fe8571ccc944548fef77339b8c649347202.jpg)  
Alfons Bach, ca. 1970s (courtesy Anita S. Bach).

iigrate with his parents t Chicago  .He atteed Wshburn TradeSchool h studying mechanical engineering drafting, then served an apprenticeship learning furniture design in Europe, and returned to Grand Rapids, Michigan, then the center of furniture design in the United States. He worked for an office furniture company there while attending Davenport Business College.

The Aluminum Cooking Utensil Company of New Kensington, Pennsylvania, a division of the Aluminum Company of America (Alcoa), bought the services of Lurelle Van Arsdale

![](images/5491daef44f6275705d11cfdd254c936f320ae81de39f941d91536e560a661c0.jpg)

Guild (18981986) in November 1932 to design its line of Kensington Ware kitchen giftware utensils, which would be aggressively advertised and remain in production until World War II. Guild, born in Syracuse, New York, attendedSyracuse Universiy where he studied painting and 17th- and 18th-century design and crafting methods. He also did some freelance design work. After graduation, he and his wife relocated to New York City. Using his training in art and mechanical drawing, Guild worked in art industries, mainly home furnishings and the decorative arts.

Lurelle Guild, ca. 1934 (February 1934 Fortune magazine. Photograph by Rudolph Hindemith).

In 1924, Guild started his own business, Lurelle Guild Associates, which, with a staff of eight, made models and worked on product development. He also started Dale Decorators, a doorto-door decorating service, which employed 100 women who marketed wallpapers, curtains, and carpeting. He scoured the country for antiques and sold them. He placed samples of his products in retail shops and surveyed potential customers. When he invented a new product, he patented it and then assigned the patent to the manufacturer, charging a fee plus royalties. His early interest in crafts led him to study Chauncey Jerome (17931868), a New Haven clockmaker who was one of the fathers of mass production. Fascinated with history, Guild moved an entire early American village from New Hampshire to Darien, Connecticut, where he lived.

Guild designed the permanent museum at Alcoa's New York offices, which displayed individual pieces as works of art, utilizing modern display tables of extruded aluminum and Z-shaped window treatments that eliminated glare and allowed daylight to enter the room at various angles. For Kensington, Guild also designed the Kensington showroom in Rockefeller Center in New York City. Indirect lighting was used from below display shelves. The walls were painted so that they graduated from dark blue-green to white, and the floors were colormatched to them.

What attracted these early designers to enter a strange world of machinery and mass production? Of course, as mentioned earlier, industrial design had suddenly become a financially lucrative field, as manufacturers scrambled to find talent to meet intense public demand. But aside from that, there is something very satisfying to a creative person to know that his or her design would not just be printed in a magazine, exhibited in an art gallery, win an award, or sold in small quantities as a decorative craft, but would be mass produced, sold by the thousands, and distributed throughout the country to a large audience of the general public who would use and interact with his or her design on a daily basis. It was a feeling, not only of public recognition and acceptance, but one of a personal contribution to society, making the world a better place to live. This was a powerful incentive.

New York, of course, was the American center of culture, business, and style, and many of the early leading designers were working there. John D. Rockefeller, Jr. (19741960), had developed Rockefeller Center, a 12-acre complex in Midtown Manhattan, beginning in 1929. In 1930, Donald Deskey had designed home interiors for Rockefeller, a client connection that helped him win the competition for the interior design of Radio City Music Hall in Rockefeller Center, an extravagant Art Deco landmark that opened in December 1932 with a lavish stage show featuring Ray Bolger and Martha Graham. Deskey would work on the interior until 1934. The Music Hall would be restored to its original condition in 1999.

But the biggest attractions in New York were its famous skyscrapers. New York skyscrapers were then capturing the imagination of the world. The Chrysler Building in New York City had been completed in May 1930, becoming the tallest building in the world (71 stories, 1,046 feet). Started in 1928, it was a classic Art Deco design by architect William Van Allen (1883- 1954) for Walter P. Chrysler (1875-1940), president of the Chrysler Corporation he had formed in 1924 to manufacture Chrysler automobiles. Chrysler had been Time magazine's Man of the Year in 1928. Eleven months after the Chrysler building was completed, in April 1931, the 102-story Empire State Building was completed to capture the title of the world's tallest, at 1,454 feet.

These skyscrapers had been the inspiration of Paul Frankl for his stylish Skyscraper furniture in 1925, and he had written several books to publicize his business and to promote the new modern styles. In 1928 he had published New Dimensions: The Decorative Arts of Today in Words and Pictures, and in 1930, Form and Re-Form: Practical Handbook ofModern Interiors. Now, in 1932, he published Machine Made Leisure, capitalizing on the popular term "machine," which to the public was shorthand for the wonder of industrialization and mass production, just as "digital" or "computer" would be shorthand for the "Information Age" of the 1980s and 1990s.

But the skyscraper styles popularized by Frankl would soon be replaced in popularity by "streamlining," a style based on the public fascination with high speed, as celebrated in the new age of aircraft and racing cars, such as the Supermarine S.6B British seaplane which set the world speed record of 340 mph at the Schneider trophy air race of 1931 (and which would be developed into the famous British World War II fighter plane, the Spitfire), and Malcolm Cmbe Blu Bircr .h. he eek metal embodied in these vehicles symbolized speed and were used by Norman Bel Geddes to envision the future in his 1932 book, Horizons. Bel Geddes based his designs on the concept ofspeed, as he said in his book, "speed is the cry of our era, and greater speed one of the goals of tomorrow."1

BelGeddes did not invent the term "streamlining," although he popularized it in America. The term was originally used by English biologist/mathematician D'Arcy Wentworth Thompson (1860-1948) in his influential 1917 book, On Growth and Form. He used the term to describe organic structures that offer the least resistance when they are in motion, and how a fluid medium tends to impress its "stream lines" on a deformable body, like snowdrifts and sand dunes. He concluded that the same principle occurred in the evolution of the bodies of fish and birds. German Count Ferdinand von Zeppelin incorporated the same concept into the design of zeppelins, rigid airships named after him that he developed at the start of the 2 cnury  whi  aiz theary   an  Gemany to the United States.

Early aircraft and racing cars emulated similar streamlined forms. A pioneer was Austro-Hungarian Paul Jaray (1889-1974), an employee of the Zeppelin Company in Germany, who osv that utbile roll alongthe roun, but they omovroug theirHevelope cale-mode cars to test in he Zeppeln wintunel to determine the best shape toower air resistance. The ideal shape was a teardrop with rounded windscreen and enclosed bottom. In 1922, he had a full-scale prototype, the Ley T6, built by the German carmaker Rudolph Ley AG in Armstadt. Jaray was soon consulting with a number of European carmakers, and his principles were soon incorporated into racing cars by Maybach, Audi, andMercedes-Benz, as well as aircraft designs. In 1922, Romanian engineer Aurel Persu (18901977) also applied a a. I in 1927.

Detroit had picked up on the streamlining concept in 131, when Amos Northup, then the chief body designer of the Murray Corporation, first used streamlining principles, although modestly, in the design of the Reo Royale Eight, the first mass-produced car to display the streamlined esthetic by smoothing the traditional sharp angles of car bodies into soft curves. A rounded, peaked shell surrounded a vertical V-shaped radiator, and the headlights were mounted in elongated pods. Front fenders swept rearward almost to the middle of the car. A curved panel provided a tapered rear end and covered the fuel tank. Murray claimed the curved fenders increased strength and that the shape reduced wind resistance, although this was negligible under 60 mph.

The design was an immediate public sensation, proving that streamlining could sell cars and encouraging Murray and Northup to design a much bolder streamlined body for the 1932 Graham Blue Streak, which had the first chromed, tilted back, V-shaped grille concealing the functional radiator, the first fender skirts to hide the spatter zones" behind the wheels, and a trailing "beaver tail" that concealed the gas tank. This design made all previous automotive design obsolete and began a progression of automotive streamlining that continued wellinto the 1950s and beyond. By 134, there would be no U.S. car without front, and smeies rear, fender skirts.

A few early American industrial designers, besides Harley Earl of General Motors, were attracted to automotive design, which was, as it is today, a romantic and popular icon oflifestyle, speed, and freedom, for example, Walter Dorwin Teague and his son, Walter Dorwin Teague, Jr. (1910-2004, later called W. Dorwin Teague). Then a 19-year-old engineering student at MIT, the son had teamed up in 1929 with his father to design the Marmon 16 for the Marmon Motor Company, headed by Howard C. Marmon. Both father and son later claimed individual cit  e de.Th r ws iduce   n ws classi n esi , but it could not be described as streamlined. Four hundred would be produced before the Depression put the company out of business.

Raymond Loewy patented his first car design in 1930, and in 1932 designed a "cycle fender" Hupmobile for the Hupp Motor Car Company, which was in a fairly traditional style. He would design a more radical, more streamlined Hupmobile in 1934 called the Aerodynamic Hupmobile. Because executives were skeptical of his proposal, he decided the only way to get the job was to build a car by hand, using an existing chassis, to show them he was right. It took him several months, but when the executives saw the innovations in three dimensions, they agreed, and he got the job. The exercise cost Loewy over \$20,000, in 1932! (In 2013 dollars: \$341,000.) But he said it was worth it. He was to have been paid for the design as a percentage of all Aerodynamics sold over 10,000, but Hupp sold fewer than that, so he never received a dime. Later in his career, Loewy would become the most renowned non-Detroit auto designer. Fascinated with transportation in general, Loewy even ventured into ship design by designing the Princess Anne ferryboat in a streamlined style for the Pennsylvania Railroad Company in 1933. It was the year he took an office on the 54th floor of a skyscraper at 500 Fifth Avenue.

Bel Geddes, as we know, had entered the industrial design field in 1928 designing cars and was well aware of these streamlining developments in automotive design. He apparently concluded that transportation with streamlining was the wave of the future. His book, Horizons, not only astounded the design world with futuristic designs of transportation that bordered on science fiction, but jolted the transportation industry into action. The book was filled with advanced concept designs that were never built, but seemed realistic, detailed, and easily achievable in the foreseeable future, because they included not only dramatic artist renderings of the external appearance, but detailed cross sections, mechanical specifications, and plans for interiors.

![](images/20ccedc1e28c7e1741d7f238eb44b3ba602a3d16d9a13a7ea9b5f291e939fc60.jpg)  
1934 Aerodynamic Hupmobile by Loewy.

His Motor Car Number 5 sedan with a teardrop shape had a huge rear fin to stabilize it at high speed, which anticipated General Motors' car fins of the 1950s by several decades. His double-decker Motor Coach Number 2 had a similar teardrop shape and fin but seated 53 people and included a lavatory and buffet service. Bel Geddes's Locomotive Number 1 resembled and anticipated 1960s aluminum "bullet trains" with coaches and locomotive having nearcylindrical cross sections, as wel as a rear lounge observation car, also bullet shaped, with generous glass windows.

Horizon concepts included designs for airports, such as a floating rotary airport at the tip of Manhattan, and an incredible Air Liner Number 4, a swept-wing, giant, amphibian, tailless, flying wing. The wingspan was 528 feet, four times that of the 1958 Boeing 707, and on top of the wing were mounted 20 engines, producing a maximum speed of 150 mph. The wing itself, which varied in thickness from 10 feet at the tip to 22 feet at the center, provided adequate space for 451 passengers (with sleeping facilities) and a crew of 155.

Bel Geddes's Ocean Liner in Horizons was a 1,088-foot-long behemoth of 70,000 tons that looked more like a torpedo than a ship, with streamlined funnels and 10 decks. The stern deck was enclosed in glass, which opened in good weather, and the stern itself tapered to a point, completing the teardrop appearance. The liner included an aircraft in a hanger, a tennis court, a sand beach, swimming pool, gymnasium, cinema, and three restaurants, with 2,000 first-class passengers and a crew of 900.

Also in the book were concepts and plans for private homes, theaters, auditoriums, openair cabarets, and a 256-foot-diameter rotating aerial restaurant which seated 1,500 at the top of a 32-foot-diameter, 27-story-high central tower. Bel Geddes intended the design for the 19331934 exposition being planned in Chicago. Another was a design of a glass-walled factory and machine shop for the Toledo Scale Company, as well as product concepts for counter and island scales, but the latter were never produced.

The book also included a few more mundane commercial designs by Bel Geddes, including his Franklin Simon windows (1929), his car design for Graham-Paige (1928), his counter and island scales for Toledo Scale 1929), his steel beds for Simmons (19281929), his famed 1931

![](images/f2763015e3b423a0710719912930941c84509fe3988cdfdfca0c5d669482cf5b.jpg)  
C. Stowe Myers, ca. 1950s (courtesy Industrial Designers Society of America).

gas stove for the Standard Gas Equipment Corporation (SGE), and his radio for RCA (1931). But it was his incredible visions of H sremlining style that would earn him the ti he P.T. Barn of Industrial Design" by some. Ironically, the noncredited renderings of many of the spectacular designs in Bel Geddes's book were actually drawn by a young recent graduate in architecture from the University of Pennsylvania, C. Stowe Myers (1906- 1995). Myers was born in Altoona, Pennsylvania, and received his degree in architecture in 1930. He would go on to work for Walter Dorwin Teague in 1934.

But the future visions of Horizons were by Bel Geddes alone. Later in life, Raymond Loewy admitted that he and most other ps had ittwt e l biT just making a living by improving products with good appearance:

Looking back to the early days ... I can say that we proceeded with considerable naiveté. We didn't realize what the repercussions might be decades later .. that a new profession was beginning around us!²

But Bel Geddes did. He recognized full well that he and others

were ransormigthevisual landcape  America.On pge ohis book, he state, quit esciently,

In the perspective of fifty years hence [1982], the historian will detect in the decade of 1930-1940 a period of tremendous significance. He will see it as a period of criticism, unrest, and dissatisfaction to the point of disillusion—when new aims were being sought and new beginnings were astir. Doubtless he will ponder that in the midst of a world-wide melancholy owing to an economic depression, a new age dawned with invigorating conceptions and the horizon lifted.3

He was absolutely right. These were the men and women who pioneered in the new field of industrial design before it became well known by either the business community or by the public. Compared to many other artists practicing in the art industries, they were boldly entering the business and industrial world of mass-produced mechanical products, where artists normally feared to tread. They embraced the change that modern design offered to a nation mired in traditional, historical, and antiquated design habits. They committed their careers to advancing that change and sought to make America a leader in international design, rather than a follower.

![](images/0e6a9df73f8c6a47205337b07a5bd836c4dd0d67708226e43c30d500406576ae.jpg)  
Ocean liner by Bel Geddes (Patent No. D91,579, U.S. Patent Office).

By January 1933, when President Franklin D. Roosevelt initiated his administration, America became focused on several burning issues. The Depression was affecting not only the many individuals who had lost their jobs (25 percent) and/or investments (millions). Farm commodities were only at 40 percent of what they had been in 1929 as a severe drought ravaged the agricultural heartland. Seven billion dollars in deposits had been frozen in failed banks after the March Emergency Banking Act (called the "Bank Holiday"), which closed all banks for four days and allowed only Federal Reserve-approved banks to operate. The day after the Bank Holiday ended, the stock market rose from 53.84 to 62.10 points, a gain of 15.34 percent, the largest single-day percentage gain to this day. When you are at the bottom, there's nowhere to go but up.

Nevertheless, American exports had declined from \$5.2 billion in 1929 to \$1.7 billion in 1933. Manufacturing was struggling to survive in an environment where consumers had little means or incentive to buy products. The lowest point of industrial production in the Great Depression had been reached, which was at 50 percent of what it had been in 1929 before the crash, as were retail sales. Auto production was at a quarter of its pre—Depression levels. The Depression had never been darker, despite the sudden rise in the stock market.

A boost in national morale was needed, and several events in 1933 contributed toraising spirits. First, Roosevelt repealed Prohibition, the policy that had made public use of alcohol against the law since 1920, but which had done little to curb the illegal activities of rumrunning gangs or speakeasies that provided liquor to continued public demand. The second was the opening of the Century of Progress International Exposition, a World's Fair, held in Cigraey'Thea bee  siJ 1928 and had been slowed by the start of the Depression in October 1929. Now, on May 27, 1933, the fair opened on 427 acres along the Lake Michigan shoreline between 12th and 39th Streets in Near South Side, Chicago.

The Chicago Century of Progress World's Fair opening in May 1933 was a showcase of American technology, transportation, architecture, and modern design. The Fair's motto, "Science Finds, Industry Applies, Man Conforms," emphasized the belief that "man" needed to, as we say today, "get with the program." The program was the future. Buildings for the exposition were multicolored, creating a "Rainbow City" as opposed to the "White City" of the 1893 Chicago Fair (the World's Columbian Exposition) that introduced electric lighting. The 1933 fair opened when light from the star Arcturus was detected by photoelectric cells in a seriastronomical observatories and was transformed intoelectrical energy that was transmitted to Chicago. The star was chosen because its light had started its journey to earth 40 years before, at the same time as the previous Chicago Fair of 1893.

Unlike the 1893 fair, which featured neoclassical architecture and themes, this one genolwrn  yR bridge perpendicular to the shore of the lake on which one could ride from one side of the fair to the other in "rocket cars," or ride elevators up the two 628-foot towers for a spectacular view. The midway was flled with nightclubs and the re-creation of important scenes from Chicago history. Future stars such as Judy Garland and the Andrews Sisters performd at the cubs along with nancer Sally Rand The frst Major LeagueBsbalAllStar Game was held at Comiskey Park in conjunction with the fair, and yes, Babe Ruth hit the first All-Star home run.

AHx ern conveniences and practical new building materials. A 12-sided, glass-and-steel-framed Hou T  cn partitions, air conditioning, fixed window panes, and, incredibly, an airplane hangar. The Briggs Company exhibited a Kitchen of Tomorrow designed by John Tjaarda, which included

Entry to the exposition was through "Passimeters," modern turnstiles designed for the Perey Company by John Vassos, who, since his entry into the design field in 24,had written, illustrated, and publishe a number of fiction books:Contempo in 9, Ultima in 1930, and .Nowi3, he etablihe n teal ndustrial des eparment  e Radio Corporation of America (RCA) and would remain as RCA design consultant until 1,whil continuing to practice independently. In 34,he would design RCAs first plastic tabletop radio cabinet, the International, replacing what Vassos called the "wooden tombstone type radios of the 1920s that preceded it, and he also would design the first living room console radios without furniture type "legs."

What Vassos clled "wooden tombstones" were Philco'tabletop radios shaped in  Gothic arch, not unlike a tombstone. In 1932, there were a lot of them, including Models 36B, 71B, and 91B, all designed by Clyde F. Schuler (18921969), including a later Model 66MB.

Shuler was born in Pottstown, Pennsylvania, and educated at the Philadelphia Museum and School of Industrial Art. He earned a certificate in industrial drawing in 19ll, a second for interior decoration in 1913, and in 1921, a diploma in interior decoration. He received his architectural registration for the state of Pennsylvania in January 1930 and opened an office with a partner, Schuler & Gurney, practicing domestic architecture and product work, such as for Philco. But by 1933 he had joined the faculty at the Philadelphia College of Art, where he would remain until 1952.

Major manufacturers mounted numerous pavilions or exhibits at the Chicago Exposition, many of which provided opportunities for designers. Alfonso Iannelli, the Chicago sculptor/ industrial designer mentioned earlier, designed pavilions, rides, entrance gates, and ornaments for the fair, including the Havoline Thermometer building. Norman Bel Geddes had been appointed "designer of illumination" for all buildings and grounds in 1929, and he also de-

signed the Aquarium restaurant, the Seats-Surrounded by Stage Theater, the Water Pageant Theater, the Island Dance restaurant, the Arial restaurant, the Divine Comedy Theater, the Open Air Cabaret, and the Temple of Music.4

Walter Dorwin Teague designed the Ford Rotunda, which featured the "Briggs Dream Car," a rear-engine prototype aimed at Ford's need for a "small Lincoln," designed by John Tjaarda (1897 1962), who had been hired as chief ofbody design in 1932 by the Briggs Manufacturing Company, Detroit's largest body supplier, which supplied body designs to Ford.

Tjaarda was born in Arnheim,

![](images/825ef5f928354079116539bc348b85350cceed1a3764eb1f42b6bc7a1498760b.jpg)  
Briggs "Dream Car," 1933, by John Tjaarda (U.S. Patent No. D94,396, U.S. Patent Office).

the Netherlands, of a titled family in the Sterkenburg area, as Joop "Jan" Tjaarda van Sterkenburg. He trained in aeronautical design at the Alexander Klemins School of Aeronautics in London and served as a Dutch Air Force pilot before immigrating to America in 1923 and changing his name to John Tjaarda. He initially worked as a design assistant with Locke & Company in Rochester, New York, a manufacturer of bodies for Stutz, Pierce Arrow, and Packard. In 1926, fascinated with aerodynamics, he developed streamlined automotive designs in clay models he called the Sterkenburg Series, after his family's home in the Netherlands. These would become the basis of his Dream Car for Briggs. He had worked for Harley Earl's Art and Colour Section at General Motors in 1927, for Duesenberg from 1928 to 1930, and had returned to Harley Earl from 1930 to 1932.

The Briggs cr although muc sleeker more stemline, andmore refine, bore  striking resemblance to the 1932 prototype of the Type 32 Kleinauto, designed by Dr. Ferdinand Porsche (1875-1951) in Germany, which would later in the decade become the famed Volkswagen (People's Car) subsidized by Hitler. Other auto manufacturers displayed concept cars at the Chicago Exposition, such as Cadillac's V-16 limousine and Pierce-Arrow's Silver Arrow, for which it used the byline, "Suddenly it's 1940!" But Packard won the best of show.

Another unusual car design appeared and was demonstrated at the exposition by R. Buckminster Fuller (1895-1983). It was a prototype of his teardrop-shaped Dymaxion Transport Unit, which looked a bit like an aircraft without wings. It had a rear engine that drove two front wheels, with a third wheel in the rear that was controlled by the driver with the steering wheel to change directions. Three such prototypes were built, but a fatal accident with one in 1 would doo the projet.Fuller was ne  the most intelletual nventor/desers.

![](images/8bd7326dc0aed62dd1e01e627917f2da2e23b7231cd8d45f843bd7c8c935caf0.jpg)  
Dymaxion Transport Unit, 1933, by Fuller.

Born in Milton, Mashuses, hattended Milton Academy and studmathematic a Harro   .H vn U.. avu Wrl Wr I for the Armour Company of the meat-packing industry in Chicago. By 1927, at age 32, he was bankrupt, jobless, and depressed. But he resolved to think independently to find out what a single individual could contribute to a changing world to benefit all of humanity.

![](images/2127c0ab63b950fd2e51504634fde74c72fcfdce0f3210a4de1da14e0e1c0ef1.jpg)

By 1928 he was living in Greenwich Village in New York, decorating Romany Marie's popular café in exchange for meals, and displaying concept designs for his mass-produced house and bathroom, which he called Dymaxion House and Dymaxion Bathroom, respectively. He coined the term Dymaxion from the wors ynami, "maxium," and"tenson. At the cafe, he me Japanese-American artist and landscape architect Isamu Noguchi (1904-1988), and they teamed up to model the Dymaxion car based on the recent work of Aurel Persu, the Romanian aeronautical engineer mentioned earlier.

R. Buckminster Fuller, ca. 1960 (courtesy Industrial Designers Society of America).

Major electrical equipment manufacturers were also represented at the exposition. Donald Dohner, then the Westinghouse director o art, painted huge murals for the company's pavilon. One of the exhibit designers for the General Electric (GE) pavilion was Raymond E. Patten (1897-1948), who had joined the Edison GE Company in Chicago in 1928, and who supervised appearance design. In 1931 he had designed an Art Deco, tilt-type, Hotpoint Gazelle toaster for the company, as well as waffle irons, clocks, and kitchen ranges. In 1933, GE established an internal design group at its headquarters in Bridgeport, Connecticut, and named Patten as its head. Patten was born in Malden, Massachusetts; attended Harvard; and studied engineering at MIT. His first position was with the Hume Body Corporation in Boston. He later designed custom auto bodies for the Dayton Wright Company and spent five years at the Packard Motor Car Company before joining Edison in Chicago.

![](images/9bd3a08373a263f7a4a9bc393c345856d82c18a9f893df26fbd9ae9af54525d1.jpg)

Under Patten's leadership in 1933, GE engaged Henry Dreyfuss to design a new flat-top refrigerator under the Hotpoint brand name to replace its clumsy 1927 monitor-top model, designed by engineers with an exposed cylindrical compressor on top, the name suggestive of the Civil War ironclad "cheesebox on a raft" warship, the Monitor. Dreyfuss enclosed the compressor and located it beneath the cooling compartment, rather than on top, creating the clean rectangular vertical box we are accustomed to seeing in

Hotpoint "flat-top" refrigerator, 1933 (courtesy Museum of Innovation and Science, Schenectady, New York).

refrigerators today. Dreyfuss also designed a modified version of the monitor top. Dreyfuss that same year had completely redesigned McCall's magazine and designed a Toperator washing machine for Sears, Roebuck & Company, with a replica of his signature on each of the 20,000 units sold within the first six months. Already, the names of prominent industrial designers were being used as sales features. It was a time when manufacturers were willing to do anything to increase sales.

Patten's earliest employees included Helen Hughes Delany, who restyled the GE electric stove line; George A. Beck, mentioned earlier, who had worked at Montgomery Ward and Sears, and joined GE in 1934; and Gordon Florian (1909-1984), who studied at Yale University of Fine Art in New Haven, Connecticut; at the Grand Central School of Art in New York; and had joined GE in 1931 as a market analyst. By 1934, the Hotpoint and GE brand names would be integrated, and in 1935, Raymond C. Sandin (1910-ca. 1986) would become a one-man industrial design department of the Hotpoint division of GE, and would remain as head of the department until his death. Sandin was born in Sweden, came to the United

![](images/8355396330408e5c73e14a8b88f933ed6aef82ffd20848fb4aaf19ae1229c336.jpg)

David Chapman, ca. 1940s-1950s (David Painter papers, Ryerson and Burnham Archives, Art Institute of Chicago. Courtesy Art Institute of Chicago).

States in 1930, and studiearchitecture at he Illinois Instiute of Technology before joining GE.

Other exhibit designers on the design staff at the fair would become prominent in the industrial design field. One was David Chapman (19091978), a 1932 architectural graduate of the Armour Institute of Technology (which later became the Illinois Institute of Technology) in Chicago, who, at the same time he was working on fair exhibits, joined Montgomery Ward as an architect. Ann Swainson, head of Ward's Bureau of Design, appointed him head of product design. Chapman was born in Gilman, Illinois.

Another exhibit designer at the fair was Jean Otis Reinecke (19091987). Reinecke was born in Bourbon County, Kansas, and started his own sign-painting company at age 17 in 1926. He attended Kansas State Teachers College, then moved to St. Louis and attended Washington University's Art School. He began his career as a display artist at General Display Studios in St. Louis, and a designer of automated convention and World's Fair displays, where he soon became a partner and art director. He opened his own Chicago general display office in 1930 specifically to work on the fair. In 1934, he would establish a Chicago industrial design firm, Barnes & Reinecke, with partner James Barnes, with Reinecke providing the design talent and Barnes in charge of sales.

In the closing days of the Chicago Fair, on October 26, 1933, one of the final highlights was the arrival of the76-foot German airship, the GrafZeppelin, which circled Lake Micgan for two hours near the exposition site before Commander Hugo Eckener landed at the nearby Curtiss-Wright Airport in Glenview at 1:00 p.m. It remained on the ground for 25 minutes and then took off ahead of an approaching weather front, bound for Akron, Ohio. For some folks, it was ot a welcome ht,since, with a hugeswastka on tside, it was a gri remi of the rise of Adolph Hitler to power in Germany earlier that year. In fact, German theoretical physicist Albert Einstein had just arrived in the United States nine days before, on October 17, a refugee from Nazi Germany. There would soon be many more. Nevertheless, the fair, which closed on November 12, was so successful that it was decided to reopen it again to run from May to October the following year, 1934. Over the previous 170 days, it had already attracted well over 22 million paid admissions.

Before the end of 1933, Robert Davol Budlong (1902- 1955) had joined the growing ranks of industrial designers and started his career with the Hammond Clock Company. He would begin a relationship with Zenith in 1934 as a radio designer and consultant that would last until his death. Budlong was born in Denver, Colorado, studied art at the Cummings School of Art in Des Moines, Iowa, and graduated from Grinnell College, Iowa, in 1921. He then studied art at the Chicago Academy of Fine Art and opened an advertising office in Chicago. Around the same time, there were several start-up firms in New York: Cushing & Nevell in 1933, and Jim Nash Associates in 1934, both of which would survive into the 1960s.

![](images/93ec3591a57929597e2cbcdffdd38222e845fe790b04a8fc3a5bf61616e21c7a.jpg)

In 1933, Norman Bel Geddes exhibited his work in Detroit, Michigan; Milan, Italy; and the Louvre in Paris. He also designed a medallion to commemorate the silver anniversary of the General

Robert D. Budlong, ca. 1950s (courtesy Merrily and Erik Holladay).

Motors Corporation that year. Aside from his normal business routine, the rumblings of potential war in Europe inspired him to renew his interest in realistic games, and he developed what he called the War Game, a scale model simulation of a 20,000-square-kilometer battlefield on a 24 x 4 foot table, representing the coastline of two imaginary countries, complete with 9,000 cities and towns, mountains, valleys, tunnels, and bridges. It was much larger and more complex than his Nutshell Jockey Club of the 1920s.

Thousands of pins of various colors, sizes, and shapes stood in for 40-plus types of military units, including armies and navies of 28 belligerents. A complicated rulebook determined chesslike moves by a general staff regarding speed of movement and degree of destruction by attacks. The game ran once a week during winter months, with an evening of play commensurate with three and a half days of war. Players, consisting of brokers, architects, shipping magnates, and rare book dealers, took the game seriously, often sacrificing vacations, business, or family commitments. By 1935, Bel Geddes would put the game into storage because of time constraints, but his skills of miniaturization and three-dimensional landscaping would be valuable in a number of his future professional assignments.

But the real war against the Great Depression was still raging. Industry was still stalled, and most companies were still unaware that one of the reasons was the obsolete design of their products. By and large, they were also unaware of what to do about it. This was about to change dramatically.

# Industrial Design Unleashed

By 1933, eight years after the Paris Art Deco Exposition of 1925, which had sounded a wake-up call, warning that America was far behind Europe in modern design and therefore in international economic competition, there were perhaps three dozen industrial designers who were working in the so-called artless industries, a drop in the bucket compared to the 50,000 needed in the post—World War I consumer markets, as recommended in 19i8 by Walter Sargent (1868-1927), then director of the Department of Fine and Industrial Art at the University of Chicago. Of course, World War I had dramatically suppressed the European advance in industrialization, and the Depression had stopped America's economy in its tracks.

At the bottom of the Depression in 1933, industry was stalled. Many manufacturers still resisted investment in new tooling for new designs because sales had declined dramatically, and they were in the red, struggling to stay in business. Many were bewildered by a situation foreign to their experience—a situation in which the functional but homely merits of their products were not enough to sell them. Those who sought qualified designers to design new products found that the few industrial designers that were available were too busy to take on new clients. But the vast majority of manufacturers, businessmen, and engineers were simply unaware of modern design or industrial design. They were not among the elite sophisticates who frequented art museums and international art expositions, or who read art magazines. They were practical people, concerned about the nuts and bolts of manufacturing, not art. Many of them were about to learn about industrial design and its leading practitioners for the first time, through an influential article that reached the entire business world.

The success of the Chicago Fair in 1933 was the first glimmer ofhope for the future experienced by the public in three years. Now industry needed a morale boost, and it came in February 1934 when Fortune, the premier magazine of business, published a nine-page feature article about industrial design, which would have a profound effect on the new field, as well as on manufacturing and the economy itself. In fact, it was the first time the term "industrial desig" was usein the popular press, rather than thetradiional, more common termndu-

Thearice wasited "BothFish and Fowl,"intende tgest that industrial deses w bo r uesme nam cudi uen, nk  i iv  p e Te ey businessmen. A cartoon accompanied the article on the title page, which showed a company president addressing his board of directors with the statement,GentlemenI am convince that our next new biscuit must be styled by Norman Bel Geddes."

## 5. Industrial Design Unleashed

The Fortune article was written anonymously, but is now known to have been authored by architect George Nelson (19081986), 26 at the time, and the winner of the 1932 Rome Prize in architecture. Nelson was then the assistant editor of Architectural Forum magazine, and would within a decade become a nationally known industrial designer. Nelson was born in Hartford, Connecticut, and graduated from Hartford Public High School in 1924, and from Yale in 1928 with a degree in architecture. In 1929 he was hired as a teaching assistant while obtaining his second bachelor's degree in fine arts at Yale in 1931. In Rome, he traveled throughout Europe and met many modernist pioneers, whom he interviewed for articles in Pencil Points magazine. Through these articles, he described the work of Walter Gropius, Mies van der Rohe, Le Corbusier, and Gio Ponti. In 1933 He returned to America to devote himself to writing. This F r w obabyh al magazine.

![](images/5b3ac4e1826b292c6ddf95881448dcf6162165eb744448c1309bfe18c962f9db.jpg)  
George Nelson, ca. 1950s (courtesy Industrial Designers Society of America).

Nelson began the article with a quotation from Oliver M.W. Sprague (1873-1953), who hadjust i Decb 133 regneas pel assitant to the ceary the resur i Washington in order to return to Harvard Business School and resume his teaching there after a leave of absence of four years with the government. Now he was free to express his opinions pulcyt aiih S ae meening Depression squarely on manufacturers:

Failure of industries to adapt policies designed to open up additional demands for industrial products is, in my judgment, the chief cause of the persistence of the Depression.

In other words, manufacturers had failed to attract new consumers through the design of new and different products, precisely the service that industrial design offered. Nelson stated the essential case for industrial design:

Whenever two products are equal in point of utility and price, the one that looks more attractive to the purchaser will be bought first. When the demand is sufficient to take only one of two products, the maker of the attractive product stays in business, the maker of the other goes bankrupt.²

After this do-or-die challenge to his business audience, Nelson briefly provided historical context by describing how the art industries traditionally and effectively used design, but that the engineer-run artless industries of the 20th century were unprepared and unstaffed to do so when style became important to their largely functional products. He then described in detail how industrial designers worked, and what benefits they could bring to business. The main thrust of the article focused on "a few leading industrial designers" and what they had done in the last few years by citing their previous experience, their clients, their fees, their typical achievements, and photo-portraits of ten of them. Those included were many who have already been described in this book. Quotes that follow are from the Fortune article.

Norman Bel Geddes, now a tousled, curly-haired 41-year-old, was described by Nelson as a "bomb-thrower" and the "P.T. Barnum of industrial design," but three pages are devoted to his background in stage design, his business practice, and his book, Horizons, including an entire sidebar page, "Story of a Stove," that was devoted to his stunningly modern and simple, but practical, stove concepts for the Standard Gas Equipment Corporation (SGE). The president of SGE, Frank Roberts, had walked into Bel Geddes's office and requested a new stove design within two weeks and offered to pay \$1,500. Bel Geddes explained that he did not just draw pretty pictures, that study to produce a better stove would take a year, and required \$50,000 as an advance of royalties. Shocked, Roberts conferred with his directors, but he decided to take the risk.

Bel Geddes put an engineer in the SGE plant, studied competitors' methods, and interviewed hundreds of housewives and store dealers. His report and recommendations numbered 300 pages. Photos in the article showed how Bel Geddes reduced the number of stove components currently stocked by SGE from hundreds to just 16 through standardization, which he demonstrated like a showman with colored block models. When the stove appeared on the market in January 1933, the first one all white, Robert's decision was vindicated: "Housewives wanted the Geddes stove. Utilities that had never heard about the line before welcomed it. Sales rose, doubled, held their gains."

In the same year as this article, Bel Geddes designed an Electrolux air-cooled gas refrigerator, two years before Loewy's Coldspot for Sears, as well as an Electrolux vacuum cleaner; a refrigerator for Servel, Inc.; a prefabricated service station for Socony-Vacuum Oil Company; metal and plastic beds for the Rome Manufacturing Company; cigarette cases, boxes, cocktail shakers, candlesticks, and ashtrays for the Revere Copper and Brass Company; and a Plymouth car design for the Chrysler Company. He also wrote an article entitled "Streamlining" for Atlantic Monthly magazine. Allthe while, he continued to design and produce theater sets and to revise theater architecture.3

In the article, Nelson praised designers, not merely for attractive and modern styles, but also for their contributions to manufacturing cost reduction and user convenience. Donald Dohner at Westinghouse found a way to save \$40,000 on tooling costs by making a few changes to his model. He changed a metal vacuum cleaner to plastic, making it lighter, quieter, and more powerful, putting Westinghouse into the vacuum cleaner business. Finding that fat men operated most locomotives, he widened passages and spaced levers and wheels for fat hands. Dohner is shown as a serious-looking, slim, dark-haired 41-year-old with a "Hitler"- style mustache.

John Vassos, shown as a dark-haired, handsome 41-year-old, noted for his radios for RCA, his turnstiles for the Perey Manufacturing Company, and a Coca-Cola drink dispenser, also invented a hook for a wall telephone, which the company found patentable. Henry Dreyfuss, the younget a ge 30, a milg you man wih glasse, plac all the cnrols  e, on the top, in his design of the Toperator washing machine for Sears.

The article featured detailed biographies and operating styles of key designers. Lurelle Guila H u, and makes an average of four finished drawings for each product." Guild claimed that he "has never desgne a product that was a coercial failure, has never lost aclient. Desis by Guild cited in thearticle include rerigerator for the Norge Corpration, cooking utensls for the Wear-Ever Aluminum Company, and designs for Montgomery Ward for everything from stoves to roller skates. His coffeepot for Wear-Ever had a lower production cost but ic le by0 ent. His l aier ee was ,000,his daily ew \$100 \$200, or alternatively, he offered flat fees of \$300 to \$25,000, plus royaltis.

Walter Dorwin Teague, now 51 and shown with a mustache and wearing a dapper fedora, was described as "suave, able, and successful" and "is the generally acknowledged doyen of the profession" with an "unbroken record of commercial successes." His designs cited included cameras for Eastman Kodak, with whom he had a six-year retainer. In 1934 his famous Art Deco Kodak Baby Brownie camera was introduced, priced at only \$1, and four million of them would be sold. He also had designed a furnace for National Radiator; a line of Taylor Instruments; a line of Pyrex ovenware; a mimeograph machine for the A.B. Dick Company; a dramatic Art Deco Bluebird radio with a blue, circular mirror face for Sparton; and an oil heater for American Gas Machine that increased sales by 400 percent. His retainer fees were \$12,000 to \$24,000, and his flat fees were \$500 to \$5,000.

Donald Dohner, whose department at Westinghouse reportedly cost \$75,000 per year, was describeas mong he naos  East Pitturgh," ndhis desi or a watercol vious models seven to one," a 700 percent increase, with lower production costs. His dieselev   y flor. u' oe Dn'lci e  s  ea Railroad's famous GG-1 locomotive late in 1934. More on this later.)

Gustav Jensen, shown as a blondish, serious-looking 36-year-old,"generally regarded as the top man from the purely artistic point oview," was recognized for his design ohis Monel metal sink for International Nickel Company, a telephone for AT&T, and a water heater for the L.O. Koven & Brothers Company. His retainer fees were cited as \$500 to \$20,000.

A brief biography of George Sakier, shown as a scowling, dark-haired 37-year-old, ebelye ul o and mentioned his work for American Radiator and Standard Sanitary Corporation, designing bathtubs, washbasins, vacuum equipment, and bathroom units stamped from metal, which "are the first prefabricated house units actually in production. The entire bathroom with the three major fixtures and the walls and floor can be assembled as a single unit, or the parts can be used separately. One hundred and thirty-three of them are now being put into a Washington, D.C. apartment house." His income was estimated at \$15,000 to \$25,000, plus retainers, from his design work. Also mentioned was his glassware work for Fostoria, with the result that "American open-stock glass for the first time took precedence over European, and finally he won the distinction of having his own designs for glass pirated in Europe."

Raymond Loewy, pictured as a dark-haired, mustached 41-year-old, was described as "the least publicized and one of the largest earners," with retainer fees from \$10,000 to \$60,000, plus royalties. His work cited included a globe-shaped radio for Colonial which registered a sales increase of 700 percent; his Hupp automotive designs; kitchen sink and bathroom units for Sears, Roebuck; and his duplicator (copy machine) for Sigmund Gestetner. Loewy was referred to as "he who discovered that all Americans like the color green and the smell of lilac."

Henry Dreyfuss was recognized for having already been written about in the New Yorker, American, and Saturday Evening Post magazines for his work in stage design, and in fact he was still designing one show on Broadway every season. His product designs cited included the Toperator wash machine for Sears, Roebuck; his Protectograph check protector for the Todd Company (introduced in 1935); and alarm clocks for Western Clock Company, but he was also designing interiors for Cities Service corporate aircraft and an insulated thermos jug made by the American Company while working on a "telephone of the future" for Bell Laboratories. His flat fees were \$1,000 to \$25,000, or an hourly rate of \$50.

John Vassos was mentioned as a well-known writer of psychoanalysis, but also for his designs of a Coca-Cola dispenser; his turnstiles for Perey Manufacturing Company, which increased sales by 25 percent; and his radios for RCA. He generally charged \$12,000 for retainer fees, or flat fees from \$1,000 to \$7,000. Harold Van Doren was described as "handsome," and his design cited was his scale for Toledo Scale, which permitted lower manufacturing costs and multiplied sales by 900 percent. His consultation fees were \$100 per day, or flat fees from \$500 to \$5,000. Joseph Platt's Vacuumatic fountain pen for Parker increased sales by 100 percent, with lower factory costs.

Jo Sinel's Accoustican hearing aid for Dictograph (1932) was cited for having resulted in a reduced price, with a net profit increase of 50 percent. Sinel had also just designed a typewriter for Remington, a Juicy Fruit vending machine, two lanterns for Dietz, and a Toastmaster Jr., none of which were mentioned in the article. In fact, in another 1934 issue, Fortune gave a fullpage color spread to Sinel's packaging and trademark designs, for which he was also well known. Donald Deskey and Russel Wright were both mentioned as newcomers to the field. Deskey had just been retained by the Libby Glass Company in Toledo, Ohio, and was not only designing a champagne glass for them, but advising them on their entire line of products. Wright had recently designed elegant sterling silver flatware that the Museum of Modern Art would much later honor by commissioning the production of replicas.

## Nelson concluded the article,

Most manufacturers agree that the industrial designer has stimulated all those he has met in the company's organization, given them new ideas, new energy, a new atitude. Designers will not cure our industrial ills, for our ills lie deeper than design. But the success of industrial designers is symptomatic of a changing attitude on the part of the manufacturers, a growing recognition of the old American ideal of progress: cheaper and better goods for all.4

Nelson would establish a New York architectural design office in 1936 with William Hamby, which would continue until after the war, and Nelson would become a prolific and dynamic leader in postwar furniture design and establish his own office in New York in 1947.

The article had an immediate impact, not only on the small community of industrial designers, who benefited enormously from the national publicity and recognition, but on the large segment of the business community that had little or no previous knowledge of industrial design and how it could increase sales. Suddenly they made the connection between their primary problem of diminishing sales and the obvious solution of new designs to generate new demand. The traditional manufacturing image of industrial art as simply the addition of decorative surface embellishment, had been transformed into industrial design, the reshaping and modernizing of the entire product.

In a sense, this was the reestablishment of the artist as a multidisciplinary creative individual in the modern world, as emphasized in Nelson's title, "Both Fish and Fowl," not unlike t  o and production of artifacts, as well as the appearance. Industrial designers not only became the 20th-century craftsmen, controlling the form of mass-producd products, but could command eyy cati.Precaby, emstl increased overnight.

The attraction for designers is not difficult to understand. In 194, the average annual salary  a i as 5,00,an evbo' py was 50 ct One could buy a three-bedroom house from Montgomery Ward for \$1,000, a men's dress shirt for \$1, and an icebox refrigerator for \$11.95. These fees were enough to attract some of the most talented people in other fields. Some H the more lucrative world of independent industrial design consultants. Among these were

Carl W. Sundberg (1910-1982) and Montgomery Ferar (1910-1982). Sundberg was born in Calumet, Michigan, graduated from Northern High School, and attended Wicker Art School in Detroit. He began his career in 1927 designing custom car bodies under Ray Dietrich at

![](images/7b0a252718492d94dc039cd2ca803cda5d451695ce218b7bd4139617ab5173e9.jpg)  
Carl W. Sundberg, ca. 1930s (courtesy Sundberg-Ferar, www.sundbergferar.com).

![](images/8e5f8c04ed3a82cdb374a476e9c260f0bd2f134f4b58cf5c2692f69f6a974f5c.jpg)  
Montgomery Ferar, ca. 1930s (courtesy Sundberg-Ferar, www.sundbergferar.com).

Dietrich Body Company, and then worked in plastics design at Kurz-Kasch, Inc. He then joined Earl's Art and Colour Section at GM.

Ferar was born in Boston and received a bachelor's degree in architecture at MIT in 1932, and took a year of study in Europe with a fellowship in 1933. He worked for several architectural firms in Boston before moving to Detroit to work for the Detroit Planning Commission, and he joined GM's Art and Colour Section, where he met Sundberg. In 1934 they both left GM to form the industrial design firm of Sundberg-Ferar, Inc., in Detroit, beginning a lifelong partnership.

Another automotive designer at GM, John Richard "Jack" Mor-

gan (19031986), left GM to become chief product designer at Sears, Roebuck's first design department in Chicago in 1934, which was formed as part of Department 817, the Merchandise Testing and Development Laboratory. Morgan was born Juan Ricardo Morgan in Guatemala City, Guatemala, and moved with his family in 1913 to Canada. At age 11 he won first prize for a watercolor at the Canadian National Exhibition. He became a student of renowned landscape painter Thomas John "Tom" Thompson (1877-1917). As a painter, Morgan traveled abroad to Brussels, Berlin, Copenhagen, Oslo, Stockholm, Paris and London, and eventually became an accomplished artist with a studio in Wheeling, Illinois. He had two one-man shows in Chicago and exhibited in Palm Beach, Connecticut, and Pennsylvania.

But Morgan's love of automobiles inspired him to attend the Detroit Technical Institute, and in 1927 he joined the C.G. Spring and Bumper Company. In 1928, he was hired by Harley Earl's infant Art and Colour Section at GM, which by then numbered 50. Working in the Pontiac Studio under Frank Hershey (1907-1997), Morgan designed the first auto bumper to be integrated into the body. At Sears in 1934, Morgan designed the Sears Waterwitch outboard motor and would head Sears Department 817 until 1943, designing vacuum cleaners, kitchen cabinet consoles, and much of Sears' appliance merchandise, including the first "turtle neck" washing-machine tub. One of Morgan's key early designers was Clare Hodgman (191l1992), born in Buchanon, Michigan, who had started his career in General Motors' Oldsmobile studio with Harley Earl in the early 1930s and had been previously working for Loewy.

Another designer, Brooks Stevens (19111995), like John Morgan, was also working on outboard motors, needed for a popular new outdoor recreational activity, power boating. His current client was Evinrude, and he had just opened his own office in Milwaukee in 1934. Twenty-three-year-old Clifford Brooks Stevens was born in Milwaukee and studied architecture at Cornell University from 1929 to 1933. He won a contest for designing a new trademark for his father's firm, Cutler-Hammer, and would now continue to design equipment and displays for the company as a consultant designer. His first product design for another firm was a glass peanut butter jar for Holsum Products.

![](images/4d6a633442c76e3f34fcd793073ffd86c08ad9829724f913c65fa1b0a39837b8.jpg)  
Brooks Stevens, ca. 1936 (Brooks Stevens Archive, Milwaukee Art Museum, Gift of the Brooks Stevens Family and the Milwaukee Institute of Art and Design).

The February 1934 Fortune article spurred industrial designers to toot their own horn. They organized an Art and Industry Exhibition at Rockefeller Center, sponsored by the National Alliance for Art and Industry, that featured 1,000 products by 100 designers, to illustrate "what designers are doing .. to help the engineer sell his mechanical devices." The exhibit included Raymond Loewy's iconic chrome-plated, teardrop, highly streamlined pencil sharpener (which was never produced); Henry Dreyfuss's check printer, a bathroom scale by Harold Van Doren; furniture by Gilbert Rohde; and a Wurlitzer radio by Russel Wright. By this time, the popularity of Art Deco and French Art Moderne was fading, and streamlining was in the ascendancy. Egmont Arens reported "an amazing response and welling up of national enthusiasm for 'streamlining,' which had captured the American imagination to mean modern, efficient, well-organized, sweet, clean, and beautiful."

In a more abstract manner, the Museum of Modern Art (MoMA) mounted a Machine Art Exhibition curated by architect Philip Johnson (1906-2005) from March 6 to April 30, 1934, celebrating the "abstract beauty" of shapes made by machines, and a poster on April 30, 1934, celebrating the abstract beauty of shapes made by machines. The exhibition catalog began with an ancient Greek quotation by Plato:

By beauty of shapes I do not mean, as most people would suppose, the beauty of living figures or of pictures, but to make my point clear, I mean straight lines and circles, and shapes, plane or solid, made from them by lathe, ruler, and square. These are not, like other things, beautiful relatively, but always and absolutely.

In the exhibit catalog, MoMA director Alfred H. Barr, J. (19021981), followed up on tical applications of geomety." The catalog continued,

The exhibition contains machines, machine parts, scientific instruments, and objects useful in ordinary life. There are no purely ornamental objects; the useful objects were, however, chosen for their aesthetic quality. Some will claim that usefulness is more important than beauty, or that usefulness makes an object beautiful. This exhibition has been assembled from the point of view that though usefulness is an essential, appearance has at least as great a value. The objects displayed were divided according to use in six categories:

Inustrial units: Machines and machine parts, insulators, cable sections, propeller blades [airc and ship ec. prg, bal bergisatrs, car pss, an  Sear Watewi - board motor designed by John R. Morgan].

Huol:  r backing for a washbasin, by George Sakier], dishwasher, carpet sweeper, mirrors, fans, vacuums, and business machines [including a National cash register designed by Walter Dorwin Teague].

Kiew: Toaser, pots n pans coffee rs, bowls, dles, and theie
