The listings of 400 items included the name of the item, the manufacturer, the designer, walls throughout the exhibit, in clear black lettering, were the names of U.S. companies: Aluminum Company of America, U.S. Steel Corporation, American Radiator Company, Bingham Stamping and Tool, American Sheet and Tin Plate Company, etc. In this advertising-like environment, visitors recognized nearly all of the items and could handle and test them. The New Yorker wrote, "The place itself looks, more than anything else, lk a very elaborate hardware e. It w  h balls, and plumbing fixtures, demonstrating that in America, art and everyday objects fused into a single experience. Over 31,000 people visited the exhibit.8

MoMA's purpose, of course, was to establish machine-made products as a legitimate form of modern art, in the same way it had promoted that status for architecture in its 1932 exhibition, and to obsolete the Arts and Crafts movement, as Philip Johnson said, "to demonstrate . that the decorative arts were no longer in existence, but that art could still be made without the handcraft approach—so it was an anti-handcraft show." In fact, the Arts and Crafts movement had already expired. The famous Wiener Werkstätte (Viennese Workshops), founded in 1903, had just been closed, not by the Nazis, but because the high prices for its products had caused it to fail in its social cause of ensuring that the life of everyone was enhanced by everyday objects designed by artists. Now industrial designers were actually implementing that idealistic objective in America.

MoMA had another less obvious agenda, and that was to co-opt industrial design, this new art, under the leadership wing of architects, as had been the case in Europe. MoMA hated streamlining, which was harshly criticized by MoMA director Alfred Barr: "Their designs are aimed at sales appeal of the most superficial kind."1 MoMA resented that advertising artists, set designers, writers, automotive and furniture designers were dominating industrial design and being heralded in the press and business world. MoMA wanted to take over the new profession for architects. This goal remained for the rest of the decade, when MoMA would launch a full-scale attack to dominate industrial design, which would succeed only in modifying the general style from streamlining to "organic" design. But the term "streamlining" still lives in our language, to describe the simplification and speeding up of things, as in phrases such as we streamlined our office procedures."

The MoMA Machine Art Exhibition also established MoMA's collection protocol for the future, as MoMA acquired 100 of the manufactured objects in the exhibition into its permanent collection, a practice it would continue for decades, just as traditional museums acquired collections of paintings or artifacts. The exhibit boosted the sale of American-made goods, strengthened the growing alliance between artists and manufacturers, and broke down the wall between art and commercial products, just as Duchamp had done in 1917 with his urinal as art. When MoMA says it is art, it is art. Over the two months of the exhibit, more than 31,200 people attended, and the cultural elite and critics embraced it. Its effect on the magazine article.

Meanwhile, in Detroit, George Walker had been working since 1929 for Dura, an automotive parts supplier, when in 1934 he opened his own industrial design office in Detroit and would soon design an upright vacuum cleaner for Apex, which would be marketed under the Montgomery Ward (MW) brand. But Walker would soon become inyolved with the automotive industry far beyond his imagination, as we will see later.

Car companies had been following the lead of General Motors and establishing internal design departments. Ford in 1933 began to update the appearance of its cars yearly, and to do this, Edsel Ford (1893-1943), Henry's son, established a styling department under the leadership of Eugene T. "Bob" Gregorie (1908-2004). Gregorie was born in New York City and raised in Cedarhurst, Long Island. In 1927, with no formal education, he started as a nautical draftsman at the Elco Boat Works in Bayonne, New Jersey, then moved to the more prestigious yacht designers Cox & Stevens in New York in 1928. In 1929, he followed his first love of automobiles by beginning at Brewster & Company, maker of custom bodies for Rolls Royce. Later that year he joined Harley Earl's Art and Colour Group at GM, but the stock market crash cost him his job. After a time unemployed and staying with his family in South Carolina, he was offered a job as body draftsman at the Lincoln Motor Company in Detroit in 1931. He jumped at the chance.

In 1928, following the lead of GM, Chrysler had established an internal Art and Color Department of five, headed by Herbert V. Henderson, but the department was subordinated to Chrysler's powerful engineering staff, and designers were relegated to cosmetic details such as grilles and hood ornaments. Chrysler's genuinely new innovative engineering design, under way since 1927, was by leading engineers Carl Breer, Fred Zeder, and Owen Skelton. Breer had designed the successful first Chrysler in 1924 and claimed it was his idea in 1927 to "follow nature" by designing a scientifically engineered, streamlined car. It was radically innovative in engineering and esthetics.

Wind tunnel experiments led to an ideal streamlined form. To achieve the tapered aerodynamic tail of a teardrop, the engineers moved the engine up over the front axle and placed the rear seat within the wheelbase rather than over the rear axle. This more evenly distributed the weight between the front and rear wheels, making a more comfortable ride and better handling. Rather than a single flat plane, the windshield was comprised of two panes meeting in a V and sloping to the rear. The body was the first unitary construction, with the frame and body shell integrated into a lightweight but rigid structure.

After the basic body form had been determined aerodynamically, stylists under Henderson were allowed to add cosmetic Art Deco details, such as parallel, decorative chrome elements on the grille, bumpers, and hood vents. The grille looked like a chrome waterfall splashing over the huge hood. Norman Bel Geddes, the country's foremost industrial designer, was engaged as a consultant in September 1933, and he reduced the number and widened the vertical chrome bars on the waterfall grille, raked the windshield back, and added side vents for cooling. The modernistic interior had rubber mats instead of carpets, and the seats were mounted in a frame of steel tubing. Introduced to the public in 1934, the car was hailed as the first functionally streamlined production car, the Airflow, used for both the Chrysler and DeSoto new models. Bel Geddes happily promoted the design to the public.

But to many, the bulbous sloping, front end that blended into the fenders looked clumsy, front heavy, and crudely shaped. It looked a bit like Dr. Porsch's 1932 prototype Kleinauto on steroids, devoid of grace and beauty. The engineers had achieved an innovative structural design, but potential customers stayed away in droves because the design, particularly the waterfal rille was too adicalor ustomer ccustoe t seei eentially vertical u vnally dmoes svehe py om nl ui.The is c Walter Chrysler that engineering innovation alone was not enough, and he hired Raymond

Dietrich (1894-1982) to become head of the Art and Color Department, and at the same time elevated the responsibility and power of the department.

Dietrich was born in the Bronx, and at age 12, his father sponsored his apprenticeship as an engraver with Manhattan's American Bank Note Company. He earned money s a pitcher for numerous smiro bsebal teams onance hi ucation n the arts. In  he coach builder Brewster & Company, which sonsored him in study at the Andrew F. Johnsn Technical School in Manhattan to learn advanced drafting and body design. Graduating in 117, he frst went to Chevrolet but within a year rejoined Brewster. As a "master craftsman, he finishd desins in a cosedroom to protect secet tecnques.

In 1920, he and Thomas Hibbard (1898-1982) left Brewster to start their own firm in Manatn,Lar rs.Teh  wol on paper to achieve a harmonious appearance, a technique that later would become standard automotive as well as industrial design practice. In 1924, the firmmerged with the Bridgepot Body Company, forming LeBaron, Inc., but in 1925 Dietrich sold his stake and joined the Murray Corporation of America, the company that built most of Lincoln's and Ford's production bodies. Murray and Dietrich shared ownership in a subsidiary manufacturing plant called Dietrich, Inc. Dietrich assisted Amos Northup in his design of the 1931 Reo Royale Eight, the predecessor of the groundbreaking 1932 Graham Blue Streak, mentioned earlier, but Dietrich was forced out of Murray in 1931.

At Chrysler, Dietrich was given the job of redesigning the objectionable hood of the illfated Airflow, which he did by extending the hood and adding a more conventional-looking, vertical grille. He also styled a conventional companion for the Airflow, called the Airstream, which would be produced in 1935-1936 for Chrysler and DeSoto, and marketed beside the Airflow, meeting the public need while hoping to sell enough Airflows to offset its development.

The Airstream would outsell the Airflow by five to one the first year, and nine to one in 136. The market failure of the Airflow would go down in automotive history as the result of styling too advanced for popular acceptance, and the failure of engineers to grasp the subtleties ofappearance design. The entire industry concluded that even meticulous and innovative engineering could not sell an ugly car. The Airflow failure would become a powerful argument for stylists, who possessed the elusive skill of designing for public emotions and tastes, rather than just for practical and functional purposes like engineers.

Despite the disaster of the Airflow, and despite the scientific fact that streamlining of cars had little effect on operation except at high speeds, streamlining would remain a popular style for the remainder of the decade, because the style was imprinted on consumers' minds as an icon of modernity and progress, which they hoped would take them out of the continuing Depression.

Streamlining was front and center on May 26, 1934, as the Century of Progress Exposition reopened in Chicago for its second year. Headlining the opening was the national demonstration of several brand new, streamlined trains. On May 26, the diesel-powered Burlington Zephyr, known as the "Silver Streak," set a speed record for travel between Denver, Colorado, and Chicago, Illinois, by making a "dawn to dusk" dash over the 1,015-mile distance in 13 hours and 5 minutes (normal trains took 25 hours) at an average speed of 77 mph, and arrived at the Century of Progress Exposition on opening day to appear on the Wings of the Century stage for display. The Zephyr was built by the E.G. Budd Manufacturing Company, Philadelphia, for the Chicago, Burlington and Quincy Railroad (CB&Q), commonly known as the Burlington, to promote and advertise passenger rail service. Christening was on April 18.

The Zephyr consisted of three cars: the power car, baggage-coach combine, and the coachobservation car. It was made of bright stainless steel, requiring no painting or maintenance, and was fastened together using a new technique of electric spot-welding developed by Budd. The entire train weighed the same as a single conventional train car and reached 112 mph during its record run. A 600 hp, eight-cylinder, Winton engine powered a generator, and the electricity generated was fed to electric traction motors connected to the axles in the front wheel truck. There were four trucks, one between each car, and one at each end. The nose design was a slanted half cylinder, often referred to as a "bathtub shrouding" or "shovel nose," with forward-facing windows to provide full visibility for the operator.

![](images/932acc346219ff0935e0dfd6c70fb5f927a29e55f54216644462d7a353408d49.jpg)

![](images/a671418a9a901f11e24c53ddb9ba4072baad1b4579fd578cf482a7af7d21f9ce.jpg)

![](images/c0576ac01874ffa849a585f696ff64581925686caf4de127b99825e98eb3f0d9.jpg)

The exterior streamline design was by aeronautical engineer Albert Gardner Dean, in collaboration with Philadelphia architects John Harbeson (1888-1986) and Paul Philippe Cret (1876-1945). After display at the exposition, the train was taken on a 31-state, 222-city publicity tour. More than two million people saw the train before it entered regular service in November between Kansas City, Missouri, and Lincoln, Nebraska. The very name, Zephyr, would soon be applied to products to capitalize on its popularity as a term of speed and modernity, such as Kem Weber's streamlined Zephyr digital clock design of 1934, and the Lincoln Zephyr of 1936.

The Burlington Zephyr was a hard act to follow, but there was another streamliner that tried to do so. The gasoline-powered M-10000 (its first design patent number was D100,000, followed by U.S. patents D100,001 and D100,002), produced by Union Pacific Railroad, visited the Chicago exposition after a tour of 68 cities that totaled 13,000 miles, everywhere attracting press attention and crowds that approximated one million. It was the first internal combustion, lightweight, streamlined, express passenger train in the United States, delivered to the railroad on February 12, 1934. Named on the design patent were Eugene Adams, Martin P. Bloomberg, William H. Mussey, and William B. Stout, all engineers at Pullman-Standard, the body developer.

T  r e r t 204 feet in length and weighing 77 tons. All were built of Duralumin, a hardened aluminum ally, and the bodes slanteinward at the bottom. The entie train was supported on just four wheeled trucks, located between cars, and at front and rear. The 600 hp engine, built by Winton, a subsidiary of General Motors, powered a generator of electricity that fed electric traction motors. The engine was housed in the front power car and featured a turret cab with windshields and a bulbous nose with two large air intakes for cooling. The body was painted in Armu Yl   L Bro onn Ovh 0  v closely resembled Norman Bel Geddes's Locomotive Number 1, his futuristic, never-produced design in his book, Horizons, in 1932, which also had three cars and a bulbous nose. M-10000, nicame theT Worm," woul enter rgular servic as the iyalina, for e Kan City-Salina route it served.

The Chicago Fair closed on Halloween, October 31, 1934, with a total two-year attendance record of over 48 million. Newspaper accounts described the last night as resembling New Year's Eve celebrations, with merrymaking running wild. For the first time in American history, an international fair had paid or itsel, paying off the 10 million debt from a bond issued on October 28, 1929, the day before the stock market crashed.

But it was the modern trains exhibited there that would inspire the railroad industry to follow the automotive lead in modern design and draw industrial designers into the business.

# Chapter 6

# Recovery, Plastics, and Education

The two pioneering train designs connected to the Chicago exposition had a significant a mmedia ffec he ailroaindustry. I November , the Pennsylvani Raod (PRR) hired Raymond Loewy to refine the Westinghouse design of the GG-1 design for mass production. Loewy had designed a trashcan for Pennsylvania Station in New York in 1932, an at the time had hown the PRR presiden a futurisic sket o sremlined lcooive.

The GG- v  C head wires. Twelve 385 hp traction motors drove the GG-'s 57-inch-diameter driving wheels on six axles. The body, over 76 feet long, was built in two halves joined with a ball-and-socket opposite directions, so it could be driven in forward or reverse.

In 1933, PRR had decided to replace its current P5a electric locomotives with ones that were more powerful and more streamlined. PRR asked General Electric and Westinghouse to design prototypes that were capable of 100 mph, a streamlined body shell, and a central control cab. Both companies submitted prototypes in August 1934, and both worked with PRR's Baldwin Locomotive Works as the design progressed. Features from both prototypes were refined and combined to produce the final design.

The Westinghouse prototypes were based on six plaster models made by Donald Dohner (mentioned earlier), Westinghouse's director of art in its engineering division, in 1933 when the project began. One of these models, shown in photographs, closely resembles the final design of what became the GG-1 prototype, which essentially was completed by August 1934, three months before Loewy was called in. By all traditions of attribution, Dohner should have been credited with the styling of the GG-1.

Loewy was called in for consultation on October 25, 1934, by PRR vice president and heir apparent Martin W. Clement to "enhance the GG-I's esthetics." A fee of \$5,000 was agreed upon, but he had only about a week before the production order was to be placed, so his contributions on the existing prototype were limited to the color scheme, which became Brunswick Green, with five gold pinstripes running along the sides and curving down on the front and rear toward the ground. Loewy also claimed he had recommended a smooth welded body instead of the riveted one used in the prototype, but there is some evidence that the rivets were the result of rushing the completion of the prototype and that they were never intended for use on the production models. One hundred thirty-nine production models would be built between 1934 and 1943, and many remained in service until the 1970s.

In any event, Loewy was retained for \$20,000 annually as the primary design consultant of PRR for the rest of the decade. Until his death, he publicly claimed the design of the GG-1 as one of his most important achievements and was exclusively credited with its design in all literature pertaining to the GG-l. Loewy was its ultimate promoter, not Dohner, who was essentially a modest man, who would, within a month after he designed the models, leave Westinghouse to begin his career in industrial design education, for which he is better remembered.

![](images/6ce7a81708835fb7c64388c4d98641b02de9b0367d391c324b858958133b6f55.jpg)  
Pennsylvania Railroad GG-1 (Classic Trains, Summer 2009. Photograph by Karl Zimmermann).

The main reason Donald Dohner never received credit was that after February 1934 he was no longer employed by Westinghouse. In fact he was fired as a result of the Fortune article on industrial desn that month.The article, desribe in detailearier, revealed the fact that his department at Westinghouse was costing the company \$75,000 per year (nearly \$1.3 million in 2013 dollars), a fact apparently not known by Westinghouse management, which was understandably shocked, and in the middle of a Depression took executive action to cut expenses.

![](images/c4fef1a2392363076c3a2edb89ca1b7ac053de3b48ef3c28e4e78189f89c2e58.jpg)  
Alexander Kostellow, ca. 1950s (Industrial Design magazine, October 1954).

Ironically, this unfortunate turn of events resulted in a new career for Dohner, and a milestone for the industrial design profession. As you may recall, before joining Westinghouse in 1930, Dohner had taught design as an instructor at the Carnegie Institute of Technology (CIT) starting in 1929. Upon hearing of Dohner's loss of job, his old colleagues at CIT were overjoyed at the opportunity of welcoming back someone who was now a nationally known industrial designer, recently featured in Fortune magazine. Painting and Design Department painting instructor Alexander Kostellow (18961954) reportedly shouted to departme eWil Read, "alhe oe!Lt'  hi! I l ! !r   !

Alexander Jusserand Kostellow was born in Iran (then Persia). He studied in Paris and the University of Berlin and came to the United States in 1916, where he studied painting at the National Academy, the Art Students League of New York, and the Kansas City Art Institute, where he met and married Rowena Reed.Both came to Pittsburgh in 12 to teach at CIT, he in painting, and she in sculpture.

Carnegie was not the first school with interest in industrial design. As mentioned earlier, there were anumber  art schools already teaching it invarious programs for engineers, and

sultant and lecturer in industrial design." Students, who previously would have studied architecture, were now attracted to industrial design as an alternative, and indeed many did see it as the wave o the future, as declared by Kostellow.The Chicago Art Institute offered industrial design courses in 1932, the same year the University of Cincinnati initiated an "Art and Industry" option in its School of Applied Arts, and in California, Kem Weber initiated a program in industrial design at the Art Center School in Los Angeles. He would teach there until 1943.3 In 1933 the Detroit Society of Arts and Crafts had made national news by recognizing the automobile as an "art form," and at the Cleveland School (now Institute) of Art (CIA), Viktor Schreckengost (19062008) had initiated a pioneer program of industrial design in 1933, which would become well known for automotive design.

![](images/63e50b0c7a19ab91089e7d03a37d9eb039e7f08c6609cea3a96e64a4baf1dfa6.jpg)

Schreckengost was born in Sebring, Ohio, to a potter father and had studied design and ceramics at CIA, graduating in 1929. After studying at the Kunstegewerbeschule (Arts and Crafts School) in Austria, he joined the faculty at CIA and worked part time at the Cowan Pottery Studio. There he created his famous

Viktor Schreckengost, ca. 1939 (courtesy David Nowacek and Gene Schreckengost).

28-inch-diameter Art Deco "Jazz" punch bowl that had been commissioned anonymously by Eleanor Roosevelt, who later ordered more for the White House and Hyde Park. Schreckengost soon became a consultant to the White Motor Company in Cleveland, and would later create

an innovative truck design with the cab over the engine to provide more storage space in the back.

![](images/b39401a9b00c2953db053ded43b1eceb34a0d930496f18985d45a16724b70ae6.jpg)  
Robert Lepper, ca. 1940s.

At Carnegie in 1933, optional classes in industrial design, based on Dohner's 1930 syllabus, had been offered to students and were in progress as Dohner rejoined the faculty in 1934 and initiated a new degreed program in industrial design at CIT, the first school to do so. Assisting Dohner was Art and Design Department head, Wilfred Radio, and faculty members Kostellow, Russell Hyde, Fred Clayter, and Robert Lepper (1906- 1991). Lepper had been hired to fill in for Dohner's position when he left for Westinghouse in 1930. Lepper was born in Aspinwall, a suburb of Pittsburgh, and graduated with a degree in painting from CIT in 1927. He spent a year or so in Europe soaking up modern art and joined the faculty at CIT in 1930, where he painted "machine art," such as his 1932 Crankshaft, a realistic image ofa Lincoln 12-cylinder crankshaft with pistons floating in the air. Because of his interest in mechanical things, he was more or less drafted into the new industrial design degree program in 1934.

So in the fall semester of 1934, third-year students were offered an option for a degree in industrial design, along with other third-year options of illustration, interior decoration, or art education. The first class included three women and two males, all to become the first degreed graduates of industrial design in the world. Dohner took them to manufacturing plants, such as Westinghouse, Fostoria Glass, and ALCOA (Aluminum Company of America), to show them how things were made, and then instructed them to design new products based on what they had seen.

Elsewhere, modern design continued to be publicized. From November 5, 1934, to January 6, 1935, the Metropolitan Museum of Art mounted an exhibition, Contemporary American Industrial Art, by 20 architectural and industrial designers that featured entire-room arrangements by architects except for a full-scale replica of an industrial design office and studio designed by Raymond Loewy. Architects and museums related all industrial design to architecture because they believed that architects should dominate the new field, as was the case in Europe. Accordingly, many of the rooms were traditionally credited to name architects, who selected all the decorative furnishings to keep industrial design "in its proper place" under the supervision of architects.

However, this time, a number of industrial designers were invited to participate in the event, including Viktor Schreckengost (chinaware), Donald Deskey (an entire dining room), Gilbert Rohde (Herman Miller clock and furniture), Gustav Jensen (rugs, wall treatments), Peter Müller-Munk (silverware), Walter von Nessen (display case and metalware), Lurelle Guild (silverware), Russel Wright (metalware, floor lamps), Alfons Bach (furniture) and Walter Dorwin Teague (on the cooperating committee). Still, the products shown were all from traditional ers, such as the elegant gas range Teague had recently designed for the Floyd-Wells Company in Royersford, Pennsylvania, that rivaled Bel Geddes's SGE range in simplicity, whiteness, and modernity. But attempts by architects to supervise and control industrial design were too late. The horse of industrial design was out of the architectural barn. Industrial designers already owned the field ofboth mechanical and decorative, modern mass-produced consumer products.

Streamlined trains were still in the national news at the end of the year. On December 27, 1934, the New York Central Railroad (NYC) exhibited its new steam locomotive at Grand Central Station, with a streamlined shell painted in gunmetal gray and called the Commodore Vanderbilt. Named to honor the original Commodore Vanderbilt locomotive of 1870, it was the first to utilize streamlining by adding a streamlined shell over a steam locomotive, NYC's J-1E 46-4 "Hudson" No. 5344.

An undergraduate student at Cleveland's Case School of Applied Science, Norman F. Zapf (19111974), designed the shell for his 1934 senior thesis, "The Streamlining of a Locomotive." Zapf tested scale models in a wind tunnel and concluded that at typical passenger speeds of 75 mph, streamlining reduced drag by 30 percent and required 350 less horsepower than a nonstreamlined locomotive. Nearly all his suggestions were incorporated into the final Commodore Vanderbilt. The nose form was similar to the "bathtub" design of the Burlington Zephyr. Carl Kantola, an in-house New York Central employee, worked on adapting the design to the required mechanical modifications. A number of sources cite Henry Dreyfuss as being a design consultant for the Commodore Vanderbilt, but his contributions, if any, are unknown.

ci on the NYC system, which was a smashing success, was highly photographe, and was Century Limited between New York and Chicago, the grandest, most elegant passenger train in America. For the rest of the 1930s, the Commodore Vanderbilt was the most popular Lionel O-gauge model train.

Although 4 was ne  the worst years  the ten-yar Great Deression, it ws the most exciting time for the new profession of industrial design, which became a household ly iutral a be Mance bl  h wih he wav design swirling around them by seeking new designs and new designers, hoping to extract themseve from the ltany  decinin lesreports. Newdes dearentsroute dozens of corporations. National spirits were rising due to the optimism generated by the to be the light at the end of the tunnel.

T in the world of design and in the public arena, were looking to legally protect their innovative, modern, American designs from the wholesale copying that had previously been common practice for European designs. That had been the primary concern of the American Union of Decorative Artists (AUDAC) when it was formed in 1927. Now, in 1933, to address AUDAC's accusations of design piracy, representatives and designers in the furniture industry joined together to found the National Furniture Designers' Council (NFDC) in order to draw up a code for enjoining piracy for the National Recovery Administration (NRA).

The purpose of the Roosevelt administration's establishment of the NRA in 1933 was to eliminate "cutthroat competition" by bringing industry, labor, and government together to create "fair practices" and to set prices. Industries were invited to submit codes of fair competition, and the furniture industry did so. But in 1935, the U.S. Supreme Court found that the NRA law was unconstitutional, ruling that it infringed on the separation of powers under the constitution. The NFDC disintegrated, but the seed for a furniture industry design organization had been planted, and in July 1935, Lawrence H. Whiting, director of the American Furniture Mart, called a special meeting of furniture designers, including those from the now defunct NFDC, and engaged Richard Bach, of the Metropolitan Museum, and Alfred Auerbach (18811944), design and merchandising consultant as well as editor of Retailing magazine, to serve as speakers for the meeting. The proceedings of the meeting are unknown, but the result, within a year, would be the forming of a furniture design organization, which will be described later.

That year, Al Lambach, mentioned earlier, returned to Chicago to work for Merton Gershun at the Chicago Furniture Mart, and also for the Tell City Furniture Company in Tell City, Indiana, where he designed radio and phonograph cabinets for Zenith, Clinton, Traveler, and Emerson. Because home radios of the day were traditionally enclosed in furniture for placement in living rooms, a number of furniture designers in the 1920s and early 1930s were actively designing in this field.

Furniture, as one of the traditional art industries, was stillin the good graces of museums, such as Bach's Metropolitan Museum, and of course the architects as wel, since many famous European architects, and equally famous American architects such as Frank Lloyd Wright, had been designing furniture for generations. A number of pioneer industrial designers had designed furniture too, including Norman Bel Geddes (Simmons Company, 1928); Paul Frankl (Skyscraper furniture, 1925); Gilbert Rohde (for Herman Miller Furniture Company, 1932-1935); John Vassos (RCA radio cabinets and his portable phonograph of 1935); Nathan Horwitt (Beta Chair for the Howell Company, Geneva, Illinois, 1930); Kem Weber (armchair by Lloyd Manufacturing Company of Menominee, Michigan, 1934); Donald Deskey (since 1926); and Russel Wright (Haywood-Wakefield, 1934). But most of those above had designed in the modern styles, not the traditional historic styles of many designers in the furniture industry.

Throughout the 1930s, Wright and Walter Von Nessen had designed scores of modern metal accessories for the Chase Brass & Copper Company. In 1935, Wright had renamed his firm from Russel Wright Incorporated to Russel Wright Accessories, and designed a natural maple line of furniture called Modern Living, made by Conant Ball Company for Macy's, which was widely promoted. The popular line would later be called American Modern, produced in bleached wood "blond" maple, and would make Wright famous. He opened a showroom for his business in New York, with the help of businessman Irving Richards, under the name Russel Wright, Inc.

In 1934, one of the pioneer furniture designers, Paul Frankl, relocated to California and became an active interior designer, working in a style that would be called "California Modern" at his gallery on Rodeo Drive in Beverly Hills, where celebrities such as Fred Astaire, Katherine Hepburn, Walter Houston, and Alfred Hitchcock became his clients. Other designers were working for celebrities. Around this time, Margaret Bourke-White (1904-1971), the famous Fortune photographer, approached John Vassos to design her studio in the Chrysler Building in New York, as well as the interior of her apartment in Tudor City. He did, and they remained close personal friends until her death.

Furniture and furnishings were still the mainstream of the traditional art industries except that now modern design was replacing some of the traditional styles of historical periods. But the new mechanical, mass-produced products of the artless industries remained in artistic limbo as a category yet to be accepted by museums as legitimate art.

Museums, including the Museum of Modern Art, and architects still had serious reservations about other mass-produced, mechanical products in which industrial designers were exclusively excelling, such as stoves, refrigerators, sinks, toilets, radios, office machines, etc. Elitists balked at calling these items furniture let alone works of art. To the elite artistic world, these products were commercially embarrassing, far exceeding the profitability of furniture or the decorative arts, and far more financially rewarding to the designers than any architect could expect. Accordingly, many regarded this arena of design as pure commercialism, the enemy of fine art, and were reluctant to embrace such mechanical products on an equal footing with furniture.

Nevertheless, evidence of the influence of industrial design was everywhere in the consumermarketplace. Sears, Roebuck & Companyhad hire Raymond Loewy i 32 tregn its Coldspot refrigerator after Henry Dreyfuss had declined the project, due to his similar refrigerator project for General Electric. It was an unwritten code of ethics in early industrial desn and stil) that you didn't accept projects for products that were competitive with your own designs for another client. Upon accepting the job, Loewy described the current Colpot nilproortievertical shoexdeoratewih aaze oldngs, pl, etc. perched on spindly legs high off the ground."4

Loewy's design was actually done by Clare E. Hodgman, of Loewy's staff, who after the ance models and came out in 1935—a sleek, white, vertical box with three, vertical, parallel ribs running from top to bottom in the center of its front, accenting the rectangular chrome Coldspot name escutcheon at eye level. Loewy had been inspired by his design of the interior shelf pattern from the radiator of the Hupmobile, on which he was working at the time. Sears p L    a .

But sales of he Coldspot in 1935 would double to \$1,388,000, and in five years would n  ha ecesor. Loe'reputation was made. He now had 12 clients and was doing very well

Sears hired Robert E. Bourke (19161996) in 1935. He was born in Chicago, went to Morgn r H l   kiwas v pa. eean ke os. HChiAr I cleaning floors and latrines to pay for tuition. He took courses in architecture and engineering at Armour Institute during the Depression and began working at Sears under Clarence Karstadt. His first assignment was to design a manure spreader by the Oliver Farm & Equipment Company. Jack Morgan headed the department. Just like his fellow employee, Clare Hodgman, Bourke would later work for Raymond Loewy.5

In May 1935, the French oceanliner Normandie, at the time the largest and fastest passenger ship afloat, completed her maiden voyage from Le Havre to New York in four days, three hours, and 14 minutes, winning the traditional Blue Riband (an unoffiial accolade given to passenger liners for the highest average speed). The Normandie's luxurious interiors were in the latest Art Deco and Streamline Moderne styles, and the Normandie was quickly heralded as a ship for the rich and famous.

![](images/0b8649719b5c809791bac812f4c2e189d2f6d4f2ecd71d782d1963fa9f08afd2.jpg)  
Peter Müller-Munk, ca. 1950s.

She was also the inspiration for an elegant, chrome-plated, brass pitcher designed by Peter Müller-Munk (1907-1967), the New York silversmith mentioned earlier. The Normandie Pitcher, shaped like the prow of its namesake, was made by the Revere Copper and Brass Company in Rome, New York, and would be produced until 1941.

Müller-Munk was born in Germany, studied as a silversmith at the University of Berlin, and immigrated to the United States in 1926. He worked at Tiffany's in New York as a metalworker from 1926 to 1928, exhibiting at the 1928 Macy's exposition. In 1929, he established his own silver studio and exhibited at the 1929 and 1930 Metropolitan Museum shows.

Now Müller-Munk was jumping into the nascent industrial design education field. At Carnegie Tech, in Pittsburgh, upon finishing the first year of the degree program he initiated, Donald Dohner was contacted by his former employer in the Pittsburgh

public schools, James Boudreau, who had become dean of Pratt Institute's Art School in 1928. Boudreau wanted Dohner to organize and lead an industrial design program at Pratt, similar to the one he had just started at Carnegie. Having also worked with Boudreau as a lecturer and consultant at Pratt in 1932, Dohner accepted, and Müller-Munk was called in to replace him as head of the program at Carnegie.

In the fall, Müller-Munk and Alexander Kostellow would continue Dohner's curriculum and practice of taking students to tour local manufacturing facilities and having them design products based on the production methods they had seen. As a matter of fact, it is this unique accommodation of the client manufacturer's production and economic limitations that distinguished industrial designers from their predecessors, the pure artists and industrial artists. Pioneer industrial designers were developing the unique philosophy of industrial design as a multidisciplinary process that was not only art, but a balance of economics, engineering, materials selection, and human convenience considerations.

Their other distinguishing characteristic was their commitment to modern design, rather than the imitation of traditional period design styles, still clung to by many industrial artists, and the most modern style, now known as streamlining, was at the forefront of transportation design. On May 29, 1935, the first of a series of streamlined Hiawatha trains, the Twin Cities Hiawatha, began service from Chicago, through Milwaukee, to the twin cities of Minneapolis and St. Paul in morning and afternoon runs. Operated by the Milwaukee Railroad to compete with the Burlington Twin Cities Zephyr and North Western Railway's Twin Cities 400, the Class A 4-4-2 steam locomotive, built by the American Locomotive Company (ALCO), was designed by ALCO design consultant Otto Kuhler (1884-1977) with a nose form similar to the Burlington Zephyr, but without the front cab windows, the cab being in the rear as with all traditional steam locomotives. The nose was graced with horizontal stainless steel wings with a red engine number at its center. Kuhler also designed the famous Hiawatha "beaver tail" observation car at the rear, and all the interiors down to the napkins and draperies.

Kuhler was born near Essen, Germany. At age 19 he was commissioned to illustrate a catalog of steam locomotives. His auto body design for a Mercedes chassis won a gold medal in 1913. He served in the German army in World War I, after which he enrolled in the Academy of Art in Dusseldorf to learn etching. He was employed as a stylist with N.A.G. in Berlin and designed sets for the silent film The Tunnel, then became associate editor of Der Motor magazine. He styled car bodies for Snutzel Pére & Fils in Brussels and a 1914 roadster for Fiat in Italy. Kuhler immigrated to the United States in 1923 and worked as a commercial artist in Pittsburgh. After becoming a citizen in 1928, he opened a studio in Manhattan, promoting the streamlining of railroad equipment and working for the J.G. Brill Company styling trains and trolley cars, including a smooth front-end trolley called the Brill Bullet in 1931. In 1932, the American Locomotive Company (ALCO) commissioned him as a design consultant.

In April 1935, Business Week magazine announced that "Depression is a forgotten word in the automobile industry, which is forging ahead in production, retail sales, and expansion o podctive apacty i ar icent heentis.At he Ford Motor Cy in Detroit, the young styling department established in 1933 under Eugene T. "Bob" Gregorie had been struggling under the dominance of engineers, as the one at Chrysler had been. Ford stylists were subjugated to body engineers, who resented the stylist's role in design, and to Charlie Sorensen, who placed speed of production over beauty and style.

Both were under the powerful Henry Ford, who staunchly resisted modernization and importance of automotive appearance: Henry's son, Edsel (18931943), who was a sophistict paton  the rts, nd who had an excelent yeor des. Esel waseei  wn control o styling for Gregorie's department and assisted Gregorie in battles with engineers and production supervisors. Unlike GM, which had a complex bureaucracy, Ford had only Hey ll  G of his designs.

Edsel had seen the styling disaster of the 1934 Chrysler Airflow by engineers, so when it was time to design the new, low-cost 1936 Lincoln, Edsel made sure Bob Gregorie was in full charge. The early concept for the car was the Briggs Dream Car, designed by John Tjaarda at o  Ciu  n, hic cr not unlike seeker version  Ferdinand orsch Kleutohen ndr evelon Germany, with a front sloping hood that now reminded people of the discredited Chrysler

A     T German origins of the Kleinauto.

Tjaarda was reluctant to change the design, so Edsel called Gregorie in to work with Tjaarda to resolve the dilemma. Gregori decided tochange the front end by relocating the a inspiring styling innovation. The fenders and teardrop rear end were retained, but with the

![](images/d94246144a73dc40664881f274d6ef116ab923b78ad86841d6aeb1309aaac177.jpg)  
Lincoln Zephyr, 1936.

new prowlike front end, the front fenders seemed to suggest water being sprayed up and to the sides from the prow. The final design would be introduced as the 1936 Lincoln Zephyr, capitalizing on the name of the popular streamlined Burlington Zephyr train. It would be hailed by many as

the first commercially successful streamlined car, and its unique front-end design would be imitated by dozens of competitors over the next five years. It was indeed a watershed of automotive design and in fact remains a classic design today.

Another transportation marvel was introduced in 1935, the Scarab, engineered by aviation engineer William Bushnell Stout (1880-1956), with the exterior designed by John Tjaarda. Unlike any car of the time, the Scarab was what many call the first minivan, with a large interior compartment with a flexible seating system that could accommodate up to ten passengers. Stout envisioned it as a mobile office. To create the space, he did away with the normal chassis and drive shaft by using a rear engine and a unitized body structure, permitting a low flat-floor interior. The body design was without fenders, presenting a fully streamlined aluminum aircraft-type shell with built-in headlights. Although the Scarab was offered for sale at \$5,000 (a Chrysler Airflow then sold for \$1,345) and up to a hundred a year were to be manufactured, only nine were actually made.

The major star at the November 1935 New York Auto Show was the Cord 810 Winchester Sedan, designed by Gordon M. Buehrig (1904-1990) and made by the Auburn-Cord-Duesenberg Automobile Company in Connersville, Indiana. Cord was the brand name, after the owner of all three brands, Erret Lobban Cord, whose philosophy was to build innovative, different cars. The Cord 810 was indeed innovative and different. It featured front-wheel drive, a rear-hinged hood, retractable hidden headlights, absence of a running board, no visible grille, and was offered with a supercharger. It was highly streamlined, with a rounded, boxlike hood featuring a grille of horizontal louvers that wrapped around the lower half of the box, which some dubbed the "coffin." The crowds at the New York show were so dense that visitors had to stand on the bumpers of nearby cars to get a look. Many orders were taken at the show, but the cars would not be ready for delivery until April 1936, due to problems of production. Over 1,000 were sold the first year. In 1936, Buehrig would also design the 1937 Cord 812, which in 1951 would be displayed by the Museum of Modern Art as a work of art. But by 1937, Errett Cord's empire would collapse and all production would cease, another casualty of the Depression. In 1936, Buehrig would go to the Edward G. Budd Company in Philadelphia as chief designer of prototypes.

![](images/c26c8f5af2dd5538e7b35b67b947efae3d2a0c068fcb979404556802ac437d94.jpg)  
1936 Cord by Buehrig.

Buehrig was born in Mason City, Illinois, and attended Bradley Polytechnic in Peoria before becoming an apprentice at the Gotfredson Body Company, Detroit, in 1924. He worked for Dietrich, Inc., in 1926, then at Packard, and in 1927 was one of the first employees hired by Harley Earl at the newly established Art and Colour Section at GM. In 1928 he worked for the Stutz Company in Indianapolis, and in 1929 became chief body designer for Auburn-Cord-Duesenberg, where he designed the Duesenberg Model J line of luxury cars. In 1933 he returned to GM, and in 1934 he returned to Auburn-Cord-Duesenberg.

Harold Van Doren and his partner, John Gordon Rideout, had developed a growing industrial design business in Toledo, Ohio. In 1933, they had designed a bright green, plastic, skyscraper-style table radio for Air King Products, a large Magnalite (cast aluminum with varying thicknesses, allowing for maximum heat conductivity) teakettle for Wagner Manufacturing Company (designed by Rideout), and plastic stemware for the National Silver Company. In 1934 they designed a Magnalite aluminum covered casserole for Wagner, and a trendsetting sheet-metal gasoline pump for the Wayne Pump Company. In 1935, they designed a lightweit, moden Seniel Duplex retail scale rToleoScal, using the largest plasticoi of the time, and developed an entire line of streamlined children's bicycles, tricycles, scooters, and wagons for the American National Company, including the Skippy-Racer Scooter (1933), the Skippy Sno-Plane Sled and the Skippy Stream Line Racer Wagon (1934), and the Streamline Velocipede and the Pioneer Streamlined Tot Bike (1935). Now even kids could enjoy streamlined transportation!

However, in 1935 Rideout left Van Doren to open his own office in Cleveland, Ohio, John Gordon Rideout and Staff, and hired Arthur N. BecVar (1911-2003), who had graduated from Notre Dame University in 1933 with a BFA, received an engineering certificate from Purdue University, and an MFA at the Institute of International Education, Charles University, Prague, Czechoslovakia, in 19331934. With Rideout gone, Van Doren renamed his Toledo firm Harold Van Doren & Associates. The reason for Rideout's move was not business, nor problems with Van Doren, but the more romantic result of his relationship with Alice Chalifoux (19082008), a concert harpist with the Cleveland Symphony Orchestra and head of the harp department at the Cleveland Institute of Music, whom he married in 1937.

![](images/d7afb458edbc88365a398fa820b6e7a604c474c71793ea849b62510d5affef85.jpg)  
Arthur N. BecVar, ca. 1970s (courtesy Industrial Designers Society of America).

The 1930s saw the development of all kinds of plastics, new synthetic materials with intemeal miihi  pai Be es, he p produc was Bakelie  hermetting henol fomaldehyd esn evelope  07 y Belgian-born chemist Leo Baekeland in New York. But it was only available in black, dark brown,ordark maron, not particularlyttractive consumercors.

Te  eT - hyde called Plaskon used by Van Doren and Rideout in the white Toledo scales, mentioned abov.Polystyrene and acrylc, the latter called by trade ames Plexiglas r Lucie, had been developed in 1930. Polyethylene was developed in 1933, nylon in 1934 by DuPont, polyester in 1936, and melamine formaldehyde, called Melamine, and Dacron, both in 1938. Plastics transformed the look and feel of consumer products.

Nohau a ohe  Nel ing machines melted a stream of small pellets of raw plastic into semiliquid form and force it I the machine would begin the next cycle. Hundreds of parts could be produced within hours.

Inal Be an o e     a Warrn Telecon Copany, wrorien  plasti magzine urgimauacu use plastics, which she described as

a material with tremendous possibilities . which no manufacturer, if he be alert and watchful of his competition, can afford to overlook. Radios, clocks, dishes, jewelry—all being developed today—have an enormous significance.

Kogan also pointedly advised manufacturers to study the "feminine viewpoint," citing the fact that

thirty million women—all potential customers--constituting practically the entire buying structure of the nation—comprise a force, which cannot, or should not, be disregarded. The tastes of the American woman, her reaction to color and form, are of vital importance to the manufacturer.6

In another article, Kogan also advised designers to do their homework:

It is the designer's job, therefore, to know materials, to know their qualities, their basic structures, their resistance to wear, and their cost—as well as to know markets and trends—in order to intelligently design for the manufacturer.7

Her articles were typical of others being written by industrial designers in trade magazines—stressing practical, technical, and cost-reduction results of design rather than simply artistic beauty. Kogan had an office in New York, producing houseware designs in silver, aluminum, ceramics, glass, wood and cloth for Quaker Silver Company and many other clients. In 1936 she would design a modern double serving dish in silver for Reed & Barton.

Regular commercial air transportation was just beginning, inspired by Charles Lindberg's solo flight across the Atlantic in 1927. The China Clipper, a Martin M-130 four-engine flying boat built for Pan American Airways, initiated mail service on November 22, 1935, from San Francisco to Manila. Its interiors, intended for long Pacific trips (59 hours, 48 minute flying time, with overnight stops at Honolulu, Midway, Wake Island and Guam), with sofa-type seating that converted into sleeping berths, lavatories, bar, kitchen, and pantry, were designed by Norman Bel Geddes in 1934. The twin-engine Douglas DC-3 airliner initiated commercial domestic flights shortly thereafter, on December 17, 1935, after refinement with development models DC-1 (1933) and DC-2 (1934). The DC-3 carried 21 passengers, and a transcontinental flight took about 15 hours, with only three refueling stops. People were astounded at the potential for future air travel.

Kem Weber was probably inspired to design his famous Airline chair in 1934 by earlier versions of the DC-3. Weber had labored for months to design a chair that could be sold disassembled and readily put together by the consumer, and the Airline chair was the result. Weighing 17 pounds and packed flat in a box, it was to sell for \$24.95. The chair design failed

![](images/fddc915aeeb0189d42484904814189e8c59808ae604f12a87ac6b83b197ffcea.jpg)  
Airline chair, 1934, by Kem Weber.

to find a manufacturer, and so craftsmen, rather than machines, built 300 for the Walt Disney Studios in California. Weber is also noted for being the main architect of the Walt Disney Studios complex in Burbank, California. Mickey Mouse had been created in 1928.

At the same time that industrial design began to flourish, the Roosevelt administration's New Deal was pouring funds into new relief programs to help the unemployed, to provide social entitlement programs for the elderly, and to allow unions to organize. Industrial design, which was now driving industry, would become a potential political target of the newly empowered, organized American labor movement.

## Labor Design and Design Organization

Wia tepubliiynte unheiablaroen oogy and design, as well as on the high unemployment levels, strikes and labor disputes of the time, it was perhaps inevitable that industrial design would become involved with government and the intensive labor activism of the Depression era. In April 1935, the Works Progress Administration (WPA) was created by an act o Congress upon the order of President Franklin Roosevelt as part of the Second New Deal. At the time, there were 20 million persons on relief. In order to employ the unemployed, the act provided \$4.9 billion in funds for the construction ofroads, bridges, and local public buildings, but it also employed musicians, artists, actors and writers in public works. A number of artist/designers, including some mentioned in this book, took advantage of WPA grants to paint murals in post offices, universities, and other public buildings.

In July, the Wagner National Labor Relations Act validated union authority. By August, the Social Security Act provided retirement benefits, the first federal assistance to the elderly. In September, the Hoover Dam (named after Herbert Hoover, president when it was begun in 1 was dedicate, creatig Lake Mead, the word' largest reservoir at the time, with President Roosevelt presiding. The Roosevelt administration quickly renamed it Boulder Dam, but the confusion between names continued. In 1947, the name would be changed back to Hoover Dam.

On December 2, 1935, in New York, a frigid and blustery day, several hundred men and women lined up outside the 35th Street offices of the WPA for a chance to enroll in the Design Laboratory, a pioneering school sponsored by the Federal Art Project (FAP), one of the WPA's cultural projects. It was the first institution to offer free, comprehensive education in modernist desig. Its brochure caimed it had been create to supply a hithertounfulfilled and pressing need in America" for people with skills to devise, package, and promote consumer products that were styled to sell, thereby stimulating economic recovery. Clearly, this noble purpose was inspired by the incredible success of industrial designers in doing precisely that over the previous eight years.

By emphasizing "coordination in the study of esthetics, industrial products, machine fabrication, and merchandizing," the school would "train designers, not specialized craftsmen, by correlating thorough instruction in the general principles of design and the fine arts with shop practice." Two hundred twenty people enrolled that day, more that the school's administration anticipated, and there were still hundreds more on a waiting list.

The school was the result of political action by the Depression-era avant-garde, the unprecedented public arts bureaucracy of the New Deal, the militant industrial unionism of the Congress of Industrial Organizations (CIO), which had just been formed within the American Federation of Labor (AFL), the United Auto Workers (UAW ), and the radical socialist politics of the Popular Front (a broad coalition of different political groupings, often made up of centrists and leftists).

The collaboration was an example of how modernism was viewed by some as a means to control cultural production by political action and thus prevent its dominance by business interests and capitalism. The idea of democratizing American culture was attractive to many elites of the time, who resented the new lucrative art form of industrial design being controlled by big business and, by the way, a handful of industrial design celebrities getting rich in the process.

The school organizers recruited well-known industrial designers such as Raymond Loewy, Walter Dorwin Teague, Donald Deskey, George Sakier, and Russel Wright to lend legitimacy to an advisory board by the use of their names, and lured furniture designer Gilbert Rohde to serve as director. It is likely that these men were attracted by the idealistic intention of training young designers, as stated in the brochure, and as desperately needed in the field, rather than by the hidden leftist ideology behind it.

This ideology was obvious in major study texts required by the curriculum, including The Theory of the Leisure Class, by Thorstein Veblen, published in 1899, which claimed that reform of industrial art had to be through the technology of machines, and highly influential sociologist/historian Lewis Mumford's book, Technics and Civilization (1934). Mumford considered streamlining as mere expressive and "outmoded decoration" and insisted that functionalist esthetics (such as the geometric abstract forms at the Bauhaus) was more appropriate for society, a view shared and promulgated by the Museum of Modern Art (MoMA), which venerated Bauhaus architects, and which would enthusiastically take up the cause of functional esthetics. The recent closure of the leftist Bauhaus by the Nazis as "a Marxist manifestation" generated much political sympathy throughout the liberal and progressive art community, which actively sought to bring exiled Bauhaus refugees to the United States to lead the modern m B of patriotic defiance against Nazism and war. Others saw communism as a similar deterrent t theevil ffscism, snce Hite's government was fervently anti-communist, andcmmuists were fighting incessantly against Hitler and fascism.

Despite internal political and philosophical differences of faculty members, the school implemented a provisional three-year design curriculum in August 1936, which promised to nt hetnarend tyl h -calleInial tyleh invented and promoted by MoMA starting in 1932 and based on the Bauhaus).

At the same time, an essay by the Federal Art Project (FAP) historian published in The thirohairpins ooiv fbulous price,"iakabl efeece leading industrial designers.

Many students and faculty of the Design Laboratory joined militant organizations of cultural workers, including those where communists, socialists, or Trotskyites (those supporting the political and economic theories of communism advocated by Leon Trotsky and his followers, usually including the principle of worldwide revolution) predominated, and instigated strikes and demonstrations demanding autonomy of the school, calling for a "United Front W u . several students and faculty were arrested for public protests and demonstrations.'

Despite the labor unrest and strikes, 1936 was dawning with signs of economic recovery. The main economic indicators had regained the levels of the late 1920s, except for unemploym s    , hou  u han e  .Il increasing design activity.

In 136, Montgomery Ward volume rose from 2 percent to 82 percent over Sears. By thn A S i e y  Bu  , an wen     t  Dv Chaa B c t. d his wn office n Chicag ,whic would becoe the lrgest ndustril ds i of the chief legal counsel for Montgomery Ward at the time.3

Famous celebrity industrial designers we beig called upon to serve in positions of civic responsibility. In New York City, mayor Fiorello La Guardia had appointed Norman Bel Geddes to the Municipal Arts Committee of New York in 1935,4 and Walter Dorwin Teague was appointed as one of the eight directors on the New York World Fair's Board of Design in 1936, as planning began for the 1939 event.

In 1935, Bel Geddes designed and produced Dead End in New York. He toured the city in police cars for atmosphere and recorded river and street sounds for four days for use in the play's background. He had hired Robert Gruen (19131999) that year as a set designer, and Gruen would open his own glassware design office in New York in 1940. Ålso in 1935, Egmont Arens established his own design firm in New York, promoting what he called "industrial humaneering," emphasizing simplicity and function.

John M. Little of Minneapolis joined Van Doren & Associates in Toledo in 1936. Little knew Van Doren from Minneapolis, where Van Doren had been assistant director of the Minneapolis Institute of Arts (MIA) until 1930, and Little had opened an industrial design office there in 1931. In 1937 Donald Dailey (1914-1997) would join Van Doren's firm as design director. Dailey was also from Minneapolis and had attended art classes at the MIA. He had also studied marketing and engineering at the University of Toledo (1934-1936) and design at the Toledo Museum School of Design (19361937).

Henry Dreyfuss had been retained in 1934 by the Hoover Company to design vacuum cleaners. His annual retainer fee was \$25,000 (\$435,000 in 2013 dollars), and his first design, the Hoover Model 150 upright vacuum cleaner, debuted in 1936, beginning Dreyfuss's association with Hoover that would last for 20 years. That year, he also designed a typewriter or Royal, accessories for United Airlines DC-3s, and his first streamlined Mercury K-5 Pacific train design for the New York Central Railroad, marketed as the " Train of Tomorrow." The first Mercury in the series operated daily between Cleveland and Detroit starting in 1936.

![](images/8547367016d1e765717210e2477b147dc95321a1447bfa906784a1152a027e7e.jpg)  
Hoover Model 150, 1936, by Henry Drey iSS

For the locomotive, Dreyfuss covered the exterior pipes, whistles, and fittings with a smooth, "bathtub" cowl, with cutaway sides to display the driving wheels and rods, which were dramatized at night by concealed spotlights under the cowling on either side. Dreyfuss also designed the interiors on the train, including the coaches, kitchen, dining car, smoking lounge, parlor car, and observation car, all with arrangements designed to avoid the traditional "linear" look of railroad coaches and to have the atmosphere of a private club.

For example, the dining car was in three sections, two with conventional seating, but the middle section was made up of tables for two seated side-by-side, facing the center aisle. In the rounded-end observation car, Dreyfuss lowered the windowsills to maximize the view through four-foot-high windows. Instead of having seating arranged around the walls, facing in, he placed the seating in the center, facing out toward the windows. Later versions of the Mercury included the Chicago-Detroit Mercury and the Chicago Cincinnati Mercury. The first Cleveland-Detroit Mercury would last run in 1959.

Dreyfuss was not the only train designer. In February 1936, Raymond Loewy, with employee Carl Otto (b. ca. 1910-ca. 1986 ), hired in 1935, designed the Pennsylvania Railroad's K-4s streamlined shroud for steam locomotive No. 3768, hiding most of the mechanical details. Otto was born Carl Louis Otto in Michigan and was trained as an engineer at Michigan State University. He began his career as a draftsman with Duesenberg in Auburn, Indiana, and then went to New York to work for Norman Bel Geddes.

Loewy did not follow the "bathtub" streamlining shrouds of previous trains, but decided to retain the unique character of the traditional, cylindrical boilers of steam locomotives. He basically designed a bullet nose for a cylindrical boiler and placed it upon a streamlined platform cvrig eriver wheel that  the  hal  bulle. He wul efine e en into the S-1 in 1939.

Loewy had also acquired the Studebaker account in 1935 and was retained for the design of exteriors of the 1937 models, at the same time as Helen Dryden, former art director of the Dura Company in Ohio, who had been retained at \$100,000 per year for Studebaker car interior designs since 1934, worked on the interior. At the time, she was acclaimed as "the highest-paid woman artist." Loewy's exterior design, completed in 1936, featured a narrow, vertical grille with horizontal louvers, the top three of which extended rearward along the side of the hood. Headlights were teardrop shaped. Dryden was publicly credited in Studebaker ads as "the stylist" for the 1937 Studebaker, but she was dropped as a consultant that same year. Loewy would continue to design Studebaker cars until the company went out of business in 1961.

In 1935, Walter Dorwin Teague pioneered in the branding of gasoline service stations when he designed his first for Texaco, which included not just the logotype, but a functional, rain-protecte rchiteture and layout that woul beome sandar bmark oral vie stations over the next two decades. In 1936, he also designed the Kodak Bantam Special camera in classic Art Deco style for his longtime client.

That same year, Brooks Stevens designed a custom Zephyr "Land Yacht" for William Woods Plankinton, consisting of a streamlined truck cab and 17-foot hitchable trailer with an electric icebox, full kitchen with electric stove, bathroom with shower, living room, hunting and fishing equipment, and sleeping quarters for eight. The entire length of the rig was 45 feet, diplayienooho  wel.

Norman Bel Geddes in 1936 was busy designing exhibits for the 1939 World's Fair, a e Corporation, as wel as exhibiting his designs at the Museum o Science and Industryin Chicgo and designing stage sets for The Eternal Road in New York.

IT e W to receive a degree was Maud (Bowers) Rice (1913-1998), a Philippine national. Donald D   I on Deprtent  CarngiAlexanr Kosellow,  the Crneg dusril des culy his wie, Rowna Ree would depart   Doner  Pratt  8.Kostellow ouo become a prominent and influential faculty member in the Pratt program.

In 1936, the Homer Laughlin China Company introduced a line of mass-produced ceramic tableware for the home called Fiestaware, designed by Frederick Hurten Rhead (1880- The riy cloremix-na,moeie w hihly scul  hea and is still a classic design valued highly by collectors. Rhead had emigrated from the United Kingdom in 1902 and worked for several ceramics factories, including Roseville, where he was director from 1904 to 1908; Jervis Pottery on Long Island; and from 1917 to 1927, he directed research at American Encaustic Tiling Company in Zanesville, Ohio. From 1927 until his death, he would remain as art director for Homer Laughlin.

In July 1936, the Spanish Civil War began, and in November, Franklin Roosevelt was elected by a landslide to his second term as president of the United States. Unemployment was still at 14 percent. Roosevelt would initiate payroll taxes to pay for Social Security, and with the threat of unrest in Europe, he would establish the Neutrality Act intended to avoid any involvement in taking sides in disputes between nations.

In Chicago, Lawrence H. Whiting, director of the American Furniture Mart, invited a group of furniture designers to a luncheon at the Furniture Club to hear speakers Donald Deskey, Gilbert Rohde, Kem Weber, and Leo Jiranek, all mentioned earlier. Possibly influenced by the Wagner Act of 1935, which established federal rights of workers to organize unions, to engage in collective bargaining, and to take part in strikes, attendees at the 1936 meeting at the Furniture Club agreed to form a new organization: the Designers' Institute of the American Furniture Mart (DIAFM).8

This appeared to be intended as a union of furniture designers, since a main requirement of membership restricted member design activities to furniture design, a restriction that was not appreciated by many industrial designers, who were designing everything from locomotives to wash machines. As we noted earlier in the formation of the Design Laboratory, this was a time of intense labor unrest. From September 1936 to May 1937, half a million workers were involved in sit-down strikes nationally. A strike against Republic Steel resulted in four deaths and 84 injuries.

By the spring of 1937, the WPA designated the Design Laboratory as one of the many programs to be discontinued, as the school struggled with layoffs, violence, and confusion. A radical, leftist, professional trade union, the Federation of Architects, Engineers, Chemists and Technicians (FAECT) came to the rescue as the new sponsor and to provide direction of the school. In the summer session, the curriculum was revamped along the lines of the Bauhaus Foundations Course and was turned into a four-year BA and BFA program comparable to standard universities. Enrollment increased to 400, and administrators hoped it would become a "labor university."

Other private educational institutions of design were being established or initiated at the same time. The University of Illinois at Champaign-Urbana established a BFA program of industrial design in 1937. Cooper Union in New York offered a four-year certificate in plastic design in 1937, with instructors Paul Feeley and Lila Ulrich, a Bauhaus graduate.

The California Graduate School of Design in Pasadena organized a two-year graduate program in industrial design with advice from Richard F. Bach of the Metropolitan Museum of Art, Royal Bailey Farnum of the Rhode Island School of Design, Peter Müller-Munk of the Carnegie Institute of Technology, and architect George Howe. It was the only graduate program of industrial design in the country. Graduate engineers were taught the principles of product engineering and industrial design.

Architect Hunt Lewis was named initial director of the school, and he invited as faculty members Walter P. Baermann (1903-1972), a 1932 émigré from Munich who had served as educational director of the Springfield (Massachusetts) Art Museum; Karl With (18911980), a German art historian and educator who had fled the Nazi regime in 1933; and Kem Weber, who also taught at the Art Center School of Design since 1932, and who had recently designed his famous 1934 Airline chair. Baermann was soon appointed director, and he remained in that position until 1941, when the school was merged into the graduate engineering curriculum of the California Institute of Technology (CalTech) and renamed the California School of Design.

At the Cranbrook Academy of Art near Detroit, Charles Eames (1907-1978) joined the faculty in 1937 and headed the department of experimental design to explore new materials such as plastics and plywood for furniture. Eames was born in St. Louis and studied architecture at Washington University from 1924 to 1926. He worked with an architectural firm in St. Louis from 1925 to 1927, and then as a partner in the firm Gray & Eames from 1930 to 1937. At Cranbrook, Eero Saarinen (1910-1961), the son of Cranbrook's founder, Finnish architect Eliel Saarinen (1873-1950), had graduated from Yale in architecture in 1934 and joined his father in 1935. In 1937, Eero began working with Eames, along with Italian émigré Harry Bertoia (1915-1978).

With the expansion of industrial design education and the intense publicity generated by the new profession, men and women were entering the field in scores. Counting the autoers was probably in the hundreds by this time. The purpose of this book is to focus on those pioneers who were active in the profession before 1935, so while important designers from this point on will be mentioned, their biographies and careers will be minimal, and the text will focus on those earliest dozens of machine designers that are of specific interest in this book.

German refugees from the Bauhaus were flocking to America. Bauhaus master Josef Albers (18881976) and his wife, Anni (18991994), had arrived in 1933 to become faculty members at Black Mountain College in North Carolina. Walter Gropius, former director of the Bauhaus, who had opened an ce in London after his resignation from the Bauhaus in 1928, arrived in the United States in 1937 to become a professor in the graduate program at theHarvard School  Architeture, along wit his Buhaus sudent, Marcel Breur 02 1981).

Làszló Moholy-Nagy, also of the Bauhaus but in exile in London at the time, became, upon the recommendation of Gropius, director of the School of Design at the Association of Arts and Industries in Chicago, which he opened in 1937 and promptly renamed the New Bauhaus: American School of Design. Hin Bredendieck (1904-1995), a former Bauhaus student; Georgy Kepes (1906-2001), a Hungarian designer who had worked for Moholy-Nagy in his Berlin studio; and Alexander Archipenko (1887-1964), a Russian artist and sculptor who had worked in Berlin and immigrated to the United States in 1929, all joined Moholy-Nagy at the school. The school attracted only 20 students and would close in 1938 under financial difficulties, but it would be reopened in 1939 as the School of Design, under the patronage of Walter Paepcke, president of the Container Corporation of America.

Man inutril dsners we enjying a busy year   Detroindustrial e cultant Geore Walkr bgan esi Nasoiclient e work Brooks Stevens designe aModel B tractor o Allis-Chalmers, which was ghte n cheaper than its key competitor, Ford's Farmall, and a streamlined sales van for the Western Printing and Lithograph Company based on his 1936 Zephyr land yacht design.

Henry Dreyfuss designed a whitewall Double Eagle automotive tire for Goodyear. Bell Labs introduced the "Phone of the Future" that Dreyfuss had been working on since 1930— a black phenolic plastic 300 tabletop phone that combined the transmitter and receiver into a single combined handset resting in a horizontal cradle. It replaced the "candlestick" model that had been introduced in 1916, and which, in 1919, had a rotary dial added. The classic 300 would be produced until 1950.

Otto Kuhler, working with the Baltimore & Ohio (B&O) Railroad as art director of the Omagaziee lon inbulleo s o, known as the "Kuhler type," for B&O's Royal Blue train on the Washington to New York run. The locomotive, a 462 Pacific" Class, was painted light gray and royal blue. Time magazine reported it as swashbuckling" and as symbolizing the newera in railroading."The de was virtually copied by Raymond Loewy for his 1939 S-1 locomotive. Unfortunately, the Royal Blue line only ran as far as Jersey City, and passengers had to be transported to Manhattan by White Motor Company buses, which Kuhler also designed.

Despite all the streamlined shrouds designed for steam locomotives, the days of steam engines were numbered. Diesel power would replace it. In May 1937, as opening ceremonies for San Francisco's Golden Gate Bridge transpired, the flagship of the Acheson, Topeka, & Santa Fe Railway, the Super Chief, began regular service between Chicago and Los Angeles. Departing Los Angeles at 7:30 a.m., it reached Chicago in 36 hours and 49 minutes, setting a record by averaging 60 mph, often reaching 100 mph. Called the "Train of the Stars" because of the many celebrities from Hollywood it carried, it had been under development since 1935, when General Motors' Electro-Motive Corporation (EMC) delivered two diesel-electric units, not much more than boxes on wheels. Just before regular service was initiated, the heavyweight standard cars were replaced with lightweight stainless-steel cars built by the Budd Company in Philadelphia, including a dome-topped observation deck in one.

The streamlined engine was distinguished by its raised, forward-facing operating cab, with split windows similar to an automobile or airliner. This was not unusual, considering that the General Motors Art and Colour Section department under Harley Earl executed the styling. A dramatic "warbonnet" color scheme of a red nose and cab with a bright yellow cartouche with Santa Fe logo was styled by Leland Knickerbocker from the GM Art and Colour Section. Philadelphia architects Paul Cret and his partner, John Harbeson, designed the Budd Company car interiors. The Super Chief would operate until Amtrak would take over the nation's passenger service in May 1971.

Raymond Loewy in 1937 considered himself an expert in locomotive design, and at the height of his involvement with the Pennsylvania Railroad, he published a 7.5 x 10 inch book with 125 illustrations, The New Vision Locomotive, showing the aesthetics of locomotive evolution, and including photos of his K-4 design as it was evolving into the S-1. That year, Loewy also designed a less dramatic cream separator for International, and a Purma Special camera for Purma Cameras, Ltd., an English company.
