Peter Müller-Munk, who had headed the Carnegie Tech degree program in industrial desig since 1935, now also began to practice professinally in the open market by desning an electric, two-speed kitchen Blendor (with an o) under the Waring name in 1937. The liquidblendindevice, invented by Frederick J. Osius, was name afterts fnancial backr, pron bandleader Fred Waring (1900-1984), and was introduced at the National Restaurant Show in 1937 for bartender use, but it would soon become a popular home appliance. Müller-Munk's design, called the Miracle Mixer, was classic Art Deco, the base of the blender a cylindrical aleBy 3Müulzehe

Müller-Munk Associates (PMMA), in Pittsbugh,hianywnustrial design students and graduates from the Carnegie Tech program.

Another classic appliance design was introduced in 1937, the KitchenAid Model K stand mixer, designed by Egmont Arens for the Hobart Manufacturing Company. Arens had designed previous mixers and coffee grinders for Hobart, but this design of the Model K, except for minor cosmetic changes, would remain relatively unchanged to the present day, a success story exceeding 75 years. Another classic designed that year was the Model XXX (30) tank vacuum cleaner designed in a mod-

![](images/44b72533db2dc19c8641333ecc77a5f390bedff35013e20be9663c197f14b9af.jpg)  
Electrolux Model 30, 1937, by Lurelle Guild (Patent No. D106,662, U.S. Patent Office).

enistic style by Lurelle Guild for Electrolux. Millions would be sold between 137 and 1954, the longest run of any vacuum cleaner design of the era, at a retail price of \$69.95.

The long-term success of many product designs of the 1930s seems to conflict with the concept described in 1932 by Elmo Calkins of "planned obsolescence," which predicted that annual design changes spur consumer spending. But the longevity of certain product designs can be explained by several reasons. First, while many consumers are eager to obtain newer versions of a product, there are also some who are not and who will keep and use a favored product until it expires mechanically.

Second, many new, industrial-designed products, for the first time in the 1930s, acquired a shape, style, and configuration that seemed right"for its assigned task, establishing what is called a "typeform," that is, a form that seemed to many the ideal for a particular type, or category, of product. Other manufacturing competitors, noting the popular success of a particular brand, would imitate the design, therefore increasing public familiarity and acceptance of the standard typeform until some substantially different, more popular, configuration replaced it as the new typeform for that particular product. The KitchenAid mixer survives because it is regarded as the ideal typeform for this particular category of products.

The power of historically successful designs is illustrated by the automotive industry, which has had dozens of different, successive typeforms over the years, but would remain nostalgic about car designs of the 1930s, which produced the first designs that were truly modern. Detroit in the late 1990s and early 2000s would develop a number of so-called retro designs, intending to recall that period, and which were intended to replicate the original typeforms. They included the (new) Volkswagen Beetle (1994), the Plymouth Prowler (1994), Chrysler's PT (Personal Transportation) Cruiser (1999), Buick's 2001 Blackhawk, Ford's 2002 Forty-Nine, and Chevrolet's HHR (Heritage High Roof) in 2006, all in 1930s styles.

But in the late 1930s, the problem was too many cars, and not enough roads. Since half of all households now owned an automobile, the U.S. road system was being challenged by an increasing number of cars on narrow, crowded roads. It was an obvious problem addressed by Norman Bel Geddes, on assignment for the J. Walter Thompson advertising agency in 1937. Based on his experience with his scale-model racetrack of the 1920s, and his war game of the early 1930s, he designed a model City of Tomorrow with expressways featured in a national advertising campaign for the Shell Oil Company. The model would be expanded to become the Futurama exhibit for General Motors at the 1939 New York World's Fair, which in turn would inspire the future federal interstate highway system. Jumping from highway systems to cocktail parties, Bel Geddes also designed a Skyscraper cocktail shaker and a Manhattan serving tray in 1937.

The automobile industry was transformed during the Depression. Car production fell from 5.6 million in 1929 to 1.4 million in 1932. Dozens of auto manufacturing brands in 1929 were reduced to 10 in 1935, which included the Big Three of Ford, Chrysler, and General Motors. GM, with five new models since 1930, led the pack and in 1936 was unionized. In 1937, GM's Art and Colour Section would change its name to the Styling Division. Harley Earl had a staff of 200 designers, cranking out annual model changes on the fleet of GM cars. Earl supervised five studios, one for each division (Cadillac, Buick, Oldsmobile, Pontiac, and Chevrolet), as well as 12 special studios.

Yet he wanted more designers, and so GM and its Fisher Body Division initiated a talent search by sponsoring and promoting a national, 1:12 scale model (one inch equals one foot) "Dream Car" design competition that awarded college scholarships to the best original student designs for a four-door sedan (later various different body styles were added). It was called the Fisher Body Craftsman's Guild, which would continue for 30 more years, encouraging a new generation of automotive designers, many of whom would end up in Detroit, and not a few in Earl's studios.

Actually, the contest was a major addition to an earlier Fisher Body Craftsman's Guild contest that was initiated in 1930, not to innovate designs, but to accurately replicate Fisher plans for a miniature scale model (1:15 to 1:18, where one inch equaled 1.25 to 1.6 feet) of a 19th-century horse-drawn Napoleonic coach, the trademark adopted by Fisher in 1923, and which appeared on all production Fisher bodies made for GM. There were two competitive categories: the Junior Division (ages 12 through 15) and the Senior Division (ages 16 through 1). Winners won prize money for a scholarship to a college of their choice: \$5,000 for each of the top two winners in each category. That was more than double what a man typically earned in a year! The Napoleonic coach contest would continue along with the new model car competition until 1940, so applicants could choose either one to enter, but the Napoleonic contest ended at that time. Students would no longer be interested in historical craftsmanship, but in futuristic cars.

In 1935, Amos Northup, automotive designer for the Murray Corporation of America, the independent Detroit auto body manufacturer, had patented his famous "shark-nose" hood design, which generally resembled its namesake. The design would be copied by most manufers in he  0 ncuding the 8 Graham, he Wilyiwet the 1936 Lincoln Zephyr, and the 1940 Studebaker, Chrysler, Plymouth, and Ford. But in 17Am Noru 8  ul sp tayhull. His designs were a powerful influence as cars in the 1930s were transformed from boxes to the sleek forms that are familiar to us today.

Most GM productin designs during the1930s were ot futuristic,dramatic or inovaiv, Earl developed a more radical prototype Buick called only the Y-Job. Like the similarly sounding devices, the Y-Job predicted the shape and style of most postwar cars.

Why the name "Y-Job"? Most experimental cars were tagged with an "X," sometimes w.u     t in the alphabet. The Y-Job was designed by Oldsmobile studio head George Snyder under ners wnta il us pu-utn doanele wos a ecvetl nfusee bo wio il boards.

Most unusual was the Y-Job's low, long sihouette with a 58-inch (less than five feet) h at  e lanhlAzcoseyapalle l crome rispe onte werhalbot fonn r enders, tem bumpers wrappe around the body corners, and a chrome "bombsight"hoodornment, which would become a Buick trademark, was the icing on the cake. The Y-Job was never shown to te u e  u r lot of public attention that way.

Some of its features would appear in the 1938 Cadillac 60 Special, designed by Bill Mitchell (19121988), who had been hired by Earl in 1935. But after the war, the 1950 Buick would look almost like a clone of the 1938 Y-Job. In 1938, Earl became a vice president of GM, and he would rule GM styling for the next 20 years. In 2001, Buick would recreate the Y-Job with modern advancements called the Buick Blackhawk, drawing extensively from the Y-Job and the 1939 Buick. It was entirely handmade, cost \$475,000, and would be featured in the 2003 film Bad Boys II.

On June 15, 1938, Dreyfuss saw the introduction of his design oflocomotive shrouds for the New York Central Railroad's 20th Century Limited, which was a refinement of his Mercury Kee eus year.Te r hiesblyo alv, p

operating parts of ten new J-3, 4-6-4 Hudson-type steam locomotives. The shrouds, similar to Loewy's K-4 for the Pennsylvania Railroad, featured bullet-nosed boiler shapes, but with a center fin that resembled ancient warrior helmets. Dreyfuss also designed the cars using blue, gray, and silver, and like the K-5, they were designed with asymmetric furnishings to avoid the tunnel look of most train interiors. Probably the most famous American train, the 20th Century Limited was an express train that operated from 1902 to 1967 between Grand Central Station in New York and LaSalle Street Station in Chicago. The new train by Dreyfuss made the trip in 16 hours.

That same year, Dreyfuss also designed Model A and Model B tractors for John Deere, a new client he had acquired in 1937. Raymond Loewy was having a banner year in 1938, the year he became an American citizen. He opened a branch office in South Bend, Indiana, to service his major client, Studebaker. To head the group, he appointed Virgil Exner (1909-1973), formerly with Harley Earl at General Motors, who had joined Loewy in 1937. Exner, born in Ann Arbor, Michigan, studied art at Notre Dame but had to drop out in 1928 due to lack of funds. He took a job as an advertising artist at an agency handling the Studebaker account. He was hired by Earl in 1933, where he became styling chief for Pontiac and designed Pontiac's famous "Silver Streak" hood ornament.

![](images/7ae5a9b3cc649011a3927dec49cf321cb6c9cb5a6054aeca7181ee28c6f8bf6c.jpg)  
Pennsylvania Railroad's 20th Century Limited, by Henry Dreyfuss.

Loewy also hired Clare Hodgman, who had started his career in the early 1930s with General Motors in the Oldsmobile studio, and who had worked for Loewy on the Sears Coldspot refrigerator project in 19321935. The first task of the group was to begin work on the 1939 Studebaker Champion, a low-cost model. They also designed a new Studebaker "Big S" logo and emblem. Other Loewy designs that year included a Metro series of light- and medium-duty trucks and vans for the International Harvester Company.

![](images/4d9fa769176cdb834a74c3775754731926d887f36c2f5db2b02b12f42d837329.jpg)  
Virgil Exner, ca. 1953.

Loewy had established a personal office in London in 1935, but now in 1938, he staffed it to function on a continuous basis, with Carl Otto (d. 1986) as head, along with Claire E. Hodgman. The new expanded office designed a logo for the British Petroleum (BP) Company, while the New York office designed the main hall for the ocean liner Panama. Hodgman worked on the Hillman Minx and the Rootes Sunbeam Talbot, and also designed conceptual racecars for the British Autocar magazine of March 1938. One of Otto's accounts was Standard Auto (Triumph).

In 1938, Brooks Stevens designed the first commercially successful steam iron for the Edmilton Company in Milwaukee, the Steam-O-Matic, which was introduced at Macy's in New York and Marshall Field's in Chicago. Viktor Schreckengost, head of the Cleveland School (now Institute) of Art, began creating stage and costume designs for Cleveland theater productions and continued to produce award-winning ceramic sculptures for Cleveland Museum of Art shows. Kem Weber was designing a line of tubular steel chairs for the Lloyd Manufacturing Company in Menominee, Michigan.

In Chicago, Ann Swainson, head of Montgomery Ward's Bureau of Design, designed a plastic radio cabinet, and her staff designed special china called Cascade, which was exhibited at the large-scale exhibition on modern design at the Metropolitan Museum of Art, and which became one of Montgomery Ward's most popular patterns. Swainson had traveled in 1937 to her native Sweden, visiting factories, department stores, and art schools, and was impressed with the Swedih ovenent's part-tim aining idustrial dersi hools tories.

In 1938, a major corporation was now expanding its industrial design activity into all visual aspects of corporateidentity. Libbey-Owens-Ford (LOF) was formed in 1931 by the merger of Libbey-Owens Sheet Glass Company and another Toledo company, Edward Ford Plate Glass Company, which pioneered in automotive safety glass. In 1937, H. Creston Doner had been invited by LOF to leave his post with Goodrich in Akron, Ohio, to come to Toledo direct the deparent o desherestarting in 38 a postion he would hold untl 1967. He supervised the architectural and interior design operations, as well as promotional camretail stores; and the design of a new corporate image for truck fleets, building signs, business forms, and advertising materials.

Meanwhile, in 1935, Libbey Glass Manufacturing Company in Toledo had been acquired Owens-Illinos Glass Company and became  subsidiary. In , Edwin W. Fuerst was nt head he ent  Libby Gass whil  roher, Dwh us, headed the design area for bottling and packaging at Owens-Illinois. Edwin designed Greenbrier pattern stemware for Libbey.

![](images/101dbe793a1480d1a0c5278acd31eb5cb762430afee1d5999cc40887d93e17a8.jpg)  
Philco refrigerator, 1938, by Harold Van Doren (Patent No. D131,103 U.S. Patent Offce).

Elsewhere in Toledo, Harold Van Doren & Associates were also quite busy. Van Doren himself had worked as a color consultant to Libbey-Owens-Ford's Vitrolite division in 1937. But in 1938, Van Doren's office was now designing refrigerators for Philco, a Philadelphia company, and personal weighing scales and the Guardian Duplex Scale for the Toledo Scale Company.

Another industrial designer, Walter Dorwin Teague, also had clients in Toledo in 1938. He had been called in as a consultant to the Owens-Illinois Glass Company, which had acquired the Libbey Glass Company. Teague was to work with two divisions: bottling and packaging, and structural. He designed an Insulis glass construction block in a Circon pattern for the Owens-Illinois Glass Company's structural division, which then asked him to design the division's offices in the Chrysler Building in New York.

Additional design organizations began to be formed. In Chicago in July 1938, the Designer's Institute of the American Furniture Mart (DIAFM), founded in 1936 under the auspices of Lawrence H. Whiting, director of the

American Furniture Mart, met for the first time since its founding, "at the instigation of Whiting, Alfred Auerbach, and Richard F. Bach of the Metropolitan Museum of Art."10 The principal speaker this time was John Vassos, then head of design at the Radio Corporation of America (RCA) in New York.

In his address, Vassos urged that the organization divorce itself from the patronage and r euu Mar, eu  imerwor  . In, most members were furniture designers, and Vassos himself designed radios, which were often enclosed in wooden furniture. Vassos suggested the organization extend its practices beyond that of furniture design into consumer products, packaging, automotive design or any number of additional design opportunities then available.

The group, comprised of 45 cofounding members, agreed with him. Among them were certainly Alfons Bach, Leo Jiranek, Al Lambach, Ernie Swarts, Belle Kogan, and probably, although not confirmed, Lionel C. Algoren (1896-1984), Kem Weber, Donald Deskey, and Gilbert Rohde. They changed the name of the organization on the spot to the American Designers' Institute (ADI) to indicate that it was a national, not just a Chicago organization. Even though Vassos was not currently a member of the group before his presentation, the group elected him the first president of ADI, which would become incorporated in the state of Illinois in 1940. Belle Kogan was elected secretary and treasurer, and she set up meetings, often with speakers, keeping the minutes and other records. Her only help came from her own office staff. ADI also established a chapter in New York."

![](images/26f53e72f3baef14047f109957b67fbf7aa624d5e97c05c86f39b41d46c60c9c.jpg)  
ADI logo, 1938.

That same year, 1938, another industrial design organization was formed, also in Chicago, which would separate itself to escape from what it saw as furniture designer domination.

# War Drums and the New York Fair

Accordnculpto/sAoanear oC designers, including himself, Dave Chapman, "Chick" Waltman, H. Creston Doner, and Jean Reinecke, formed the Chicago Society of Industrial Designers (CSID), which limited its members only to the design of products for mass production, a much more attractive and permissive option to many industrial designers, who were in the process of trying to define their scope and marketability to industry in the broadest manner possible. Prior to and during this period, noted sculptor Iannelli had established Iannelli Studios in Chicago, and in addition to his sculpture projects, he designed consumer products, such as mixers, hair dryers, knife sharpeners, and hair clippers, for the John Oster Manufacturing Company, Sunbeam Electric Company, Wahl Eversharp Company, Mueller Products, and Fairbanks Morse Stoker.

During 1938, the international situation was growing worse, almost by the day. In March, Germany annexed Austria to Hitler's Third Reich by means of the Anschluss (Connection), with little international resistance. In May, Hitler awarded "cultural prizes" to Fritz Todt for the Autobahn (Auto Way), the first "super highway," of which 3,000 kilometers had already been completed; to Ernest Heinkel for his He 1l bomber design; to Wilhelm Messerschmitt for his Me 109 fighter plane design; and to Dr. Ferdinand Porsche for his design of the new Type 38 Kraft durch Freude (Strength through Joy) or KdF Volkswagen (People's Car). Porsche had initiated the design in 1932 as the Kleinauto (Small Car), and Hitler subsidized its development when he came to power in 1933. Thirty sample cars were built in 1937 for promotional purposes, orders were taken, and Hitler introduced a savings scheme where the public could collect stamps that would eventually pay for the car.

![](images/5230301f24ca2827a24bedb56d6350c6a08d77eebff236ef109e4f9a9530104a.jpg)  
Volkswagen Type 38, 1938.

So on May 26, 1938, witnessed by 70,000 people and 150 reporters, Hitler laid the cornerstone of the Volkswagen factory in a small village in Lower Saxony, named the car as described as above, and renamed the village KdF Stadt (KdF City). The first production was to start in September 1939, the same month that World War II would officially start. Then on July 29, 1938, Japan invaded Russia, and on September 29, neutral European nations agreed to German occupation of part of Czechoslovakia, called the Munich Agreement, which British Foreign Secretary Neville Chamberlain called "Peace in Our Time." Only a few Volkswagens were produced before World War II, and those were exclusively for Nazi dignitaries.

These dramatic events in Germany demonstrated to many that Hitler threatened world peace and that war was inevitable. To others, however, they demonstrated the incredible technological, organizational, and production capabilities of Germany. This initiated the reputation, still acclaimed today, of "German engineering." Perhaps Germans were "the Master Race," as Hitler claimed, and would lead the world out of the Depression with public works. Most people just wanted America to stay out of yet another European war, because the memories of World War I were still vivid. The U.S. economy had suffered a brief recession in early 1938, but with war already building in Europe, the economy began to recover, and by autumn, it was booming from orders from European countries for arms and war equipment.

Into this politically charged international atmosphere stepped the Museum of Modern Art (MoMA) with a highly publicized retrospective exhibition, Bauhaus 1919-1928, which ran from December 7, 1938, to January 30, 1939. As we know, numerous faculty and students of the Bauhaus had already immigrated to the United States after Hitler closed the school in 1933. The latest to arrive, in 1938, was Ludwig Mies van der Rohe, the last director of the Bauhaus, to teach design and to design new buildings in the new, MoMA-approved International Style for the Armour (later, Ilinois) Institute of Technology in Chicago.

Herbert Bayer (1900-1985), a former Bauhaus print shop master who had resigned with Gropius and Moholy-Nagy in 1928, created Nazi publicity campaigns in Germany until he came to the United States in 1938 to work with Walter and Ise Gropius in designing the MoMA exhibition. He had managed to spirit many Bauhaus artworks out of Germany. Bayer would stay here after the exhibition as a consultant to major U.S. corporations, including the Great Ideas ofWestern Man advertising series for the Container Corporation ofAmerica starting in 1946.

The Bauhaus exhibit featured the sculptures, housewares, furniture, and architectural renderings of such Bauhaus luminaries as Gropius, Mies, and Marcel Breuer. The show and catalog, edited by Bayer with Ise and Walter Gropius, downplayed the school's Expressionist beginnings and excluded the political turmoil periods under Hannes Meyer and Mies. Mies reportedly was asked to take part but declined. The names of Bauhaus artists still living in Germany were suppressed for fear of reprisals against them, and Alfred Barr, MoMA director, stopped well short of condemning Hitler in his preface to the catalog; equally concerned that the show would be viewed as anti-German and that the Bauhaus would be seen as a "Jewish-Communist abal,"he propos toinclud  satement specifyng the mall number  cly who were Jewish. Gropius apparently dissuaded him from doing this, but had to be prevailed o in retu to mit n assertin that he hool une e dirships hiel Mis had always been non-political in character."

MoMA desired to dmstratehat the Bauhaus ha already beeretablished in the United States. The "New Bauhaus: American School of Design," established in Chicago in 1937 by Bauhaus master Làszló Moholy-Nagy, would have been a perfect example to demon-hi  ps a v be p y MoA the yr be Buthe h closed in the spring of 1938 and would not reopen until the fall of 1939, although this was not known at the time. It was no longer a good example for MoMA.

Students from the Design Laboratory School, based on Bauhaus principles, nowurged MoMA to exhibit their work. By the summer of 1938, the left-leaning Federation of Architects, Eeers, Chemist, anTecicians (FAECT)ha eiati shitheol due to Depression cutbacks, and Gilbert Rohde had resigned as director. By changing its name tLabaty School  Inuil Dsn,aiarom h Ne or B tinued their participation in Popular Front activities.

To by its token inclusion of student work, tucked away in a corner of MoMA's Bauhaus exhibition, as MoMA's example of how the innovations of the Bauhaus were "gradually being introduced in the U.S."2 MoMA's Bauhaus exhibit elicited many negative reviews from critics, who found it manipulative, chaotic and gadgety. Lewis Mumford's positive column in the New Yorker was a rare exception.3

The exhibition fit perfectly with MoMA's political agenda, initiated six years earlier at i Ineaial Exhibiti tablishi he prac Eurean ritcur  e modern movement. But it also provided additional ammunition for MoMA's campaign to establish the Bauhaus as the original and "proper" source of the new profession of industrial design. If this was established, American industrial design could be described as a poor or misguided imitation of European innovation and could be "corrected" by MoMA to achieve maturity. This political objective stemmed from MoMA's basic assumptions (some would say biases) that guided its policies.

First, like virtually all museums in the United States for decades before, MoMA at that time believed that Europe was, and should be, the center and global leader of allartistic endeavors, particularly in modern art, architecture, and design. MoMA had touted the Bauhaus exhibition as modern design based on the "best of Europe," implying that America should imitate Europe. In fact, in the previous March, MoMA had exhibited the architecture and furniture designs of Finnish architect Alvar Aalto (1898-1976) to reinforce European superiority in these fields.

As a result of this Eurocentric template, European architects were regarded by MoMA as more significant than American architect Frank Lloyd Wright, even though Wright had inspired many of these same European architects nearly 30 years before and recently had designed a simple, modern Usonian ranch house community that would become a model for most U.S. postwar suburban developments. With similar logic, European Bauhaus designers were regarded by MoMA as more significant than American industrial designers, despite the fact that Americans were the first to actually design mass-produced products for mass consumption in a highly industrialized environment.

Second, MoMA, at that time, believed that architects had been, and should be, the leaders not only in architecture, but in all artifacts within a home, including furniture and accessories. Historically, this had been true for many famous architects, both in Europe and in the United States. This was certainly true at the Bauhaus as well, and since MoMA was under the control of architects, there was an assumption that because of this history, architects should rightfully be leading the new profession of industrial design. Many architects were particularly upset not only because industrial design in the United States over the previous ten years had been led, not by architects, but by advertising artists, stage designers, display designers, commercial artists, writers, and art critics, to name a few, but even worse, the leaders, such as Loewy,

Dreyfuss, and Teague, had become national celebrities and commanded incredible fees far higher than architects could hope for.

Lastly, at that time, MoMA, like many art museums, defined fine art as pure, aesthetic, individual expression, based on philosophy, theory, or rationality, and without any intent of commercial gain, mass production, or popular appeal. In fact, it was generally believed that art was good only if it defied popular tastes, traditions, and commercial interests. The Bauhaus fit this idealistic template combining artistic, counterculture, avant-garde, academic experimentation, historical context, and intellectual theory.

American designers did not fit this template. Their crassly commercial designs used popular "gimmicks" such as streamlining or skyscrapers, were acclaimed by the public through mass purchasing, and made lots of money for rich industrialists. What was there for a sophisticated, elite art museum to like? MoMA's curator of architecture and industrial art, John McAndrew, praised the Bauhaus industrial designs as 'timeless,' and "free of both modernistic and streamlined aberrations," but lambasted the designs of designers like Loewy, Dreyfuss or Bel Geddes as "not properly modern," for "imitating superficially the forms of modern art," and for "restyling almost everything so that what we owned might look old fashioned as soon as possible" (the accusation of "artificial obsolescence" surfacing).

These assumptions dominated MoMA policies for years, and put them at loggerheads with many practicing industrial designers and leaders of the profession, who believed that it was American industrial design practitioners of the Machine Age in America who defined the practical implementation, process definition, education, practice, and commercial success of industrial design in mass production and marketing. Industrial design in America was much more than aesthetic purity or 'fine art, as MoMA narrowly defined Bauhaus contributions. It was the complex and iterative process of design: the advance planning of a product to include a reasonable balance of functionality, engineering and manufacturing feasibility, user emotional wants and ergonomic needs, manufacturing costs, appropriate retail prices, suitable materials, and also an artistic and attractive visual presentation to persuade consumers to buy. Fine artists did not do this.

MoMA's claim that the Bauhaus "invented" modern industrial design was a specious one. From Christopher Dresser (18341904) in the 1870s to the Bauhaus in the 1920s, there were dozens of designers and Arts and Crafts workshops that produced individual designs that could b cllemodernin the sense of modern simplicity and style, but they were all proc by hand or in limited quantities. The Bauhaus itself, as mentioned earlier, was patterned after medieval craft guild workshops. Its so-called "invention" was based on an academic theory of Purism described by Reyner Banham, a respected international authority on architecture and design:

[Corbusier stated in Esprit Nouveau in the early 1920s that] "Purism is evidence of the Law of Mechanical Selection"  [which was] clearly intended to share the status of Darwin's Law o Natural Selection  This law f Purism] establishes that objects tend toward a type that i determined by . the ideal of maximum utility and the demands of economical production, which conforms to the laws of nature." Therefore they concluded that "objects of maximum utility and lowt pricehave simple gemetric hapes"such s cylinders shere nd cones. Tomostit  wp a, u  uc ee it wpr to be useful, and demonstrably false in its oute.

In her wors, Purism was seen as  absolute and irrefutable law  nature. Bauhaus weot,g, potn pns gas nd iehan r whic - such forms. There were no designs of complex mechanical devices having multiple parts or functions. The American machine designers of the 1930s were the first to create truly industrial designs, in collaboration with engineers, for industrial mass production and consumption, including electromechanical products at low prices for middle-class consumers and mechanical monsters such as locomotives. No other group of designers had ever accomplished this before. The terms "industrial" and "machine" were appropriate and unique adjectives for this group.

What did they think of Bauhaus design? Harold Van Doren, in his book Industrial Design in 1940, would write:

TB I l  l I believe, to acclimatize the esoteric ideas of the Bauhaus in the factual atmosphere of American industry.

The American styl criticized by MoMA in the 130s were popularly referr to a elning rt uturisiAr Modere,dernisache desi, "contemporary." Most used modern imagery that appealed to popular taste and public expectations of the times. What they were called was much less important than what they accomplished. They were new, exciting, and different from traditional designs, and together represented a uniquely American style that changed the look of the country's industrial products.

Because of this, for the first time, world industrial design leadership resided in the United States, not in Europe. This had been achieved, not by following any formal design standards, academic theories, or museum promotions, but by individual industrial designers getting down and dirty into industrial environments and determining what consumers actually wanted and needed. This was pure capitalism at work, restoring the American economy. Everyone was happy, except perhaps for MoMA and engineers.

Engineers were not all that happy with industrial designers intruding on their traditional turf in manufacturing firms. Because for decades they were the highest authority in most companies on how to design and produce practical mechanical products at the lowest possible costs, they were not pleased with the demands of industrial designers to change traditional production processes and materials or to add cosmetic costs for purely nonfunctional purposes. Many designers, including major ones like Dreyfuss and Loewy, had to fiercely battle in-house engineering cultures for their proposed design changes.

The day after the Bauhaus exhibition closed at the MoMA, on January 31, 1939, the Pennsylvania Railroad (PRR) completed the streamlined shell designed by Raymond Loewy for its S-1 class steam locomotive, the longest and heaviest rigid-frame reciprocating steam locomotive ever built, and the only one with a 6-4-4-6 wheel arrangement. The engine and tender was 140 feet long, almost half the length of a football field, and too big for many curves on PRR lines. It was intended to replace PRR's aging K-4s locomotives, designed in 1914.

Loewy had designed a streamlined shroud for the K-4s in 1936, and his design of the S-1 shroud was a refinement of that design, with its bullet-nosed cylindrical boiler with headlight at the tip, resting on a half-bullet "cowcatcher" base. The overall design was very similar to Otto Kuhler's 1937 streamlined, bullet-nosed, steam locomotive for the B&O Royal Blue line. The photos of Loewy standing by, or on, the S-1's base are among his most famous publicity poses. In a more human-scale result, Loewy's group in South Bend, Indiana, had just completed the design of the 1939 Studebaker Champion. The car was actually designed by Clare Hodgman, on Loewy's staff, who would also work on the 1940 line of Studebakers for Loewy.

As the Bauhaus exhibition ended in January 1939, America's designers were as busy as ever. Raymond Loewy designed the Farmall tractor for the International Harvester Company,

one of his famous "before-andafter" transformations of an ironwheeled contraption, looking a century old, into a clean and modern piece of farm machinery. This led to the design of a distinctive new sales center building for International Harvester, of which 1,125 would be built, and also to the design of the Tractractor caterpillar tractor.

By this time, the Barnes & Reinecke design office in Chicago had a staff that included David Painter (1913-2003), vice president in charge of design; James Teague; Fred Priess; and George Mendenhall. Reinecke had also served in 1937 as a part-time instructor at the New Bauhaus under Moholy-Nagy. Barnes & Reinecke's new client was the McGraw-Edison Electric Company, which had introduced the first "pop-up" toaster in 1926. Now Reinecke designed its Toastmaster model 1B9 for 1939 in a glittering,

![](images/c66f18ed99da6dff43f6ebad6d264600f269cd4ccefba3bc814f5141e7f80669.jpg)  
Hamilton Beach Drinkmaster, 1939, by Barnes & Reinecke (Patent No. D118,225, U.S. Patent Office).

chrome-plated steel, streamlined box. It would increase sales dramatically, be widely imitated, and serve as the typeform for toasters well into the 1960s, by which time seven million Toastmasters would have been sold. In 1980, the company would change its name to Toastmaster. Barnes & Reinecke also patented an updated version of Hamilton Beach's classic soda foun-

Russel and Mary Wright were making headlines in 1939 with the introduction of their highly successful line of ceramic dinnerware called American Modern, made by Steubenville Pottery in East Liverpool, Ohio. Wright designed the line in 1937, but production had been delayed due to the 1938 recession, during which Wright attended New York University School of Architecture. The name American Modern

Gilbert Rohde, who had resigned as director of the Design Laboratory in 140, while continuing as director of design for the Herman Miller Furniture Company, now taught an industrial design program with Donald Deskey at the New York University School of Archi-hu IRic brass tips. He would soon convince the company o discontinue production of l s period reproductions because of his belie that imitation of traditional desgn was insincereathetically. This was the first furniture company to do so.

In Toledo, Van Doren & Associates (VDA) designed a Speedway scale for Toledo Scale and a Maytag Model E Master Washer, the first to use white rather than the speckled gray and green finish common at the time. VDA had worked with engineer Thomas R. Smith to design the sleek, unified form that concealed the motor and hoses, increased capacity by 50 percent, a and Model E's appearance went essentially unchanged for 44 years, becoming one of the most succssful housholdplianc of the cuy.

Since 1936, in addition to their normal projects, many designers were deep in preparation for the New York World's Fair, scheduled to open on April 30, 1939. As the approaching fair dominated the news, Vogue magazine asked the leading industrial designers (Teague, Loewy, n Dreyfuss) tes shions  eworn n teuture.V publishe theeultn e on February 1 in an article entitled "Dress of the Future."

All the top designers would be represented at the fair. Planning had begun four years earlier. In 1935, at the height of the Depression, a group of New York City retired policemen decided to create an international exposition to lift the city and the country out of the Depression. They formed the New York World's Fair Corporation (NYWFC), elected former chief of police Grover Whalen as president, and invited a number of political and business leaders to plan, build, and organize the fair and its exhibits, with countries around the world taking part in the biggest international event since World War I. Walter Dorwin Teague was one of eight directors on the fair's Board of Design, and was not only designing four exhibits, one building, a pavilion, and a diorama for the fair, but had a full plate of consulting work.

One of Teague's projects in 1939 was designing glassware for Libbey Glass in Toledo, the design specifically to be used in the State Dining Room at the New York World's Fair. Teague designed a series of proposals, including a footed tumbler with a fluted, rectangular-shaped stem, inspired by the Ionic Greek columns on the front of the fair's Federal Building, and stemware with a rounded, futed stem and cylindrical bowl. The first became the State Dining Room design, which had the etched crest of a spread-winged American eagle surrounded with thirteen stars. One hundred and twenty place settings were produced, with ten different styles of glasses and finger bowls.

This same pattern without the etching would become a stock Embassy pattern in the Modern American line of 1940, developed with Owens-Illinois' design department head since 1935, Edwin W. Fuerst. The second design, with the rounded futed stem, would become the

Monticello pattern in the same line. That same year, Teague designed an internal grinder for the Heald Machine Tool Company, a tank vacuum cleaner for Montgomery Ward, a 620 Special for Kodak, an executive desk lamp for the Polaroid Company (designed by Frank Del Guidice),

![](images/27d3710d45b736df4a34fac1db9a898a9907ca73ea660cdda0db52374504d92b.jpg)  
1939 World's Fair brochure (courtesy Victoria Matranga).

and a grand piano for Steinway & Sons.

On April 30, 1939, the World's Fair in New York, optimistically titled The World of Tomorrow, held its grand opening attended by 206,000 people, with President Franklin Roosevelt giving the opening address. His speech was not only broadcast on radio networks but was also televised. NBC used the event to initiate regularly scheduled television broadcasts in New York City. An estimated 1,000 people viewed the Roosevelt telecast from about 200 television sets scattered throughout the New York area.

In the Radio Corporation of America (RCA) building, its theme and concept designed by John Vassos, RCA president David Sarnoff (1891-1971) in April also introduced the first consumer television sets, which were designed by Vassos and wrapped in a clear Plexiglas enclosure also designed by Vassos. Sarnoff's speech was titled Birth of an Industry." He predicted that television would one day become an important entertainment and information medium. His crystal ball was 100

u  n tube facedupward on top of a wooden cabinet with a raised, tilted mirror in back of the tube to view the picture horizontally.
