The trademark symbols ofthe fair were a 610-foot-tall Trylon spire, whic was conneced by what was then the world's longest escalator to a 180-foot-diameter sphere called the Perisphere, both gleaming white and dominating the site at Flushing Meadows. The Perisphere housed a diorama called Democracity, which depicted a model future utopian city and suburbs of 2039 on the banks of a river. The city radiated outward in widening concentric circles around a single towering skyscraper at its center. A great hydroelectric plant on the riverbank powered the city, and the surrounding, rolling countryside was full of parks and towns.

Designed by Henry Dreyfuss and Julian Everett, the diorama was viewed from above on a moving sidewalk, as if seen from an airplane at 7,000 feet, while a multi-image slide presentation was projected on the interior surface of the sphere, serving as a movie screen as large as an acre. In the diorama, miniature cars entered and left the model city via high-speed roads that connected to beautiful green towns clustered around factories where they worked. As night fell, the domed sky grew dark and stars came out, like a real night sky, and lights came on in the model city. Then a thousand-voice chorus was heard, and images of a dozen different groups of people (farmers, miners, engineers, etc.) were projected on the dome. The images repeatedly dissolved and were replaced by larger images, as if people were converging on Democracity. When they had arrived, shimmering light rose from the horizon to engulf the entire dome, then faded and the show was over. After the presentation, visitors exited the Perisphere on a 950-foot-long Helicline, an elevated spiral ramp that partially encircled the sphere threequarters of the way around on the outside.8

The message for the future was clear. People would no longer have to live in a city. They would live in the countryside or garden apartments, around the city. Factory workers would live in green towns just like executives and white-collar workers. Democracity was a vision of modern suburbs and planned communities. Henry Dreyfuss Associates (HDA) also designed the fair's administration building and the AT&T exhibit, which featured "Pedro the Vodar," an early voice synthesizer robot.

The fair was divided into differently themed "zones," such as the Transportation Zone, the Communication and Business Systems Zone, the Food Zone, the Government Zone, the Community Interests Zone, the Production and Distribution Zone, etc. Different wall colors and colored lighting distinguished zones from each other. The Transportation Zone included the General Motors (GM) pavilion that featured a 36,000-square-foot Futurama exhibit designed by Norman Bel Geddes in the Highways and Horizons building. It was a diorama of a section of the United States, comprised of miniature highways, towns, waterways, 500,000 individually designed homes, 50,000 miniature vehicles, and a million miniature trees. Incredible details, such as "working" waterfalls, low clouds fashioned with chemical vapors clinging to mountainsides, clotheslines, and cow patties that many visitors never noticed, created a spectacular overall effect. Bel Geddes had perfected the diorama from his 1937 City of Tomorrow exhibit for Shell Oil, and from his earlier models of horse racing tracks and war games in the 1920s and early 1930s, described earlier.

![](images/756358766fd0c34c9f580dabac05d5a161546a9d8834b42b710fceb63abcb3f8.jpg)  
World's Fair map.

Futurama was by far the most popular exhibit of the fair, with long, winding waiting lines in front of the enormous, high, narrow cleft flanked by a huge silver "G" and "M" on the modernistic, silver, GM pavilion shaped like a long, low triangle. Upon entering the bright red cleft, visitors descended a ramp to a darkened room with a large map of the United States on the far wall, and a narrator gave a talk about the country's overburdened road system, then announced: "General Motors invites you on a tour of future America. The moving chairs below the map will transport you into 1960."

Each chair had its own piped-in sound track with music and narration. The train ofchairs was more than 1,500 feet long. The chairs moved smoothly as they floated on a rising and f delivered the narration in perfect sync with the passing scenery. How was this done without computers or audiotapes? At the time, there were only two ways to record and play sound: vinyl phonograph records played on a turntable, or sound recorded on movie film.

At Futurama, this feat was accomplished by something called a Polyrhetor, developed by Westinghouse: a 20-ton, 12-foot-high cylinder holding 22 bands of movie film that recorded and played only sound, each individually triggered by the moving cars on a specific segment of chair track. At the end of the ride, the last you saw of the diorama was a typical city street e o w a ni M cm

T  alaeTe arriving by airplane was especially powerful for the two-thirds or more of the public who had vBGe ts, c e abuildig the Natinal DiyCompany, he exhibit nd buildigthe Wiln Pckig Compny, anheCrystal LasTheatr e below Durig  B Gee aloe afull line f radios o the Majestic Radio and Television Company; radios for the Emerson Radio and Phonograph Corporation; and prepared, at President Frankln Roosevelt's request, a program for establishing a National Motorway Planning Authority.0

Next to the GM building was the Ford pavilon, designed by Walter Dorwin Teague. By this time, Teague's offce had grown to 5 eployees, including architects, egineers, rts and industrial designers. The Ford pavilion featured a spiral roadway, like a coil of rope with wider coils at the bottom, with cars zooming up and down on part of the "Roadway of Tomorrow." Movig into the ntrance hal a large, round room, there was avast mural ianges, in and out, a three-dimensional spark plug, factory production lines, crisscrossing conveyor belts, and smokestacks of the Ford River Rouge factory. Henry Ford's first engine and first car were displayed reverently. The first Ford Mercury was introduced, designed by chief designer BoGreDuer   al  ase were drawn of Mercury, Ford, and Lincoln by Tucker Madawick (1917-2006), a newly hired Ford apprentice designer who had just graduated in Pratt Institute's first class of industrial designers.

In the next room was an immense wedding cake turntable displaying the "Ford Cycle of Production," a round white turntable, 30 feet high and 100 feet in diameter, with stepped sides, topped by a platform on which three blue Fords were mounted in a triangle, nose to tail, canted outward toward viewers. The slopes of the turntable were covered with small, colorful, moving models of stylized men performing the jobs that contributed to the finished product on top. They mined and harvested materials, refined and finished them, made parts and assembled cars. The turntable floated on pontoons on a concealed lake and was rotated with motors.

On the "Road of Tomorrow," also designed by Teague, visitors rode in late-model Fords on the tight loops of the coiled, spiral ramp under a succession of flying buttresses on a wide half circle on the outer edge of the hall that housed the turntable, tunneled straight through the entrance hall, and looped back down the spiral again. The spiral ramps were intended to show how traffic could be lifted to the express level and descend to street level in the heart of a city without wasting space. The private lounges Teague designed for the Ford building were often described as the most elegant interiors at the fair."

Teague designed a number of additional exhibits for the fair, including the interior for the DuPont exhibit, which had a large sign: "From Miscellaneous Ores & Inorganic Raw Materials, Chemistry Makes ." The multiple answers included the introduction of nylon, primarily to make women's stockings, which excited at least half of the population in the country. Teague also designed exhibits for the Kodak Company, as well as buildings for the U.S. government and U.S. Steel, the latter appearing to some as a big blue helmet or a steel hemisphere lifted off the ground and supported by curving steel ribs of some sort running along the outside. Teague claimed that the steel was too interesting to hide beneath the stucco, so the intended concealment wall was eliminated. Teague designed National Cash Register Company's (NCR) new Model 100 and created a seven-story replica of the design on top of the NCR building, which was also displayed at the 1939 Golden Gate International Exhibition in San Francisco, which ran concurrently with the New York Fair.12

![](images/7e0288979e26b275419dfb5bb13ef7547d5003dcc0efe9844f37c383a2a93792.jpg)  
National Cash Register Model 100, 1939, by Walter Dorwin Teague Associates (Patent No. D113,900, U.S. Patent Office).

Teague's son, W. Dorwin, who worked for his father's firm since 1934, claimed he designed the Model 100 in 1935, but explained that the rule in all the early design offices was that every desig was atributed to the head o the offe, regardless of whoactually created it. The Teague office was no exception. The explanation given for this practice by Teague was, "clients are p  us    il hi cheated." Both father and son's names are on the patent.

Teague designe anothermasterpiecdiorama, the largest i the worl, which thrilled thousands and was located in the Con Edison building. Called the "City of Light," it was locat along the concave wall oa giganicroom where peole stood in  central viewig well t wa thehow. Unli hr iams, it wasviweo the oun, but om e, showing all of New York City, centering on the towers of Manhattan. Buildings to the rear and the ones in front were freestanding. The Empire State Building was 22 feet high. At the - oudTef w reak  at c w he wo y compressed into perhaps a dozen feet.

There was much activity in the model city, with trains moving, cars crossing bridges, and elevators moving up and down in the towers. During the show, there was a thunderstor with realistic light and sound effects, and as it darkened, all the countless tiny lights came on and the buildings turned deep blue. When the stor ended a peaceful evening fell and still more lights came on. It was a magically beautiful sight. 3

Gone with the Wind won the Academy Award for best picture that year, but fairgoers who saw The Wizard of Oz found Emerald City to be a familiar city of the future, just a bit more modern than the many model cities they saw at the fair.

Behind the GM and Ford buildings was the Chrysler Motors Exhibit building, designed by Raymond Loewy. Chrysler's building housed the Transportation Zone's focal exhibit, which featured Loewy's Rocketport exhibit. A futuristic rocket, shot out of a giant gun, would take passengers to London. Every twenty minutes, visitors were admitted to a semicircular area, in total darkness except for the spotlighted rocket on its launchpad. There were blinking lights, white and colored, along with a deep rhythmic sound, the whirling of motors, compressors, and high-frequency sound waves, conveying a sense of activity and suspense.

Then the sound of sirens: Liftoff! A blinding flash of hundreds of strobe lights and the roar of compressed air suddenly released conveyed the optical illusion of the rocket disappearing overhead into the blackness. Then total silence while a gradually diminishing point of light faded away into the stars.4 Public statements by the fair's science director acknowledged that "rocket flight is at present impossible, but the problem is almost wholly limited to the invention of a proper fuel."is Within five years, rockets would in fact be raining on London, not from New York, but from Hitler's rocket bases in Germany. In the building, Loewy also presented models and drawings of "the Automobile of the Future," "the Taxicab of the Future" (an electricpowered, three-wheeled vehicle), and "the Motor Coach of the future." Loewy also designed the "House of Jewels" interior at another part of the fair.

The railroad exhibits in the Transportation Zone included Loewy's huge Pennsylvania Railroad S-1 locomotive and tender, absolutely stationary, but screaming at full 60 mph speed on special rollers beneath its wheels during the entire fair. At the fair's "Railroads on Parade" extravaganza, the S-1 met face to face with Henry Dreyfuss's 20th Century Limited, pitting against each other the two most dramatic, streamlined, steam locomotives in the world, and their two most famous industrial designers.

In the Production and Distribution Zone was a focal exhibit designed by Egmont Arens that had a large, concave movie screen showing simple, everyday activities, such as ordering food in a shop, and then all around it were scenes of the far-flung businesses upon which this simple act depended. More interesting was a display showing boxes devoted to each of the main categories of consumer spending; food, clothing, housing, health, etc. The government decided on four income levels: "Subsistence," a family income of \$800 per year; "Maintenance, "\$2,000; "the Good Life," \$2,500; and "Luxury," \$5,000. The average of these categories was \$1,266, far below the "Good Life." In 2013 dollars, these arbitrary income levels would be \$13,400, \$33,500, \$41,900, \$83,900, and \$21,200, respectively."6 The claim in 1939 was that a third of the country makes less than "Subsistence," and nine-tenths, less than the "Good Life." In 2013, the median income, about \$51,413, would be considered well above the "Good Life" using 1939 standards.

The focal exhibit for the Community Interests Zone, designed by Gilbert Rohde, made the point that people depend much more on each other than in the past. In a huge room with a semicircular wall, a series of scenes were arranged before it. One by one, each scene was lit up chronologically, and a narrator intoned, Scene 1, 1789: "No leisure, no time to play. Eight hours to sleep and sixteen to work." The last scene was 1939: "Eight hours' work, eight hours' rest, eight hours' leisure." The lights fade and a spotlight picks out the words, "For What?" An electric phonograph, according to Rohde, controlled the entire timing of the presentation.17 Rohde also designed the exhibit for the Home Furnishings pavilion. That same year, the Herman Miller Company, for which Rohde headed design, pioneered in the use of showrooms for controlled furniture selling in New York and Chicago.

Russel Wright designed the focal exhibit for the Food Zone, which was by general agreement the most entertaining of the focal exhibits. The climax of the exhibit was a sixty-foot egg, inside of which was an avocado with five glowing jewels in its skin: a flock of lobsters flying inland from the sea, an aqueduct spilling roses into the desert, an eye blinking within a cave, and a can holding a clock racing backward. The jewels in the avocado symbolized carbohydrates, proteins, fats, vitamins and minerals. The lobsters signified that inlanders now eat fresh seafood. The blinking eye reminded all of "man's victory over night blindness through vitamin A foods." The aqueduct meant that irrigation made wastelands flourish, and the backward clock illustrated that "canning had perpetuated harvest time."18

The amusement area of the fair had a carnival-like feel that included pornographic exhibits, which were ignored by many attendees in favor of the showmanship in the main areas, but still were quite popular. One of these was the Crystal Lassies show, claimed at the ticket booth to be "The Lovliest Dancing Girls at the Fair." None other than Norman Bel Geddes designed the show. A nude manikin stood on a pedestal outside, and behind her the words, Ine faces were completely mirrored. Two spectators could stand at each facet of the octagon and peep through one-way mirrors at the many dancers evidently cavorting within. The dancer's costume varied at different shows, but at times she was completely naked. Bel Geddes was proabyaigthatell adal skill aic e.

Germany and China were the only major countries not represented in the Government Zone at the fair. Germany was probably not invited, and China was being invaded by Japan. offerings to fairgoers, many  whom had never travele overseas.

Du o alli8 he Nazi, Czeosovaks pavilon was t nshe. The intenational stuatin becme much wore during he   Spteber , whn the Nazis invaded Poland, officially starting World War II. By September 3, Great Britain and France would declare war on Germany. The Soviet Union would sign a nonaggression agreement with

Germany in August and on September would invade Poland from the East. The Polish pavilion would be closed when the fair reopened in 1940. On September 27, 1939, general admission to the fair was reduced to 50 cents, initially just for October, but that price would be continued for all of 1940. On October 31, the fair closed for the season under the dark clouds of war. Twenty-six million attended that first year, but the fair was \$23 million in the red.

The fair had closed, but its influence would last for ten years, when many of its predictions of the future would become the reality. Meanwhile, the Depression had become a thing of the past, and business, including the design business, continued to grow. Its leaders, the original pioneers, were becoming household words, and they were writing books about design.

# Chapter 9

# Organic Design and World War II

Lord & Taylor department store held a design competition in 1939 and presented \$1,000 awards to Henry Dreyfuss, for "a washing machine elegant enough for a drawing room," and to Raymond Patten for an electric range "as sightly as a grand piano," according to the New York Times. Harley Earl started a styling school at General Motors in 1939, attracting designers interested in automotive work. Among the students accepted were Elwood P. Engel, Joseph Oros, George Walker, and Eugene Bordinat, all of whom became important car designers and executives after the war. Their pay while attending the school was \$75 per month.

In Chicago, Sunbeam introduced its streamlined/modern-inspired C-20 coffeemaker and T9 electric toaster designed by Alfonso Iannelli as their "newest, most innovative products" in honor of the New York World's Fair. In 1940, Iannelli would end his collaboration with Sunbeam, for whom he had worked for ten years.

During the New York World's Fair, the Laboratory School of Industrial Design had published A Design Students' Guide to the New York World's Fair, supervised by a new faculty member, Paul Rand (1914-1996), who would become a famous graphic designer. The Guide studiously ignored the work of Bel Geddes, Dreyfuss, Loewy, Teague, and other mainstream industrial designers. Instead, students scoured the fair for the few functionalist architecture or designs scattered in obscure corners. MoMA curator McAndrew, who had recently joined the Laboratory's Board of Trustees, wrote an introduction to the guide praising the students for finding "good modern design among the pseudo-modern fantasies" and admonished visitors to be wary of the "soft corners and fungoid bulges on the buildings by some of our most celebrated industrial designers." It sounded like sour grapes from the jealous MoMA crowd.

Still struggling with funding in August 1939, the Laboratory School initiated a fundraising drive with which to pay their new director, respected industrial designer George Sakier; improve salaries to staff; purchase new shop equipment; and relocate to larger quarters. A reception and exhibition was held on October 12, the same day Sakier assumed his post. The e  fn u  h wasauctione ofin  court-ordred foreosure saleAbout 50students andfaculty cntiu to meet voluntarily, but by May 1940, the demise of the school would be formally announced.

That April, Germany had invaded Denmark and Norway, followed by invasions of the Netherlands, Belgium, Luxembourg, and France in May, the same month the New York World's Fair reopened, wih many changes as a result of the war now raging in Europe. In June, e Soviet Unionoccupied the Baltic States. By July, the Battle of Britain had begun, as the Luftwaffe initiated the bombing of London. Ten exhibiting nations had declined invitations to remain, including the Soviet Union, which had just invaded Finland, but Finland, a Soviet satellite, stayed. "Zones" were dropped from the literature, and "For Peace and Freedom" became the new slogan.

Theument are was mphasizes "he Grea Whi Way,"atrigusial show called American Jubilee on a 270-foot-diameter, circular revolving platform. It was a sort of historical pageant. The Consumers' Building became the "World of Fashion." However, the on October 27, "amid war and crisis," according to Life magazine. Attendance was half a million ocsig ay  cobnatin a carnial  amelanoly mo. Lastycow pp out signs and picked fowers for souvenirs. Nineteen million more people attended than in 139, but after two years, the fair was still in the red.

About this time, Raymond Loewy received a cablegram from his English friends, the Ashley Havindens, who were nduring he terribe bombingin London. The aske he could kee ther to you hildren, Venise, 10 anMichael, r e urationhe war. Lowy agreed, met the children at La Guardia Field, and sent them to school in Long Island. They returned home in 1944.3

Thre ntal er wroe bok Wal Dor Ta wroDesg This Day: The Technique for Order in the Machine Age. In it, he explain,

We shall begin with the first source of all form, which is also an incessant variable. And this is the source of fitness, which is divided into three functions: Fitness of function, fitness of materials, and fitness of techniques. It is because of this principle of fitness that the ultimate form of an object—natural or man-made—is inherent in the object itself. It must be involved naturally to suit the function which the object is intended to perform, the material out of which it is made, and the methods used in its making.4

Teague recognized the interdisciplinary nature of problem solving, stating, "We see that we are not building little gadgets. We are building an environment, and we designers have to work with scientists, engineers, technologists, sociologists, and economists." He was trying to correct the view that many held about industrial designers: that they were egotistical, independent, and obsessed with personal aggrandizement.

Norman Bel Geddes wrote his second book, Magic Motorways, using materials from his design of Futurama in the General Motors pavilion of the World's Fair, which, as you recall, was based on Geddes's 1937 City of Tomorrow exhibit for Shell Oil, as well as his futuristic concepts in his 1932 book, Horizons. These high-speed road concepts had been evolving since 1908, and the Automobile-Verkehrs und Übungsstrasse (Automobile Traffic and Training Road, or AVUS) implemented in Germany in 1921 was the first controlled-access road in Europe, which was also used as a racetrack. Nazis built the more extensive Autobahn starting in 1932. As auto traffic increased in prewar America, these concepts had become influential in the development of U.S. freeways and postwar interstate systems. In fact, both the Pasadena Freeway and the Pennsylvania Turnpike, the earliest such major systems constructed in the United States, were both opened in 1940.

The third book in 1940 was by Harold Van Doren, who wrote Industrial Design: A Practical Guide. It was essentially a how-to book on how to become an industrial designer, with detailed illustrations, specifics of presentation, model making, basic principles of design, educational programs, sketching and rendering techniques, mechanical drawing, design patents, and working with clients. In short, it was a textbook for students of industrial design. Van Doren was the first to formulate a good definition of industrial design:

Industrial design is concerned with three-dimensional products or machines, made only by modern production methods. Its purpose is to enhance their desirability in the eyes of the purchaser through increased convenience and better adaptability of form to function; through a shrewd knowledge of consumer psychology; and through the aesthetic appeal of form, color, and texture.

Van Doren also recognized that designers needed to suppress their egotism and celebrity status to blend in with industry:

Once the industrial designer had made a dent on industry, it was perhaps natural for him to exaggerate his own importance to the scheme of things. Indeed, one might almost say that he would not be a good designer unless he had that sort of excited enthusiasm that makes salesmen sell and designers create. But in sober moments, he must have realized that, important as his contribution might seem to him, its relative importance might not be so great. As a rule, the artist is, and should be, only one of the gears in the train that includes management, sales promotion, advertising, engineering, research—all those departments making up the complex mechanism of modern commerce.7

These designers clearly recognized that industrial design had matured into a formal profession essential to the business world and wanted to document their personal leadership in the new field. They sensed that a new era was dawning and could see the expanding opportunities ahead.

Despite, or perhaps because of, the threat of war in 1940, the Depression of the 1930s was essentially over. People were spending again, a housing boom was in progress, 80 percent of American households were wired for electricity, 66 percent owned a car, 52 percent had electric washing machines and refrigerators, and 42 percent had vacuum cleaners. But now the problem became one of inventory shortages of household goods in the face of increasing consumer demand. As industry was preparing for increases in production to meet this new demand, the Defense Department was offering lucrative defense contracts for essential military supplies and equipment. Although still neutral, America was providing allies such as Britain, China, and France with large quantities of essential war materials, and many industries shifted to war production, leaving consumer needs unfilled. Many stores were limiting sales to their regular customers only.

Although the number of industries that converted to war materials increased during 1940, many consumer product manufacturers continued to produce at record rates, which meant that industrial designers were kept busy. Peter Müller-Munk Associates designed a Silex vacuum coffemaker. Egmont Arens, along with Theodore C. Bookhart, designed a chrome, streamlined meat slicer for the Hobart Manufacturing Company. George Walker designed Eureka's vacuum Model D-171, which won a prize for beauty. Adolph "Al" Lambach was a staff designer for Admiral in Chicago, patenting a number  radio/television cabinets from 140until 1945. Gordon Florian became assistant director to Ray Patten at General Electric's Appearance Design department in Bridgeport.

Norman Bel Geddes designed the Patriot, a red, white, and blue plastic table radio for Emerson Radio and Phonograph Company. He also designed a refrigerator for the Kelvinator Corporation; interiors for the La Rue restaurant; redesigned the stage of Rockefeller Center Theater for ice skating; and designed costumes and scenery for It Happens on Ice, starring international skating star Sonja Henie.8

Brooks Stevens in 1940 had a staff of five, and 70 clients. His group designed an architectural Kuro jukebox for Chicago juke maker Gabel, and a red, streamlined research vehicle for the Johnson Wax Company in Racine as a tribute to the brightly colored office building that Frank Lloyd Wright had recently created for Johnson. In 1941, Stevens would design the streamlined appearance.

Henry Drefu Associates (HA) had esiged the clas Bi Ben alarm clocko-He also designed the Hoover Company's Model 60 upright vacuum cleaner.

Barnes & Reinecke, working with Minnesota Mining & Manufacturing Company (3M), as well as a cast-iron desktop tape dispenser. In 1940,

Reinecke, then 39, was included in a feature article, Today's Young Men," that interviewed 70 men who achieved notable success while still in their 20s and 30s.

Robert Davol Budlong continued as design consultant to Zenith, designing many of Zenith's prewar radios, which would include the Universal Portable AC/DC radio with batteries in 1940, the Poket radio in 1941, and the Transoceanic Clipper radio in 1942. Zenith tried several times to offer him a full-time job as a Zenith employee, and as head of an internal industrial design group, but he wished to remain independent so that he could service other clients. However, he did relocate his office to the building on 333 Michigan Avenue that housed Zenith's showrooms.

![](images/f0fe3687f7f6bf01fa9a4367d91a1f69d882d3a294253c8e5ee948fae8d6ced0.jpg)  
Scotch tape dispenser, 1940, by Barnes & Reinecke (Patent No. D118,629, U.S. Patent Office).

At the Ford Motor Company, Bob Gregorie (and staff that included Tucker Madawick) designed a new Lincoln Zephyr Continental Cabriolet, with financial support by Edsel Ford. Edsel had conceived of it as a custom-built luxury car for himself, but its enthusiastic reviews convinced Ford to allow limited production of it in 1940. One of the most sophisticated designs of the time, it would become a classic and would win acclaim as a work of art at the Museum of Modern Art in 1951.

Donald Deskey designed a prefabricated weekend cabin called the Sportshack, with multifunctional furniture and walls of fir plywood striated mechanically to look like barn wood. It was displayed at the 1940 Metropolitan Museum of Art's annual series, Contemporary Industrial Art, with room displays created by architects and designers. Deskey would patent the specially treated plywood in 1942 and name it Weldtex. In about 1943, Deskey would found Donald Deskey Associates in New York, specializing in package design, which would operate until about 1975.

![](images/1d8cb1552c96578bea6a36f91d7eb006bd89dfae9f711dd31158b0b3c54aeede.jpg)  
Lincoln Continental, 1940, by Eugene "Bob" Gregorie.

In March 1940, Loewy was visited in his office by George Washington Hill, president of the American Tobacco Company, who bet him he couldn't design a better package for Lucky Strike cigarettes, which was essentially a red target disk on one side with "Lucky Strike" on it, against a background of dark green. Loewy bet him he could in the amount of \$50,000. In 1942, Loewy's new package design would be introduced, which repeated the red target on two sides, and replaced the green background with pure, shiny white. Loewy had won the bet. Lucky Strike explained the change with the public slogan, "Lucky Strike green goes to war." Raymond Loewy in 1940 also designed the Electrolux L300 refrigerator and was working on Greyhound bus designs.

![](images/1bbe62e378d156467d87d60c74acaa3b76f465ca0030f9bf88d3f30706dabda1.jpg)  
Greyhound bus, 1940, by Raymond Loewy (Patent No. D113,009, U.S. Patent Office).

Jo Sinel was still in New York freelancing for advertising agencies, designing calculators for Marchand and typewriters for Remington, as well as numerous design projects in packaging, interiors, and publications. He also taught industrial design courses at the Pratt Institute. But in 1940 he returned to California and would teach in a number of colleges, primarily at the California College of Arts and Crafts in San Francisco, where he had taught in the early 1920s— this time to teach a program along with package designer Walter Landor (1913-1995). Sinel, who reveled in variety and wanderlust, would also teach at other colleges in California, including Chouinard in Los Angeles, Claremont College in Claremont, and the Rudolph Schaffer School of Design in San Francisco.

Design schools were continuing to develop new programs in industrial design. Syracuse University sought the advice of Norman Bel Geddes, Lurelle Guild, and Henry Dreyfuss, and reul vnn rDeInds o "Inu De. Hin Bredendieck had left the School of Design in Chicago to initiate and direct an industrial g program  e Georg Schol ater, Insiute TecnolgyThe Iio Inue of Technology was formed from the Armour Institute in Chicago, which had been offering a that had been established over the last six years had by 1940 graduated only several dozen designers, but these would provide the start of a second generation of postwar practitioners, the first generation with some formal university training in the new profession

Russel Wright and his wife Mary closed the home furnishings accessories business they had begun in 1935 and in 1939 formed a new company with Irving Richards called Raymor, which would continue to sell Wright products with exclusive distribution rights for five years. Their line of American Modern dinnerware had been included in the Museum of Modern Art's (MoMA) January 1940 annual exhibition, Useful Products under \$10. Inspired by the W tiated the American Way, a nationwie craft, design, and merchandising consortium f 100 designers to promote low-cost American style todustry, nAmericanfurshings  consumers. The American Way was launched at a highly promoted event in December 1940 at Macy's in New York, featuring First Lady Eleanor Roosevelt. That November, her husband, Franklin, had been elete for thr term  preident, violating the nwritten traditin the two-term limit. His radio fireside chat in December urged the country to become the Arsenal  Democracy" to support Britan, France, and Chia.Due tohe growig publc preoccupation with the war, the American Way would be abandoned within a year.

TheMMl tll y i tenw weletablishefelustrilde. In ceateparate epar of industrial design, headed by its first director, architect Eliot Noyes (1910-1977). Walter Gropius had recommended Noyes to MoMA, since he had studied architecture under Gropius at Harvard and worked for him after graduation. Noyes was born in Boston, and while a student, he worked as a curator at MoMA.

That same year, Edgar J. Kaufmann, Jr. (19101989), wrote to Alfred Barr director ofMoMA, proposing an Organic Design in Home Furnishing Competition. Kaufmann had been a resident apprentice in architecture at Frank Lloyd Wright's Taliesin East School and Studio from 1933 to 1934, and his father, Edgar J. Kaufmann, a wealthy businessman and owner of Kaufmann's Department Store in Pittsburgh, had commissioned Wright to design Fallingwater, Wright's famous 1936 modern house built over Bear Run Creek in Fayette County, Pennsylvania.

Kaufmann's proposal fit MoMA's strategy perfectly on a number of levels, and in fact was highly influential in determining the postwar direction of furniture design, and eventually the entire field of industrial design. First, MoMA saw furniture as a field in general that, except for Herman Miller, had yet to be dominated by modern design, particularly modern American design by pioneer industrial designers, as had the automotive and appliance fields. Most furniture makers were still imitating historical period styles, such as Colonial, Chippendale, Queen Anne, Arts and Crafts, etc. So if MoMA could establish and promote modern design standards for furniture, it could claim leadership in a category traditionally viewed as the historical province of architects.

Secondly, the use of the term "organic" was distinctly an architectural term used almost exclusively since 1908 by Frank Lloyd Wright, America's most famous architect, for his own work. It was derived from his mentor, Louis Sullivan (18561915), whose slogan "form follows function" became the mantra of modern architecture. Wright changed the phrase to "form and function are one," using nature as the best example of this integration. In his 1939 book, An Organic Architecture: The Architecture of Democracy, Wright wrote, "So here I stand before you preaching organic architecture to be the modern ideal .. determining form by way of the nature of materials."

But since 1932, when the Museum of Modern Art promoted the new International Style based on European architects, Wright was regarded by many U.S. architects as an old-school, irascible, irrelevant has-been. So it was somewhat ironic that MoMA would use this term at all for this competition. However, the term served MoMA's purpose of relabeling 'modern design' with an obvious architectural term, rather than "streamlining," a common term that had been associated with American industrial designers during most of the 1930s, and which MoMA had repeatedly denigrated as "modernistic and streamlined aberrations."

In any event, the exhibition, Organic Design in Home Furnishings, ran from September to November 194l at the Museum of Modern Art. Noyes organized and directed the competition and its terms, which included Noyes's definition of "organic design":

A design may be called organic when there is an harmonious organization of the parts within the whole, according to structure, material, and purpose. Within this definition there can be no vain ornamentation or superfluity, but the part of beauty is none the less great—in ideal choice of matial, ivial t, n eal nhir

One can almost read "streamlining" in place of"vain ornamentation or superfluity." According to Noyes, "The purpose of the contest was to discover good designers and engage them in the task of creating a better environment for today's living." Noyes defined design implicitly as teamwork, so all contestants except textile designers were to be teams of two or more designers. The competition itself was to be a business deal, a collaboration between museum, designers, and corporations. Each of the winning designers was to be awarded a production and distribution contract with a major American corporation. The exhibit and awards were to be sponsored by 12 leading department stores. In MoMA's political context of achieving architectural leadership through furniture, it would have certainly been helpful if architects, rather than industrial designers, would win such a competition. MoMA ensured that that would be the result.

As we know, a number of pioneer industrial designers over the last ten years had designed a number of acclaimed modern furniture: Norman Bel Geddes, Paul Frankl, Gilbert Rohde, John Vassos, Nathan Horwitt, Kem Weber, Donald Deskey, and Russel Wright, to name a few. But none of these were participants in the MoMA competition.

We do not know how competitors were chosen, but we do know there were 31, at least half (16) of whom were architects, based on a list in MoMA's 1941 exhibition publication, whic lte i tem  rhite copetin in urniture desg, he estnfabric, weves, prints, interiors and lamp design, as well as some Latin American designs. So it was no surprise that architects won the furniture design competition, as obviously preferred by MoMA.

The overwhelming winner was a team of architect/educators, Charles Eames and Eero Saarinen, both from the Cranbrook Academy of Art, for their designs of chairs and storage pieces. These architects and other winners were recognized at MoMA's 194l exhibition, Organic Design in Home Furnishings, and their work was displayed as well in the accompanying publication. Other competitive furniture design teams of architects were Martin Craig and Ann Hatfield, Emrich Nicholson and Joseph Maier, Oscar Stonorov and Wilhelm von Moltke, Carl Anderson and Charles Bellah, and Harry Weese and Benjamin Baldwin." After the war, Eames and Saarinen would receive contracts with Herman Miller Furniture Company and Knoll Associates, and a new era of furniture design, led by MoMA, would unfold.

In 1941, Harold Van Doren's East Coast clientele, in particular Westinghouse and Philco, required him to establish a second office in Philadelphia. Van Doren relocated there with Donald Dailey (1914-1997), leaving the Toledo office in the charge of Jack Little. Dailey soon designed a two-tone electric iron for Westinghouse. In Cleveland, Viktor Schreckengost had been designing a number of streamlined tricycles for the Murray Bicycle Company since 1938 and now designed for Murray a new concept appropriate for youngsters born in the age of the airplane: a Pedal Pursuit Plane, simulating a fighter plane. Raymond Loewy designed an electric razor for Schick and components for the 1941 Harley Davidson 74FL Knucklehead motorcycle.

Norman Bel Geddes designed a slicing machine for the Globe Slicing Machine Company a cigarettevending achine or the UNeed-Pack Company, and stapler r the Markwell Manufacturing Corporation. Based on his Futurama exhibit at the World's Fair,

![](images/c91ad18cd2e266c51c3a9daafb1f68e871c10556074c249cd18fe99e35a9c7bf.jpg)  
Norman Bel Geddes, ca. 1941 (State Archives of Florida. Photograph by Joseph Janney Steinmetz).

John RiliNort 903185RgliC  - sota, Florida, had employed Bel Geddes in 1940 to design the circus layout of the midway, tents, posters, and costumes for the 1941 Ringling Brothers and Barnum & Bailey Circus. North's intention was to update and improve the "Greatest Show on Earth. Bel Gees tintehe wdust i rigs of re white and blue;introduced modern lighting;added large-scale production numbers, and made the visual style of the circus more colorful and unified.

Joseph Platt and Marlin S. Baker designed the classic Parker 51 fountain pen, but due to wartime government restrictions on civilian pens, few were produced until after the war, when it would be produced from 1951 until 1972. It cost a quarter of a million dollars to develop, and Parker would spend \$20 million on its sales and promotion.

Platt's main career was as an interior designer and art director. He served as style director for Marshall Field's department store in Chicago and decorated sets for Broadway plays and Hollywood films such as Gone with the Wind (1939), Rebecca (1940),

and Portrait of Jenny (1948). Platt's wife, June, designed home furnishings under her husband's name, as well as continuing to write cookbooks, such as June Platt's Plain and Fancy Cookbook (1941).

Throughout 1941, the war in Europe continued to intensify. In April, the four Axis powers, Germany, Italy, Hungary and Bulgaria, invaded and dismembered Yugoslavia. Greece fell. In June, Germany invaded the Soviet Union, overran the Baltic States, and laid siege to Leningrad. In December, a Russian counteroffensive drove the Germans from Moscow suburbs into retreat. Finally, when Japan bombed Pearl Harbor on December 7, President Roosevelt declared war on Japan the next day. Within a week, Germany and its Axis partners declared war on the United States.

Now on a full war footing, Roosevelt's "Arsenal of Democracy" quickly moved into full speed. Most domestic manufacturers were overloaded with military contracts for aircraft, tanks, arms, munitions, and military equipment, essentially ceasing to produce consumer goods. The last passenger car, a Pontiac, was produced in February 1942. While General Motors was making tanks, the Styling Section under Harley Earl was designing camouflage for military vehicles and weapons during the war. Ford was making Jeeps; the Hoover Company was making bomb fuses and helmet liners; and General Electric was making gun turrets, howitzers, bazookas, and aircraft engines.

To avoid inflation, the Office of Price Administration (OPA) established price ceilings on manufactured goods still on the market, including refrigerators, irons, radios, ranges, typewriters, phonographs, and washing machines. Americans were told to "use it up, wear it out, make it do, or do without." Rationing was imposed on gasoline, shoes, butter, four, fish, canned food, coffee, cheese, sugar, and meat. New home construction was stopped to make construction materials available for the military or government purposes. People started "victory gardens" in their backyard to provide food for the table and were urged to collect metal scraps, tin cans, paper, silk, nylon, and grease for the war effort. There were no products made of steel, chromium, tin, aluminum, copper, or nickel, or even some plastics such as acrylic, nylon, or Bakelite, all of which were required for the war effort.

Many young men were enlisting and being drafted into the armed forces and being replaced in the workforce, in many cases by women. Registration for the draft had begun in the summer of 1940 for unmarried males between the ages of 18 and 45, and male enrollment in colleges decreased dramatically as many potential students entered the services, as well as many recent graduates. Thousands of men and women relocated to new "defense towns" being built near major new manufacturing plants around the country to work on production lines of war goods. Everyone was affected, including those in the industrial design profession.

In 1942, Frederick Hurten Rhead, the designer of colorful Fiesta dinnerware in 1936, passed away at age 62 while still heading design at the Homer Laughlin Company in Newell, West Virginia. Fiesta had remained popular until the war, when the government took control of all available uranium for the atomic bomb, thereby taking away the vibrant red-orange uranium oxide used by Homer Laughlin in Fiesta. Without that dramatic color, sales of Fiesta declined gradually, and it would finally be discontinued in 1973. But in 1986, on the 50th anniversary of its introduction, the line would be revived at a time when vintage design would be embraced.

The American Designers' Institute (ADI) held a conference in New York, Design in the National Emergency, and promised that its members would be available to the government and manufacturers who needed assistance to meet difficulties. Leo Jiranek chaired ADI's committee, which submitted a report to the National War Production Board (NWPB) and the Office of Price Administration (OPA) suggesting how designers could best serve the country.

Many of the younger industrial designers, who would form the second generation of industrial designers after the war, chose to enlist or were drafted into the armed services and would serve bravely on the front lines, in the air, or at sea during the war. Others would use their artistic skills in the military to design camouflage, war-related models, instrument colors, or training devices.

Typical was Arthur BecVar, then 30, who left his design job at Revere Copper and Brass to work in the Navy's Ordinance Department. Charles (then 34) and his wife Ray Eames had relocated to Los Angeles in 1941 to work for MGM, but after the war began, they contacted the Naval Hospital n San Diego o propose amolded plywoodsplint replace he padded metal splint then being used. Eames and Eero Saarinen designed it, the Navy placed an order for 5,000, and Eames formed the Plywood Wood Company to produce them in quantity. Evans Products would purchase the rights to produce them in 1943.

Although most pioneer machine designers were beyond draft age, nevertheless a few chose active military roles. Viktor Schreckengost, head of the design program at the Cleveland Institute of Art, and then 37, joined the Navy in 143 as an offier, where he worked in raar detection systems, battle models, and watercolors of war devastation. He was made head of design in the Department of Training Devices, supervising other designers. Eliot Noyes, then 3, served as a major in the Air Force

John Vassos, then 57 and head of design at the Radio Corporation of America (RCA), enlisted in the Army Air Corps in 1942 as a lieutenant. In 1944 he was reassigned to Washington, D.C., to serve with the Office of Secret Service (OSS, the forerunner to the CIA). There, he developed camouflage techniques and produced a series of war posters encouraging vigilance on the home front. His most important job was to produce training manuals for the OSS, one of which was for the underground Greek forces fighting against German occupation.

He parachuted behind Nazi lines in Greece to train and act as the U.S. liaison with the Greek underground.

O l h. Henry Dreyfuss, Walter Dorwin Teague, and Raymond Loewy were called upon to design a r e Ji Chi  Sahi kir .ry  - ment and the U.S. Navy. One room featured a world map on a curved surface, 12 feet high by 25 feet wide, and the other was equipped for the projection of movies, slides, and diagrams. On a deadline of six weeks, the furnishings were built in the loft of an old brewery building in New York and then transferred to Washington for installation in the Public Health Service Building. The project served as a prototype for the planning facilities of many postwar transportation authorities and utility companies.

Dreyfuss designed and built four rotating world globes, 13 feet in diameter, one each for President Roosevelt, Winston Churchill, Joseph Stalin, and the Joint Chiefs of Staff. Due to ar  e ow cao The Drefu  ssuheme 0- Bnze,    ui from 15 to 3/ minutes.12

Loewy's office provided assistance to the Office of Strategic Services in the Department of Visual Presentation. Other work included new ideas for the Medical and Engineering Corps, for Ordinance, and for the Quartermaster General. For the Medical Air Corps, they modified a military glider into a field hospital that could be towed into a combat area, landed, and set up for operation. They also designed tents, swamp shoes, and a first-aid kit for airborne troops. 13

Norman Bel Geddes's offce developed photo recognition training programs for the military. Using experience gained in constructing his elaborate, recreational war game of the early 1930s, he prepared war-related scale models of military operations to prepare for invasions, such as a model-map of Tunis for the Army Signal Corps. He built models simulating accurate shorelines for conducting invasion instructions for combat officers; designed an aircraft identification system for the U.S. Army; advised a New York blackout technique in greater New York for the U.S. Army; designed self-camouflage techniques for U.S. Army engineers; designed electric, radio-controlled model aircraft for Strategic Command, Army Air Force, supervising their construction at Orlando, Florida; and designed a simulated tank battle in the USSR.14

Teague's organization worked for the Naval Bureau of Ordinance and completed more than 150 projects during the war. In 1942, Teague's son, W. Dorwin Teague, decided he should be working on something more useful for the war effort than on World's Fairs and vacuum cleaners. He joined the Research Engineering Department of the Eclipse-Pioneer Division of Bendix Aviation in Teterboro, New Jersey, as a junior engineer. His first assignment was troubleshooting the Boeing B-17 bomber's cabin supercharger. Dorwin would remain at Bendix working as an engineer until he rejoined his father's firm in 1953.

Egmont Arens advised on military instrumentation colors for easy readability; instructed how colors could be used to make bomb shelters, command centers, and factories more bearable and efficient; and suggested color-coding of the wiring, hydraulic lines, and the control systems of aircraft. Buckminster Fuller developed the Dymaxion Deployment Unit (DDU), a 20-foot circular hut constructed by the Butler Company of Kansas City using corrugated metal, which was insulated and had a porthole, a door, and a domelike ceiling with a hole for ventilation. Two hundred were built in 1942 for the Army Signal Corps to serve as radar huts. Kem Weber tried to interest the government in a panelized construction system he had invented for defense-

related housing projects but could not secure a contract.

Brooks Stevens designed military products including a 1941 snowmobile for use as "motorized cavalry," a paratroopers field kit, an individual water filter, field stoves, and dehydration units. In 1942 he came up with an ingenious plan for rebuilding existing Ford and Mercury passenger cars to increase capacity from six to twelve, for use as ambu-

![](images/e55cbe15748b75d0f413dd95a694b3c783786cc0fcf2e8cb9fbf4dcd671364bf.jpg)  
Dymaxion Deployment Unit, 1942, by Buckminster Fuller (Patent No. D133,4 , U.S. Patent Office).

lances and personnel carriers for the Red Cross, defense plants, and army camps. In 1944, he collaborated with an inventor on a device for legless veterans, replacing foot pedals with a motorized steering column. Pulled back, it accelerated; pushed forward, the car braked.

In 194l, a patent disagreement between John Tjaarda and the officers of the Briggs Manufacturing Company, his employer since 1932, resulted in his employment being terminated, and many years of legal battles would follow. In 1942, Tjaarda organized his own design firm, John Tjaarda and Associates. He attempted to design, finance, and manufacture his own automobile, the Cortez, but due to lack of financing, it was unsuccessful.5

Locomotives were essential to national wartime transportation for troops and materials. Railinsily hedev as ortai In PeRTp

That same year, Loewy released several renderings of "A Conception of an Automobile of the Future" to the press, claiming to represent a "step in the direction of clean, uncluttered body styling." I 1 thentr e roesn toootude. I azie Ineriors, published since 1940 by Charles E. Whitney, initiated a section called "Industrial Design." It featured mass-manufactured products and was conceived and written by Donald Dohner, whohadjust regne s hePratt Insttuteustrial desg proram  tablish ut and packaging firm in partnership with a fellow educator at Pratt, J. Gordon Lippincott (19091998), and with the Douglas T. Sterling Company in Stamford, Connecticut.

The new firm, Dohner & Lippincott, hired its first employees: two 1943 graduates of Pratt Institute, Budd Steinhilber (b. 1924) and Read Viemeister (19231993), who illustrated many of Dohner's design concepts for his articles. Tragically, Donald Dohner, at age 51, died accidentally of carbon monoxide poisoning on Christmas Eve, 1943, and his Interiors articles were taken over by his partner, J. Gordon Lippincott. The new head of the industrial design program at Pratt would be Alexander Kostellow, with the title "supervisor of industrial design and supervisor of design and structure." Kostellow would become an influential national voice in industrial design education and enhance the reputation of Pratt as a leading design institution. Nevertheless, it was Donald Dohner, who initiated and headed the first degreed industrial design program at Carnegie Institute of Technology in 1934, and who at that time taught Kostellow, then a painter, about industrial design. It would be Dohner who would be honored in a posthumous Special Recognition Award for Dohner by the Industrial Designers Society of America (IDSA) in 1998, and who would receive a formal citation recognizing Dohner as The Father of American Industrial Design Education."

Walter Von Nessen also passed away in 1943, at age 54. After his death, his wife closed their studio, but she revived it after the war to continue production of his lamp designs, as well as innovative lamps of her own. Designer Stanley Wolf, who would purchase the business in 1954, joined her and would continue the Von Nessen tradition with his own designs, as well as designs commissioned from Elizabeth Kauffer and George Nelson in the 1960s. Today, Nessen Lighting in Mamaroneck, New York, is still producing Von Nessen's famous swing lamps of 1927, as well as a complete line of modern lighting fixtures.

By this time, the increasing advance of military operations overseas had made it clear that the war would eventually be won by the Allies, and that the invasion of Europe would soon begin. Many companies began to plan for postwar business opportunities with new products and materials. Material suppliers were at the forefront of these efforts. Durez Plastics and Chemicals, in order to stimulate consumer interest in plastics after the war, initiated a series of magazine advertisements and a promotional brochure featuring proposals of prominent industrial designers for innovative postwar products using Durez plastics, which stated,

Industry may not solve the entire problem of unemployment in the immediate period following our inevitable victory, but by being ready it can contribute to the major part of the problem. Many pre-war products are out of date. Many production methods are obsolete, Tremendous advances av bee  n

Participants included Jean Reinecke, Raymond Loewy, Egmont Arens, Peter Müller-Munk, Brooks Stevens, Dave Chapman, Carl Sundberg, Henry Dreyfuss and Donald Deskey.

![](images/c48ebca6bd1b06fe3c863c7faeac9edabab1431b242ef9cb379c05f7fde3d647.jpg)  
Peter Müller-Munk sewing machine concept, 1942, for Durez Plastics (courtesy Victoria Matranga).

Most were highly futuristic in concept. Arens proposed a cylindrical refrigerator with sterilization capabilities, an ice cube maker, and a cold water faucet. Loewy proposed chairs made from molded plywood. Dreyfuss proposed a modern aircraft passenger interior. Sundberg proposed a sleek, modern bathroom. Müller-Munk proposed a lightweight sewing machine with lustrous, integral colors. Deskey predicted push-button and cordless telephones, intercom systems, and telephone directories replaced by microfilm. Stevens proposed a Supermix tabletop mixer, a plastic car, and a vaporizer, which was built after the

Many companies and design consultants needed no encouragement to initiate postwar planning and promotion of new products. They had been waiting for this opportuny for years.

# Chapter 10

# "The Club" and Postwar

Other companies not actively engaged in defense production were offering linesof new products for business expansion after the war. The Herman Miller Furniture Company, under the direction of Gilbert Rohde, introduced its Executive Office Group, the first integrated lin o modular furniture that could be asseblein various confgurations, the forerune postwar open offce systems. Dave Chapman, Inc., advised a number of client companies in adapting to peacetime production, including Parker Pen Company, International Harvester Company, Brunswick-Balke-Collender, and Scovill Manufacturing Company. Norman Bel Geddes designed a postwar line of radio cabinets for RCA, Coca-Cola vending machines for Mills Industries, and domestic ranges for Floyd Wells Company. He also conducted development work on the Nash automobile.1

During the war, the Crane Company, a manufacturer of domestic bathroom fixtures, m e valv r he U..Navy c hi  ,00 1,lv pe hi.Be Crane had discontinued making domestic products for the duration of the war, it did not need the services of Henry Dreyfuss, its design consultant, but continued to pay his retainer so he would not work for a competitor. Dreyfuss set up a satellite office in the Crane building in Chicago to study the company's entire line. His staff chose products that should be carried over and designed new products to be introduced after the war. So when the war was over Crane would have a new line of products ready to go immediately.2

Libbey-Owens-Ford (LOF) had been challenged by National Geographic magazine to create a kitchen of the future. H. Creston Doner, head of LOF's department of design, accepted the challenge, and the result was the Kitchen of Tomorrow, built in the home of an LOF executive, which was featured prominently by major magazines and accompanied by a promotional campaign that included a short feature film by Paramount to be shown in movie theaters. Three full-sized models toured nationally for two years, starting in 1944.

The kitchen promoted the use of glass extensively, but also proposed appliances and furniture for a "peacetime revolution of convenience and comfort." The kitchen and dining area were joined in an open space arrangement, made possible by a dining table that fipped up to attach to a wall, a pass-through counter area, and a refrigerator accessible from both kitchen and dining area. The glass-front refrigerator and cabinets brought dinnerware and food into view. The traditional stovetop was replaced with a unified wall unit that included a glass-walled oven/rotisserie grill, built-in toaster, mixer/juicer/meat grinder, waffle maker, and digital clock/thermometer, as well as recessed cooking/baking vessels. Foot pedals controlled the faucet in the sink.³ In 1944, H. Creston Doner wrote Wartime Accomplishments of Libbey-Owens-Ford.

As mentioned earlier, college campuses during the war were at a low level. By 1943, there were very few male graduates or applicants, since the draft swept up many high school graduates. Some schools contributed to the war effort, such as the faculty at Pratt, by designing camouflage for military installations. Others were planning for an expected postwar resurgence and determining how to educate students of the new profession, industrial design.

The American Designers' Institute (ADI) was under the leadership of its president, Alfons Bach, and its education committee, headed by Alexander Kostellow, and with members László Moholy-Nagy and Donald Dohner had surveyed its membership and solicited the advice of John Vassos, Ben Nash, and George Kosmak (b. 1905) to develop a prototype educational program for industrial design. In 1944, ADI announced its Educational Program for Industrial Design, a four-year program combining technical, business, and academic courses. It in fact would become a template for the many new programs of industrial design that would be initiated after the war.4

In 1944, many designers were repositioning themselves for postwar business opportunities. Harold Van Doren expanded his firm, already with offices in Toledo and Philadelphia, to include a New York office, Van Doren, Nowland, & Schladermundt, which he established in collaboration with designers Roger Nowland and Peter Schladermundt. Donald Dailey, of Van Doren's office in Philadelphia, designed the familiar Boston pencil sharpener, which would be used in millions of schoolrooms over the next 50 years. J. Gordon Lippincott opened a design office in New York with Walter P. Margulies, titled Lippincott & Margulies. He hired Read Viemeister as director of styling. Donald Deskey designed a bowling center ball-return system for the Brunswick-Balke-Collender Company, which would become an integral part of hundreds of modern recreational bowling alleys that attracted families after the war.

Harley Earl established an independent styling studio in Detroit, Harley Earl, Inc., to design nonautomotive products, but continued as vice president of styling at General Motors. John "Jack" Morgan had left Sears, Roebuck in 1943 and in 1944 opened his own office, Jack Morgan Associates, in Chicago, with a smal staff. Jon Hauser (19161999) replaced Morgan at Sears. Peter Müller-Munk left his position as head of the industrial design program at the T u studio, Peter Müller-Munk Associates in Pittsburgh. Richard I. Felver, a Pittsburgh industrial designer, replaced him at CIT. John Gordon Rideout had closed his Cleveland office in 1941 but reopened it in 1943 in Chagrin Falls, Ohio, near Cleveland. In 1944, he joined with architect Ernest Payer to combine industrial design and architecture. That year, Edwin W. Fuerst, head of design at Libby Owens Glass Company, left Libby to open his own design office in Toledo, specializing in package design, which would operate until about 1952.

In February, 1944, the International Business Machines Corporation (IBM) Automatic Sequence Controlled Calculator (ASCC) was delivered to Harvard University by IBM, which had engineered, built and funded the project, but it had been conceptually designed by Harvard University's computing pioneer Howard H. Aiken (1900-1973). The ASCC used 765,000 components, 500 miles of wires, three million connections, and was 5 feet long, eight feet high, and two feet deep. It was the industry' largest eeceanical calculator nthe s oerating machine that could execute long computatins automatically. The computer was later called the Harvard Mark I. Futuristic industrial designer Norman Bel Geddes, who had been hired by IBM for \$50,000 to do the job, designed its enclosure. Aiken considered the clculator euimet. IM was ot happy because Aiken caiiselas the ole in the press release for the formal presentation to the university in August, ignoring several IBM employees who had helped build various components.

That same year, Gilbert Rohde, whileill heading design Herman MillerFuriture Company, passed away at age 50. In 1945, Herman Miller would appoint George Nelson as the cmpany' newdrector sn Nelon ha becme oaai ditr cl Forum magazine in 1943 with Henry Wright and had that year written an article, "Grass on Main Street,"in the Saturdy Eveng Post whic introduc the novaiv e  pedetran malls. In 1945, Nelson and Wright developed a unique concept of storage wall systems, an outgrowth of their joint book, Tomorrow's House, published that year. The storage wall was featured in Life magazine, attracted national attention, and led to Nelson's position at Herman Miller. As his first project as director of design, Nelson designed a collection of 70 pieces. He would become aninnovative and infuential leader in postwar modern furnishing design.

Since 1941, the membership base of the American Designers' Institute (ADI) in Chicago had been shifting to New York. In 1942, its New York chapter questioned whether the organization should still be incorporated in Illinois. The ADI board apparently agreed, for in 1943, the Illinois secretary of state dissolved the corporation for failing to file an annual report as required. In May 1944, the American Designers Institute (without the apostrophe) was granted a new charter in Delaware based on an application filed by Alfons Bach, Ben Nash, and George Kosmak. ADI's administrative offices were promptly relocated to New York, and its new president for 1944 was Belle Kogan, who had been secretary/treasurer during the previous term of office.5

Most prominent industrial designers of the 1930s practiced in New York, and their wellpublicized financial success over the decade had attracted the attention of New York State tax authorities, who decided that industrial design earnings were taxable under the unincorporated business tax statute. Accordingly, in 1940, Henry Dreyfuss, Walter Dorwin Teague, and Raymond Loewy were billed nearly half a million dollars in back taxes for the years 1935, 1936, and 1937. The three argued that their earnings were similar to architects, derived from services rendered rather than the sale of goods.

So the Big Three shared costs to engage attorneys to represent them in court, with Teague as their representative standing trial on April 30, 1941. The attorneys argued for exemption of Teague's income from the unincorporated business tax because "the vocation of industrial design is a 'profession, rather than a trade' or 'business, and section 386 of the tax law 'excludes the practice of law, medicine, dentistry, [and] architecture, which under existing law cannot be conducted under corporate structure, and in any other case in which more than eighty percent of the gross income is derived from personal services rendered by the individual." The case was dismissed.

This victorious result has been taken as legal evidence that industrial design is, in fact, a profession. From that time, subsequent attempts to tax industrial designers under the same law were unsuccessful. However, after about 1976, when taxes for "professional corporations" would be implemented, all professions, including architects and industrial designers, would be subject to taxation similar to business corporations. However, the tax challenge of 1941, and the intent to protect their legal identity as an individual service provider, probably explains the traditional obsession of designers such as Loewy, Dreyfuss, and-Teague in requiring only their own name to be credited on al the work produced by individuals on their staff, a restrictive and resented practice that essentially disappeared in the 1970s, when tax codes were modified.

After the tax issue was resolved, New York's leading designers, including Dreyfuss, Loewy, Bel Geddes, Deskey, Arens and Wright, met informally to discuss how the profession could further its objectives of professionalism, influence the business world, and establish standards of practice. There were serious concerns regarding the American Designers Institute (ADI), the only such organization of national scale, as an appropriate organization for industrial design. First, the term "designers" in its title did not specifically refer to "industrial designers," the now commonly accepted specific term for the group of professionals who had successfully established the profession in the business and public worlds. That meant that any kind of "designer" could join ADI, not just industrial designers.

Second, Chicago furniture designers who were specialists in a single industry had formed ADI. Some still were, and yet, many of its members worked in many different, unrelated industries. The consensus of the leading designers mentioned above was that a national organization should specifically refer to "industrial designers," should be more exclusive, and should be restricted to those practitioners who had demonstrated proficiency in a broad range of products, not just one. At the time, there were about 2,000 industrial designers, but many were not well qualified in mass-production experience or skills.

On February 7, 1944, 15 industrial designers met to discuss and conclude the formation of a new organization in accordance with these objectives. They defined an industrial designer as "one who has successfully designed a diversity of products for machine and mass production." This was the fundamental philosophical difference between the two groups, as many members of ADI specialized in furniture or home furnishings. The Society of Industrial Designers (SID) was thus formed.

On August 20, 1944, at the first annual meeting of the SID, the group elected Walter Dorwin Teague as president, Henry Dreyfuss as vice president, Raymond Loewy as chairman of the executive committee, Harold Van Doren as treasurer, and Egmont Arens as secretary. Philip McConnell, formerly with the U.S. Treasury Department, and former secretary to the New York World's Fair Design Commission, was appointed executive secretary of the new organization.

In addition to those mentioned above, founding members included Donald Deskey, Norman Bel Geddes, Lurell Guild Ray Patten, Joseph B.Platt,John Gordon Rideout, Georgakier, Joseph Sinel, Brooks Stevens, and Russel Wright. Wright recalled that George Nelson, Charles Eames, and several other members of the ADI were considered for membership, but were thought to be not acceptable at the time, probably because their work was limited to furniture des.Each of the D founders invite e aditinal desiger to jon the next year, and after that, membership would be open to qualified designers. Some called the new organization Club," exclusiv gazain e by he BigThree Tegu, Ly, and Drs.
