As the postwar era evolved, these distinctions would become blurvan.Teulll l rs.Bu w the formal definition of industrial design and industrial designers.

This confusion extended to educational institutions, which were faced with the new challen  how o educate industrial designers. In , Richard F. Bach, dean  educaton the Metropolitan Museum, hosted a conference for administrators of art schools to discuss the matter. Invitees included Alexander Kostellow and James C. Boudreau of Prat, as well as administrators from 44 other art schools. A summary of the conference stated that "the conference carefully avoided definition of the term industrial design,' fearful that the effort to arrive at a definition might constrain discussion."

Indeed it might. Most art schools' design programs were quite broad in scope and were not strictly industrial design programs. Only 15 of the 44 art schools had programs specializing in industrial design. Major changes in curriculum recommended by industrial designers included courses in the physical and behavioral sciences and the addition of workshops and laboratorycur similar stituteechnology. Industrialdesmode buildin tion required well-equipped workshops with heavy equipment such as lathes, band-saws, planers, drill presses and vented paint-spraying booths, as well as model instruction in soft and hard clay, plaster cast and forming, plastic heat forming, mold making, woodworking, metalworking, and high-quality finishing techniques, all requiring heavy expenditures. Specialized training in concept sketching and presentation rendering using pastels or gouache was also required to meet the standards of the profession.

These potential changes challenged the traditional "applied art" mentality of art schools and would require new specialized equipment and faculty. For art schools to recognize industrial design as a unique entity would be to separate it from existing curricula and require new faculty, or it could lead to the establishment of independent schools of industrial design, something art schools did not want to see.8 Art school administrators argued that these sorts of changes would drive away some artistic students, which was probably true. As a result, many new industrial design courses in art school programs would not be as effective as in more technically adapted schools and would often be taught by existing art faculty with no experience in the field.

Walter Dorwin Teague, attending a later meeting of this same group, commented,

We have created a profession, and some of [you educators] are delighted to teach it, with only the vaguest idea of what it is all about. By our practice we will create a definition of industrial design and I trust the conference committee will be acute enough to discern it.9

What Teague was saying was that practitioners would define the profession as it evolved, and educators needed to figure out how to teach it. This is pretty much the way the issue was resolved over the next 50 years. In fact, the profession would constantly evolve and expand into all aspects of design (graphic, interior, architectural, etc.) as well as into dozens of related technical fields such as psychology, science, engineering, sales, and marketing. Every new technology would present new opportunities for industrial designers, who always sought to make technology understandable and user friendly. Industrial design would forever defy a fixed definition, and industrial design programs would continue to vary in quality, scope, and structure.

The invasion of Europe began in June 1944. On the day that the American troops entered liberated Paris in August of that year, Raymond Loewy, now a naturalized American citizen, was invited by the U.S. Department of State to speak on the radio to his former countrymen Fnci id n R

In May 1945, the war ended in Germany, and in August with the atomic bombs in Japan. Military personnel by the millions returned home to begin civilian life anew and needed housing, education, and jobs. Many married and went to school under the G.I. Bil. Those who had stayed at home had been deprived of new consumer products for four years. Aging cars, furniture, appliances, bathrooms, and kitchens, many from the 1930s, needed replacement or modernization.

Perhaps more significantly, the national mood was much deeper than simple relief that the war was over. Many had been economically deprived since the Depression began in 1929, unable to afford the many new products and advances of the 1930s, which were then unavailable during the war. To these people, they had endured 15 years of scrimping and making do without any of the wonderful modern goods of the "World of Tomorrow" that had been promised at the World's Fairs of both 1933 and 1939. Now, many had enjoyed good jobs and steady income during the war. Average weekly pay had doubled since 1940, from \$24 to \$44 in 1945. A large number of families had double incomes, because many women had worked on defense jobs. Millions had accumulated considerable savings, and they were eager to spend it.

The country struggled to make a rapid transition from wartime production back to domestic, which was, in a sense, almost as difficult as it was to make the opposite switch in 1941. New designs and tooling were required, both of which took time. But America was the wealthiest and most powerful nation in the world with the greatest manufacturing base and the largest consumer market. An economic boom began that would last for the next 15 years. From 1945 to 1950, annual housing starts increased from 200,000 to 1,154,000.

Developers were building new suburbs using crews of masons, carpenters, plumbers, electricians and painters who followed each other down streets and completed 30 homes per day. Thesemass-produchome include yard garage,hrubbery, shutters, and builnpliances, including refrigerator, stove, washer and dryer—the perfect new owner package. All that was needed to start housekeeping was furniture, lamps, dinnerware, accessories, lawnmowers, ladders, and a car to drive to work. One of the popular accessories in such houses was a clothes dryer with a unique window in the front door that Brooks Stevens had designed in 1941 for the Hamilton Manufacturing Company.

The demand was high for the design of mass-produced housing, but most architects did not contribute because of their concern that it could reduce their commissions on individual houses. But anumb nonarchitec entrereneurs seppei wih ieas, monthemBucminster Fuller, who in 1945 redesigned his original Dymaxion house of 1929, which had evolved into his 1942 wartime Dymaxion Deployment Unit.

Now, his final design used a central vertical stainless steel strut on a single foundation, which supported bicycle-wheel-like structures that hung down to support the roof, while beams radiated out to support the floor. Roof, ceilng, and floor were formed of wedge-shaped fans of sheet metal, creating a round floor plan. The entire exterior was aluminum, with many components built by the Beech Aircraft Company, which was seeking new postwar products. Several prototypes, including the Wichita House, were built, incorporating a prefabricated batroo, water storage, heating r conditionig and a convcin ventilator built nte roof. Reactions to the prototypes were positive, but it was never produced industrially because Fuller considered the prototypes as "unfinished." Potential buyers found other options, and Fuller's company went bankrupt.

Industrial designers were again in high demand by manufacturers as they developed and tooled up for high-volume domestic production. The profession was attractive because of its and because graduates were in high demand. In March 1946, the Society of Industrial Designers (SID) published an educational bulletin listing schools with programs in industrial design. these programs would not reach the market before about 1949 or 1950.

At this point I need to remind you of something I mentioned in the preface. After 1934, and particulary tr World War I,he nuber ndustrial degners increase gniinly. Since the subject of this book is the machine designers of the early 1930s, those who were the earliest pioneers prior to about 1935, these are the designers who are primarily followed throughout the book. To follow the careers and work o all industrial designers after 1935 would regretfully have been beyond the scope of this book. Although some are mentioned, information about them and their designs is limited. Accordingly, if you fail to find details of the many well-known and highly successful designers who have influenced the profession since 1935, or of their designs, this is the reason for their necessary omission.

Immediately after the war, of course, the supply of designers was limited to older prewar practitioners already at work, or to young prewar graduates who were returning from military service. This meant that the large design firms and companies that were in existence before and during the war, led by the machine designers of the 1930s, would be handling the lion's share of postwar demand for industrial design, and would be enjoying tremendous success.

Among those pioneer design firms was Raymond Loewy Associates (RLA) of New York, which in 1945 opened offices in Los Angeles, California, and Chicago, Illinois. For the latter, Loewy hired Richard S. Latham (1920-1991), a returning serviceman who had started before the war as a designer at Montgomery Ward. RLA designed a streamlined diesel locomotive, built by Fairbanks-Morse in General Electric's Erie, Pennsylvania, facility and introduced in 1945. The "A" units had an engineer cab above the engine with an automotive-style split windshield, similar to the 1937 Electro-Motive Super Chief, with which it was competing on the Atcheson, Topeka & Santa Fe Railway. The "B" units were cabless. Eighty-two A units and 29 B units would be built for American Railroads over the next four years.

RLA in 1945 was on retainer to no fewer than 79 American, British, and Swedish corporations as a design consultant. Britain was at the time considering its own renewal and regeneration after the war and invited Raymond Loewy to offer an American designer's view of British industry. He responded with a letter published in the London Times in November that expressed the typical philosophy of American industrial design, titled "Industrial Design: The Aesthetics of Salesmanship—an American View":

In America the industrial designer has developed a design technique that has been successfully tested in the field during the last 15 years, and American products most generally carry with them the advantage of collaboration between the professional consulting designer and the industrialist. This partnership with industry and our philosophy of design are based on securing greater sales appeal—increased trade. I have heard much here in England about the Aesthetics of design. This to me is strange. We seem to find that the aesthetics of an industrial product will take care of themselves automatically after we have provided a balance between function, simplicity, and utility. In the average manufacturer's mind the word aesthetics has annotations of philanthropy and culture after office hours. Industrial design did not become standard practice in America because of aesthetics. There were too many other good reasons for using it—increased sales appeal of products and increased trade. And the final result is no less beautiful for having been designed on sound, unemotional business lines."

This philosophy, which embraced the purpose of design as a business strategy to increase product sales by appealing to the tastes and desires of common consumers, was the antipathy of those at the Museum of Modern Art (MoMA) and other designers who saw the fundamental purpose of industrial design as an artistic, sculptural, and personal expression by an individual artist. This difference was the root cause of MoMA's criticism of streamlining, its general disregard of market value, and its dismissal of popular taste. It was why MoMA promoted product designs on the sole basis of aesthetic superiority, a quality judged by MoMA alone. This, of course, was the traditional mission of art museums for centuries—to educate the general public how to appreciate and understand the best of fine art. MoMA's acclaimed historical fountain of "good design," the Bauhaus, was founded with the legitimate objective of marrying art and industry. Now that this had been successfully accomplished in America, MoMA wanted to reseparate them into traditional, and unequal, categories of "good design" and "commercial design." Fortunately, the public would not recognize this elite distinction.

In 1944, Paul Block, Jr., publisher of the Toledo (Ohio) Blade newspaper, had commissioned Norman Bel Geddes to prepare a study suggesting future developments for the city of Toledo, which had lost population and appeared to be in decline. It was to serve as a model for urban planning, not just for Toledo, but for other cities in Ohio as well. Bel Geddes was well known for his work in city and traffic planning, for his City of Tomorrow for Shell Oil in 1937, for his Futurama at the 1939 World's Fair, and for his 1940 book, Magic Motorways. Geddes was offered a fee of \$50,000 plus travel expenses to complete the exhibit within three to six months. The final cost would be closer to \$75,000.

In response to his commission, Bel Geddes created Toledo Tomorrow, a giant model fiftynine feet in diameter depicting Toledo transformed by a major reorganization of the city's transportation infrastructure. At a scale oneinc equals 100 feet, the model howe an r of about 150 square miles, with limited-access freeways crisscrossing the metropolitan area. A new passenger depot located near city center served air, rail, and bus travelers in a single location. The model was created in Bel Geddes's New York office in 47 panels, each four by six feet, and shipped to Toledo for public unveiling on July 4, 1945.

V al from an altitude of 10,000 feet, and a special sound system provided audio descriptions at five points. The audio assured visitors that "if only a part of this master plan were brought to frin, your Tole would be a bettr ciy."As aaaefe twr the en o thevir walk, the lights were dimmed to suggest night, and special ultraviolet lamps were turned on to illuminate the model. Ultimately, while a few changes in the city resulted from the Bel Geddes model, they were not very noticeable, and the proposed central air-rail-bus terminal was never built. 12

Also in 1945, Bel Geddes designed motorboats for Gar Wood Industries, luggage for Anroucnodehorapy or EcBi hGra Pyramid and the Panama Canal. In 1946 he would design a line of radios, washing machines, and company trademarks for the Federal Telephone and Telegraph Company, and for the U.S. Navy he would write, construct models, and advise in preparation of the offcial recordof the Battle of Midway, the Naval battle that destroyed the Japanese Navy.

At Ford, after the death of Edsel Ford in 1943, the man he had appointed head of Ford's sylig sud 3, Bo Gregorie,  he cpany an esablished his wn conui business in Detroit. But at the request of Henry Ford II, he returned in 1944 to head the postwar styling section, which had been revised by management in an effort to blend engineering and design. However, in 1946, the new management (Henry Ford II) engaged industrial design cuan Geo W  r Fr  rh u the 1949 Ford. Walker, who had designed Nash cars as a consultant since 1937, had hired Joe Oros (19172012) in 1940, and just recently, Elwood P. Engel (19171986) in 1946. Henry Ford I had invited both Gregorie and Walker to submit competing design proposals, and Ford chose one of Walker's submissions.

Gregorie saw his days at Ford numbered, so he resigned, closed his consulting office, and returned to his other love, designing boats. He designed his own 42-foot trawler, The Drifter, wul  re ex  rToas .Hibr vec , left General Motors to replace Gregorie as head of Ford styling and would also contribute to the design of the 1949 Ford.

In 1946, Raymond Loewy Associates' (RLA) Chicago offce, largely through the work of RicharLahnwrki wi Gou en ill .Car vpe of concepts for a 40-foot Greyhound double-decker Scenicruiser bus to provide a high view oenic landscapes from a second-level dome, similar to those popularized by railroads for cross-country travel. After some years of development by the General Motors Truck and Coach Division, the final design based on RLA's cncepts would be inrduced in 94. Over  thousad would be built over the followg two years and would prove to be very popular wih the traveling public.

RLA also designed the most innovative and modern-looking postwar upright vacuum cleaner in 1946, the Magic Carpet S-2, for the Singer Company. Instead of the typical "broom"- type single handle, the S-2 had twin chrome handles that spread apart as they descended to the cleaner head, straddling a flat, closed, plastic housing for a cord reel. The motor housing was no higher than a pack of cigarettes, accomplished by turning the motor on its side, which enabled the cleaner to go easily under furniture. The cleaner could also be hung on a closet wall, saving considerable storage space. That same year, Richard Latham of RLA designed logical layouts for "ham" shortwave radio receivers I-20 and No. S-38 for the Hallicrafters Company.

Another diesel locomotive in 1946 came from Ray Patten, head of General Electric's Appearance Design division. Along with his assistant director, Gordon Florian, Patten designed passenger (PA) and freight (FA) versions of the American Locomotive Company (Alco) and GE diesel locomotives. Between 1940 and 1953, these companies jointly made and marketed large road locomotives. Patten described the design as "a locomotive so distinctive and powerful looking that it actually helps railroads sell their services to passengers and shippers." By 1946, Patten's design group at GE numbered 50 people, including 15 to 18 model makers.4 Gordan Florian soon left GE to open his own design office in Bridgeport.

Otto Kuhler had designed more locomotives and railroad cars than Raymond Loewy, Paul Cret, and Henry Dreyfuss combined. But after the war began, his German accent became a liability, and he abandoned freelance work. In 1944 he worked for the American Car and Foundry, developing double-deck sleepers and subway cars, but was laid off in 1947. He retired to a ranch near Pine, Colorado, where he painted western scenes, exhibited them in museums, and raised cattle.

After the war in 1945, Teague, like Loewy, changed his sole proprietorship to a partnership, allowing senior staff, including C. Stowe Myers, with Teague since 1934, and Frank Del Guidice (19161993), who later became the company's president, to be partners in Walter Dorwin Teague Associates (WDTA). Del Guidice represented WDTA in seeking commissions from the Boeing Company, which met with success, and WDTA established a branch office in Seattle, Washington. Teague established an engineering division to handle the complexity of the projects at Boeing. Their first assignment was to convert Boeing's B-29 World War II bomber into a commercial airliner by transforming the raw unfinished interior of the plane's body into a more comfortable setting. They installed vinyl-clad panels for an easy-to-clean surface, introduced pleasing colors, and added cabin lighting as well as call buttons for flight attendants and overhead sections with individual reading lights and oxygen masks, all standard features of today's planes. The result was called the Stratocruiser, introduced to commercial service in 1946. WDTA would design interiors for a sequence of Boeing airliners far into the future, including the 707 (1958), the 737 (1963), the 747 (1969), the 767 (1982), the 777 (1995), the 777 Worldliner (2005), and the 787 Dreamliner (2011).

![](images/06f0e217b913327d681c87855c64d6e8667d09e14bf1716372930ab74d97b90b.jpg)  
Patten locomotive design (Patent D153,896, U.S. Patent Office).

Donald Dailey, who had headed Harold Van Doren's office in Philadelphia since 1940, established his own firm in Philadelphia in 1946. That year, Lászl6 Moholy-Nagy, who headed the School of Design in Chicago, wrote and published a book, The New Vision, but sadly passed away that same year. His wife, Sybil, would publish another of his books, Vision in Motion, posthumously, the next year. Moholy-Nagy's School of Design would be absorbed by the Illinois Institute of Technology as the Institute of Design after his death, and taken over by Serge Chermayeff (1900-1996). Brooks Stevens designed Ben Hur, the first home food freezer, which was blue, unlike all previous white home appliances. George A. Beck became manager of industrial design in General Electric's new electronics department in Syracuse, New York. Donald Deskey in New York expanded his firm, adding architectural, product design, and packaging departments."5

In 1945, Henry Dreyfuss had moved to California and opened a second office near his home in Pasadena. His office in New York, managed by a partner, William F.H. Purcell, and an associate, Robert H. Hose (19151977), both hired in 1946, designed RCA radios, televisions, and phonographs in 46. In California, Dreyfuss's new client, Convair in San Diego, was seeking entry into the postwar aviation boom with a flying car developed by Theodore P. Hall, Convair's chief engineer. Styled by Henry Dreyfuss, it would be introduced in 1947. The Model 118 ConVairCar was a four-seat, two-door car with an aircraft engine and wings attached to its roof. Convair planned to build 160,000 of them, costing \$1,500 each, and rent them at airports. The first prototype, Model 116, made 66 successful fights, but Model 118 crashed durgmran uige t.The lot istken euto for the aircraft's fuel gauge and ran out of fuel in midflight, which is what happened to Convair's plans.

Mos o the automobile indusry ws fying, not to et in the ir, but to t back to making cars for a market that was desperately in need. Millions of prewar cars were at the end of their life span, with rust, worn-out parts, or dated looks.

# Chapter 11

# Celebrity and "Good Design"

Raymond Loewy's fascination with automotive design extended into his private life. In 1946 he took two 1940 Lincoln Continentals and custom redesigned them for himself and a friend. The result was a modern version of the traditional brougham style car body in black with the front seat fully visible beneath a Plexiglas roof, as if for a chauffeur, and an enclosed cabin with round opera side windows for passengers in the rear seat. The custom hood ornament was a Frigidaire door handle.

Loewy often used the car for the showmanship that was his trademark. One of Loewy's student apprentices at the time, Budd Steinhilber, relates the time when he was asked to

take Loewy's leather presentation portfolio down to a parking garage on 6th Avenue, and put it in a designated Ford convertible. Loewy was scheduled to make a presentation in Hartford, Connecticut. In the meantime, Loewy's personal chauffer was instructed to drive in from Long Island in his customized Lincoln Continental, and park it at curbside in front of the office building on Fifth Avenue. Large crowds began to gather, and soon Loewy, his wife, and her French poodle emerged ceremoniously from the building, stepped grandly into the Lincoln and drove off accompanied by the rumble of its 12-cylinder engine. However, they only drove around the corner and down 46th street to the parking garage where the Loewy's then transferred to the rental Ford convertible. The chauffer was dispatched back to Oyster Bay. Pure and simple theater!!

Meanwhile, Loewy's office in South Bend had been busy during the war with the first newly designed postwar cars for the Studebaker Corporation, introduced in May 1946. Recall that all automotive companies were required to devote all their manufacturing capability to produce military equipment during the war and to suspend work on consumer goods. General Motors and Chrysler made M-4 Sherman tanks at the rate of 2,000 per month. Ford produced 18,000 B-24 Consolidated Aircraft Liberator bombers. Plymouth made Bofors antiaircraft guns and aircraft landing gears. Packard made V-12 engines for PT boats and aircraft. Willys-Ovlan Bnm, and Formade Jees. Stuebaker mad truckara gies, - sonnel carriers.

At the war  fe preane cars uch s Packar Huon,and Nash, wee quicky introduced in 1946, using old tooling, and with only minor changes. Norman Bel Geddes designed a new grille and dashboard for the prewar Nash. Willys-Overland began production i e Je o MJe designed by Milwaukee industrial designer Brooks Stevens to look like prewar "woodies.

But Loewy's ffce in South Bend, being independent of the Studebaker Corporation, was exempt from the war production restrictions of the automotive companies, and thus, rgit   be  eveSubak ar el

Champion, Commander, and Landcruiser during the war. They were designed by Virgil Exner, head of Loewy's studio in South Bend, who was hired by Loewy in 1937, and Robert E. Bourke, who was hired by Exner in 1940, both members of Loewy's Studebaker team.

But Loewy had fired Exner in 1944 for conspiring with Roy Cole, Studebaker vice president of engineering, to bypass Loewy. Exner went to work as an employee of Studebaker under Cole,andLwy plac hi wh Gorn Bueg whohad been hi he L's Studebaker studio. Since he left Auburn in 1936, Buehrig had served with the Budd Company until 1938, after which he had worked as a freelance designer. Although an excellent designer, Buer was a hy andunassumig persn and simpy did not have e take-hargedipoi needed to manage the studio. Bourke was soon put in charge, and both worked on the 1947 models, which were ready for tooling as soon as the war was over.

When they were introduced in late 1946, design professionals praised the innovative design, with is distinctive wraparound, greenhouse"rear windows on two-door models; it lolung, fush se ying; anthe at-back tuistothe tradtnal tped look the time. The tu popte a joke t the tie that e could not elhe r was c or going. But it was new and quite different from any Detroit models. Eliot Noyes said it was so refreshing it made other cars look out of date." Life magazine published a ten-page article on Loewy, Studebaker, and its new models. Over the next three years, Studebaker would break all its peacetime records for sales and profits.

![](images/b0d5d0e2ebea9f5bf3e2b382eb62b9f7464aad7c5cedf31a3693066a8ea2d821.jpg)  
1947 Studebaker, by Raymond Loewy Associates.

Other design activities in 1947 included work by Raymond Loewy Associates (RLA) in its New York office. As consultant to Coca-Cola, RLA designed the red, streamlined dispensers made by Dole Deluxe that sat on soda fountain counters across the country, the work of Carl Otto and Clare Hodgman of Loewy's office. RLA also slimmed down the hourglass shape of Coca-Cola's iconic bottle and designed open-sided delivery trucks to expedite stock delivery and handling.

Tucker Madawick joined RLA in 1947 and with Carl Otto reopened Loewy's London office on Grosvenor Street, which had closed down in 1940 as the bombing of Britain began. Madawick handled the accounts of Electrolux of Sweden, Austin of England, Unilever, Gestetner, Lyon's Tea House, the Rootes Group, and Allied Ironfounders. In Chicago, John Morgan took on Mel Boldt as a partner in his consulting office, and Dave Chapman designed streamlined sewing machines for Montgomery Ward.

Lurelle Guild was engaged by Alcoa to design hardware/aluminum furniture, which included chairs, tables, consoles, vanity desks, bed headboards, nightstands, and chests. Alcoa sold over 400,000 Kensington and Wear-Ever chairs before discontinuing the line in 1952. Guild had a long list of clients and was so well known that many manufacturers featured his name and photograph on marketing materials.

In New York, Norman Bel Geddes had hired Elliot Noyes, who had returned from military service after the war, to be his design director. The office designed a secretarial desk for International Business Machines (IBM), a cigarette vending machine for Rowe Manufacturing Corporation, and a jukebox for the Lion Manufacturing Company. Bel Geddes was soon commissioned by IBM to design a new typewriter and had started working on it, but Bel Geddes's office closed in 1947 due to financial mismanagement. Noyes opened his own office that same year and completed the typewriter design started under Bel Geddes, which would be introduced in 1948 as IBM's Model A. Thomas J. Watson, Jr., president of IBM and a glider pilot friend of Noyes, retained him for product design at IBM.

Nathan George Horwitt designed a wristwatch in 1947 with a round, black face without numerals, symbols, or lines to mark hours or minutes, and with only a single gold dot marking the twelve-o-clock position, intended to be evocative of a sundial. Vacheron & Constantin-Le Coultre, a Switzerland firm, originally manufactured it. But Zenith Movado would proceed to produce the exact same design in 1948, without any credit or compensation to Horwitt. A prime example of design piracy, it would take 27 years in the courts until Movado finally settled with Horwitt for \$29,000 in 1975. The watch would be included in the permanent design collection of the Museum of Modern Art in 1960 and become known as "the Museum Watch." A wall clock of the classic Horwitt design was produced in 1960 by the Howard Miller Clock Company and is still being produced today.

In June 1947, the Chicago, Milwaukee, St. Paul and Pacific Railroad introduced its streamlined flagship, the Olympian Hiawatha, which traveled from Chicago to Seattle/Tacoma, Washington. Brooks Stevens had designed it from stem to stern. The dramatic orange and maroon train was pulled by a massive Fairbanks-Morse diesel engine topped by a high, forward cab with automotive/aircraft-type split windshield, and a nose like a vertical cylinder with chrome horizontal bands. Many described the Skytop solarium observation cars as the most distinctiverailway cars ever built. The rear lounge had gass windows, not just at eye level, ut also in three tiers overhead like a greenhouse, to afford an uninterrupted view of scenery. In the forward area were sleeping compartments. The separate ladies' and men's lounges were designed with distinctly feminine and masculine character, respectively. That year, Stevens also designed a Trav-ler portable plastic tabletop radio, an Evinrude Sportwin outboard motor, a line of luggage for the Wheary Company, and an aviation plant in California for McCulloch Corporation's aviation division.

The Societyf Industrial Designers (SID) in 1946 hanitiate amassive effort to prmote the work of its growing membership. Prominent designers were busy writing articles, lecturing, and granting interviews. The October 1946 ssue of Electrical Manufacturing published an article by Walter Dorwin Teague titled "Industrial Design in Partnership with Engio pp Th  T were featured in a May 147 issue o Architectural Record. The June 147 issue o Coronet magazine fatured a biography and personal interview with Henry Dreyfuss.

B 147, he Ameican Desers InstiuteADI) ha sevealhundremembrs, hil the Society of Industrial Designers (SID), a more exclusive organization, had only 78. For it was. This was obviously the intention of the founders. Ten years after its founding, in 1954, SID membership would total 153, a tenfold increase over its original 15 founders. At that same time, membership in the original organization, the American Designers Institute (ADI), would be 200.

Unlike the ADI, the SID did not offer recommendations for a postwar industrial design education curriculum. Instead, in February 1947, it announced a program of evening courses at New York University for industrial designers, both professional and nonprofessional. Four crss cnsisting  5 leces  we heul   smeter e 1481- ubcA  teD nize a hoo exibin   mebers opened in Philadelphia and traveled to 12 other cities.

Harley Ear, thedominant force in autooive desin, starte the 10-year Detroit battle Loc  piy H   i whogenerally avore simpler n cleaner designs after the wr.Streinigha be a derogatory term.

In his 1940 book, Industrial Design, Harold Van Doren had explained how the unfortunate term of "streamlining" had been associated with industrial design since about 1934:

Streamlining has taken the modern world by storm. We live in a maelstrom of streamlined trains, refrigerators, and furnaces; streamlined bathing beauties, soda crackers, and facial massages. True streamlining is not nearly so important, nor so frequently met with in problems of industrial design, as the general public probably thinks.

The manufacturer who wants his laundry tubs, his typewriters, or his furnaces "streamlined" is in reality asking you to modernize them, to find the means for substituting curvilinear forms for rectilinear forms.. to conform to the current taste, or fad if you wil..He expects, too, that unnecessary exposure of mechanical parts will be eliminated, that buttons will be substituted for levers, and control panels and dials will be organized into simple and easily read groups, tied in wherever possible with related elements of the principle forms.

In 99 cases out of 100 this procedure is utterly unrelated to genuine streamlining. Then why does the client refer to it as such: . Simply because, in the unbelievably rapid growth of the American language, words soon lose their specific and restricted meanings and assume a general significance embracing far wider fields than originally intended.²

The original term "streamlining" correctly referred to automobiles, aircraft and trains, which moved at high speed and therefore were made more efficient with streamlining. But when used on stationary objects, it was understandably criticized as unnecessary, and postwar designs generally abandoned the practice, except for Detroit and the automotive industry in general, ando course it is still used there today. But in the late 140s, Detroit was the recipient of criticism, even though automobiles had reasonable justification to use streamlining under the original meaning. But streamlining was no longer the problem. Simply put, it was more a matter of an excessive amount of decorative chrome and extraneous details such as tail fins, elaborate hubcaps, and hood ornaments that Ravmond Loewy would call "jukeboxes on wheels" in a 1955 speech he would deliver to the Society of Automotive Engineers.

The same critics also did not approve of the Detroit term "styling," which by now had become almost synonymous with decorative surface embellishment, rather than the ergonomic consideration and convenience of passengers. Cars were so low that entry was made difficult even without a hat. There were no seat belts or air bags. You may recall that Harley Earl had changed the name of his group at General Motors from the "Art and Colour Section" to the "Styling Division" in 1937. The term "styling," for many, suggested cosmetic decoration, excessive streamlining, and insincerity, rather than the more comprehensive practice of industrial design, which would have considered safety devices and user convenience. Had Earl used the term "industrial design" instead of "styling," he could have avoided such criticism, but he did not. It seemed that he and his colleagues envisioned their work as something different than designing toasters and refrigerators.

It was the beginning of a sustained split within the industrial design community, with three more or less independent groups: the single-product designers, who worked mainly, but separately, in the home furnishing industry (American Designers Institute); the automobile industry (Detroit); and the multiproduct/multiclient designers who had founded the Society of Industrial Designers in 1944. As the number of industrial designers began to increase exponentially after about 1948, when the first wave of new graduates arrived in the field, these three groups would slowly blend into the single identity of industrial design over the next 20 years.

In 1948, Ray Patten at General Electric passed away while heading GE's Appearance Design Division. Arthur BecVar, who had joined GE in 1944 after returning from military service, was appointed to fill his vacate psiion. Becar changed the name  his group t the Industrial Design Division. Industrial designers like BecVar, who were employees and heads only 15 percent of the members of the Society of Industrial Designers (SID). They were informally referred to as "in-house" designers. The remaining 85 percent of SID members were

![](images/3804342824e27da88cd75dcf5fa43dd6311edf6fd111cc189bfc50fda43650b0.jpg)  
Polaroid camera Model 95, by Walter Dorwin Teague Associates.

attached to independent consulting firms. And no, they were not referred to as "out-house" designers, but "consultants." "

As 1948 progressed, Alfonso Iannelli became a consultant for appliance manufacturer John Oster, designing mixers, hair dryers, knife sharpeners, and hair clippers. Walter Dorwin Teague Associates designed the first Model 95 Polaroid camera, produced by the Polaroid Land Company, which became an instant success. Brooks Stevens designed a refrigerator for the Coolerator Company and the iconic Willys-Overland Jeepster phaeton (a roadster with four seats rather than two), a sorty convertible that retained the familiar fenders andvertically slotted grille the wartime Jeep. Ten thousand were sold the first year. In 1949, Stevens would design Harley Davidson's Hydra-Glide motorcycle and a Roadmaster bicycle for the Cleveland Welding Company.

By 1948, Barnes & Reinecke

iblihehi n ff, J.Rec &ate lizig desg and product plannng, withoffice on Ohio Street in Chicago, takingwith him designers Jon Hauser, Harold Hart, Don Lowe, and Jack Knight, as well as a number of accounts. Clients included Caterpillar, Emerson, AT&T, Johnson & Johnson, 3M, McGraw-Edison, Westinghouse, and Zenith.

In Cleveland, Viktor Schreckengost had returned from military service to teach industrial design at the Cleveland School of Art (now the Cleveland Institute of Art). He also continued to design many consumer products, such as for his client Murray Bicycle Company, for whom hedesigneany childre's tranoration products, icuding pedal crs, tricc n an  l  u and painting. In the early 1950s, he created monumental ceramic sculptures for the Cleveland Zoo (now Metroparks Zoo).

Ford introduced its first postwar car design in 1949 that would become an automotive classic. As mentioned earlier, Henry Ford II had in 1946 selected the design concept of industrial design consultant George W. Walker over that of Bob Gregorie, the head of Ford' styling studio, causing Gregorie to resign. Walker had actually submitted three quarter-scale clay concept models to Henry Ford II, one each by three of his staff members: Elwood Engle and Joseph Oros, Walker's key designers, and a model maker, Dick Caleal, just hired by Walker because of the clay model he had made at Walker's request. It was Caleal's model that got Walker the job with Ford.

How Caleal, a lowly model maker, created a concept that won out over two of the best automotive designers (three, if you include Gregorie) in the industry was unknown at the time. But a story of intrigue would come out, essentially because of Robert Bourke, then head of Loewy's Studebaker team in South Bend. Studebaker's chief engineer had ordered Bourke to cut Loewy's staff because of budgetary reasons. Bourke had to fire several people, including one of his model makers, Caleal. Now unemployed, Caleal had approached Walker for a job and was told by Walker that he would be hired if he built an acceptable quarter-scale clay model of a concept proposal for the 1949 Ford, for which Walker was trying to get a contract, within a week.

Caleal excitedly called Bourke with the good news and asked if he could help him plan the model. Bourke, wanting Caleal to get the job with Walker, gave him a wooden "buck" or armature for the quarter-scale clay model, and some wheel models. Bourke and three other designers agreed to help Caleal with the model on evenings in Caleal's Mishawaka, Indiana, home when he had the model up to rough size, in order to help him meet the deadline. They did so on Caleal's kitchen table, heating the modeling clay in the kitchen oven. Based on Bourke's rough sketches, they all worked on the model at the same time, Bourke handling the front grille and motif.

![](images/871795d1fe6bd8831e1cb8c4acb9da26a4d28c10d388d6ce4e14a67e0503578b.jpg)  
1949 Ford, by George Walker staff member Dick Caleal.

Bourke had been inspired since 1940 by aircraft propeller hubs that looked like the tip of a bullet and had sketched numerous ideas with such a spinner hub on the nose of concept sketches (see also the 1950 Studebaker, described later). So it was not unexpected that this device would appear in the center of the clay model's grill. When it was finished, the model was 99 percent like the Ford that would be produced in 1949, with the spinner centered on the middle horizontal grille bar as its most distinctive feature.

Caleal took the model to Walker on the backseat of his car; Walker liked it, and Caleal got the job. Bourke was silent, since he had secretly used Loewy's staff and materials to help Caleal and didn't want to jeopardize Caleal's job. As a result, Walker, in the tradition of the day as head of his office, received full credit for the design, By 1978, the design would be hailed as "The Car That Saved an Empire—The 1949 Ford." But Bourke would spill the beans in 1985, and the story would be out.³ In 2002, Ford would introduce a retro concept model called the Forty-Nine, which emulated the original, and in 2003, Ford would offcially credit Caleal with the design of the 1949 Ford.

Gordon Buehrig left Loewy in 1949 and went to Ford, as head of the body development studio, and would work on the 1951 Victoria coupe and the 1952 Ranchwagon. George Nelson, who had opened his own office in 1947, was by now producing a flood of highly acclaimed furniture designs as design director of the Herman Miller Furniture Company, including his 1949 Basic Cabinet Series, his 1945 Storage Wall concept, and his 1949 Atomic Clock for the Howard Miller Clock Company, named because of individual balls marking the hours, which atom ecer i sis. It ws hersnyic .

Nelson, who had been the anonymous author of the influential 1934 Fortune magazine article "Both Fish and Fowl," now wrote another article in the July 1949 issue of Fortune titled "Business and the Industrial Designer," this time under his own name. He retraced the brief 20-year history of the profession and acknowledged that increasing sales is an integral objective of industrial design, but that following fashion trends or fads can be detrimental to small manufacturers. Using the example of automotive design, he explained,

T hope that this course is the safet, although in point o fact it is the most riskybecause it means competing on the most unfavorable terms possible.

He then cited the 1947 Studebaker Champion designed by Raymond Loewy (with a lot of help by Bob Bourke),

whic dmnstrate wih great cariy thee wa pn tothe mall manuacturedes - —   I ew e      u.

inustrial designersbility n integrity be establihed

ek

In other words, he wanted designers to have an influential managerial role in industry, not simply function as artistic stylists. Nelson himself was a living example of design's future beyond "styling" as an influential director of design for Herman Miller. Over the next decades, many designers would climb the corporate ranks to become vice presidents with significant influence in business strategies.

m  SIal  IDubeU..I Design, 1949-1950, with 190 pages and 389 photographs, showing the work of members Egmont Arens, Robert Davol Budlong, Dave Chapman, Donald Dailey, Donald Deskey, Henry Dreyfuss, Gordon Florian, Lurelle Guild, Raymond Loewy, Peter Müller-Munk, Joseph Platt, George Sakier, Brooks Stevens, Viktor Schreckengost, Walter Dorwin Teague, Harold Van Doren, Russel Wright, and many others.

Speakers included Walter Dorwin Teague, who spoke Rightness Resurgent";Donald Dailey, who presented a case history; Carl Sundberg, who n DeigningorThermoplasticMateria" Emont Arens, whopoke n Low esu Molding of Plastics" J. The seminar fee was \$500.

By 1949, Raymond Loewy Associates was incorporated and became known as the Raymond Loewy Corporation (RLC), which at that time had a staff of 200 working under partners Loewy; William Snaith (managed all department store work); A. Baker Barnhardt (in charge oll packaging, product, and transportation); and John Breen (business manager). Raymond Loewy Inc. also included many soon-to-be-famous designers who worked for Loewy without any public recognition for years, since only Loewy was allowed to receive design credit. Among them were Richard Latham, Peter Thompson, George Jensen, Clare Hodgman, Robert Bourke, Virgil Exner, Robert T. Tyler, Franz Wagner, Joseph Parriott, Jay Doblin, Gordon Buehrig, Carl Otto, Don Defano, Tucker Madawick, and countless others. In 1949, Loewy's organization designed a complete line of electrical home appliances, including refrigerators, ranges, automatic washers, ironers, and dryers for the Frigidaire Division of General Motors. The refrigerator was a simple white box, with only the handle end accented by a delicate chrome circle, a visual "target" to open the door.

But the most successful result of SID's publicity campaign was when Loewy pulled off the greatest postwar industrial design public relations coup by appearing on the cover of Time magazine on October 31, 1949, with the caption, "He Streamlines the Sales Curve." On the cover was a color drawing by Boris Artzybasheff (18991965), the famous artist who created many Time cover portraits of world personalities. This one featured a large image of Loewy's face, surrounded by ghostly images of Loewy's designs, including his 1950 Studebaker, S-1 locomotive, Greyhound bus, aircraft, boat, furniture, and appliance packages, all foating in a bright, blue sky background around Loewy's head.

"Blue sky," ironically, is a common design term describing imaginative but often l  e.Teruv was in the "Business and Finance" section on page 68, titled "Modern Living: Up from the Egg" (Loewy had often described the egg as a perfect design), and reviewed a number of Loewy's current designs in progress, which included the new two-level Greyhound Scenicruiser, designed in 1946 but just being road tested, a face-lift for Manhattan's Gimbel Brothers department store, and models for three new ocean liners for American President Lines.

Included in the text was a brief history of industrial design and references to Loewy's pioneer colleagues who brought art to the Machine Age, including Teague, Dreyfuss, and Van Doren. Egmont Arens was cited as making chairs more comfortable by taking clay impressions of fat, skinny, and in-between posteriors to make a one-piece plastic chair; Dreyfuss for his form-fitting modern toilets for the Crane Company; Teague for Boeing Stratocruiser interiors, and Bel Geddes for transforming 10,000 square feet of lobby, dining, and storage space into a modern theater that extended the stage into the audience.

Loewy was the Steve Jobs of the day, and for similar reasons. They didn't invent the technology, but they took it and turned it into life-changing physical products that consumers loved and valued. There was a colorful description of Loewy as a person in the Time article:

The Shy Salesman. Suave, grey-haired, medium sized (5 ft. 10 in.), Loewy talks in a subdued voice that is, at the same time, apologetic and compelling. His face is reposed, gentle, sad, and as inscrutable as a Monte Carlo croupier. Obsessively shy, he is always "Mr. Loewy" to his associates.. Despite his shyness, he is a crack salesman who throws no artistic tantrums. Far from turning out designs with offhand sureness, he works them over painstakingly until the client is satisfied. He also has an almost hypnotic power to impress, persuade and convince the toughest tycoon.

The Time article also described Loewy's extravagant lifestyle, and what he did with his annual gross income that exceeded \$3 million (equivalent to \$29 million in 2013 dollars), compared to \$1 billion in yearly sales of Loewy-designed products (equivalent to \$9.77 billion in 2013). In addition to his Manhattan apartment atop a building on 58th Street, in 1946 he had designed and built a \$100,000 (\$977,000 in 2013) dream house, Tierra Caliente, in the desert near Palm Springs, California, complete with an outdoor swimming pool that curved into the living room. Summer found him in France, where he had three homes, one a red-tiled villa, Uriane, in St. Tropez, purchased in 1930; another a 16th-century manor, La Cense, outside Paris, built by Henry IV, and purchased by Loewy in 1933; and an apartment on Paris's Quai d'Orsay.6 Not mentioned was the Loewy home in Tetelpan, Mexico, built in the 1940s and in 1948.

The use of the term "streamline" on the cover of Time magazine with Loewy's picture illustrated the persistent popularity of the term in 1949 to describe industrial designed products. By this time, the surviving holdout for 1930s "streamlining" was the automotive industry, led by General Motors. Most design professionals were abandoning the term, as well as the practice, on stationary products. Nevertheless, by coincidence, that same month, Harold Van Doren, then president of SID, who was also part of SID's promotion campaign, wrote a feature article, "Streamlining: Fad or Function?" in the October 1949 issue of Design, the monthly journal of the British Council of Industrial Design (CoID, established in London in 1944), which had just begun publication of its professional journal. Van Doren defended streamlining, which had been under fire by both the Museum of Modern Art (MoMA) and Europeans: "Much so-called streamlining is imposed on the designer by the necessity of obtaining low cost through high-speed production." Using the refrigerator as an example, he compared the traditional costly process of bending sheet metal at sharp angles and assembling multiple components, versus the large metal stamping presses adopted by most manufacturers since about 1936:

Since steel stampings techniques make it impossible to produce hard, 90 degree edges of pointed cors ay g y reihe radii and softer contours.

He concluded his defense: "Is it fair to damn the designer to eternity because his designs echo the one really new visual phenomenon of his age?

Van Doren essentially was saying that most of what critics were calling streamlining was the result of manufacturing requirements for economy, which was exactly the explanation he more r tha.

The AT&T 500 telephone designed by Henry Dreyfuss Associates (HDA) was introduced in 1949, a sleeker and more compact version of the original 1937 model. The unsung designer was Robert Hose, an associate of HDA. In 1954, the 500 would be the first telephone to be offered in colors other than black, and would still be the most commonly used phone in the United States in 1995. In December 1949, contributing to the Society of Industrial Designer (SID) ongoing campaign to promote industrial design, Dreyfuss made an address to the American Society of Engineering Educators. He suggested a revision to the traditional three eleents manufacturing relie upon for success: "utility, quality, and price," which he said He e trial designer must consider":

Convenience of use (including utility and safety).

2. Maintenance.

Costs (consideration of tooling, generalmanufacturingcosts, anddistribution problems).

Sales (merchandising knowledge, public acceptance, and awareness of competition).

Apeance astbut not least, the ese ust hiswlee fo, e, - tion and color to integrate the product into a pleasing whole).

These principles would become the credo of HDA, repeated in magazine interviews and lectures, and were picked up by many industrial designers well into the future. In his address, Dreyfuss then criticized the traditional use of extraneous decoration, imitation of materials other than those used in manufacture, and sentimental icons:

With the application of this formula by the industrial designer, the extraneous decoration which the manufacturer had previously thought sufficient attraction to sell his product, began to disappear. Sullivan, that great forerunner of modern architecture, said that form should follow function. The industrial designer picked up that watchword, and rose buds, griffen's heads, and other doo-dads became increasingly conspicuous by their absence. In short, the industrial designer permitted the function of the product to dictate its appearance.

Mindful that he was speaking to engineers, Dreyfuss tactfully added: "I should like to underscore here the fact that no industrial designer worthy of the name could contemplate this work whout e co catonngs.

In the fall of 1949, the 1950 Studebaker was on the market, priced at \$1,487 (\$14,500 in 2013) and designed by Loewy's South Bend Studebaker studio, with a staff of 30 under Robert Bourke. The body was basically the same as the successful 1947 model for cost-reduction purposes, so the design needed a dramatic front end to make it obviously different than the 1947. Bourke was competing for its design with Virgil Exner, who was in 1947 working directly for Studebaker's vice president of engineering, Roy Cole. Bourke won the competition and secured the Studebaker account for Loewy, Inc., over the next seven years with his famous aircraft nose spinner (see the 1949 Ford, described earlier) to create the now classic "bullet-nose" Studebaker. The first month, Studebaker sold more cars than any in its history. During its production life of three years, over 500,000 would be sold. Studebaker would become the largest independent carmaker, fourth under the Big Three, and the only serious challenger to Detroit styling. In 1948, Exner had left Studebaker and returned to Detroit to work for Chrysler, where he would become director of styling.

Ever since 1940, when it created its own separate department of industrial design, the Museum of Modern Art, led by architects, had been trying to establish its influence on the field of industrial design, which had been monopolized by nonarchitect Machine Age pioneers. MoMA had consistently criticized the designs on the market as "modernistic and streamlined aberrations." In 194l it had conducted its Organic Design in Home Furnishing Competition, won by architects, and promoted "organic" design (no "vain ornamentation or superfluity"; read: no streamlining) as its aesthetic standard. In 1946, the new director of MoMA's industrial design department, Edgar Kaufmann, Jr., had invited members of the American Designers Institute (ADI) and the Society of Industrial Designers (SID) to attend a Conference on Industrial Design: A New Profession. The discussion was largely philosophical and resulted in no specific actions. If MoMA's objective was to gain a voice in the new profession, it failed to do so. So MoMA decided to go it alone and jump into the consumer market, acting as an arbiter of public taste.

In January 1950, the first Good Design exhibition opened in the marketing center of the Chicago Merchandise Mart. It was conceived and launched by Kaufmann, in collaboration with MoMA's director, René D'Harnoncourt, and Wallace O. Ollman, general manager of the Mart, who issued this joint statement:

It is the first time an art museum and [a] wholesale merchandising center have cooperated to present the best examples of modern design in home furnishings... [We] believe and hope that in combining their resources they will stimulate the appreciation and creation of the best design among manufacturers, designers and retailers.10

There would be three Good Design exhibitions annually over the next five years: at the winter market in January and the summer market in July, both of these at the Merchandise Mart, and in the fall, selections were transported to New York for display at MoMA during the pre-Christmas period. Selection of home furnishings for the exhibition was by a threeperson committee consisting permanently of Kaufmann, and a designer and retailer appointed for each show. Public opinion was studiously avoided. Selections of 150 to 250 products were chosen from the showrooms of various manufacturers at the Mart and relocated to the exhibition on the 17th floor. At the winter market, the exhibition was open only to wholesale buyers, but after that it would be open to the public, with a guided tour offered for a fee. A well-known designer or architect designed each exhibition. A "Good Design" tag was attached to each selected design, all of which were available in the open market, and each manufacturer could purchase a kit of promotional materials, including "Good Design" tags, labels, suggested window displays, and literature to promote sales of their product as a MoMA-selected "Good Design."

What exactly was "Good Design"? Kaufmann defined it like this:

A frequent misconception is that the principle purpose of goodmodern design i tofacilitate trade, and that biale are a proof of excellence. Not so. Sales are episodes in the .

Thus sales, the fundamental purpose of industrial design, was toss aside. This seemesmewhat hypoal from a man who conceived the entire "Good Desgn" p-"Good Design" labels. Further, the first consideration for selection seemed to not be use, but simply aesthetic, abstract, and artistic presentation of form. Elliot Noyes in 1941 had said "Good Design shows the taste and good sense of the designers. On none is there arbitrarily applied decoration."I

![](images/639c0d316336fe5b52d4353916dcaa3246f2bc5e1597ef6c6e019ad92bdb1095.jpg)  
"Good Design" tag.

"Arbitrarily applied decoration," to MoMA, included any commercial message, such as an obvious brand name or decal, or any element that interrupted the purity of geometric form. This definition was "art" masquerading as "design," with the only criteria being the personal aesthetic choice of the individual designer. Genuine designers design not for themselves, but to meet the limitations and sales needs of manufacturers, to adapt to the physical requirements o users, and to appeal to consumers at the point of sale. Eva Zeisel 06-2011), revered ceramic tableware designer who won countless "Good Desn awards, at ge 0 said Good D  MMA u. 'Good Design' was nothing more than lack of applied decoration.

Actall, it ws more than that.The MoMA sndar  "Go Des" wee es make designs more interesting, more personal, and more meaningful to average consumers. Faber Birren (1900-1988), a famous color consultant of the 1950s who monitored and published color trends, once remarked that any product recognized by MoMA as art was "the kiss odeath" commercially. The bottom ine was this: "Good Design" depended entirely on how it was defined and judged, and by whom. It is interesting to compare MoMA definitions of "Good Design" with the pragmatic business definitions of industrial design by Henry Dreyfuss. He was not impressed with the museum's idea of good design. When asked by visitors from an English museum in which museums he would like to have his designs exhibited, his reply was, "They are called Macy's, Marshall Field's, and the May Company—similar to your Eaton's, Simpson's, and Morgan's."13

A number of "Good Design" awards went to imported European products. Europe had largely recovered from World War II, found distribution for their products in the United States, and promoted them through trade exhibitions. Germany, Japan, and Italy had rebuilt their manufacturing capabilities, opening the gates for exported automobiles, furniture, and electronic products to the American market, and stiffening the competition for all U.S. manufacturers. Among these imports were Scandinavian products in 1948; the Volkswagen Beetle from Germany, which entered the U.S. market in 1949; and the French line of porcelainenameled cast-iron cookware, Le Creuset, in 1950.

The Museum of Modern Art and other U.S. museums promoted European design aggressively through exhibitions of Italian designs and designers such as Marcello Nizzoli (1887- 1969) of Olivetti (typewriters), and Corradino D'Ascanio (18911981) of Lambretta (motor scooters) and Giovanni Ponti (1913-1993). It seemed as if art museums were clinging to their age-old assumption that European design was always superior to American design and were urging U.S. designers and industry to imitate.

It never seemed to occur to museums that U.S. design had a unique personality of its own, or that it was U.S. design that was inspiring and leading the rest of the world to seek American markets. If other countries wanted to access the enormous U.S. market, they had to design for the U.S. consumer, and to accomplish this they often needed American industrial designers to advise them, or to hire American designers to work overseas.

But now, another war began, only 10 years after the last, this time in western Asia.

# Competition and Controversy

The Korean War began in June 1950, threatening industry with restrictions of critical materials. Peter Müller-Munk used the threat of austerity to urge manufacturers to reduce excessive decoration of products:

The electrical and automotive industries.. have reveled in . superfluous shiny decoration . to catch the customer's eye and attention... The effect of this has been deplored by every serious designer.. Chrome and fancy trim used freely without a design need now can be easily taken off.

In 1950 the New York and Philadelphia design offices of Van Doren, Nowland, & Schladermundt announced they would operate as two independent firms. Van Doren remained in control of his Philadelphia office as Harold Van Doren & Associates, and the New York firm became known as Nowland & Schladermundt.

Brooks Stevens designed the Skylark pattern for Formica, the postwar surface of choice for kitchen and bathroom countertop surfaces. Skylark was called the "boomerang" pattern, because of the multicolor, thin line shapes resembled boomerangs, as well as alluding to supersonic airplane wings and modernity. Henry Dreyfuss Associates designed an aluminum outdoor telephone booth for AT&T, which became ubiquitous across the country until cell phones became the norm in the 1990s. C. Stowe Myers was recruited from Teague to join Loewy, Inc., in New York. He would manage Loewy's Chicago office from 1952 to 1954. Arthur BecVar, head of design at General Electric in Bridgeport, Connecticut, relocated in 1950 to Louisville, Kentucky, to become GE's manager of industrial design for major appliances. Donald McFarland would become manager of product planning at GE Bridgeport and would later replace BecVar as head of small appliances.

In Chicago, Robert Budlong designed a transverse, two-slot Radiant Control T-20 toaster for Sunbeam that lowered bread automatically and raised itself silently when done. It would remain in production until 1995. Also in 1950, Budlong designed, with Sunbeam staff designer Ivar Jepson, the compact Sunbeam Shavemaster electric shaver with a smooth rounded head, the first compact, ergonomic shape held in the palm of the hand, rather than the previous elongated shape held like a hammer. He continued to design radio cabinets for Zenith.

Five years after the war, the U.S. economy was back to normal, and businesses were humming along, despite the Korean War, which had reinitiated the draft for single young men. T into the smallest of manufacturing companies. Annual salaries for designers started at about \$4,500 (\$43,500 in 2013 dollars). There was no longer a question of the value of industrial design to successful products, but there remained a residual resentment by some engineering managers, who were still in charge of most product development, and who guarded their traditional authority in matters of product design. Proposals by industrial designers, even the most famous ones, were not always accepted.

But designers persevereandinteratedthemselves int sometime hostile envionments. Americans were in the mood to spend, manufacturers still had little international competition, T good times.

By 1951, many Americans were living comfortable middle-clas lives, with all the latest appliances, cars, and furnishings. Russel and Mary Wright were designing paper Christmas pottery. They also wrote and published A Guide to Easier Living, which placed them in the gested a variety of ways that young amiles could better rganize their homes and make housekeeping easier, such as buying furniture that was easy to clean, how to organize a room for maxiu li acsl wa mait pent natti  ement.

The American Designers Institute (ADI) changed its name to the Industrial Designers Insiute D)ier res heuiciicm byhe SctyIl Designers (SID) that "industrial design" was absent in its title, thus accepting members from any specializ producidustry, r evn oneo-kincame. SID, ocourse, nlycc designers who designed products in a variety of mass-production industries. This remained as a fundamental difference in the two organizations, as well as their geographical separation of headquarters (ADI in Chicago, SID in New York). The subtle change in title was enough to convince the Chicago Society of Industrial Designers (CSID), founded by Dave Chapman and others in 1938, to merge with IDI. Chapman had been president of SID in 1950, and Russel Wright in 1951. SID had chapters in New York, Chicago, Detroit, and Los Angeles. The competing organizations were growing closer. By 1955, IDI would relocate its headquarters from Chicago to New York, where many of its 200 members resided.

In 1951, IDI, possibly inspired by MoMA's "Good Design" exhibitions, initiated the first national industrial design awards program, which recognized the best designs and credited the actual designers by name. This did not sit well with their rival society, SID, where large offices like Loewy, Dreyfuss and Teague never credited anyone but their namesake. But over the next 15 years of these awards programs, most SID members began to credit individual designers, except Dreyfuss, who remained adamant about the issue. No designs from Henry Dreyfuss Associates (HDA) ever won IDI awards, probably because none were submitted. Nevertheless, the awards broke the ice, and many individual designers finally received public design credit for their work, which greatly enhanced their reputations and careers.

Dreyfuss appeared on the cover of Forbes magazine in 195l, part of the Society of Industrial Designers' publicity campaign. His firm that year designed an innovative round wall thermostat for Honeywell, the interiors for American Export Lines' SS Independence and SS Constitution ocean liners, and a lavatory sink for Crane.

Carl Otto resigned from Raymond Loewy in 1950 to establish his own firm, Carl Otto Design, with offices in New York, Stockholm, and London. His New York office was in the Squib building on Fifth Avenue. He hired Harold Vanderhyde; Jay Doblin (1920-1989), recently director of the night school at the Pratt Institute; Albrecht Graf von Goertz, the equivalent of an Earl (all ex-Loewy men); and Alban Gifford Jackson (b. 1922), a New Zealand boat designer who had just arrived in America in 1949, and who in 2013, at age 91, would receive New Zealand's Order of Merit for his distinguished career in industrial design.

Otto and his staff designed the 1951 Schick Model 20 electric shaver in collaboration with Norman Gray, chief engineer at Schick, Inc., reducing the bulk and length of the shaver by rearranging the internal components from the traditional "in-line" configuration to a more compact, wider shape, more easily held. The design won an IDI design award in 1951 and became the standard typeform for shavers thereafter, reaching six million in sales.

Otto's firm would also design the 1952 Edison Voicewriter portable recorder (competitor to Dictaphone), which would win an IDI award in 1953. That year, Otto worked on an account in England from the Standard Motor Company to design the Triumph Vanguard. He had hired Tucker Madawick, an automotive design expert with Loewy, to help with clay models and the final design. Otto's international firm would close in 1954, and Madawick would return to the Loewy office as an account executive. Clare Hodgman had also resigned from Loewy in 1951.

John Gordon Rideout, one of the founders of the Society of Industrial Designers in 1944, had closed his Chagrin Falls, Ohio, office in 1946 and passed away at age 53 in 1951. Greta Von Nessen designed the Anywhere lamp, which could be used on a table, mounted on the wall, or suspended from the ceiling. Franz Wagner, Richard Latham, and Don Defano, of the Raymond Loewy Corporation, designed the elegant Flight bathroom scale that the Borg-Erikson Corporation would introduce in 1953. It would be selected by Fortune magazine as one of the top 500 designs of all time and would win an IDI national design award in 1954. In Chicago, John Morgan patented a deep fryer.

In 1951, Walter Paepcke, head of the Container Corporation of America, initiated the International Design Conference in Aspen (IDCA), which would become an annual event, bringing top designers and philosophers to the lovely natural beauty of the Colorado ski resort. Among the founding members of IDCA was H. Creston Doner, director of the Libby-Owens-Ford design department.

T reputation. In 1951, Walter Dorwin Teague, in an article promoting American design in Britain's Art & Industry magazine, chided his British colleagues for

a certain amount of "ribbing" …on what they consider our penchant for "streamlining" … [which has become a popular metaphor or anything mooth and sveland it i carefully avoide by designers except when it can be used with technical accuracy [as in aerodynamics].

Oure hi e he t w ding enal   Aeiul design in general, the automotive industry continued to utilize streamlining "with technical accuracy." Harley Earl and General Motors (GM) led the automotive design field. In 1951 Earl exhibited a concept convertible called LeSabre, named after the North American F-86 Sabre p   Warh  s. It e curved, wraparound windshield, which had been in development by Libby-Owens-Ford Glass Company since 1946. Tail fins and a grille simulating a jet plane air intake completed the aircraft theme. LeSabre would set GM style trends for the next ten years, as cars became lower, sportier, and costlier.

Few American car designs were included in the Museum of Modern Art's exhibition 8 Automobiles in 1951, MoMA's attempt to subtly criticize the current style trends of Detroit, and to suggest what Detroit should be designing. The eight examples were chosen as historical works of art, MoMA's usual definition of "Good Design." Five of the eight were European; the only U.S. designs were the 1937 Cord, the 1940 Lincoln Continental, and the 1941 wartime military Jeep.

Jeep, 1941.

In 1951, Loewy wrote and published his book, Never Leave Well Enough Alone, which was a flamboyant, romanticized, and self-promotional autobiography that influenced scores Loewy enjoyed. As a hobby in 1952, Raymond Loewy established an office in Paris, Compagnie de L'Esthetique Industrielle (CEI, Company of Industrial Esthetics), headed by Harold Barnett. F Europe.

design salaries and fees were being reduced because demand no longer exceeded supply, as it had in the 1930s and 1940s.

In 1952, Hall Cutlery introduced a stainless steel flatware pattern called Highlight, designed by Russel Wright, to match his design of Highlight dinnerware, introduced by Hall in 1951. The dinnerware had won MoMA's Home Furnishing Award. The Highlight flatware was the first modern and contemporary flatware designed in the United States since World War II and would be imitated by many competitors. Wright thought it was his best and informally called it "Pinch" because of the way the narrow part of the

![](images/917dc4e4df346f58ce5a76cccc26c3399240311faf9ad9b133527165c778ee8f.jpg)  
Hall Cutlery Highlight flatware, 1952, by Russel Wright (from Russel Wright Designs, by Kerr).

handle just before the business end was "pinched" into a small but strong diameter. In 1952, Wright's wife Mary died; their only adopted daughter, Annie, was only two years old. Wright closed his studio and retired to his retreat, Manitoga, a wooded mountainside in Garrison, New York, where he would build his modernist home and Dragon Rock, his studio. There, he would raise and care for his daughter as a single parent.

![](images/1f3c2a10c737d6b0c54c70e797edd3c17fd6471470107cdf5b8ba9802e58be47.jpg)

In 1953, the Northern Industrial Chemical Company would introduce Wright's innovative and inexpensive plastic dinnerware called Residential. It would be the first-ever plastic tableware, which was injection molded in high quantities using Melmac, the trade name of a melamine plastic compound. With Wright's name, known for his informal casual living designs and styles over the previous 15 years, Residential was highly popular. The design would receive MoMA "Good Design" awards that year and the next.

The Korean War ended with an armistice in July 1953, allowing a return to peacetime activities. Veterans were eligible for benefits under the G.I. Bil, including state and federal for education and unemployment compensation. Returning veterans would result in another surge of graduates by 1957, including additions to the growing number of industrial designers.

Walter Dorwin Teague Associates (WDTA) had grown to about 200 designers, one of the largest consultancies in the country. Its office, at 444 Madison Avenue in New York, was spread out over three floors. Teague's son, W. Dorwin, had left Bendix and rejoined the organization in 1953 as an associate. A year later, he was made a full partner, with 8 percent shares of the profit, the same as all the other partners, of which there were six (his father received 42 percent). Dorwin designed a .22 rifle for Remington Arms, and WDTA soon designed a Steinway piano and delivery trucks for UPS.

The year 1953 saw several innovative automobile designs. One was the Chevrolet Corvette, designed by Harley Earl and his GM staff. It was he rs U.S. srt cr u i and the first with an all-fiberglass body. It was an instant commercial success and became an immediate classic. Another classic was introduced the same year, the Studebaker Starliner, designed by Robert E. Bourke of Raymond Loewy's group in South Bend, including Tucker Madawick. The Starliner blended European and

W. Dorwin Teague, Jr., ca. 1960 (courtesy Harry Teague).

U.S. design styles into a low, sleek, and graceful masterpiece, acclaimed by the Museum of Modern Art as a work of art, an extremely rare compliment to any American design by MoMA. The Raymond Loewy Corporation was also responsible for Greyhound's double-deck PD-4501 Scenicruiser bus, largely designed by Richard Latham, that would be introduced in 1954. Belle Kogan won an IDI award for excellence in glassware.

DvChaan Dvhaan, Inc Chico oIstrial ers Ie (IDI) national design award in 1954 for his innovative School Furniture Line, manufactured by the Brunswick-Balke-Collender Company. Chapman that yearorganizeda subsidiary company, Design Research, Inc., to provide long-range planning for clients.

Gustav Jensen, one of the ten most prominent achine desinersof the 1930s, who continued as a one-man studio after World War II making designs and sculpture, passed away at age 56 in 1954, largely forgotten.

Aeh and had expanded the program to four years. In 1952 he had created an Experimental Design Laboratory at Pratt, providing professional company designers office space adjacent to student drafting and shop areas. They conducted experimental work, lectured, and advised students. Pcati incu Gal otors he esC

Corporation. In 1954, both Kostellow and his wife, Rowena, designed a Kitchen of Tomorrow for GM's Frigidaire division that was exhibited at GM's annual Motorama.
