The United States' 20-year monopoly in industrial design was coming to an en, with manv ue  parhv oan international design community. New professional design organizations and international consulting groups were formed in a number of European countries and Japan. Japan established industrial design programs with the assistance of a number of U.S. designers, including Russel Wright, George Nelson, Freda Diamond and Jean Reinecke, who traveled to Japan after 1950 to assist in this process.

Germany's Rosenthal China engaged the Raymond Loewy Corporation (RLC) as consultants. In 1954, urged by Richard Latham, head of Loewy's Chicago office, Rosenthal began using international designers for its special division of Studio-Linie products, starting with Coffee Service 2000 by Latham for RLC. These products were distributed internationally in special showrooms called Studio Houses, and over the next 30 years, Rosenthal would employ more than 100 designers from 11l nations for this exclusive line. Industrial design was in the process of becoming an international and global business. Latham in 1955 would team together with Bob Tyler and George Jensen, all previously on Loewy's Chicago design staff, to form the new firm of Latham-Tyler-Jensen, with a staff of 14.

In 1954, The Tenth Triennale was held in Milan, Italy, with the theme "Industrial Aesthetics." Peter Müller-Munk, then president of the Society of Industrial Designers (SID), sent exhibits of 40 U.S. products designed by its members for exhibition in Milan, including two Buckminster Fullergeodesi domes, the innovative architectural structures that would make Fuller famous. It was also the tenth anniversary of the founding of SID, and to celebrate its anniversary, SID published Industrial Design in America, a 224-page hardcover book with color illustrations and case histories, publicizing the work of its members. In addition, because industrial design was becoming a worldwide phenomenon, the name of the organization was changed to the American Society of Industrial Designers (ASID). At the time, there were 153 members with chapters in New York, Chicago, Cleveland, and Los Angeles.

![](images/c5af5683758048baed77a6c8ecf49dfe21aa1b1e97f1caf9ac7bfad0e4ef3fdd.jpg)  
Raymond Loewy with Studebaker Starliner, 1953.

Also in 1954, Industrial Design, the first magazine dedicated solely to the profession, founded and edited by Jane Fiske Mitarachi (later, Jane Thompson), published its first issue. Thompson had been editor of Interiors magazine in which a regular "Industrial Design" section had been initiated by Donald Dohner in 1943, and which had been continued after his death by his partner J. Gordon Lippincott in 1944. The first issue of Industrial Design magazine initiated its Annual Design Review, which featured the magazine's selections of the "best designs" of the year, which would become a promotional tool for designers to submit entries and be recognized for their work. Recognition by ID magazine was a highly coveted honor valued by designers, clients, and employers.

Stowe Myers opened his own design office, Stowe Myers Design, in Evanston, Illinois, in 1954. He would receive awards for his design of buildings and exhibits at the U.S. exhibit for the 1959 International Trade Fair in Casablanca, Morocco, as well as designs for Tinkertoy packaging, a CENCO Instruments laboratory meter, and a Rheem Manufacturing Company water heater drain valve.

By 1954, interest in MoMA's annual Good Design exhibitions was slowing down. During that year's winter market, in addition to the Good Design exhibit, the Merchandise Mart staged an exhibition called Good Companions: Today in Tradition, which showed "what the manufacturers of traditional furniture, fabrics and lamps are providing for the homes of today." The reality was that "Good Design," as defined by MoMA, was not popular either with the general public, or with the majority of home furnishing manufacturers, who, unlike MoMA, depended on public acceptance for their livelihood. Even today, traditional (antique) home furshiy el allmehi bywn.

So much for the home furnishings industry, but MoMA still wanted to expand its architectural idea of "Good Design" into what had become the exclusive territory of industrial designers: electromechanical products, major appliances, and automobiles (the latter at its 1951 8 Automobiles show). Accordingly, the jury for the 1955 winter market exhibition of "Good Design" included Arthur BecVar, head of design at General Electric in Louisville, Kentucky, and then president of the American Society of Industrial Designers (ASID). It was MMA' first token recognition organized design on any "Good Design" jury. BecVar, Edgar Kaufmann, Jr., and Just Lunning of Georg Jensen's New York store selected 400 products for this final Good Design exhibition, which Interiors magazine noted with surprise included a numbe dustrial desis that hardly qualifes hoeurishings," suc  cmeas, t phones, kitchen equipment, and household appliances.3 The historical dividing line between household furnishings (the former art industries) and mechanical equipment (the former artlesindustris) was gradually beingerased, but he Good Desig exhibitins were concluded with the resignation  Edgar Kaufmann, Jr., from MoMA in ."Good Design" ha un its course.

Howe  G  B iv. I    y Hocchu GluH I For hi  Ul "New Bauhaus" and funded by the German government and the American High Commission

to foster national economic recovery for Germany. Max Bill (1908-1994), a former Bauhaus student, was named director. The school initially focused on product design, but by 1956 it would abandon this in favor of pure theory.

At the same time, Max Braun's sons, Artur and Erwin, were reorganizing the German appliance company Max had founded in 1921. They were inspired by the HfG in Ulm and initiated a corporate design program emulating the Bauhaus tradition, headed by Dieter Rams. The company's products—spare, undecorated, and geometric in form and neutral in color—soon began to be introduced internationally.

![](images/a84ee4d298e5c7b84c0b1964db0eb71a52bb40407719e37de11d78b5115d0d76.jpg)  
Braun Kitchen Machine, 1957, by Gerd Alfred Müller.

In 1954, Henry Dreyfuss Associates had designed the interiors of Columbine, President Eisenhower's presidential plane, and in 1955 his design of the unique, spherical-shaped Model 8 canister vacuum cleaner (that foated on its own exhaust air) for the Hoover Company debuted. But Hoover balked at his high fees and, according to some engineers at Hoover, at his arrogance in insisting on impractical design changes. Hoover dropped him for a newer, younger, less costly, and local consulting firm, Smith, Scherr, & McDermott of Akron, Ohio, which had been founded in 1947. In Philadelphia, Philco introduced Harold Van Doren's design for the first refrigerator with a two-way opening door in 1955, with a V handle in the center that pulled left or right. Detroit firm Sundberg-Ferar designed IBM's 702 computer.

Chicago industrial designer Robert Budlong passed away in 1955 at age 53, and his business was taken over by one of his associates, Ken Shory, Sr. (19211991); the business was renamed Ken Schory Associates. Schory would later partner with Tom Steinbach, and their firm would be named Schory-Steinbach Associates. Also in Chicago, Waltman Associates, headed by Chauncey "Chick" Waltman, was designing a sewing machine for Sears, Roebuck, and a three-way mixer for Nutone.

Ann Swainson, the lady who initiated the Bureau of Design at Montgomery Ward in 1931, died in 1955 at the age of 55. Swainson remained head of the Bureau as Sewell Avery was decimating the company. Avery had led the firm through its phenomenal growth in the 1930s, but during and after the war, he was convinced there was going to be another Depression. He pulled back on any modernization or expansion. In 1953, as Sears was approaching its second billion-dollar mark, Montgomery Ward fell below one billion. By 1955, the Bureau was reduced to Swainson and an assistant, confined to a desk in a corridor of the warehouse building. That year, Avery would be ousted, but it was too late. The loss of her Bureau was too much for Swainson.4 In 1955, Dave Chapman would return to Montgomery Ward as a consultant, where he had worked from 1934 to 1936 under Swainson, to reorganize and reform the Bureau of Design, which would now be headed by Frederick W. Priess, one of Swainson's former designers. The department would survive until 1976, when Mobil acquired Sears.

The U.S. Department of State in 1955 retained Russel Wright to survey possibilities for developing cottage industries in Southeast Asia. He explored native handicrafts and established centers of manufacturing for these products. George Nelson incorporated his office in New York as George Nelson Associates, Inc., and relocated his studio to 251 Park Avenue South. He had opened his initial design studio in 1947.

Clare Hodgman and Robert E. Bourke, colleagues from both Sears and Loewy organizations, joined to form an independent office, Hodgman and Bourke, Industrial Design Consultants, in New York in 1955. Their accounts would include Coca-Cola, Mack Trucks, GAF cameras, Volvo, Superior Coach, and Electrolux, SA. Hodgman lived in Mamaroneck and was awarded dozens of design patents.

In 1955, the first retractable hardtop car by Brooks Stevens, the Gaylord Grand Prix, was introduced, as was the Ford Thunderbird, designed by George Walker and his styling staff. That year, Walker became vice president of styling at Ford, a position he would hold until 1961. But criticism of Detroit design was still rising.

Henry Dreyfuss published his book Designing for People in 1955, which encapsulates his personal design philosophy on the title page: "We bear in mind that the object being worked u  it i, lokea,alkntoatia era   sh w used by people individually or en masse."

His book included recollections of his career and illustrations of his most important work. Perhaps most importantly, it introduced "Joe and Josephine," diagrammatic charts of typical anthropometric males and females, in typical standing, sitting and reaching positions, each with appropriate average body dimensions indicated as if on a mechanical drawing, as well as comparable measurements of the largest (97.5) and smallest (2.5) percentiles of people. Joe and Josephine's children were also diagrammed. Dreyfuss had accumulated most of these measurements during World War II, when he measured large cohorts of military personnel in order to design equipment and control panels. The Joe and Josephine charts would be invaluable to the profession as scientific measurement tools.

The book established Dreyfuss as a leader in what was at the time called "human factors," later better known as ergonomics: the specialty area of adapting product designs to human sizes and functional physical capabilities. Henry Dreyfuss Associates would later expand and improve the Joe and Josephine measurements into numerous detailed charts that would become essential industrial design tools. The next year, Bell Telephone Laboratories debuted a wallmounted telephone designed by HDA, which became popular in kitchens where counter space was at a premium.

The postwar period was over. The United States became involved in the Vietnam War in 1956 and would see an escalation of that war over the next 20 years, complicated by the Cold War with the Soviet Union that would last for 35 years. These events not only dominated national priorities but also contributed to vast cultural, social, political, and generational changes in the country over this period. Despite this, the industrial design profession advanced anepande owighe eaple an ndar t by theain der f the 1930s.

The Cold Wa was raging in 56 when Nikita Kruhcev made his We will buryou n      H olT moting U.S. interests. That same year, Walter Dorwin Teague Associates (WDTA) was selected Dare Cec he e  vi xhi M at Gorky Park. At the time, WDTA was also working on the interiors and furnishings for the new Air Force Academy and a new museum at West Point.

Eliot Noyes was eaie tdevelo ew IBM cooate es ylepo poration, much of which is still being used by IBM todav. Also in 1956. the Raymond Loewy Corporation was amended as Raymond Loewy and William Snaith: Ine. with William Snaith as managing partner.

In 195". the year before Sputnik ignited the Cold War space race to the moon. Walter Dorwin Teague Associates (WDTA) designed the U.S. pavilions at both the Zagreb Fair in Zagreb, Croatia, and the Vienna Fair, both funded by the U.S. Information Agency (U'SI.A). and both led and supervised by W. Dorwin Teague. Jr. Henry Dreyfuss Associates designed the "deer" logo for John Deere: Sundberg-Ferar designed the Miracle Kitchen for Whirlpool with a robotic vacuum cleaner; the Raymond Loewy Corporation designed the Alouette utility helicopter for the French firm, SNCASE; and Donald Deskey designed packaging for some of the most common and popular everyday household items, including Cheer detergent (1958) and Gleem toothpaste (1954).

Sadly, Harold Van Doren, one of the founders of the American Society of Industrial Designers. passed away in 1957 at age 62, and his Philadelphia firm was taken over by Harper Landel (1918- 1997). a Van Doren employee of ten years. The firm would be known as Harper Landell & Associates until it would dissolve in 19"5. Van Doren's papers can be found at Syracuse University Library, Special Collections.

During the decade after the war, John Vassos became involved with Greek war relief efforts through the American Relief for Greek Democracy (ARGD), whose honorary chairperson was Eleanor Roosevelt. Vassos became one of the most honored Greek Americans and developed close friendships with other well-known Greeks such as Aristotle Onassis. who would marry Jackie Kennedy in 1968. Besides his work for RCA and other companies. Vassos designed murals for theaters and studios, including the Riviera. Syosset, and Rivoli theaters in New York; the Egyptian Theatre in Los Angeles; the Condado Beach Hotel in Puerto Rico: the WCAY studio in Philadelphia; and the Radio and TV Center in Washingron. D.C. Vassos also designed trade and exhibition pavilions in New York. India, and Pakistan.

Brooks Sevens Desn Asiat esne a range o prouct pooype in 195r e Evinrude division of the Outboard Marine Company. including the 1+-foot-diameter Fisherman circular boat; the 16-foot Lark runabout: the Starflight cabin cruiser: the Housetloat houseboat: the Heli-bout, a combination helicopter boat: and the Showboat. a miniature version  1th-century Missppi iver packet.The were all bui attractattention at boa a    l produced in quantity.

In 195. Jean Reinecke relocated to Los Angeles and opened a second office in Pasadena, California, where he designed the 3M Decor Dispenser Model C-15. a swooping plastic desktop tape dispenser in numerous colors and textures, that would be introduced in 196l. and which wu  Heu  v. including MIT, Cal Tech, UCLA, Stanford, ITT, the Chicago Art Institute, and the University of Illinois.

Arthur BecVar, head of GE's industrial design group of +5 for major appliances in Louisville, Kentucky, designed a new kitchen center with Robert W. Blee. The nine-foot-long icu ahe A quph ne enl le

The Industrial Design Educators' Association (IDEA) was formed in 1957 for industrial design educators, who were denied membership in both the American Society of Industrial Designers (ASID) and the Industrial Designers Institute (IDI) because they were not active commercial design practitioners. The first president of IDEA was Joseph Carriero (1920- 1978), chair of the industrial design department at the Philadelphia Museum School of Art.

In London, the International Council of Industrial Designers (ICSID) was formed, comp nalionali l MID    h .  rs uol r the use of industrial design.

At a 1954 talk to the local advertising club in Minneapolis, Brooks Stevens had described the mission of industrial design as planned obsolescence," a phrase that would become almost as controversial in industrial design as "streamlining" had been. By 1956 Stevens was using the phrase frequently and defining it as instilling in the buyer the desire to own something a little newer, a little better, a little sooner than is necessary."Actually, to us in the 21st century, this sn lxa what aps wih theatt newt vrsippone new version of a popular product. Although Stevens admitted to originating the phrase, he did not, and he may not have known it, because he was a student at Cornell at the time when Elmo Calkins, the "Dean of Advertising" partner of Calkins & Holden advertising agency, in his 1932 book, What Consumer Engineering Really Is, wrote that

obsoletism is another device for stimulating consumption. The consideration of style is a consideration in buying many things. Clothes go out of style and are replaced long before they wear out. That principle extends to other products-motor cars, bathrooms, radios, foods, refrigerators, furniture.7

Calkins went on to say the principle of obsoletism was the solution for solving the Depression because buying things increases prosperity for business, consumers, and the country. In fact, that is precisely what happened.

But in Vance Packard's 1957 book, The Hidden Persuaders, Packard (1914-1996) accused industry for "manipulating" consumers and "inducing" desire. He pointed to Stevens' planned obsolescence," and by implication, all industrial designers, as the leading culprit in this "immoral" practice. In fact, he called Stevens "the crown prince of planned obsolescence." Packard's book reached a wide audience and was hailed by many as a noble cause, because the idea of secretly and deliberately manipulating consumers was considered underhanded in some way. Packard viewed the practice as wasteful, but producing only products that are functionally "necessary" because "old ones" were no longer usable might be morally "frugal," but would be totally unacceptable to modern consumers wanting something newer and better. Consumers have not just functional "needs," but also upward mobility "wants and aspirations." Packard, a reporter and writer, obviously knew little about business, design, or consumers, but it was a time when the public had become suspicious of industry in general, partly because anti-Vietnam War activists referred derisively to the "wartime military-industrial complex."

Nevertheless, Packard's book put industrial design on the defensive, and in 1958, Dave Chapman, Walter Dorwin Teague, and Henry Dreyfuss mounted a campaign to publicly address critics. They openly spoke out against what they called "phony obsolescence" that added gimmicks and superfluous changes to products, but they agreed with "planned obsolescence" that resulted in genuine product improvements. Stevens himself argued that the item that is replaced by a new product often finds its way into a lower-priced secondary market and is purchased by someone who otherwise would not have been able to afford a new one. He saw it as essentially recycling of material goods that benefited the lower class at the same time as it produced a healthy economy.

The controversy faded in a year or so, but it was an early warning that social issues were becoming more relevant to the public than technological or business issues. The generation of Depression survivors was aging. Many of them had since 1930 been without a decent standard of living and had struggled for five years after 1945 to acquire the material wealth and comfort of the middle class.

Now their children, the so-called baby boomers, blessed since birth with all the comforts and security their parents provided, had the luxury of concerning themselves with social, political, and moral issues such as civil rights, clean air, clean water, and environmental concerns. These would soon become mainstream preoccupations with the boomer generation, along with the nuclear age. The first nuclear power plant, in Shippingport, Pennsylvania, went into service in 1957, and in 1958, the first nuclear-powered merchant vessel was launched. In 1958, Walter Dorwin Teague Associates designed the U.S. Atoms for Peace exhibit for the Atomic Energy Commission, held in the Palais des Nations in Geneva, Switzerland, and Brooks Stevens Design Associates designed a futuristic Oscar Meyer Wienermobile, about which he declared, There's nothing more aerodynamic than a wiener."

Machine Designer giant Norman Bel Geddes passed away in 1958 at age 65. Since his ofos  he     e and theaters, including a television stage for NBC. This shift in priority may have been his tribute to his daughter, who had become a famous stage and screen actress. His autobiography, Miracle in the Evening, which he wrote starting in 1957, would be edited by William Kelly and published posthumously in 1960. It is a wonderfully written story of his early life but is primarily about his personal experiences, with very little about his professional industrial design career, other than a listing of his designs and clients at the end of the book.8

By the time of Bel Geddes's death, his daughter, Barbara Bel Geddes (1922-2005), had become a famous actress in films such as I Remember Mama (1948), for which she won an Academy Award for the Best Supporting Actress, and Broadway productions such as The Moon Is Blue (1951-1953). Before his death, Bel Geddes had the satisfaction of seeing the Federal Interstate Highway System under construction, initiated by President Eisenhower in 1956. It was the full-scale embodiment of Bel Geddes's 1939 miniature Futurama concepts, and at \$25 billion over ten years, it was the largest public works project in American history throughout that time. The archives of Bel Geddes's theater and industrial design papers reside at the Harry Ransom Center at the University of Texas in Austin.

The automotive industry was being threatened by foreign competition. From 1955 to 1959, as imports of Volkswagens, Karmann Ghias, Porsches and Mercedes from Germany; Toyotas and Nissans from Japan; Morris Mini Minors, Triumphs and MGs from England; and Saabs from Sweden increased from 1 percent to 10 percent of the U.S. market. Traditional Detroit brand names such as DeSoto disappeared, or merged to form Studebaker-Packard (1954) or American Motors (1954, Hudson/Nash).

But Detroit continued with the same styling approach it had followed the previous ten years. In 1957, the first vice president of styling at Chrysler, Virgil Exner, had introduced a new Fr or  l h h GenMs.T x tion to this pattern. In 156, under George Walker, Ford staff, including Gordon Buehrig, chief body designer, had sought to create the elegance of its classic 1940 Lincoln Continental with the new Lincoln Continental Mark II. It had no excessive tail fins, and in fact it seemed to be part of the reason Ford outsold GM for the first time in history in 1957. In 1961, the desig would winnational desiwardfrom the Industrial Designers Inste

On the other hand, in late 1957, Ford introduced the least successful car design since the 194 Chrysler Airflow, this time the 1958 Edsel, a new brand name honoring Henry Ford's son, designed by vice president of styling George Walker and his staff after extensive market research. The vertical air intake in the Edsel's front nose, resembling an open-mouth human u horizontal grills and air intakes. The market failure would cost Ford \$350 million and force cancellation of the Edsel brand in 1959. In June of that year, Walter Dorwin Teague expressed the general consensus ofmost industrial dsigners when e sokea a dinner meeting of the Detroit chapter of the Industrial Designers Institute:

design has in recent years become lousy, ugly, ostentatious, impractical, and excessively costly; and what is worse from management's the market.

![](images/fa5434b0497dfbfd8a7237d435fdfdd743a47b4dafb7d7617f7c2af543bd1ca9.jpg)

The driving force behind this Detroit design direction since 1927, Harley Earl, retired in December 1958, naming William Mitchell (19121988) his successor as vice president of styling at General Motors (GM). Earl's garish chrome-trimmed and tailfinned 20-year era had run its course. The last of Earl's creatons

Braun products in Germany, which had been transformed by its introduction of Bauhausstyle products in 1955, had attracted international attention in 1957 with the introduction of its Model KM 3/KM 31 Kitchen Machine. The Museum of Modern Art (MoMA) enthusiastically welcomed this resurgence of its favorite international style by creating a permanent display of Braun products in 1958. In fact, many designers in the United States also praised and imitated the style because of its simplicity, cleanness, and clarity. The products were obviously of high quality, but they cost more than similar American products, which limited their acceptance in the mass market. Perhaps more significantly, such characterless, colorless, spare design simply did not appeal to the U.S. mass market.

In 1958, the Aluminum Company of America (Alcoa) had initiated a series of advertisements promoting concept designs of well-known designers. In 1959, Alcoa presented the first industrial design awards, which included one for Peter Müller-Munk Associates (PMMA). PMM.A also consulted with U.S. Steel regarding its newly introduced vinyl-coated steel sheets with decorative patterns embossed into the vinyl, and which could be drawn or stamped like any sheet metal.

By this time, according to a February 1959 Fortune magazine survey, there were 300 total industrial design organizations, and 180 total members of IDI and ASID combined (a significant decline in membership). One hundred twenty corporations had internal design departments, compared to 180 independent consulting offices. The obvious result was an increase in competition in the market and the lowering of the exorbitant fees of the big-name designers. The dav when a Ravmond Loewy could get S50,000 for designing a cigarette package was gone forever. Retainer fees now ranged from \$500 to S5,000 per month, depending on a firm's reputation. The  largest industrial design firms named b Fortune, halowhich were led by original machine designers of the 1930s, included the number of employees where known: Raymond Loewy Corporation (200); Walter Dorwin Teague Associates (140), Henry Dreyfuss Associates (50); Dave Chapman, Inc. (50); Peter Müller-Munk Associates (30); Russel Wright (25); George Nelson (24); Harley Earl, Inc.; Brooks Stevens; and Donald Deskey. The remaining 10 were younger and smaller firms.

Industrial designers were becoming part of the popular culture. The 1954 film Executive Suite starred William Holden as vice president of design and development Don Walling, who wins the presidency by vowing to replace the shoddy furniture produced by the company with quality products. In the 1959 flm North by Northwest, Cary Grant meets a pretty girl, Eve Kendall, played by Eva Marie Saint, who is posing as an industrial designer.

AT&T introduced its Princess phone in 1959, designed by Henry Dreyfuss Associates (HDA), with a petite size for teenage girls and an illuminated dial to facilitate bedroom usage. AT&T was the iPhone of the 1950s. HDA also designed the NASA/AT&T Bel Labs Mercury control room for the fledgling space program, interiors for the LockheedConstellation aircraft, and a Strobonar photoflash for Honeywell. That year, HDA published Measure of Man, a book featuring Joe and Josephine that expanded the applications of ergonomic data to many more design situations and circumstances.

Now, the increasingly chilly Cold War with the Soviet Union spawned a new, political market for industrial design. Some would call it propaganda.

# Cold War, Mergers, and Computers

In te summer f 1959, the U.S. Information Agency (USIA) mounted an exhibition in Moscow, the American National Exhibition, as the first of many such cultural exchanges between the Soviet Union and the United States. The exhibit, designed by George Nelson and Charles Eames, turned into the site of a vigorous Cold War debate between Soviet premier Nikita Krushchev nd hen-vice president Richard Nixon. Featured at the exhibiton was an innovative geodesic dome by R. Buckminster Fuller, which attracted worldwide fascination u over the next 32 years would be seen in a total of 87 cities across 12 time zones by an estimated 20mill Sov ciizens t proote U.S.natial poliicl jecives, an to win te hearts and minds of the public with personal cultural diplomacy. Eventually, by 1991, Soviet citizens would be convinced that Soviet promises of free consumer goods would never be realized, and they rejected communism for a more democratic government.

Also in 1959, the Museum of Modern Art displayed three Structures by Buckminster Fuller, including his geodesic dome, plus other designs of his from 1927, which included a circular house on a central pole called a 4-D Utility Unit, and the development of a half plane, half car called the 4-D Zoomobile.

In Los Angeles, the famous "Skyscraper" furniture designer, Paul T. Frankl, passed away in 1959 at age 73. He had left New York in 1934 to establish a gallery in Los Angeles, where his clients included many Hollywood stars of the late 1930s. He had applied his talents to many projects, from interior design, posters, greeting cards and theater sets, to selecting objects from Europe, the Orient, and Mexico for sale in his shops. His fortunes had shifted often in his career from prosperity to poverty, and his personal life ranged from depression over the death of his first wife, bitterness over divorce from his second wife, and emotional security with his third.

A month after President John F. Kennedy was elected president in 1960 came the death of the most highly esteemed and famous pioneer of industrial design, Walter Dorwin Teague, who died on December 5 in Flemington, New Jersey, just two weeks shy of his 77th birthday. Just the year before, Teague had addressed the Royal Society of Arts in London, and in June of that same year, he had spoken to the Detroit chapter of the Industrial Designers Institute. In 1963, the American Society of Industrial Designers (ASID) would honor Teague, one of its founders, with the organization's first scholarship program, the Walter Dorwin Teague Scholarship, eligible to selected junior students majoring in industrial design. Teague's papers are archived at Syracuse University Library, Special Collections, and at his surviving original organization, now called Teague.

His son, W. Dorwin Teague, who had worked with his father from 1934 to 1942 and had later rejoined the organization in 1953 as partner, now was number two in Teague's professional office, Walter Dorwin Teague Associates (WDTA), with Robert Harper as CEO and president. Dorwin Teague and other WDTA associates in 1960 received a national design award from the Industrial Designers Institute (IDI) for the design of the Euphorian dental chair for the Ritter Company. A functional patent for it is in Teague, Jr's name. That same year, WDTA also designed the new Polaroid Electric-Eye 900 camera and a landmark mathematical model for the U.S. Navy's Missiles and Quality Control System, still in use today.

Another IDI award in 1960 went to Dave Chapman, Inc., in Chicago for its research report and recommendations on the design of facilities for U.S. schools, produced for the Educational Facilities Laboratories, Inc., and to William L. Mitchell and the General Motors Styling Staff for the Chevrolet Corvair. The latter, a so-called compact car, was a significant improvement in styling, with no tail fins, but its oversteering tendency earned it the wrath of Ralph Nader, who dubbed it "one of the nastiest-handling cars ever built." His 1965 book, Unsafe at Any Speed, would prod Congress, which in 1966 would pass the National Traffic and Motor Vehicle Act, empowering the government to set safety standards for new cars after 1968, which would soon be enacted.

Designs of 1961 included the Tower Capri portable typewriter for Sears, Roebuck by the Sundberg-Ferar design firm in Detroit, which won an IDI national award; the IBM Selectric typewriter by Eliot Noyes, which by 975 accounted for 75 percent of the U.S. electric typewriter market; and the Singer 660 sewing machine by Henry Dreyfuss Associates. HDA also designed the Civil War Information Building in Richmond, Virginia. George A. Beck, head of GE's industrial design light military electronics department, received one of Alcoa's annual industrial design awards. In a major corporate move, Eliot Noyes was retained by Westinghouse to dramatize its image, including logo and design standards, and to reorganize its internal design departments in order to better compete with General Electric. Also in 1961, Raymond Loewy established Raymond Loewy/William Snaith, Inc., and Raymond Loewy/William Snaith Associates, Inc., with William Snaith as president and Raymond Loewy as chairman of the board.

In 1954, the Studebaker Corporation had merged with Packard to form Studebaker-Packard, and with its combined internal design staffs, as well as its financial difficulties, it felt that it no longer needed to pay Raymond Loewy's extravagant design fees, so it canceled his contract in 1956, even though Loewy had designed Studebaker cars for the previous 20 years. Loewy's Studebaker studio in South Bend shut down, and Robert Bourke relocated to Loewy's Chiu w  Suak    ub n engaged a new president, Sherwood E. Egbert, from the McCullough Company, where Egbert had worked with Brooks Stevens on a number of design projects.

Egbert engaged Stevens to design the 1962 models of the company's mainstays, the Lark sedan and the Gran Turismo Hawk, with a meager budget of \$7 million to tool both cars. Stevens accomplished the task with minimal changes to the previous year's models, which had been designed by Loewy. But Egbert knew such minor changes were not enough to save the so as a last ditch effort he rehired Raymond Loewy to design a sensational new sports car, which would become the 1962 Avanti. Loewy retreated to his Palm Springs home with designe Bob Andres, Tom Kellgg, and John W.Ebsti. The li den, seek, ral, and unlike any car from Detroit, was an instant success, but it was too late for the company, which would fold in 1963. Stil, the Avanti would be brought to life again and again by sequential private interests until 1991.

John Tjaarda, the exceptional automotive designer of the 1936 Lincoln Zephyr, passed away in 16 at age 6. In 191, he had pursued an interest in solid-state rocketry and joined the Aerojet-General Corporation in Sacramento as a technical advisor. His son Tom Tjaarda (b. 1934) would follow in his dad's footsteps as an automotive designer and would establish his own automotive design office in Italy, Dimensione Design, in 1984.1

In 196, Chauncey "Chick" Waltman also died at age . A well-known Chicago furniture and lamp designer starting in 10 he joined the ndustrial des movement in te 130s paticing as Waltman Associates at 165 Chicago Avenue. The office later moved to 310 E. Chicago Avenue. His son, Charles T. Chubb" Waltman (19192006), joined him after World War II, and the office operated until about 1960.

Also in 1962, H. Creston Doner chaired the Advisory Board of the Showcase National Corporation for the United States and Canada, and over a number of years, he was a consultant to the architects and designers of the 1964 World's Fair in New York, Expo 67 in Canada; HemisFair 68 in San Antonio; Tomorrowland in Disneyland, California; and the Bay Area Regional Transit (BART) system in 1969.

![](images/27e561f6ecff79c349d5e07ee2131cb7c692e844120737fdbb28145d5439ba18.jpg)  
Chauncey "Chick" Waltman, ca. 1950s (courtesy William Waltman).

In the world of expositions, Walter Dorwin Teague Associ-

ates designed the U.S. Science Center at the 1962 Century 21 Exposition in Seattle, which was called by some "a virtual cathedral of science." The Spacearium held 750 people for a simulated voyage first through the solar system, then through the Milky Way Galaxy and beyond. In 1963, Walter Dorwin Teague Associates would design the Maxwell House Automatic Coffee Machine for the General Foods Corporation.

The new International Council of Societies of Industrial Design (ICSID) convened its Third Congress in Paris, and the Industrial Designers Institute (IDI) held an exhibition at the Louvre, organized by Alfons Bach, who had moved his practice to Palm Beach, Florida, in 1959. Among the large selection of American designs were the 1962 Avanti by Loewy and the 1956 Lincoln Continental Mark II by George Walker and his staff at Ford.

By 1963, international design was becoming a common practice. Sixteen American industrial design firms had offices abroad, and manufacturers from 22 foreign countries had contracts with American designers. The Cold War pitted the United States against the Soviet Union in providing a better standard of living to their respective citizens. After Russia's Luna I fight to within 3,700 miles of the moon, Soviet premier Nikita Krushchev had boldly predicted in 1959 that by 1966 the Soviet Union would "catch up" to America and would be distributing basic consumer goods to Russian citizens "free of charge." Industrial designers were well aware that they were now competing globally. At ASID's annual conference in 1962, Raymond Loewy had spoken of such competition:

Nothing great has ever been achieved—whether by a nation, a business, an individual—without qality Now, we are at a point where that belie is being put to the test. Americans are in a massive race to maintain our leadership against other nations in trade, training, goods, and taste.

Raymond Loewy had just designed the interior and external livery for Air Force One when President Kennedy was assassinated in Dallas, Texas, in November 1963. The Boeing 707 was a specially modified Boeing C-137 Stratoliner. Loewy had met with Kennedy and conducted research in the National Archives for the assignment, for which he did not charge a fee. He found the uppercase typeface called Caslon, which was used widely spaced in the country's name in the first printed copy of the U.S. Declaration of Independence, and used it for the large lettering on each side of Air Force One's fuselage, reading, "United States of America." A presidential seal was on both sides of the fuselage, and an American flag on the tail. The plane took the president to Dallas and returned his body to Washington. The following year, at the request of Jacqueline Kennedy, Loewy designed the 1964 five-cent John Kennedy memorial postage stamp for the U.S. Postal Service. That same year, Loewy established Market Concepts, Inc., a subsidiary for confidential research operations. Now reorganized as Raymond Loewy/William Snaith, Inc., Loewy's office won a national Industrial Designers Institute (IDI) Award for the design of the Time-Master, made by the Dictaphone Corporation, as did William Mitchell of the General Motors Styling staff for the Pontiac Grand Prix.

Kem Weber, the pioneer who began designing modern furniture in 1926 and was inspired by the Paris Exposition of 1925, passed away in California in 1963 at age 74. After the war, Weber had moved back to Santa Barbara, California, and worked mostly as an architect, building a handful of houses there and in nearby Montecito. By the late 1950s, his health had begun to fail. Also in 1963, Henry Dreyfuss Associates designed the first touch-button phone, the Touchtone 2500 for AT&T, and the Automatic 100 camera for Polaroid.

In 1964, Ford's sporty Mustang won the Industrial Designers Institute's national design award. It was Ford's answer to GM's Corvette and was a huge success with the 16- to 24-yearold baby boomer crowd. George Walker had resigned in 1961, so the Mustang was designed by the new vice president of styling, Eugene Bordinat (19201987), and his staff,driven by Lee Iacocca, who had been named Ford vice president in 1960.

Even as Studebaker was dying, Brooks Stevens, without Studebaker's agreement, had prototyped a promotional concept car for Studebaker, which was initially called the Mercebaker, a "contemporary classic" sports car based on the antique Mercedes SSK of the 1927-1929 period. Stevens' sons, Steve and David, built the prototype in six weeks during 1964 and were in the process of towing it to the auto show to create a sensation for Studebaker when Studebaker executives told Stevens they would not be showing it. But Stevens knew the director of the auto show, who agreed to display the prototype as a product of Brooks Stevens Associates. The car was so well received that Stevens funded the production of the car by his sons as a business enterprise, renaming it the Excalibur SSK Roadster, which enjoyed success as the first replicar," a design based on a historically classic car.

In April 1964, the New York World's Fair opened at Flushing Meadows in Queens, with the theme of "Peace through Understanding," and dedicated to "Man's Achievement on a Shrinking Globe in an Expanding Universe." The fair would run from April to October in 1964 and 1965. The grounds were dominated by a 12-story high, stainless steel, skeletonized It was designed by Peter Müller-Munk Associates for U.S. Steel and was called the Unisphere, which sill remains in Flushing Meadows today.

Attendees traveled the fairgrounds on a monorail designed by Walter Dorwin Teague Associates, who also designed the Festival of Gas, including the House of the Future, featuring a gas-powered refrigerator and a gas oven designed for Norge. Most regarded Futurama II as the most popular exhibit, designed by Henry Dreyfuss Associates (HDÅ) for General Motors. Like its 1939 fair predecessor, designed by Norman Bel Geddes, it was a show in which visitors what life might be like 60 years in the "near future" of 204. Bel Geddes, who had die si years earlier, would have been proud, but he may have wondered why, after 25 years, Dreyfuss hadn't come up with something better.

Also in 1964, HDA designed the symbol for American Airlines, the Swinger camera for Polaroid, and the Trimline desk phone for AT&T, which would be accepted into the permanent collection of the Museum of Modern Art. George Nelson won a prestigious Alcoa award for Herman Miller's Action Office I, an innovative system of office furniture, but later it became known that Robert Propst had actually designed it. Like other major designers, Nelson claimed credit for all designs in his organization. Following in the footsteps of Raymond Loewy, Buckminster Fuller, now famous for his geodesic domes, was featured on the cover of Time magazine.

Now it was time for the three separate industrial design organizations to get together.

After much discussion and negotiation since 1958, the three industrial design organizations, the Industrial Designers Institute (IDI), the American Society of Industrial Designers (ASID), and the Industrial Design Education Association (IDEA), agreed to merge and form the Industrial Designers Society of America (IDSA) in 1965 to provide a single voice for industrial design in America. The first president of IDSA was Henry Dreyfuss, one of the founders of the SID in 1944, and the first chairman of the board was John Vassos, one of the founders of the ADI in 1938 (in 1951, it had become the IDI). Vassos had retired in 1964 as a consultant of RCA design, where Tucker Madawick had taken overas managerof radio, phonograph, tape and television design in 1959.

![](images/8702849df97e549e822e565ed54e78de32ed895b2faaee7832b8181352b32789.jpg)  
John Vassos, ca. 1950s (courtesy Industrial Designers Society of America).

IDSA membership at the time was estimated at 650. The

first casualty was the IDI national design awards, which had been initiated in 1951. As we know, Dreyfuss did not like the idea of public awards because they insisted on identifying and crediting the specific designer by name. Big-name designers required that any design made in their firm must be credited to the principal's name, no matter who actually designed it. There would be no further national design awards after 1965 until 1980, when Dreyfuss would be gone.

Russel Wright was invited to Japan in 1965 to work for more than 100 companies. Later, hwul hJ  blh terBiul ing an association with public land and its reclamation for public use.

Alfonso Iannelli passed away in 1965 at the age of 77 in Chicago. In 1955, he had sculpted the large-scale Rock of Gibraltar relief for the Prudential building (now called Prudential Plaza) in Chicago, which was his last major work. In 1982, the entire contents of Iannelli's studio would be purchased by Kelms Cott Gallery in Chicago. That same year, Egmont Arens passed away from a heart attack at age 78. He was one of the founders of both the Society of Industrial Designers (SID) in 1944 and the Package Design Council (PDC) in 1952. A month after his death, his widow, Mathilde, published a collection of his early poetry. She wrote that he would often say, "You know, I came to New York to be a poet." His papers are archived at Syracuse University Library, Special Collections.

Winners of the 1966 Design in Housewares Award Program included Hamilton Beach's "hole-in-handle" electric knife and its Gourmet Center by Chapman, Goldsmith & Yamasaki, and West Bend's coffee percolator, broiler rotisserie, and Teflon-coated cookware by David Painter Industrial Designers.4 That same year, Raymond Loewy/William Snaith designed the Exxon logotype.

George Nelson's office designed an exhibition, Hand Tools USA, for travel within the

Soviet Union by the U.S. Information Agency (USIA) as a cultural exchange between the two Cold War countries in 1966. Another USIA exhibition, Industrial Design USA, would also be designed by Nelson's office in 1967. These exhibits, mentioned earlier, demonstrated the power of design as public diplomacy to change the hearts and minds of people and helped win the Cold War.

In 1967, Raymond Loewy/William Snaith Associates, Inc., designed the trademark for the Shell Oil Company. That year, Raymond Loewy/William Snaith Associates, Inc., in Chicago was merged with Raymond Loewy/William Snaith Associates, Inc., in New York Henry Dreyfuss Associates designed a prototype videophone for AT&T. The HDA organization included James Conner, Niels Diffrient, and Donald Genaro. W. Dorwin Teague resigned from Walter Dorwin Teague Associates in 1967, which had been headed by Bob Harper since Walter Dorwin Teague passed away in 1960. Milton Immermann soon took over as president and CEO of WDTA, and Dorwin resigned to start his own business, Dorwin Teague, Inc., in Englewood Cliffs, New Jersey, which specialized in both industrial design and engineering.6

Peter Müller-Munk, head of Peter Müller-Munk Associates in Pittsburgh, died unexpectedly and tragically in 1967 at the age of 60. Müller-Munk had been instrumental in the formation of the International Council of Societies of Industrial Design. Joseph Platt, one of the founders of the Society of Industrial Designers in 1944, passed away at age 73 in 1968. That year, Henry Dreyfuss retired at age 64, the same year as the tragic assassinations of Martin Luther King, Jr., and Robert Kennedy. These events, the never-ending Vietnam War, antiwar student demonstrations, and desegregation struggles created a sense of national unrest and helplessness. They were emotional and traumatic times that would be lifted only temporarily in 1969, when U.S. astronauts first landed on the moon.

The industrial design profession also suffered from mistrust by virtue of its prominent role in the corporate business world, which was now seen by many as being responsible for environmental damage, obscene profits, and neglect of global social responsibility. In 1968, R. Buckminster Fuller was awarded the World Medal of Architecture for his 250-foot-diameter, twenty-story-high geodesic dome that had housed the entire U.S. pavilion at Expo 67 in Montreal, Canada.

Fortune magazine, which had introduced industrial design to the business world in 1934, now in 968, exactly 4 years later, publishedan article, "The Decline of Industrial Designers, in February, claiming lack of innovation. This seemed to be more a matter of perception than reality. The profession continued as normal, even though many of its famous pioneers were aging or passing away, and design news was dominated by younger personalities, who were as innovative as ever.

More likely, the headlines of civil unrest, campus rebellions, and other pressing social issues had pushed industrial designers and design off of the front pages. Many in IDSA were grappling with the organization's declining membership, financial problems, and its diminishing reputation among young designers, who were criticizing IDSA as an "old boy's club." There their youth when leading industrial designers commanded top dollar, and were celebrated as national personalities. But this time had passed.

Sill Sundberg-Ferar designed the Bay Area Rapid Transit (BART) system in San Francisco, the first U.S. regional transit system since 1907. Frank Del Guidice now headed Walter Dorwin Teague Associates, and Raymond Loewy opened his third London, England, office. Nathan Horwitt iewallcocNo0r hHo MilleClocCny. Hey

A Hughes Aircraft.

In Philadelphia, Clyde Shuler, designer of"tombstone" wooden radios ofthe early 1930s, passed away at age 77 in 1969. While regarded as passé at the time, the gothic shapes of his radios in the early 130s were simply a logical extension of skyscraper"furniture design  the 120s. Shuler had taught industrial design at the Philadelphia College of Art since 1933 and had retired from there in 1952. He had established his own office, Clyde Schuler Associates, at 1717 Sansom Street, working on residential architecture and commercial design. By 1961, he was a partner in the office of his old friend, William Heyl Thompson.

Boeing 747 "jumbo jets" made their first flights. Walter Dorwin Teague Associates (WDTA) designed Boeing interiors, and Henry Dreyfuss Associates (HDA) designed American Airlines interiors. Design in Steel desig awards, initiated in 63 by U.S. Steel, wer awarded in 1969 to Henry Dreyfuss Associates for a transmission tower for the Southern California Edison Company, and to Sundberg-Ferar for a subway transit vehicle designed for the New York Metro Transportation Authority.

![](images/48475b8373002c6f0186de558312206fd87932e3181cf430ae9fcc561af5a8e0.jpg)  
Carl Sundberg with Bay Area Regional Transit System, 1969 (courtesy Sundberg-Ferar, www.sundbergferar.com).

![](images/be8ed1600c29405af96bdd7db3649ab8aa447d08717198c6c4279ae339ab4ea6.jpg)  
Playmate cooler, by Marlan Polhemus of GYS.

In Detroit, Harley Earl, the man who had most influenced automotive design over the previous 30 years, passed away at age 76 in 1969. They say he is still remembered at General Motors with this prayer: "Our Father, who art in Styling, Harley be thy Name." Amen to that. His private consulting firm had merged with Walter B. Ford II (1920-1991) Design Associates in 1964 to form Ford & Earl Design Associates. Ford had been in GM Styling under Earl. In 1970, Dave Chapman, Inc., of Chicago was reorganized with Paul Specht as president and partner in Goldsmith Yamasaki Specht, Inc. (GYS), which that year designed the ubiquitous Playmate plastic picnic cooler for the Igloo Corporation.

Dreyfuss closed his Pasadena office in 1970, and his staff was relocated to 4 West 58th Street in New York, where Bill

Purcell and Don Genaro were senior partners and Niels Diffrient and Jim Conner were junior partners. They moved the office to 888 Seventh Avenue with more space. In the early 1970s, Dreyfuss had sold his business to these four partners, and focused on international symbols by himself.7

![](images/eead0688246e800288b9334d7ed604b8c207b7f628a29ca28f00ac17fd21ae97.jpg)  
Henry Dreyfuss, ca. 1955 (courtesy Henry Dreyfuss Associates).

Dreyfuss, representing the American National Standards Institute (ANSI), chaired the first meeting in 1971 of the International Organization of Standards' Technical Committee (ISO/ TC) in Berlin, which set international standards for 145 signs and symbols. In 1972, Dreyfuss would use these in his publication, the Symbol Sourcebook, a virtual encyclopedia of international symbols. HDA designed the Hyster lift equipment and the Flicker, a woman's razor for the American Safety Razor Company. Sadly, that same year, Henry Dreyfuss and his wife, Doris, both terminally ill, designed their own death by carbon monoxide at their home in South Pasadena. Until his death, Dreyfuss had been a faculty member in the engineering department at Caltech in Pasadena. The personal archives of Henry Dreyfuss would be donated to the Smithsonian in 1973. Henry Dreyfuss Associates, however, remained active and profitable. New associates Jim Ryan and Bill Crookes were hired.

Industrial design now came under attack by Victor Papanek (1925-1998), chair of design at the Kansas City (Missouri) Art Instiute, whose 1 book, Design for the Real World, the book jacke o which caracterized Papane as n ingenious dese who charges the industrial design establishment with criminal negligence of almost everybody," featured such declarations and accusations as, "It is about time that industrial design, as we have come to know it, should cease to exist," and, "Today, industrial design has put murder on a mass production basis."

Papanek claimed the moral high ground in his book using his students' designs, which addressed child disability problems, created radio receivers made of juice cans and cottage industries for Third World countries, designed human-powered vehicles, and created packaging made of pea pods. Papanek's idealistic, socially conscious, and antibusiness philosophy appealed strongly to 20-year-old young adults who optimistically aspired to change the world, which to them appeared to be failing in many respects. They felt that their unique role, as a generation, was to challenge authority and the establishment to achieve change of any sort. Within a year, the Vietnam War would be over, conscription into the military would end, and the volunteer army began. The cus youh would then bemoreon socil n envimental s.

Ove e yr chaccuul e weub p n s al  i u pn - u social and environmental problems increased.

The government increasingy became involved with legislation to address these social and environmental problems, and the design profession participated where it was relevant. In January 1973, the Industrial Designers Society of America (IDSA) relocated its national office from New York to Washington, D.C., where an expanding government was becoming activist in nature. IDSA was receiving grants for working with government programs, such as the Product Safety Commission and Barrier Free Design. IDSA at the time had a total membership of 723 and 10 regional chapters. New York, Chicago, and the West Coast were no longer the only centers of design. Manufacturers were moving south for lower wages, and small, local design offices were popping up all over the country.

A 1973 study, "Industrial Design Education in the U.S.A.: A Factual Summary," by Joseph Carriero, chair of the Department of Design and Environmental Analysis at Cornell University, revealed that there were 4l higher education programs of industrial design, and approximately 2,000 students pursuing a career in industrial design, of which 363 were female and 1,737 male. There were also 21l graduate students pursuing master's degrees in 29 programs. Faculty members totaling 1882 were devoted to full-time teaching of industrial design, plus 129 part time, and 200 support people.

That same year, Raymond Loewy/William Snaith Associates, Inc., designed the Skylab 2 space station for NASA, launched on May 25, 1973, a project Loewy regarded as the most important of his career. It was the first manned mission to Skylab, and the first U.S. orbital space station. Loewy emphasized habitability and comfort for the astronauts, with a wardroom for meals and relaxation, and a window to view the Earth and space. Skylab missions could last for months. Each astronaut had a private sleeping area the size of a walk-in closet, with a curtain, sleeping bag, and locker. That same year, Loewy signed a contract with the Soviet Union to be its consultant for mass production and over the next few years collaborated on the design of Russian automobiles, farm tractors, cameras, and hydrofoil vessels.

Henry Dreyfuss Associates designed an open-sided street telephone booth for AT&T, a Bard Parker surgeon's scalpel, and the Polaroid SX-70 camera in 1973, and in 1974 published Humanscale 1/2/3, extensive ergonomic data and diagrams laminated in plastic for durability and frequent use for industrial designers to determine accurate dimensions for human use of products and equipment. President Nixon resigned as a result of the Watergate scandal, and Gerald Ford, vice president, took the office of president.

It was in 1973 that Virgil Exner, who started in automotive design in 1933, passed away at age 64. He had a heart attack in 1956 but resumed work in 1957: In 1962 the president of Chrysler ordered him to reduce the size of their cars. The standard-size models Exner had already designed were downsized by associates over the objections of Exner. When they failed on the market, Exner was fired as the scapegoat. He retired in 1964 and was replaced by Elwood Engle (1917-1986). Exner continued consulting for various car companies from his office in Birmingham, Michigan, teaming up with his son, Virgil Exner, Jr., to design watercraft for the

![](images/1a9a5c69f65f90942a6f426770461f8e5cadd959363828ec0c4fb656e11331d6.jpg)  
Joseph "Jo" Sinel, ca. 1970s (Designscape magazine, February 1977).

Buehler Corporation. He also designed "revival cars," including the revival of Stutz in the 1970s.

The year 1975 saw the passing of Greta von Nessen in Murraysville, Pennsylvania, at age 75. She and her husband, Walter von Nessen (18891943), founded Nessen Studios in 1927 to design lamps. She continued the family studio, which survives to this day as Nessen Lamps, Inc.

That same year, Jo Sinel, one of the founders of the Society of Industrial Designers, passed away at age 86. A New Zealander who was designing products in 1923 for an advertising agency in New York, and eventually worked for 55 such agencies, he became a noted industrial designer and educator. His papers are archived at the California College of Arts, where he taught for so many years, and where he received an honorary doctorate of fine arts.

The Smithsonian's Renwick Gallery in Washington, D.C. in 175 mounted a retrospective exhibition of the work of Raymond Loewy, which included his Studebaker Avanti. In 1974,

Loewy's partner, William Snaith, had died, and the Loewy firm was nearing bankruptcy. By 1975, Loewy had already established Raymond Loewy International by merging all his coexisting organizations.

That year, Henry Dreyfuss Associates designed the Athena 2000 sewing machine, the International Women's Year symbol for the United Nations, and in 1976 Polaroid's Pronto camera. The Concorde supersonic jet began service in 1976 with interiors designed by Raymond Loewy/William Snaith, and the Cooper-Hewitt Museum in the Andrew Carnegie mansion in New York became the National Design Museum, a branch of the Smithsonian.

Russel Wright, one of the founders of the Society of Industrial Designers in 1944, passed away in 1976 at the age of 72. He had transformed his 79 acres of quarry land in Garrison, New York, into a natural scenic wonder called Manitoga. His papers are at Syracuse University Library, Special Collections, and at Manitoga, operated by the Russel Wright Design Center. Manitoga is designated as a National Historic Landmark by the U.S. Department of the Interior and is open to the public, with tours and hiking trails.

Also in 1976, Walter Dorwin Teague Associates designed the Kenworth Sleeper truck, the frst tohous  seer abi. The desg eabled more effetive nd effnt nul driving.

In 1977, the Raymond Loewy/William Snaith, Inc., office in New York closed under Butler to form Lister and Butler, Inc.

George A. By 1977, the International Style architecture of Walter Gropius and Mies van der Rohe (both had died in 1969), which had been promoted by the Museum of Modern Art from 1932 u a uyTh desig  the Pompidou Center in Paris, a complex of public library, museum fmodern rt, uy tech look, with exposed plumbing; electrical, ventilation and safety systems in bright color codes;and wih exterior aircases,mewhat ike ntique urban e epes.

Architects began abandoning the International Style by designing buildings that blended in with the surrounding buildings, rather than standing alone as rectangular, glass-box monuments to modern design. The term "postmodern" began to be applied to such architecture, whc wlly  wiywh, local context, metaphor, and ambiguity. It was a sea change in architectural style that would soon find its way into product design.

Henry Dreyfuss Associates designed the Singer Touchtronic sewing machine in 1978. That year, Dave Chapman, who had worked as a designer at Chicago's Century of Progress Exhibition in 19331934 and founded Chicago's most successful industrial design firm in 1936, passed away at age 69, but his firm would survive under new management as Goldsmith Yamasaki Specht. Chapman's papers are at Syracuse University Library, Special Collections. CharesEames, whomiatstarfuriture desn,also diedthat y t ageDesky, Inc., was founded in Cincinnati, Ohio, in 1978 as a division of Donald Deskey Associates of New York.

During the 1970s, the computer was becoming the center of a technological and informational revolution. The first prototype personal computer, the Altos desktop, appeared in 1974; in 1975, 19-year-old William Gates III and 22-year-old Paul Allen founded Microsoft; in 1977, Steven G. Wosniac and 21-year-old Steven Jobs founded Apple Computer in a California garage; in 1978, the first electronic typewriters with memory appeared; and in 1979, Sony introduced its Walkman. By 1983, the first cell phones would appear. These new technologies would generate hundreds of new consumer products, and when they entered mass production, industrial designers would design most of them. Technology continued to provide the raw material that industrial designers transformed into products for humans.

By 1979, membership in the Industrial Designers Society of America (IDSA) would top 2,000. It had taken organizational steps over the previous few years to replace its leadership with younger members, and to address some of their concerns. IDSA was no longer a sort of small private club governed autocratically by an older generation, including many of the pioneers, to be sure, but by a more democratic representation o younger chapter chairmen on the board. Membership requirements were relaxed, automotive stylists were included, and it became professionally managed by specialists in association management, rather than by a closed-room board of past presidents. Chapters had doubled to 20, as had membership, and financial health was restored. There were 675 consulting offices nationwide, and over 6,000 industrial designers working within corporations. At the time, it was estimated that there were 10,000 industrial designers worldwide.

Also in 1979, Raymond Loewy published Industrial Design, a comprehensive, illustrated book about his design career. He was the surviving member of the original Big Three founders of the profession, and therefore still a public figure who was featured in any historical review of industrial design.

The Industrial Designers Society of America initiated annual national design awards in 1980, known as the Industrial Design Excellence Awards (IDEA), which were presented at IDSA's annual meeting that year, and which continue to this day. They were the first such awards since 1965, when the first IDSA president, Henry Dreyfuss, shut down the previous IDI awards. Also in 1980, Henry Dreyfuss Associates designed the Polaroid 600 Sun Camera and published Humanscale 4/5/6, additional ergonomic reference tools for industrial designers, following Humanscale 1/2/3, published in 1974. The final publication in this valuable series would be Humanscale 7/8/9, published in 1991.

Helen Dryden, who was the "highest-paid woman artist of 1927" and a famous cover illustrator of Vogue magazine in the 1920s, passed away in 1981 at age 94. Since 1937, when she designed the interior of the Studebaker as Raymond Loewy designed the exterior, the only thing known about her was that in 1956 she was living in a \$10-a-week hotel room paid for by New York City's Welfare Department. At the time, she referred nostalgically to her (1925) \$200-a-month Tenth Street apartment in New York.

Other pioneer designers were passing. Both Montgomery Ferar and his partner at Sundberg-Ferar since its founding in 1934, Carl W. Sundberg, died in 1982, both at age 72. Sundberg had retired in 1975. From 1970 to 1980, Ferar had taught industrial design at the Center for Creative Studies in Detroit. The firm they established continued active practice after their death and is still in business today.

Raymond Dietrich, the well-known automotive designer, passed away at age 88 in 1982. He had left Chrysler in 1940; founded Ray Dietrich, Inc., in 1949; worked for Checker Cab i 3;and forhe Lincoln and Mercury divisons at Ford until his retiremnt t Albuquerque, NewMexico Hs e Marna i pe   i  e G Classic Car Club of America Museum at Hickory Corners, Michigan.

In 1983, George Nelson installed an exhibit, Design since 1945, organized by Katherine Hiesinger, at the Philadelphia Museum of Art, along with a 247-page, illustrated catalog of the exhibition by Hiesinger. The exhibition and catalog were a sort of obituary of modern design or "Good Design" as defined by the Museum of Modern Art (MoMA) from 1950 to 1955. At te h on its history, a sure sign of age.

As early as 1979, architectural and design critic Ada Louise Huxtable (19212013) had written about the difference between the Bauhaus/Museum of Modern Art concept of industrial design in the 1950s versus the pragmatic and successful reality of industrial design as developed and implemented by the designers of the Machine Age:

Those who think they know how it was in those days [the 1950s] are in for a surprise. What was admired then is discarded now, and what was rejected is being revived.. Industrial design, that curious amalgam of promotion, marketing, technology, and taste invented by the 20th century to srve n shape a consumer eonomy whose practitioners] shared the ptimism of the tectural theorists .. who believed that the marriage of art and industry could provide answers te tasakers we o urs yhilinreliz argely

pypmi ucin uuser,huil er we y creating mass-produced objects for a universal market.

However, the pragmatism, simplicity and repetition of so-called Good Design had its limits. The design community was searching, as it often had, for new styles, new looks, and new directions.

# Postmodern and Passages

Finally, after 30 years, designers were tiring of the black boxes and white cubes of Bauhausstyle modern design. It was Italians who broke the ice in 1981, when an avant-garde Milanese group founded an organization called Memphis. It created the design media event of the year with garish furniture designs with bold colors and laminates that emphasized surface decoration more than function. Presented to the beat of rock music in the manner of a fashion show, the designs were deliberately designed to be antifunctional, preposterous, and faddish. They were colorful, cheerful, and playful, the direct opposite of Bauhaus traditions. One of the designs, the Casablanca sideboard, would become the icon of Memphis.

By 1984, some U.S. educators were seeking new theories to free design from the formal dictates of so-called modern design. They came up with likening design to language, which they called "product semantics," defined as, "The study of the symbolic qualities of man-made forms in the context of their use and the application of this knowledge to industrial design. It takes into account not only the physical and physiological functions, but the psychological, social, and cultural context, which we call the symbolic environment." Got that? Although this was a highly academic and theoretical theory, many designers began to abandon the formal "good design" of the 1950s.

Many new designs were being described as "postmodern," which meant that they had departed from the formal, Bauhaus-style, simple, geometric style to more informal, expressive, humanistic, and playful styles. These included cameras that suggested humanoid facial features, modern furniture that imitated 18th-century styles such as Sheraton or Chippendale, teakettles with a litte bird whist  h spout, a batroom hower ue with -haped ors gesting waves, and travel irons that looked like miniature steamships. In 1990, Alessi would introduce a lemon juicer called the Juicy Salif that looked like an insect and would become a cult object.

In effect, design now offered an infinite variety of styles that could just as easily be based on traditional historical forms as those of future science fiction. The "ideal" style was that which best fit the specific individual product's function and usage and its primary audience, the consumer. Ironically, these stylistic modifications were little different conceptually from thehigly criicized (by elitemodernist fthe 10s) sreiningor skyscraper stylee 1930s, which were simply 50 years ahead of their time!

One can only wonder what the remaining pioneer machine designers of the 1930s thought Fuller, inventor of the geodesic dome, passed away at age 88 in 83. In 185, he would be posthumously honored with the naming of a newly created form of carbon molecule (C-60)

as "buckminsterfullerene" (nicknamed "buckyball") and others in its class (fullerenes), such as "buckytubes," discovered in 1991, because the carbon molecule C-60 resembled a geodesic dome.

Gordon W. Florian, who had started with General Electric in 1934, passed away in 1984 at age 75. He had designed a locomotive in 1940 for GE when he was assistant director to Ray Patten, but he left GE in 1946 to open his own office. He taught design at the Junior College of Connecticut, which became the University of Bridgeport after the war, and in 1949 he etablished a four-year industrial design program there.From 1950 to 1954 he lectured tongineering and business students at MIT. A lifelong artist of landscapes and a member of the Connecticut Watercolor Society, he exhibited all over the East Coast, including Silvermine. In 1984, he was named Fairfeld (Connecticut) "Artist of the Year.

Ll G in Darin, Connecicut, i 85 after a lon career. He ha pracic desuntil the 10s. His papers reat the yracuse Universiy Library, SpeclCollectins, and is product various museums. He had been born in Syracuse and studied at the university.

In the mid-1980s, Henry Dreyfuss Associates was coming apart. The office had moved to 423 West 54th Street. Partners were Don Genaro, Jim Conner, Bill Crookes, Gordon Sylvester, Jim Ryan, and Jack McGarvey. Several associates started a separate firm and took with them hermployee and most ftheclint base. Bill Crook bout ut the rmaig partners and what clients were left, mostly John Deere Agricultural Products, and moved the office to Ann Arbor, Michigan.

In 1985, Worldesign 85, the first Congress of the International Council of Societies of Industrial Design (ICSID), was held in the United States, hosted by the Industrial Designers Society of America (IDSA). International registrants of the conference voted Charles Eames the "Most Influential Designer of the 20th Century."

John Vassos, one of the founders of the American Designers' Institute in 1938, and RCA design head from 1931, also passed away in 1985 at age 93. He had been president of the Silvermine Guild of Artists and Writers in Connecticut in 1936, 19401947, and 19491955, and had retired from heading RCA design in about 1959. In 1977, the last retrospective of his work was held at the Silvermine Guild in Connecticut. In poor health and nearly blind, Vassos remained a feisty old man until the end. Vassos's papers are also at the Syracuse University Library, Special Collections.

The year 1986 saw the passing of more pioneer designers. John Morgan, Sears, Roebuck's first designer in 1934 with dozens of design patents, passed away at age 83 in McHenry, Illinois. After his retirement, he had continued his successful career in fine art. Ray Sandin, Hotpoint's first designer in 1935, died at age 76 after serving Hotpoint for 51 years. George Nelson, the man who alerted the business world to industrial design in 1934, passed away at age 78. He revolutionized furniture design at Herman Miller after the war and invented the concept of pedestrian malls. His papers are at the Syracuse University Library, Special Collections. Carl Otto is estimated to have died around this time, but there is virtually no information about him since he closed his office in 1956. He was last known to be in England.

Raymond Loewy, one of the Big Three industrial designers, died in 1986 at age 93. He had retired in 1980 at the age of 87 and had returned to his native France, where he passed away at his Monte Carlo residence. In 1992, his second wife, Viola, and the British American Tobacco Company would establish the Raymond Loewy Foundation in Hamburg, Germany, to promote industrial design internationally. An annual award of €50,000 was granted annually to outstanding designers. Loewy's daughter, Laurence, in 1998 would establish Loewy Design in Atlanta, Georgia, to manage her father's interests in the United States. Laurence would die in 2008, and her husband, David Hagerman, would manage Loewy Design and the Loewy estate, which would catalog the Loewy archives and raise funds to open the Raymond Loewy Museum of Industrial Design. Collections of Loewy archives are in the Raymond Loewy Collection at the Hagley Museum in Wilmington, Delaware, and in the Manuscript Division of the Library of Congress in Washington, D.C.

The Brooklyn Museum in 1986 published The Machine Age in America, 1918-1941 and organized a related exhibition in Brooklyn from October 17, 1986, to February 16, 1987. The exhibit would continue at the Museum of Art, Carnegie Institute, in Pittsburgh from April 4 to June 28, 1987; at the Los Angeles County Museum of Art from August 16 to October 18, 1987; and would conclude at the High Museum of Art, Atlanta, Georgia, from December 1, 1987, to February 14, 1988. The exhibition and book was a masterful retrospective of this important era of American modern design.

In 1987, the National Endowment for the Arts presented a five-part television series on PBS, America by Design, exploring the history of American architecture and design. Raymond Loewy dominated industrial design history in the series. That same year, Jean Reinecke, who had opened his industrial design office in Chicago in 1930, died in Pasadena, California, at the age of 78. He had closed his Chicago office in 1974 and had continued designing until 1986 in his Pasadena office, where he was also on the Council of Advisors of the Art Center College of Design.

In 1988, Edwin W. Fuerst, who established his first Toledo design studio in 1929, passed away. He had taught industrial design at Ohio State University from 1948 to 1949. In the mid— 1950s he became a freelance painter and moved to New England, becoming special assistant to the vice president of sales for the Plax Corporation in Hartford, Connecticut, from which he

![](images/1234c15e90d29cdee806d92394db31645bcec2bd64639db8f250d0734e7e84c0.jpg)  
Jean Reinecke, ca. 1970s (courtesy Barbra Reinecke Sedory).

retired in 1966. George Sakier, one of the founders of the Society of Industrial Designers (SID), died at the age of 91, also in 1988. He had designed glassware for Fostoria Glass from 1929 to 1979, when he retired to continue his lifelong career as a painter of abstract landscapes, with natural elements reduced to geometric forms. His paintings included Red Mesa, #011, nColg . niverGeo ak ixelece raphy.

Donald Deskey, a prominent industrial designer since 1926, and one of the founders of the Society of Industrial Designers, died in 1989 at the age of 94 in Vero Beach, Florida, the town to which he retired in 1975. In 1988, Deskey had exhibited a one-man show, Donald Deskey, American Modern Design, an exhibit of his furniture designs from the 1920s and 1930s, at Fifty/50, a Manhattan gallery. After the exhibit he donated his papers to the Cooper-Hewitt National Design Museum in New York.

Several pioneer inustrial esiners pass awayin 90. Nathan Horwit, the eer of the classic Museum Watch in 1947, passed away at his farm in Stockbridge, Massachusetts, at the age of 92. Between 1945 and 1948, his farm was used for training exercises of the Jewish underground force, the Irgun. Horwitt supported the creation of the state o Israel and served as a member of the American League for a Free Palestine. For these efforts, in 80, he ha beeware he Jabotinsky Medal byhe pri Irael e Begin.

1960s he had founded and was president of the Jiranek School of Furniture Design and Technology in New York City, and in 1984 he was awarded a doctor of arts degree by the Kendall College of Art and Design.

Gordon Buehrig, noted automotive designer, died at age 86. He had retired from Ford in 1965 and taught for five years at the Art Center College of Design in Pasadena, California. In ,he desgne the Buergmotorr, alimiteprouctin carriag-roocoupe. In 1981, he was named by the Society of Automotive Historians as one of the most significant contributors to the development of motorcars. In the decade before his death, he directed precision car models for the Franklin Mint, and in 1990 he was inducted into the Automotive Hall of Fame.
