In 1991, Business Week magazine sponsored the annual IDSA Industrial Design Excellence Awar (IDEA), he celeratig ten yr en, an fe the wr i , illustrated article in the magazine. This annual recognition of industrial design in a major business magazine benefited both the manufacturers, the 800 to 1,000 design offices in the country, and the individual designers. The number of awards would vary from year to year, but by 1997 IDEA would confer a total of 141 gold, silver, and bronze awards.

Graduate students at the Pratt Institute in Brooklyn formed the Association of Women Industrial Designers (AWID) in 1991. Their key collaborative project would be a 1994 exhibition at the Pratt Manhattan Gallery in New York and a related catalog with the title, Goddess is in the Details: Product Design by Women.

H.Creston Doner, who opened a design office in Akron, Ohio, in 1932, and was a longtime design director at Libby-Owens-Ford Glass Company, passed away in 199l in Toledo at age 88. He had retired from LOF after 30 years in 1968, and from 1970 he was a member of the Toledo Society of Communicating Arts. At the end of 1991, the Soviet Union also came to an end and collapsed to become a republic of individual states, ending the Cold War and leading to a better life for Russian citizens.

Clare Hodgman, who started his career with General Motors in the early 1930s, died in 1992 at the age of 81. He had retired from Hodgman & Bourke in 1969 and moved to Daytona, Florida, where he excelled in contemporary watercolors. George Walker, who began his career in the automotive industry in 1928, died at the age of 97 in 1993. In 1959, he was a guest challenger on the TV panel show To Tell the Truth. After retiring from Ford in 196l, he moved to Gulf Stream, Florida, where he became mayor in 1976. By 1985, he was in Tucson, Arizona, where he was interviewed by the Ford Design History Center.

In 1994, President Bill Clinton presented eight Presidential Awards for Design Excellence to designers, including industrial designers. The automobile industry rebounded in 1994 to capture 75 percent of the U.S. market share, and for the first time in 14 years led the world in car production. But many other corporations were in serious trouble. One hundred fifty-one Fortune 500 companies, or 30 percent, lost money in 1993. The previous decade had been a litany of mergers and acquisitions, as many companies sought to convert failing markets into cash.

By this time, computer aided design (CAD) was driving the manufacturing, engineering, and industrial design of products. Installation of CAD technology was revolutionizing inhouse and consulting design firms across the industry. The historical restrictions of machine tools that controlled design were now replaced by computer-aided tooling that enabled designers to specify complex shapes without regard to those earlier physical restrictions. CAD proficiency was a prerequisite job requirement for all industrial designers and for anyone in the product development field. The days of hand-drawn renderings of product concepts were over, replaced by realistic computer-generated illustrations.

In 1995, Stowe Myers, who had been hired by Norman Bel Geddes in 1932 to illustrate Bel Geddes's book, Horizons, died in 1995 at age 89 in New Orleans. He had been a member of the faculty at the Institute of Design of the Illinois Institute of Technology, where he taught

a class entitled "Design Counsel." Myers retired in 1976 to paint and was named official artist of the U.S. Air Force in 1978. In 1988, he moved to New Orleans, Louisiana. His papers are at the Syracuse University Library, Special Collections.

In 1995, Walter Dorwin Teague Associates designed Power Rangers toys. Brooks Stevens, one of the founders of the Society o Industrial Designers in 1944, passed away that same year at age 84. His son, Kipp K. Stevens (b. 1951), had joined Brooks Stevens Design Associates in 1978 as vice president and in 1979 had become president and CEO. From 1983 until his death, Brooks taught design at the Milwaukee Institute of Art and Design.

In 1996, John M. Little, who had opened an industrial design office in Minneapolis in 1931, passed away in 1996 at the age of 90. He had joined Harold Van Doren's Toledo office in 1936 and managed it after the war. He was a lecturer at such universities as Harvard, Lehigh, Cincinnati, Toledo, and Wisconsin. Robert E. Bourke, designer of Studebakers for Loewy, passed away that same year at the age of 80. After his partner, Clare Hodgman, retired from their partnership, Hodgman & Bourke, in 1969, Bourke had moved his office to Westport, Connecticut, where his home was located.

![](images/f1c5434ff57a350cf4c4eda80b257bd9608474e7987dfddb75de7a1a25e2cb90.jpg)  
Brooks Stevens (Brooks Stevens Archive, Milwaukee Art Museum, gift of the Brooks Stevens Family and the Milwaukee Institute of Art and Design).

In Chicago, the firm of Goldsmith Yamasaki Specht (GYS), with Paul Specht as the last principal, was sold in 1997 to Sundberg-Ferar, for whom Specht served as senior design council until 2000. Sundberg-Ferar maintained four offices in the United States and one in the Far East. Also in 1997, the Cooper-Hewitt National Design Museum opened a retrospective, Henry Dreyfuss Directing Design: The Industrial Designer and His Work. Concurrently, Rizzoli International published Henry Dreyfuss, Industrial Designer: The Man in the Brown Suit, by Russell Flinchum, of the museum.

A 1997 Time agzine article, Where the Jobs Are, predicted76,000 new product dsn jos by 0n lul de o esi ancompensation.The orecast ws base n gures rom he Bureau  Labor Stati, wic aaea and were expected to number 21,000 by 2005.

In 1999, Alfons Bach, who had established his furniture design firm in New York City in 132, passed away at age 95. He had developed the Ridgeway Shopping Center in Stamford, Connecticut, in 1945, and after relocating to Palm Beach, Florida, in 1959, he developed the PalTrail PlazanDelray Beach.Heretire  Pensacola, Florida, in 9, where contd .Ie work at the Visual Arts Gallery of Pensacola Junior College. An extensive archive of his drawing and papers is at the Cooper-Hewit National Design Musumin New York.

Belle Kogan, a successful female industrial designer who opened her own office in New York in 1932, died in 2000 in Israel at the age of 98. She had closed her office in 1970; moved to Israel under contract with K.V. Design, a subsidiary of the Koor Corporation; and set up a studio offering comprehensive design services. In 1972 she left the studio to work as a consultant.

In te March 0, , issu  Tiaazi, a artice, "TheRebirh Desn,"su titled, "The Redesigning of America," claimed that form was the dominant characteristic of many product desigs.Fors were ot, voluptuous, highly ergonomic nd even exotic. The black boxes" of the 1970s were definitely out. Bauhaus geometric shapes were out. The color and form of functional details such as keys, levers, and switches had become the new decorative ornamentation of the era.

The turn of the century was a big year for retrospectives. The Metropolitan Museum of Art opened an exhibition, American Modern, 1925-1940: Design for a New Age, which traveled the country for several years. The Bard Graduate Center for Studies in the Decorative Arts mounted another exhibit, Women Designers in the U.S.A., 1900-2000: Diversiy and Difference, along with a related book, which included work by a number of female designers.

NASA launched the International Space Station in 2000, with the crew quarters designed by Walter Dorwin Teague Associates (WDTA). The station is the area of a football field, weighs 861,804 pounds, and has been in continuous operation since 2000. In 2001, WDTA would work with Microsoft in the design of their first gaming console, the Xbox.

By 2000, the Industrial Designers Society of America (IDSA) had 3,300 members in 28 chapters around the country, and by 2001, it had relocated to new headquarters near Dulles airport in Washington, D.C. In 2002, IDSA and the China Industrial Design Association (CIDA) sponsored a China-America education conference in Beijing. U.S. companies had been using Asian manufacturers for years to produce products at lower costs, and the number of U.S. designers traveling to China, South Korea, and Taiwan was increasing dramatically. China was producing thousands of industrial design graduates and competing with American practitioners.

Arthur BecVar, who began his career in industrial design in Cleveland in 1935, died in 2003, at the age of 91. He had retired from General Electric as head of design in Louisville, Kentucky, in 1976, after more than 30 years of service. His papers are at the Syracuse University Library, Special Collections.

Also in April 2003, the Cooper-Hewitt National Design Museum in New York tried to capture the current state ofdesign with an exhibition, National Design Triennial: Inside Design Now. The museum found it difficult to categorize a dominant style in the eclectic menu of what many called "postmodern," so the catalog simply referred to the body of work as being "by contemporary American designers." Some designs strayed so far from the geometric Bauhaus style of the 1950s that a magazine editor remarked, "Ornament is back and we've got to relearn how to use it."

W. Dorwin Teague, Jr., son of Walter Dorwin Teague, who started his career with his father in 1931, passed away at the age of 94 in 2004. He had relocated his Englewood Cliffs office to his home in the early 1970s and continued to design ski equipment, a water-powered toothbrush, a bicycle brake, and a snowmaking apparatus, among many others, until 1993, when he arranged for his papers to be donated to the Henry Ford Museum. Renowned designer John Barratt joined the WDTA team as CEO. Eugene "Bob" Gregorie, head of design at

Ford from 1935 to 1946, also died in 2004 at the age of 96. In 1943 he had retired to design and sail his boat, and in the mid-1970s he had relocated to St. Augustine, Florida.

In 2004, the annual Industrial Design Excellence Awards (IDEA) became international in scope, open to all designers around the world. Of the 1,300 entries, 305 were from other countries, clear recognition that industrial design had become an integrated global profession. A 2004 IDSA survey of 183 corporate design offices reported that design increased product sales by 47 percent and increased sales prices by 32 percent, while only increasing product cost by 24 percent. The numbers suggested that industrial design was still an essential business tool for manufacturing profitability.

By 2006, the Industrial Design Excellence Awards ceremony at IDSA's annual conference was becoming quite sophisticated, with black ties optional, and with an ambience not unlike Hollywood's Oscar Awards. It was the number of gold awards to design teams from Asia that was a surprise: 25 percent of the total. This was compared to 8 percent in 2004 and 2005.

Early in 2006, China's president, Hu Jintau, at a national conference on technology, had confirmed the country's new emphasis on design and innovation, stating, "Innovation is the core of the nation's competitiveness. Only with strong capacity for innovation can a country win the initiative in the international competition." American educators teaching design in China confirmed that there was excitement in the air in China, reminiscent of the postwar U.S. attitude of "can do" in the 1950s and 1960s. Over the next few years, Asian design would increase in quality and quantity, and American designers would often be working in China, Taiwan, and South Korea.3

A permanent Raymond Loewy Gallery, emphasizing Loewy's transportation designs, opened in 2006 at the O. Winston Link Museum in Roanoke, Virginia. The museum was housed in the restored Norfolk & Western Railway Station, originally designed by Loewy's firm in 1949. Loewy's daughter, Laurence Loewy, was instrumental in establishing the gallery. That same year, Loewy was celebrated as "the Father of American Design" in a symposium held by the Hagley Museum in Wilmington, Delaware, where Loewy is prominent in the Pennsylvania Railroad Corporation records held by the museum.

In 2007, the name of IDSA's Industrial Design Excellence Awards competition was changed to the International Design Excellence Awards to recognize that the competition,

![](images/ed7f88dcb4b69c0cf9b5590257aff0318a109058d899a42cfb0f518fc2b3e7a2.jpg)  
Viktor Schreckengost, ca. 2000 (photograph by Herb Ascherman).

and industrial design in general, was now global in scope.

In 2008, Adolph "Al" Lambach, Sr., who had begun his career as a furniture designer in Grand Rapids, Michigan, in 1932, died at the age of 96 in California. As a founding member of the American Designers' Institute (ADI) in 1938, he was a member of organized design, including IDSA, for a total of 70 years, probably a record. He had worked as a freelance furniture designer until his retirement in 1982, when he carved replicas of famous ships for his collection and taught at the Art Center College of Design. He donated his many drawings to the industrial desgn collections at the Hagley Library in Wilmington, Delaware, and to Syracuse University Library, Special Collections.

Also in 2008, Viktor Schreckengost, who developed an einustral de roram  heevelan InstiuA in 193, passed away at the age of 101. In 2000, he had been honorewih opectiv exhibion n ctalgat theClevean Museum of Art, called Viktor Schreckengost and 20th Century Design. Soon after, the Viktor Schreckengost Foundation was established in Cleveland. In 2006, a Viktor Schreckengost National Centennial Exhibition celebrated his 100th birthday by highlighting his work in 140 simultaneous nationwide exhibitions. That year, he was also honored at the White House as a recipient of the National Medal of Arts for "enduring contributions to the artistic life" of the United States.

By 2008, Busines Week's annual publication f the IDEA international desin awards noted that a strong sense of social responsibility ran through many of the winning entries, icudinga wheelchair that freed hands frdisablathletes; aninexpensive computer laptop for children in Third World countries; and a book that helped governments to design clearer, moreuseful ballots an polling placesignage.Desn buzzwords suchas green,"ustainably environmental," anddisability" permeated design competitions, conferences, education, and debates. In 2010, sponsorship of IDEA design awards would be taken over by Fast Company magazine.

As Barack Obama was elected the first African-American president in 2008, professor EicAndsnCrn MelonUniveiya leceI in - a H Achievement Award at the White House as part of the Cooper-Hewitt National Design Museum awards program. Harrison, the first African-American executive hired at the company's Chicago headquarters, was hired by Sears in 1961 and worked there until his retirement in 1993.

By 2009, there were about 50,000 industrial designers, of which about 3,500 were members of IDSA. Median annual salaries were around \$55,000. This was at the depth of the severe financial recession, when unemployment reached 10 percent and Chrysler and General Motors received bailout funds from the government. But industrial design is traditionally a recessionproof profession, and in 2009 there were over 20 position advertisements on the IDSA website, and thousands more posted on the Internet. IDSA had a staff of 12, an annual budget of about \$5 million, and 27 chapters in five national districts. By 2009, in the annual International Design Excellence Awards, there would be 1,631 entries, and 66 of 150 awards would go to 15 countries other than the United States.

In 201l, the U.S. Postal Service issued stamps honoring 12 of the nation's most important and influential industrial designers with an image of a design created by each designer. They were Frederick Hurten Rhead (1936 Fiesta dinnerware), Walter Dorwin Teague (1934 Baby Brownie camera); Norman Bel Geddes (1940 Patriot radio), Raymond Loewy (1934 pencil sharpener), Donald Deskey (1927 table lamp), Gilbert Rohde (1933 clock), Greta Von Nessen (1951 Anywhere lamp), Russel Wright (1950 Highlight/Pinchware tableware), Henry Dreyfuss (1937 Model 302 AT&T telephone), Peter Müller-Munk (1935 Normandie pitcher), Dave Chapman (1947 streamlined sewing machines), and Elliot Noyes (1961 Selectric typewriter).

They are all gone now, those extraordinary designers of the Machine Age, and many are forgotten. Their proud legacy is today's prospering profession of industrial design, which they invented in the 1930s, practically out of thin air, by transforming our everyday environment of 19th-century, unwieldy, unattractive, mechanical devices, from automobiles to toasters, office equipment, and kitchens, into sleek and attractive works of art that enhance our daily experiences emotionally and visually, as well as functionally. Today, we regard our man-made physical environment of mechanical, electronic, and digital devices as an unremarkable and a natural result of modern technology, as if they magically appeared out of nowhere.

But in 1930, neither technology nor engineering knew how to shape the products it produced by the millions into forms that were designed to address human convenience, utilization, or emotional needs that made them attractive and desirable to consumers. Ordinary artists, the Machine Age designers of the 1930s, bravely ventured from their comfortable lives into a new world of complex, dirty factories, business offices, and brutal sales competition in order to transform everyday products into modern design, which encouraged people in the depths of the Depression to visualize a better future and lifted a failing manufacturing industry into profitability. A recovered economy led to the highest standard of living in the world after World War II, and the machine age designers established an educational system and business models for the new profession that became integrated into the modern world of manufacturing, production, and sales.

Today's industrial designers perform the same services for manufacturers and consumers that the machine designers of the 1930s did, only better and faster. The process remains the same, except that instead of hand-drawn illustrations of proposed designs and manually prepared mechanical drawings, computers now perform these tasks with the instructions of designers. Prototype models are often not built by hand but generated into three dimensions by computers. Contrary to many pessimists in the 1960s, who concluded that all consumer products had already been "designed" to perfection, technology continues to generate new products that need to be shaped and modified for human use and understanding, and this is likely to continue indefinitely.

Many of the organizations and designers mentioned in this book ceased operations over the years due to death or business closure, but, surprisingly, quite a few of the design organizations of the founders survive to this day, a tribute to the tenacity of their founders and a source of pride to their heirs. They continue to design the world about us.

In 2006, Walter Dorwin Teague Associates, founded in 1926, now called Teague, in Seattle, Washington, celebrated its 80th anniversary as a leading force in industrial design and its 60-year working relationship with Boeing, with a Frank Gehry-designed Experience Music Project held in downtown Seattle, and an elegant publication of Teague history and designs, Design This Day, the same title as his 1940 book. In 201l, Teague's relationship continued with Boeing with the interior design of the 787. That same year, Teague acquired a Munichbased design studio to expand into the European market. Teague staff in 2012 totaled 250 designers and support staff.

In 2012, the Art and Colour Section of General Motors that Harley Earl founded in 1927 celebrated it 85th anniversary, and it continues to design new cars each year, most of them stil "treamlined, but o longercalle that. Similar earlycorporate desgn organizations, such as at Ford, Chrysler, General Electric, Westinghouse, the Radio Corporation of America, Heri have chang names through mergers oracquisitions, andstll others have gone out  busi ness.

Suer-Feake  hu firm in Walled Lake, Michigan, with a 15,000-square-foot studio and a 7,000-square-foot prowas a Precedent golf cart for Ingersoll Rand's Club Car division. Clients since 2003 include Coca-Cola, General Electric, General Motors, Procter & Gamble, Sears, and Whirlpool.

Peter Müller-Munk Associates (PMMA), founded by its namesake in 1938, continues as and public spaces.

Brooks Stevens, Inc., the organization founded in 1934 by its namesake, is still an innovative and successful industrial design firm with offices in Allenton, Wisconsin, and St. Paul, Minnesota, and with dozens of well-known clients including Kohler, Harley-Davidson, General Electric, Rubbermaid, and Herman Miller. In 2007, the firm merged with Ingenium Product Develoment, bigin togeher ver experts. Recnt products clude the 00 Ss

John Deere lawn tractor, the Remington Code Shaver, and Milwaukee's Tilt-Lok circular saw.

As of 2005, with about 10 employees, Henry Dreyfuss Associates, founded in 1929, contia w ao a ents,  w inAAro Mi William Crookes as president.

Nes l s t and manufactures historical and contemporary lamps in Mamaroneck, New York.

The firm founded by Dave Chapman in 1936, Dave Chapman, Inc., had been reorganized 0 s Goldsmih amasaki Speht GY In , Fi Design, a desig fir  Kans I  S partner. A partner of First Design, David McCormick, became president of GYS and retained the Chicago Office until 1999, when it was sold to Sundberg-Ferar.

Desk Itat ngIB teclizi ri with headquarters in Cincinnati, Ohio, was founded in 1978 as Deskey, Inc., a division of Donald Deskey Associates of New York, which had been founded in the mid-1940s by Donald Deskey. The companies were combined around 1990 into Deskey Associates, Inc. The New York office was closed in September of2001. Family trusts of famous designers such as Norman Bel Geddes and Raymond Loewy still merchandize images and books about their namesakes, but they are no longer active design organizations.

Ford and Earl Associates, founded in 1964 by Harley Earl and Walter B. Ford, is still a design firm in Troy, Michigan, but now specializes in architecture and interior design.

This is a pretty good survival record of design firms founded by designers of the Machine Age. We also have their classic, iconic designs, their books, and perhaps most of all, their legacy of today's creative, innovative, and successful industrial design profession, which continues to shape our physical environment of tools, machines, furnishings and equipment in a manner appropriate to our use, needs, and desires. We will continue to be inspired by the courage of the first machine designers to envision the modern world when there was none, and to implement it into reality despite the Great Depression of the 1930s. It was an extraordinary accomplishment that is unlikely to ever be repeated. The guidebook of the 1939 World's Fair probably said it better than anyone could:

The true poets of the twentieth century are the designers, architects, and the engineers who glimpse some inner vision, create some beautiful figment of the imagination, and then translate it into valid reality for the world to enjoy.4

TS Gantz, Carroll, 1931- 139 Founders of .G36 American industrial 2014 design

![](images/f734695b5ad203d3e35a2d0672a83ac78a22bb0e3c0a946e4a5b8aa276f29068.jpg)

s the Great Depression started in 1929, several dozen creative individuals from a variety of artistic fields, including theatre, rapi hin  uie p new profession. Responding to unprecedented public and industry demand for new styles, these artists entered the industrial world during what was called the "Machine Age," to introduce "modern design" to the external appearance and form of mass-produced, functional, mechanical consumer products formerly not considered art.

The popular designs by these "machine designers" increased sales and profits dramatically for manufacturers, which helped the economy to recover; established a new profession, industrial design; and within a decade, changed American products from mechanical monstrosities into sleek, modern forms expressive of the future. This book is about those industrial designers and how they founded, developed, educated and organized today's profession of more than 50,000 practitioners.

CARROLL GANTZ is a profesional industrial designer who holds several dozen patents. A long-time Black & Decker design director, and a Carnegie Mellon University professor, he is a past president of the Industrial Designers Society of America (IDSA). He lives on Seabrook Island in South Carolina.

## McFarland
